DOK2 Knowledge Tree

- Current FinLit Education Methods/Resources/Platforms
  - **[1] Classroom Lecturing/Basic Simulation - MOOCs**
    - Online Modules
      - [Khan Academy](https://www.khanacademy.org/college-careers-more/financial-literacy) (free, online, range of topics, not dynamic/adaptive/personalized/experiential)
        - Perfect example of modular learning
      - MoneySkill
        - Summary [here](https://afsaef.org/wp-content/uploads/2025/03/AFSAEF-MoneySKILL-Flyer-2025.pdf)
        - Audience: middle school through college (adaptable for homeschoolers and adults)
        - Content: income, expenses, saving, investing, credit, insurance (37 modules for high school/college and 12 for middle school)
        - Structure: pre/post test, customizable modules, integrated quizzes and simulations
        - Access: free and ad-free, audio options and mobile compatibility 
        - Novelty: regular updates to reflect current trends and info
        - Results: avg grade increase of 60%, linked to increased chances of students opening bank accounts and FAFSA applications
        - Benefits: 
          - Accessible 
          - Significant improvement (60%)
          - Novelty
        - Shortcomings
          - Middle school and older
          - Basic curriculum
          - Modular (not experiential/sim-based)
            - Platform feels segmented, not very gamified, is not a "chocolate-covered broccoli" where students are focused on having fun, and don't realize they are learning
    - Gamified Classroom
      - HandsOnBanking: money management simulation teaching important financial education concepts such as budgeting, comparison shopping, evaluating needs, wants, and trade offs [here](https://handsonbanking.org/wp-content/uploads/documents/Virtual-Experience-Implementation-Guide-2.pdf) and [here](https://handsonbanking.org/handsonbankingexperience/)
        - Program is a virtual classroom simulation, not a self-contained online game or app. It's designed for live, guided use over video conferencing or in-person class, using PDFs and a slide presentation
          - Instructor-led: The simulation is run by a teacher or facilitator who guides students through a PowerPoint-style presentation with embedded decision points ("stations")
          - Materials-Based - Students receive:
            - A participant profile (emailed individually)
            - A budget worksheet (fillable PDF or printable)
            - A Budgeting Tips handout
          - Presentation-Driven: The core activity takes place via a slide-based presentation shared through screen share (Zoom, Google Meet, etc.) or projected in a classroom
          - Manual Input: Students manually update their budget worksheets based on the decisions they make at each station
          - No centralized online platform: There is no login portal, user dashboard, or real-time automated scoring system. All interactions rely on the student’s engagement with emailed documents and live facilitation
        - Elements of program:
          - Participant Profiles: Each student receives a unique profile detailing career, income, education, family status, and financial obligations (like student loans)
          - Budget Worksheet: A running ledger where students track spending, income, and remaining balance throughout the simulation
          - 13 Station Slides: Students visit virtual “stations” (housing, transportation, childcare, insurance, groceries) where they choose among lifestyle options with different costs
          - Chance Cards: Inject unexpected events (hail damage, birthday gifts) to simulate real-life financial uncertainty
          - Retirement & Loan Planning: Automatically built into the worksheet to reinforce the importance of long-term financial thinking
        - Implementation Steps:
          - Before the Session
            - Distribute unique student profiles and budgeting worksheets
            - Send Budgeting tips handout
            - Prepare the presentation slides and simulation flow (~1 hour)
          - During the Simulation
            - Guide students through each station and have them record their choices and expenses
            - Introduce chance cards at appropriate moments
            - Encourage students to stay within budget (if they run out of money, they must revisit choices)
          - Debrief
            - Discuss takeaways, budgeting strategies, and how education and family affected financial outcomes
            - Reflect on the real-world implications of financial choices
          - Feedback
            - Collect feedback via the provided form to improve future sessions
      - [Charles Schwab Lesson](https://content.schwab.com/moneywise/PDFs/Jami_Witherell_Financial_Literacy_Lesson_Plan.pdf): lesson plan similar to MCE/JA Finance park, game of life (category 1 - basic simulation)
        - Classroom rearranged to resemble a life-size game of life, use ledgers to track transactions, roll dice to move around room, and have time to reflect following the lesson
        - Likely not as comprehensive/immersive/interactive as JA or MCE (JA = superior form of classroom modification + IRL simulation)
  - **[2] Niche Mini Games (Simple Games)**
    - Investment Simulations
      - [Wall Street Survivor:](https://www.wallstreetsurvivor.com/stock-market-game/) virtual stock trading game, players manage 100k of assets
      - [The Stock Market Game](https://www.stockmarketgame.org/)
    - Game-Based/Gamified Learning (more structured than sim-based)
      - [NGPF Arcade](https://www.ngpf.org/arcade/): Many different simple games involving money (limited options, simple role-playing interface, (free, online, fun, limited games) - middle and high school FL games covering a range of topics, shows updates on the status of mandatory FL classes
        - College debt game similar to Spent
        - Have games involving budgeting, investing, insurance, crypto, credit, etc 
      - [Zogo mobile app](https://zogo.com/): gamified micro-learning content (similar to Duolingo), targeting extrinsic motivation
        - Incorporating game elements like coins, streaks, XP, hearts, etc
        - Topics:
          - Save Money
          - Apply for Credit
          - Repay Debt
          - Choose a Financial Institution
          - Open a Bank Account
          - Shop Smartly
        - Somewhat of a category breaker 
          - Simple games = Short-Lived. Most gamified apps fail because points and badges eventually become boring.
          - The Zogo Fix: They replaced "fake points" with real money.
          - The Mechanism: Users earn "Pineapples" which they exchange for actual gift cards (Amazon, Starbucks, etc.).
          - Why it works: Students don't stay for the learning; they stay for the $5 gift card. Zogo effectively bribes the user to pay attention. This allows them to boast retention numbers that normal Simple Games can never achieve
        - The Business Model Innovation (B2B2C)
          - Standard Games: Sell directly to users (B2C) or show ads.
          - Zogo: Sells to Credit Unions. The Credit Union pays Zogo to white-label the app because they want to capture the Gen Z audience. The "bribe" (gift cards) is essentially the Credit Union's Customer Acquisition Cost (CAC).
      - [Banzai](https://banzai.org/course/5766104/home#/): games/modules/simulations with elementary FinLit unit
        - Free, takes ~8 hours to complete, and can include printed materials as well as digital
        - Free, fun, online, includes game + simulations + calculators for different ages
        - Elementary school unit - lemonade stand game (managing a budget + assigning money to either spending, IOU, lemonade, saving)
        - [Middle school unit](https://banzai.org/course/5766104/home#/categories/banzai-teen) - more complex life options (banking, consumer smarts, vocab practice, etc)
      - [Spent](https://playspent.org/html/): game that presents player with a series of choices (like episode) following the experiences of a low income person. The game presents difficult financial decisions to the player, challenging them to survive a month on ~$300. 
        - Teaches more about trade-offs + the difficulties of poverty + emergency/unplanned expenses
        - Clean, simple interface, but limited options and can't really play twice
        - Best for middle school and older, simulation where the player is running out of money. Each time a student makes a decision, the program explains the consequences of that choice. The game ends when the player runs out of money.
      - [GiveGet](https://give-get.finance411.com/): FinLit game used to teach budgeting, saving, and borrowing
        - Parent company: [Finance411](https://finance411.com/)
        - Format: Digital board game where students move around the board, making decisions about money. The game focuses on learning basic financial concepts and practicing good money behaviors.
        - Gameplay Core: The fundamental mechanic revolves around making decisions to "Give" money (for essential and unexpected expenses, savings, or investments) to potentially "Get" money back.
        - Learning Focus:
          - Budgeting: Students must understand essential and unexpected expenses by establishing an emergency fund.
          - Investment Categories: The game uses four key spaces (called the Four Corners) to represent different financial concepts:
            - Piggy: Represents a guaranteed savings account (e.g., checking accounts, CDs).
            - Chart: Represents a non-guaranteed investment account (e.g., individual stocks, index funds).
            - Ticket: Represents speculation or gambling (e.g., lottery, sports betting).
            - House: Represents asset ownership (e.g., primary residence, business venture).
          - Borrowing**:** Represented by the "Pillars" space, illustrating how a loan can be positive if there is a plan for repayment.
        - Goal**:** A student wins by either causing their opponent to go bankrupt or by being the first player to achieve a net worth of at least $100. The game is also used for national competitions with scholarship opportunities.
      - [Nova Financial Lab](https://www.pbs.org/wgbh/nova/labs/lab/financial/research/home/games): 3 scenarios to choose from, teaching opportunity cost, budgeting, and financial growth
        - Setting = caring for a virtual pet
        - UI is somewhat confusing - lots to look at and not super easy to tell what buttons to click at what time
        - 
          - 
  - **[3] Sandbox Economy Games**
    - The Sims
      - Release Year: 2000 (Original), 2014 (The Sims 4)
      - Consistent Players: ~8–10 million Monthly Active Users (estimated across all platforms; Steam data alone shows ~30k concurrent daily, but the majority play on EA App/Console).
      - The Game Loop:
        - Needs Management: Maintain hunger, hygiene, energy, and social meters.
        - Labor: Go to work to earn Simoleons
        - Upgrade: Buy better furniture/house upgrades which drain "Needs" slower (e.g., a better bed restores energy faster).
        - Repeat: With more free time (from efficient furniture), focus on skills/hobbies.
      - Dominant Drives: CD4 (Ownership) and CD3 (Creativity)
        - The White Hat (Positive) Loop:
          - CD4: Ownership & Possession (The Anchor): This is the strongest drive. The player essentially "owns" the humans. The more you play, the more furniture, skills, and career promotions your Sims acquire. The feeling of "I built this house" is paramount.
          - CD3: Empowerment of Creativity & Feedback: You have infinite choices in how to build a house or structure a life. The feedback is instant: buy a stove -> Sim eats better -> Sim is happy.
          - CD2: Development & Accomplishment: Watching the career bar progress from "Mailroom Clerk" to "CEO."
        - The Black Hat (Negative/Urgency) Loop:
          - CD8: Loss & Avoidance: If you don't pay bills, the Repoman comes (Loss of assets). If you don't eat, you die (Loss of progress).
        - Builder's Takeaway: The addiction comes from the CD4/CD8 balance. The fear of losing what you "Own" (CD4) drives the labor required to avoid the "Loss" (CD8).
      - Engagement Score: High Retention / Low Intensity. Players often "binge" this game for weeks and then drop it for months.
      - Builder’s Note: The genius of The Sims is Maslow’s Hierarchy of Needs as a game mechanic. The player starts desperate for basics (food/toilet) and progressively unlocks the freedom to pursue "Self Actualization" (painting, astronomy, romance). 
        - Lesson: Make "survival" annoying but "thriving" rewarding - similar to real life
    - Animal Crossing
      - Release Year: 2020 (Series started 2001)
      - Consistent Players: Peaked at ~40M copies sold. Currently sustains a lower but cult-like daily user base (estimated ~100k–200k daily concurrents globally) due to seasonal events.
      - The Game Loop:
        - Daily Check-in: Check turnip prices, dig up fossils, shake trees.
        - Collection: Catch bugs/fish to donate to the museum or sell for Bells.
        - Investment: Pay off loan to expand house or build island infrastructure.
        - Wait: Real-time clock means you must wait until "tomorrow" for buildings to finish.
      - Dominant Drives: CD6 (Scarcity) and CD5 (Social)
        - The White Hat (Positive) Loop:
          - CD4: Ownership & Possession: Collecting every fossil, bug, and fish to fill the museum. It is a completionist’s dream.
          - CD5: Social Influence & Relatedness: The "Turnip Economy" forces players to talk outside the game. "Hey, my island is buying turnips for 500 bells!" triggers massive social coordination.
        - The Black Hat (Negative/Urgency) Loop:
          - CD6: Scarcity & Impatience (The Hook): This is the game's masterstroke. You cannot speed up time (without cheating). If a shop closes at 10 PM, it is closed. If you miss a seasonal fish in March, you wait until next year. This creates artificial value through scarcity.
          - CD8: Loss & Avoidance: If you don't play for a month, your island gets covered in weeds and your favorite neighbors move away.
        - Builder's Takeaway: Use CD6 to pace the economy. By limiting how much a player can do in one day, you prevent burnout and extend the game's lifespan from weeks to years.
      - Engagement Score: Daily Habit Forming. It isn't played for 8 hours straight, but for 30 minutes every single day for years.
      - Builder’s Note: Use "Friction as a Feature." By forcing players to wait 24 real-world hours for a house upgrade, the reward feels significantly more valuable than if it were instant. Anticipation creates value.
        - Perfect example of appointment dynamics in games
    - Minecraft
      - Release Year: 2011
      - Consistent Players: ~212 Million Monthly Active Users (2025 estimate).
      - The Game Loop:
        - Gather: Punch trees, mine stone/coal.
        - Craft: Convert raw resources into tools to gather faster/deeper.
        - Build/Survive: Construct shelter to survive the night (scarcity) or automated farms (abundance).
        - Explore: Venture further for rarer resources (diamonds)
      - Dominant Drives: CD3 (Creativity) and CD7 (Curiosity)
        - The White Hat (Positive) Loop:
          - CD3: Empowerment of Creativity & Feedback: The ultimate sandbox. The economy is entirely player-driven (barter systems). The "LEGO" mechanic allows for infinite reconfiguration of the world.
          - CD2: Development & Accomplishment: Moving from wooden tools to stone, then iron, then diamond, then netherite. The power curve is steep and rewarding.
        - The Black Hat (Negative/Urgency) Loop:
          - CD7: Unpredictability & Curiosity: Exploring a cave is dangerous. You don't know if you will find diamonds or a Creeper. This slot-machine mechanic keeps mining exciting.
          - CD8: Loss & Avoidance: If you die in lava, you lose *everything* in your inventory. This high-stakes penalty gives value to the gear you earned via CD2.
        - Builder's Takeaway: CD7 is essential for "grinding." If mining stone was predictable and safe, it would be boring work. Add an element of danger/surprise to the labor.
      - Engagement Score: Infinite. Because the content is user-generated (mods, servers, builds), the game never officially "ends."
      - Builder’s Note: The "Lego Effect." If you let users build the world, they become emotionally attached to the server because they left a mark on it. *Lesson: Give players tools, not just content.*
    - Roblox
      - Release Year: 2006
      - Consistent Players: ~88 Million Daily Active Users (2025).
      - The Game Loop:
        - Socialize: Join a server where friends are hanging out.
        - Roleplay/Grind: Engage in simple loops (running a pizza shop, obstacle course).
        - Earn/Buy: Get currency (Robux) to buy skins/avatars.
        - Flex: Show off your unique avatar to peers.
      - Dominant Drives: CD5 (Social) and CD3 (Empowerment)
        - The White Hat (Positive) Loop:
          - CD5: Social Influence & Relatedness: This is the primary engine. If your friend has a cool avatar item (that costs money), you want it too. The economy is driven by envy and belonging.
          - CD3: Empowerment of Creativity: For developers, the ability to script games (Lua) and monetize them is pure CD3.
        - The Black Hat (Negative/Urgency) Loop:
          - CD6: Scarcity & Impatience: "Limited" items (UGC Limiteds) that are only sold for a short time or in small quantities create a frantic secondary market (reselling).
        - Builder's Takeaway: CD5 is the best way to monetize Gen Z. They care less about "power" (stats) and more about "expression" (skins/cosmetics) to signal status to peers.
      - Engagement Score: Viral/Social. Retention is driven by peer pressure (all your friends are playing *Brookhaven*, so you do too).
      - Builder’s Note: User-Generated Economy. Roblox wins because it allows players to be producers, not just consumers. 
        - Lesson: If you can make your players rich (even in fake currency), they will work for you.
          - Carries over to UGC idea for Aurum - let players build virtual interconnected worlds with their own in-game economies (leveraging ownership and social drives)
    - World of Warcraft (MMORPGs)
      - Release Year: 2004
      - Consistent Players: ~7.25 Million Subscribers
      - The Game Loop:
        - Combat: Kill monsters/complete quests
        - Reward: Get Loot (Items) and XP
        - Power Up: Equip new items to kill stronger monsters
        - Social Raiding: Coordinate with 10-20 people to defeat complex bosses
      - Dominant Drives: CD2 (Accomplishment) and CD6 (Scarcity)
        - The White Hat (Positive) Loop:
          - CD2: Development & Accomplishment: The visual representation of progress (Level 1 -> Level 60). The economy supports this: you need gold to buy the gear to get the achievement.
          - CD1: Epic Meaning & Calling: "Horde vs. Alliance." Players feel they are contributing to a faction war, giving economic contributions (crafting war supplies) a higher purpose.
        - The Black Hat (Negative/Urgency) Loop:
          - CD6: Scarcity & Impatience: Rare loot drops (1% chance). The entire economy revolves around items that are hard to get.
          - CD7: Unpredictability: You raid for hours not knowing *if* the item will drop. This "Variable Reward Schedule" is identical to gambling.
        - Builder's Takeaway: Combine CD1 (Narrative) with CD2 (Stats). If players feel they are saving the world *and* getting rich doing it, the grind feels like a heroic duty.
      - Engagement Score: Sunk Cost Sticky. Players refuse to quit because they have invested thousands of hours into their character.
        - Leverages loss & avoidance drive
      - Builder’s Note: "The Skinner Box." Random rewards (RNG) are more addictive than predictable ones. If a boss drops the best sword *every* time, it's boring. If it drops it 5% of the time, players will grind the same boss for weeks.
        - Leverages unpredictability & curiosity drive
        - Seems to be lacking in effective scaffolding for new players - people join and become overwhelmed easily within the advanced ecosystem of the game
  - **[4] CompSims**
    - Sim-Based Learning (gaming overlap, more experiential/open-ended)
      - [MCE](https://myclassroomeconomy.org/): (free, online) 
        - Implementation of MCE did not increase or reduce math scores. Evidence shows that **MCE is an effective mechanism to teach financial literacy among elementary-school students**. Likely not enough substance/content for high school, but good for younger students to be introduced to personal finance concepts.
        - Includes tasks like (1) earning salaries for performing assigned tasks; (2) managing expenses, (3) earning bonuses/incurring fines; and (4) making spending decisions at in-classroom stores or auctions. Initially requires ~3 hr of teacher setup. 
        - Students who participated in the MyCE had 6% higher scores in financial knowledge than the control group. [https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/full/10.1177/2047173417719555](https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/full/10.1177/2047173417719555)
          - Not a significant difference?
        - Interface:
          - 
      - [Personal Finance Lab](https://www.personalfinancelab.com/budget-game/): interactive game that teaches budgeting, investing, credit etc
        - Players manage their in-game finances in a simple, clean interface
        - Primary topics: budgeting, fixed/variable expenses, emergency fund, income management
      - [Fintropolis](https://www.allyfintropolis.com/): game within Minecraft to teach FinLit 
        - Using a medium that kids are likely already familiar with and enjoy
        - Players can navigate a city, choosing a career, open a bank account, practice with a budget, taxes, credit/vs debit, investing, etc
        - Use of IRL guided scenarios to introduce/familiarize students with financial concepts, no prior knowledge needed
        - CRITIQUE: No internal grading system, students might get distracted, associating gameplay with previous Minecraft experiences
      - [JA Titan](https://www.filamentgames.com/project/ja-titan/): role of a company exec in a manufacturing sector
        - Efficiently allocate funds, balance investor requirements, cater to various stakeholders’ needs
        - Attractive, clean interface
        - CRITIQUE: Targets financial skills, but not at the personal level: more of an economic/business administration focus, too overwhelming/complex for younger children 
      - [FutureSmart](https://everfi.com/courses/k-12/financial-literacy-middle-school/): narrative-based interactive game
        - Users make financial decisions 
        - Teaches budgeting, saving, investing, career planning, smart shopping, payment methods
        - Engaging format, but limited depth
        - Game-like but basic UI (good to be simple, but should look more elegant + updated than this
      - [Stukent](https://www.stukent.com/): tools for experiential learning, "simternships," personal finance [simulation](https://www.stukent.com/high-school/mimic-personal-finance/?srsltid=AfmBOopl7qmN7Lozu3mn4Vdxmp_SVTgFB5Qq0QpkUEp3yrfWCDhYwOZm)
        - Student decisions have IRL effects on their grades, curriculum limited to personal finance courses
        - Incorporating game elements like coins, streaks, XP, hearts, etc
        - Topics:
          - Save Money
          - Apply for Credit
          - Repay Debt
          - Choose a Financial Institution
          - Open a Bank Account
          - Shop Smartly
      - [Squirrel](https://squirreledu.co/): self-directed digital learning/simulation for ages 7-18
        - Digital + asynchonous
        - Holistic education - understanding + practical habits
        - Simulation: students earned a fictitious weekly salary, learned about entrepreneurship (e.g., cake sales), budgeting (e.g., renting textbooks), and investment (putting money into savings accounts) 
        - Targeted engagement + motivation: students earning or losing funds based on behavior, fostering a strong sense of responsibility and pride in managing their earned money
      - [MoneyAble](https://www.moneyable.in/): gamified & module-based platform for ages 8+
        - Structured Curriculum: organized modules using videos, quizzes, and case studies- students must pass assessments to advance, ensuring content mastery
        - Financial Game: students use virtual money, can compete with friends on a leaderboard
        - Parent Involvement: sends WhatsApp notifications upon module completion, prompting them to discuss the topics with their child to reinforce learning
        - Real-World Practicality: encourages practical application through worksheets and activities that guide kids to track their own finances, bridging the gap between the virtual lessons and reality
  - Financial Calculation + Management Tools
    - [SmartAsset](https://smartasset.com/): calculations for mortgages, taxes, retirement, investments
    - [You Need a Budget](https://www.ynab.com/): dynamic budgeting software, might be better suited for adults with real budgets than kids trying to learn budgeting, but could build a simulation that is similar
    - [Savings Calculator](https://www.bankrate.com/banking/savings/simple-savings-calculator/): takes into account initial deposit, contributions + contribution period, and APY --> gives final amount in savings
    - Family Financial Tools
      - [Greenlight](https://greenlight.com/): app for families to help/manage children's finances, send allowances, assign chores, facilitate investing, some life360 features like safety + monitoring
        - (Online, subscription) family money app. Allows kids to have debit cards, play FL games, invest, etc. Should be implemented for family use (instead of school use)
      - [Acorns Early](https://www.acorns.com/early/): app for parents to help children manage money
        - Custodial investment account, gifts, educational resources
        - Monthly subscription plan
        - Alternatives: 529 plan, traditional brokerage accounts, Roth IRAs
        - Runs on a UTMA/UGMA account
          - Child account managed by custodian 
          - Child owns it and takes over at 18 
          - Account earnings may be subject to taxes
          - UGMA = allows holding of financial assets like stocks, bonds, and mutual funds
          - UTMA = broader, allowing holdings of other types of property, like real estate or artwork (not only financial assets)
      - [GravyStack](https://www.impactfoundation.org/portfolio/gravystack): gamified banking platform designed specifically for kids and teens. Its core mission is to create financial competency in young people by turning financial education into a game they play in the real world.
        - **Highlights**
          - 1) Raised $10M from notable VCs, including Altrus Capital - Last $4m at $43m valuation
          - 2) Stellar Leadership: We are a team of family-focused business and subject matter experts.
          - 3) Kids In Focus: We are in this to change the world, one kid at a time
          - 4) Kids Love It: 4.5/5 avg star ratings from kids and teens for Levels 1 - 5 (Save, Earn, Spend, Share)
        - Gamified Banking: It combines a banking app with game mechanics to make learning about money engaging.
        - Real Financial Tools: It includes both physical and virtual debit cards, giving kids access to actual financial power within safe boundaries.
        - The "Money Machine": This is a proprietary system that automatically splits every dollar a child earns into three distinct "jars":
          - Spend
          - Save
          - Share
        - Chore Tracking: The app tracks household chores (both paid and unpaid), linking effort directly to earnings.
        - Smart Spending Guardrails: Kids can only spend what is available in their "Spend" jar, which naturally teaches budgeting and delayed gratification in a safe environment.
        - Parent company = [Dinner Table](https://dinnertable.com/), seems somewhat unrelated -> parenting community & methodology rather than strict FinLit emphasis, but has a "financial competency" pillar that likely includes GravyStack 
        - **WeFunder Pitch [Page](https://wefunder.com/gravystack/)**
          - Description: "GravyStack is financial literacy for the next generation"
          - Mission #1: Engaged Kids
            - Problem - Traditional financial education can be boring
            - Solution - Baked-in gamification
              - In-game currency, personalized journey feel, real-time money (with "Money Machine"), customized avatars
          - Mission #2: Financially Competent Kids
            - Problem - if kids can't earn money, they can't learn to manage money
            - Solution - Gigs = meaningful earning
            - Home gigs: done at home for extra tasks parents find valuable
            - Community gigs: done around the community (sponsored by neighbor, grandma, etc)
            - Brain gigs: activities that enrich the kid from the inside out and enrich their lives
            - Action gigs: tasks kids can complete (mow the lawn, walk the dog, clean the garage) that provide above and beyond value 
            - The result - kids who never ask their parents for money again because they know exactly how to create value that generates the income they desire
          - Mission #3: Financially Safe Kids
            - Problem - financial institutions either ignore or prey on kids. Traditional banks and credit/debit don't have our kids' best interests at heart - they only make money when it's held or spent. 
            - Solution - real financial education
            - Satisfies nationwide core FinLit curriculum
            - Satisfies uniform benchmarks of state-mandated FinLit education requirements
            - Built and overseen by child psychologists and subject matter experts
            - Goes beyond "just the basics" - while we can say we check off all the standards, we set our own standard, and the bar is high!
            - The result: kids who know how to make their own choices and avoid common and even less common financial pitfalls, and rave reviews from kids, parents, teachers, and ethical financial institution
          - How do we make money management fun and safe for kids?
            - Fun
              - Learning happens autonomously in a fun game world: Windfall!
              - Each kid gets a "Money Machine" that shows an integrated visual of how their money flows from earnings to save, spend, and share jars
              - Kids get their own debit card, a celebration of independence!
              - Banking features are gamified to use the power of play to make the generally scary topics of finances more approachable
            - Safe
              - In-game currency provides instant gratification for progress
              - The parent-approved % split on the "Money Machine" automatically manages the allocation of every dollar earned, so long term savings goals and charitable giving are never ruined by an unfortunate spending spree
              - The debit card cannot be overdrawn, and does not spend from save or share jars. Giving kids the autonomy to spend on what they want, and learn real lessons about what is a want vs a need based on their own developing value systems
              - We are backed by an FDIC-insured banking partner, way safer than a prepaid debit card or cash in their pocket
          - **Coming Soon**
            - Investing
              - Learn the power of early and often investments into long-term equity holdings
            - Circles
              - Adults can tip kids for learning and kids can challenge others in the game
            - Resumes
              - Accepted by businesses and educational institutions nationwide
          - Business Model Comparison
            - 
        - **Comparison with Aurum**
          - GravyStack is likely the closest direct-to-consumer (D2C) competitor in the "gamified financial literacy" space. It is a prominent player in the "Family Finance" sector.
          - 1. The Core Product Difference
            - GravyStack (The "Real" Bank): Their value proposition is "The Game is the Bank." They are a fintech banking platform (debit card + app) first. They gamify chores and allowance.
            - Aurum (The "Safe" Simulator): Your one-pager defines Aurum as an "immersive simulation" that provides a "risk-free environment".
              - Why this matters: GravyStack *requires* parents to move real money and open a bank account. Aurum allows users to practice *before* real money is at stake. This makes Aurum an easier sell to schools and risk-averse institutions.
          - 2. The Business Model Difference
            - GravyStack (B2C): They primarily sell subscriptions to parents (Direct-to-Consumer). This requires heavy marketing spend (high CAC) to convince parents to switch banking apps.
            - Aurum (B2B2C): You explicitly target "near-zero end-user CAC" by selling via "Institutional licensing (Schools, Banks)".
              - The Advantage: You aren't competing with GravyStack for Facebook ad space. You are getting deployed by a bank (perhaps even one that *competes* with GravyStack) to be their educational layer.
          - 3. The "Academic" Rigor
            - GravyStack: Focuses on "Home Economy" (chores, saving, spending). It is practical for younger kids (ages 6-12).
            - Aurum: Your messaging focuses on "complex financial decisions" and "academic rigor". This positions Aurum better for the High School / Young Adult / Corporate Wellness demographic ($53B market) where "chores" are no longer the primary lesson.
    - Financial configurators: interactive digital tools that use rules-based logic to model, customize, and visualize complex financial scenarios or products
      - [ProjectionLab](https://projectionlab.com/):  
        - Customize/Configure Your Plan: It's "more than a retirement calculator," can configure numerous complex variables, including different investment strategies, account types (e.g., Roth conversions), debt payoff scenarios, major life milestones (like buying a home or taking time off), and even international tax scenarios
        - Visualize and Model: It builds a living model of your whole life's finances and lets you test trade-offs and experiment with different "configurations" of your financial life
        - Use Rules-Based Logic: It uses advanced modeling techniques like Monte Carlo simulations to gauge your chance of success based on financial rules, historical data, and the specific plan you configured
  - AI/Financial Tools
    - [Danelfin](https://danelfin.com/): AI advisor for stocks to buy and sell
    - [Finchat](https://finchat.io/): AI specifically for personal finance, shows investments, like ChatGPT
    - [Tendi Ai](https://tendi.ai/#how-it-works): (Not necessary for some people) - mobile app linked to bank account that tracks activity, offering personalized advice with an AI assistant [helps with budgeting/tracking/advising]
  - Examples of Poor Design
    - [The Payoff](https://payoff.practicalmoneyskills.com/): interactive game where players are faced with various financial decisions
      - Setting = players trying to become successful YouTubers, 
      - Problems
        - Outdated look
        - Confusing interface
          - 
        - Possibly a better example of this applied simulation = [Youtuber's life](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Youtubers_Life)
        - Goal not clear - YouTuber concept mixed in with financial objectives
    - [Visa Financial Football](https://www.financialfootball.com/): players answer financial trivia questions to advance their team in a simulated football game
      - Decent 3D graphics, designed with Unreal Engine, likely engaging for football fans to learn basic financial knowledge, can do multiplayer mode + choose game time
      - Problems - jarring disconnect between content and context
        - Exogenous fantasy: football unrelated to financial literacy (violates principles of situated learning theory)
        - Somewhat distracting from main goal (increase in financial knowledge/skills)
        - Exogenous assessment: financial questions disrupt the flow of the football game - making the player know they are being tested and feeling that the questions are something to get through to enjoy the fun part of the game (football)
          - Many people would consider this endogenous assessment because assessment is within the game's interface (quizzes are not a separate platform or on paper). 
          - It is NOT endogenous, as it takes the player out of the game loop. 
  - Common Gamified Components
    - Roleplaying/simulation
    - Collaborative/social mechanics
    - Branching narrative 
    - Cross-disciplinary content
    - Rotating/evolving challenges
  - Game-Building Tools
    - [Odeum AI](https://www.odeum.ai/): AI-powered educational game authoring platform designed to help teachers, curriculum developers, and content creators quickly transform traditional lessons into immersive, standards-aligned, story-driven learning experiences, often in the style of RPGs
      - AI-Powered Authoring: The core tool allows users to input their teaching goals and content, and the AI generates an engaging, story-driven learning experience (e.g., quests, dialogue, and adventures) tailored to educational standards.
      - Immersive Learning: It focuses on turning abstract academic subjects (like History, Science, or Language) into virtual worlds that students can "live" through, fostering deep engagement and critical thinking.
      - Multi-Platform Distribution: The content created can be used by students on various devices, and the platform often includes a "Library" for sharing and accessing curriculum-aligned games built by other creators.
      - Target Audience = K-12 teachers, curriculum designers, EdTech developers, and homeschoolers.
  - UNICORN/Category Breaker FinLit Educational Platforms/Tools
    - [Intertwined](https://www.intertwinedfinance.com/): AI-powered financial education platform that provides standards-aligned curriculum, adaptive quizzing tools, and simulations - Stock Market Investing Simulator, Personal Budget Simulator, and Business Startup Simulator.
      - We've partnered with Microsoft to leverage generative AI to personalize and gamify the learning experience, immersing students in real-life financial scenarios. Thousands of students and teachers trust Intertwined nationwide, and we're featured in Forbes and Yahoo Finance as a leading financial education provider.
      - 1. The Core Product: Curriculum Wrapper
        - Unlike simple games that rely on disjointed quizzes, Intertwined offers a full K-12 and collegiate curriculum.
        - Standards-Aligned: It meets state and national financial literacy standards (like the Council for Economic Education standards).
        - Structure: It is built around modular units (Banking, Investing, Credit, etc.) that teachers can assign, making it easy to integrate into existing lesson plans rather than replacing them entirely.
      - 2. The Hook: Simulated Environments
        - Intertwined integrates "lab-like" simulations directly into the lessons to move from theory to practice.
        - Stock Market Simulator: Students trade with virtual currency in real-time markets.
        - Budgeting Simulator: A life-sim where students manage monthly expenses, rent, and unforeseen costs.
        - Tax Simulator: A module specifically designed to demystify filing taxes.
      - 3. The Differentiator: Adaptive AI
        - This is their main claim to being "more than just a simulation."
        - BudgetBot: An AI-driven chat interface that acts as a tutor, answering student questions and guiding them through financial concepts in conversational language.
        - Personalized Assessment: The platform uses adaptive questioning to identify knowledge gaps. If a student fails a specific concept, the system provides targeted remediation rather than forcing them to restart a whole module.
      - 4. Target Audience
        - Primary: K-12 Schools and Districts (selling to administrators/teachers as a classroom tool).
        - Secondary: Higher Education (colleges offering life-skills seminars).
        - Tertiary: Financial Institutions (banks/credit unions wanting to offer educational perks to younger customers).
      - Category Fluidity/Unicorn Status
        - 
- Current Standards/Metrics
  - TIAA Institute – GFLEC Personal Finance (P-Fin) Index
    - P-Fin Index Summary
      - The P-Fin Index is unique in its capacity to provide a robust measure ofoverall financial literacy using 28 questions plus a nuanced analysis of personal finance knowledge across eightareas in which individuals routinely function,
    - 2024 Report - Document [here](https://www.tiaa.org/content/dam/tiaa/institute/pdf/insights-report/2024-04/tiaa_institute_gflec_retirement_fluency_pfin_2024.pdf#page=4.13)
      - Most individuals are making financial decisions with agenerally poor level of financial literacy—U.S. adults correctly answered only 48% of the 28 index questions in2024, on average. This figure has hovered around the 50%mark since the inaugural 2017 survey
      - Women consistently score worse than men by ~10% 
      - Financial literacy tends to be low across generations, but particularly so among Generation Z — on average, Gen Z correctly answered only 37% of the index questions in 2024
      - 
    - 2025 Report - Document [here](https://www.tiaa.org/content/dam/tiaa/institute/pdf/insights-report/2025-05/financial-literacy-and-retirement-fluency-in-america-p-fin-ir.pdf)
      - 
        - 
      - On average, adults can correctly answer only half of the index questions, and this figure is even lower among certain demographic groups, such as women, Blacks and Hispanics, and younger adults
  - [FINRA Financial Knowledge Quiz](https://www.finra.org/financial_knowledge_quiz)
  - Trivial/Irrelevant Measures of Knowledge
    - Trivia-like tests with questions that measure niche/textbook knowledge - not applicable to daily life (like driver's license test asking you to memorize the stopping distance you need for different speeds//specific consequences of drunk driving)
      - Likely generalized (possibly even outdated) rules taught in a lecture setting from a textbook/generic lesson plan on personal finance 
      - Might inform what types of knowledge a successful FinLit course should NOT include
        - [Consolidated Credit](https://www.consolidatedcredit.org/financial-literacy/test/)
    - Somewhat ok tests - more applied to real life/practical knowledge
      - [NFEC](https://www.financialeducatorscouncil.org/financial-literacy-test/)
      - [CEE](https://www.councilforeconed.org/personal-finance-quiz/?)
    - Less common financial knowledge
      - [FINRA Investing Quiz](https://knowledgequiz.nga.finra.org/quiz/)
    - Basic FinLit Quiz (easy)
      - [FINRA Financial Literacy Quiz](https://www.finra.org/financial_knowledge_quiz)
      - [Synchrony](https://www.synchrony.com/blog/banking/personal-finance-101-quiz?utm_source=chatgpt.com)
  - [CEE](https://www.councilforeconed.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/10/2021-National-Standards-for-Personal-Financial-Education.pdf) Standards Summarized (somewhat redundant, low-level) - summary [here](https://www.councilforeconed.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/10/2021-National-Standards-for-Personal-Financial-Education.pdf#page=6.05)
    - National Standards for Personal Financial Education (2021) are the definitive industry benchmark.
      - Who: They are a joint publication by the two most powerful groups in FinLit: the Council for Economic Education (CEE) and the Jump$tart Coalition.
      - History: Before 2021, these two organizations had separate, competing standards. They merged them specifically to create a "single, unified set of standards" to guide the entire nation.
      - Market Dominance: When a state Department of Education writes its laws, they almost always copy-and-paste from this specific document.
    - 4th Grade
      - Earning
        - Understanding of different jobs requiring different skills
          - Make a list of different types of jobs and describe the different skills associated with each job
        - Earning an income by being hired and working at a job
          - Explain why employers are willing to pay people to do their work
        - Workers paid in different ways (wages, salaries, commissions)
          - Explain how a waitress, a teacher, and a realtor are paid
        - Earning interest by letting other people borrow money
          - Explain why banks and financial institutions pay people interest when they deposit their money at those institutions
        - Earning income by renting property to other people
          - Identify different types of property (such as apartments, automobiles, or tools) that people own and on which rent is paid
        - Business owners can earn a profit (source of income)
          - Calculate the profit from a business with the information provided on the business’s costs and revenues
        - Entrepreneurs = people who start their own business, which is risky as they do not know if their business will succeed
          - Read a children’s book about an entrepreneur and identify the type of business started, the possible risks of running the business, and what the entrepreneur expected to earn
        - Income can be received from gifts for which no work is required
          - Survey classmates and create a graph showing how many classmates receive money as gifts and how many receive an allowance
        - Income earned from other sources of work are taxed, tax revenue used to fund government services
          - Describe examples of government-provided goods and services that are paid for with taxes
      - Buying
        - Economic wants are desires that can be satisfied by consuming a good, a service, or a leisure activity.
          - Brainstorm a list of wants and then identify examples of goods, services, or leisure activities they can buy to satisfy each want
        - Individuals have to prioritize needs and wants because they cannot have everything they want
          - Rank goods/services within budget constraint
        - Consumers incur an opportunity cost when they buy something
          - Present an example of a buying choice a person made and identify the opportunity cost of that choice
        - Planning (budgeting) for spending can help people make informed choices
          - Create a budget for a set amount of allowance income that includes expenses (buying of goods and services) and savings
      - Saving
        - Income is saved, spent on goods and services, or used to pay taxes
          - Explain the difference between saving and spending and give examples of each
        - A savings plan helps people reach their savings goals
          - Select a savings goal and identify steps to take in order to reach that savings goal
        - When people deposit money into a bank (or other financial institution), the bank may pay them interest. Banks attract savings by paying interest. People also deposit money into banks because banks are safe places to keep their savings
          - Describe the advantages of saving money in a savings account rather than putting the money into a piggy bank
      - Using Credit
        - Interest is the price the borrower pays for using someone else’s money
          - Explain the reason why, when a person borrows $100 to buy a new cell phone, he or she will have to pay back more than the $100 at a future date
        - When people use credit, they receive something of value now and agree to repay the lender over time, or at some date in the future, with interest
          - Identify goods and services people often purchase with the use of a loan
        - By using credit to buy durable goods—such as cars, houses, and appliances—people are able to use the goods while paying for them
          - Explain why people do not wait to buy a car until they have saved enough to pay for the car with cash
        - Borrowers who repay loans as promised show that they are worthy of getting credit in the future. A reputation for not repaying a loan as promised can result in higher interest charges on future loans, if loans are available at all
          - Explain why a bank would charge more interest or decide not to lend to a borrower who has had problems repaying a loan in the past
      - Financial Investing
        - After people have saved some of their income, they must decide how to invest their savings so that it can grow over time
          - Describe the difference between saving and financial investing
        - A financial investment is the purchase of a financial asset such as a stock with the expectation of an increase in the value of the asset and/or increase in future income
          - Explain why a stockholder benefits if the company produces an increasingly popular product
      - Protecting and Insuring
        - Risk is the chance of loss or harm
          - Give examples of the risk associated with activities such as riding a bicycle, using a skateboard, or having a pet
        - Risk from accidents and unexpected events is an unavoidable part of daily life
          - Write a newspaper article on an unexpected “bad” event such as a tornado, car accident, or illness, and describe the effect the event would have on individuals and their families
        - Individuals can either choose to accept risk or take steps to protect themselves by avoiding or reducing risk
          - Draw a poster depicting an age-appropriate activity (e.g., owning and riding a bicycle) that illustrates how to avoid risk of harm or loss (not riding the bike) or how to reduce the chance of a bad event (riding in a safe manner) and potential harm if the bad event happens (wearing a bike helmet)
        - One method to cope with unexpected losses is to save for emergencies
          - Give examples of events for which emergency savings could offset financial losses
    - 8th Grade
      - Earning
        - Careers are based on working at jobs in the same occupation or profession for many years (different careers require different education and training)
          - Interview individuals and create a timeline that shows the education, training, and job experiences that occurred as the individuals progressed through different stages of their careers
        - People make many decisions over a lifetime about their education, jobs, and careers that affect their incomes and job opportunities
          - Conduct research on a specific career. Describe the education, job, or career decisions individuals in this field might make over their lifetime and explain how this could affect their incomes and job opportunities
        - Getting more education and learning new job skills can increase a person’s human capital and productivity
          - Explain how taking a babysitting class or getting lifeguard training can improve a young person’s human capital or productivity
        - People with less education and fewer job skills tend to earn lower incomes than people with more education and greater job skills.
          - Gather data on the average wage or salary for different jobs and explain how they differ by the level of education, job skill, or years of experience.
        - Education, training, and development of job skills have opportunity costs in the form of time, effort, and money
          - Describe the opportunity costs of attending a training course on babysitting, lifeguarding, or first aid
        - Interest, dividends, and capital appreciation (gains) are forms of income earned from financial investments.
          - Find the interest rate a bank pays on a savings account. Find the dividend rate paid on a company stock and the percentage that the price of that stock rose or fell in the last year
        - Some people receive income support from government because they have low incomes or qualify in other ways for government assistance
          - Look up government programs such as Medicaid or SNAP (Supplemental Nutrition Assistance Program) and explain the financial situation the programs are addressing
        - Social Security is a government program that taxes the income of current workers to provide retirement, disability, and survivor benefits for workers or their dependents.
          - Given information on a worker’s income and today’s Social Security tax rates, calculate what the worker and the worker’s employer will pay in taxes. Find the average benefit paid to a retiree living on Social Security today
      - Buying
        - By understanding a source’s incentives in providing information about a good or service, a consumer can better assess the quality and usefulness of the information.
          - Explain why advice from a source such as a salesperson may or may not be useful when deciding which product to buy
        - People choose from a variety of payment methods in order to buy goods and services
          - Explain how they would use the following payment methods to purchase a good or service: cash, check, debit card, credit card, mobile phone, online payment, prepaid card, layaway, and rent to own
        - A budget includes fixed and variable expenses, as well as income, savings, and taxes
          - Prepare a monthly budget for a family given their income, savings goals, taxes, and list of fixed and variable expenses
      - Saving
        - Banks and other financial institutions loan funds received from depositors to borrowers. Part of the interest received from these loans is used to pay interest to depositors for the use of their money
        - For the saver, an interest rate is the price a financial institution pays for using a saver’s money and is normally expressed as an annual percentage of the amount saved
        - Interest rates paid on savings and charged on loans, like all prices, are determined in a market
          - Explain why banks that experience an increase in the number of people who want loans may decide to pay higher interest rates on deposits
        - When interest rates increase, people earn more on their savings and their savings grow more quickly
          - Calculate the total amount of interest earned on two certificates of deposit—one with a higher rate of interest than the other—and explain how the certificate of deposit with the higher interest rate can help a saver reach his or her savings goal faster
        - Principal is the initial amount of money upon which interest is paid
          - Differentiate between principal and interest
        - Compound interest is the interest that is earned not only on the principal but also on the interest already earned
          - Use the Rule of 72 to determine the number of years it will take for their savings to double in value. Using a formula for compound interest, calculate how much two different savers, one who starts to save at age 21 and one who starts to save at age 35, will have at retirement.
        - The value of a person’s savings in the future is determined by the amount saved and the interest rate. The earlier people begin to save, the more savings they will be able to accumulate, all other things equal, as a result of the power of compound interest
          - Predict which of two individuals will have more in savings for retirement when one individual has saved $2,000 a year for the last 15 years before retirement and the other has saved $1,000 a year for last 30 years before retirement, assuming each earns the same rate of interest
        - Different people save money for different reasons, including large purchases (such as higher education, autos, and homes), retirement, and unexpected events. People’s choices about how much to save and for what to save are based on their tastes and preferences
          - Write a short story comparing the savings choices of a young college graduate to those of a married couple who recently celebrated their 40th birthdays and who have two children
        - To assure savers that their deposits are safe from bank failures, federal agencies guarantee depositors’ savings in most commercial banks, savings banks, and savings associations up to a set limit
          - Identify the FDIC and the National Credit Union Administration (NCUA) as the government agencies responsible for insuring depositors’ savings and state the limit of FDIC and NCUA coverage
      - Using Credit
        - The longer the repayment period on a loan and the higher the interest rate on the loan, the larger the total amount of interest charged on a loan
          - Explain and illustrate what happens to the total cost of borrowing under various scenarios, such as higher or lower interest rates or longer or shorter repayment periods
        - A credit card purchase is a loan from the financial institution that issued the card. Credit card interest rates tend to be higher than rates for other loans. In addition, financial institutions may charge significant fees related to a credit card and its use
          - Examine a credit card statement and identify the interest rate and fees charged
        - A credit card user can avoid interest charges by paying the entire balance within the grace period specified by the financial institution
          - For an expensive good purchased using credit, find the total interest paid and the amount still owed after one year when only the minimum payment is made each month
        - Interest rates on loans fluctuate based on changes in the market for loans
          - Explain why mortgage interest rates might be lower when people are more reluctant to buy houses
      - Financial Investing
        - Financial assets include a wide variety of financial instruments including bank deposits, stocks, bonds, and mutual funds. Real estate and commodities are also often viewed as financial assets
          - Describe the differences among the different types of assets. Find the prices of a variety of current possible investments
        - Interest is received from money deposited in bank accounts. It is also received by owning a corporate or government bond or making a loan
          - Calculate the amount of interest income received from depositing a certain amount of money in a bank account paying 1 percent per year and from owning a bond paying 5 percent per year
        - When people buy corporate stock, they are purchasing ownership shares in a business. If the business is profitable, they will expect to receive income in the form of dividends and/or from the increase in the stock’s value. The increase in the value of an asset (like a stock) is called a capital gain. If the business is not profitable, investors could lose the money they have invested
          - Determine the amount of dividends paid from a selected stock and how much the price of the stock has appreciated or depreciated over the year
        - The price of a financial asset is determined by the interaction of buyers and sellers in a financial market
          - Explain why the price of a stock might change if more individuals decide to purchase the stock. Explain why the price of a stock might change if more companies issue new shares of stock to raise new investment funds
        - The rate of return on financial investments consists of interest payments, dividends, and capital appreciation expressed as a percentage of the amount invested
          - Calculate rates of return on a number of financial instruments taking into account interest, dividends, rents, and expected appreciation over time
        - Financial risk means that a financial investment has a range of possible returns, including possibilities of actual losses. Higher-risk investments have a wider range of possible returns
          - Compare the risk of an insured certificate of deposit with the risk of a purchase of a number of shares of stock. Explain the difference
        - The rate of return earned from investments will vary according to the amount of risk. In general, a trade-off exists between the security of an investment and its expected rate of return
          - Compare rates of return of a variety of different investments and speculate on the amount of risk each of the investments entails
      - Protecting and Insuring
        - Personal financial risk exists when unexpected events can damage health, income, property, wealth, or future opportunities
          - Write a scenario describing how a storm blowing a tree onto a roof can impact a family’s financial situation
        - Insurance is a product that allows people to pay a fee (called a premium) now to transfer the costs of a potential loss to a third party
          - Explain why homeowners buy flood insurance for $300 a year when the likelihood of a flood in their area is extremely low
        - Insurance companies analyze the outcomes of individuals who face similar types of risks to create insurance contracts (policies). By collecting a relatively small amount of money, called a premium, from each policyholder on a regular basis, the company creates a pool of funds to compensate those individuals who experience a large loss
          - Explain how homeowners who are covered by homeowners insurance “share” with other policyholders the risk of loss from fire or storm damage. Identify key information that the insurance company would need to know to determine how large a premium to collect from each policyholder
        - Self-insurance is when an individual accepts a risk and saves money on a regular basis to cover a potential loss
          - List examples of potential events and costs against which people might self-insure
        - Insurance policies that guarantee higher levels of payment in the event of a loss (coverage) have higher prices
          - Explain how a deductible affects the payout on an auto insurance claim, and how the individual’s choice of deductible affects the price of the policy at the time it is purchased
        - Insurance companies charge higher premiums to cover higher-risk individuals and events because the risk of monetary loss is greater for these individuals and events
          - Explain why drivers who receive repeated speeding tickets will see their insurance premiums increase
        - Individuals can choose to accept some risk, to take steps to avoid or reduce risk, or to transfer risk to others through the purchase of insurance. Each option has different costs and benefits
          - Identify ways in which an automobile driver can avoid, reduce, or transfer the risk of being in an automobile accident. Explain why people may prefer to purchase insurance against fire in their apartment, but self-insure to handle the cost of tooth cavities
        - Social networking sites and other online activity can make individuals vulnerable to harm caused by identity theft or misuse of their personal information
          - Identify ways that identity thieves can obtain someone’s personal information. List actions an individual can take to protect personal information
    - 12th Grade
      - Earning
        - People choose jobs or careers for which they are qualified based on the income they expect to earn and the benefits, such as health insurance coverage or a retirement plan, that they expect to receive
          - Conduct research and create a list of potential income and employee benefits packages that are likely to be offered by different companies, government agencies, or not-for-profit organizations for a new worker in an occupation. Explain what decisions workers are likely to have to make regarding benefits.
        - People vary in their willingness to obtain more education or training because these decisions involve incurring immediate costs to obtain possible future benefits. Discounting the future benefits of education and training may lead some people to pass up potentially high rates of return that more education and training may offer
          - Explain how people’s willingness to wait or plan for the future affects their decision to get more education or job training in a dynamic and changing labor market. Speculate how a high school student might assess the future benefits of going to college, and describe how that assessment will affect the student’s decision to attend college.
        - Changes in economic conditions or the labor market can cause changes in a worker’s income or may cause unemployment.
          - Explain how an increase in the demand for mobile applications might impact the wages paid to software developers. Explain the effects of a recession on the unemployment rate.
        - Taxes are paid to federal, state, and local governments to fund government goods and services and transfer payments from government to individuals. The major types of taxes are income taxes, payroll (Social Security) taxes, property taxes, and sales taxes
          - Calculate the amount of taxes a person is likely to pay when given information or data about the person’s sources of income and amount of spending. Identify which level of government receives the tax revenue for a particular tax and describe what is done with the tax revenue
      - Buying
        - Consumer decisions are influenced by the price of a good or service, the price of alternatives, and the consumer’s income as well as their preferences
          - Write scenarios explaining how an individual’s decision to buy tennis shoes may have been influenced by various factors
        - Consumers may be influenced by how the price of a good is expressed
          - Write a paragraph explaining why a store might advertise the price of a flat screen TV expressed as an amount per day or week rather than the actual full price. List different ways retailers use to express the prices of their products
        - People may choose to donate money to charitable organizations and other not-for-profits because they gain satisfaction from donating
          - Brainstorm a list of charitable organizations that are operating in the students’ community. For each organization, list a possible reason that a donor might want to give to that charitable organization.
        - Governments establish laws and institutions to provide consumers with information about goods or services being purchased and to protect consumers from fraud
          - Draft a complaint letter to an appropriate firm or agency about a problem the consumer has encountered with a purchase.
      - Saving
        - People choose between immediate spending and saving for future consumption. Some people have a tendency to be impatient, choosing immediate spending over saving for the future
          - Identify instances in their lives where they decided to buy something immediately and then wished they had instead saved the money for future purchases
        - Inflation reduces the value of money, including savings. The real interest rate expresses the rate of return on savings, taking into account the effect of inflation. The real interest rate is calculated as the nominal interest rate minus the rate of inflation
          - Explain why savers expect a higher nominal interest rate when inflation is expected to be high
        - Real interest rates typically are positive because people expect to be compensated for deferring the use of savings from the present into the future. Higher interest rates increase the rewards for saving
          - Given the nominal interest rate and the rate of inflation over the course of one year, explain what will happen to the purchasing power of savings
        - Money received (or paid) in the future can be compared to money held today by discounting the future value based on the rate of interest
          - Use spreadsheet software to calculate the amount a 10-year-old would need to save today in order to pay for one year of college tuition eight years from now
        - Government agencies supervise and regulate financial institutions to help protect the safety, soundness, and legal compliance of the nation’s banking and financial system
          - Federal Reserve System, the Office of the Comptroller of the Currency, the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau, the FDIC, and state banking departments
        - Government policies create incentives and disincentives for people to save
          - Explain why traditional IRAs, Roth IRAs, and educational savings accounts provide incentives for people to save
      - Using Credit
        - Consumers can compare the cost of credit using the annual percentage rate (APR), initial fees charged, and fees charged for late payment or missed payments
          - Compare the cost of credit from various sources for the purchase of a product
        - Loans can be unsecured or secured with collateral. Lenders charge a lower interest rate for secured loans than they charge for unsecured loans
          - Give examples of collateral used to secure a loan, such as a house for a mortgage or a car for a car loan
        - People often make a cash payment to the seller of a good—called a down payment—in order to reduce the amount they need to borrow. Lenders may consider loans made with a down payment to have less risk because the down payment gives the borrower some equity or ownership right away. As a result, these loans may carry a lower interest rate
          - Explain why a borrower who has made a down payment has an incentive to repay a loan or make payments on time
        - Lenders make credit decisions based in part on consumer payment history. Credit bureaus record borrowers’ credit and payment histories and provide that information to lenders in credit reports
          - List factors from an individual’s credit history or credit application that may cause a lender to deny credit. Explain what credit bureaus do.
        - Lenders can pay to receive a borrower’s credit score from a credit bureau. A credit score is a number based on information in a credit report and assesses a person’s credit risk
          - Explain why certain factors, such as having many credit cards with large lines of credit and large balances, might hurt a credit score
        - Credit reports and scores may be requested and used by employers in hiring decisions, landlords in deciding whether to rent apartments, and insurance companies in charging premiums
          - Provide two examples of how having a good credit score can benefit a person financially. Explain why employers find it useful to hire someone with a better credit score
        - Failure to repay a loan has significant consequences for borrowers such as negative entries on their credit report, repossession of property (collateral), garnishment of wages, and the inability to obtain loans in the future
          - Write a scenario about the future opportunities a person can lose by failing to repay loans as agreed
        - Consumers who have difficulty repaying debt can seek assistance through credit counseling services and by negotiating directly with creditors
          - Identify the costs and benefits associated with using different credit counseling services
        - In extreme cases, bankruptcy may be an option for consumers who are unable to repay debt. Although bankruptcy provides some benefits, filing for bankruptcy also entails considerable costs, including having notice of the bankruptcy appear on a consumer’s credit report for up to 10 years
          - Investigate the costs of bankruptcy by examining the bankruptcy laws in their state
        - People often apply for a mortgage to purchase a home. A mortgage is a type of loan that is secured by real estate property as collateral
          - Predict what might happen should a homeowner fail to make their mortgage payments
        - Consumers who use credit should be aware of laws that are in place to protect them. These include requirements to provide full disclosure of credit terms such as APR and fees, as well as protection against discrimination and abusive marketing or collection practices
          - Explain why it is important that consumers have full information about loans. Explain the information on a credit disclosure statement
        - Consumers are entitled to a free copy of their credit report annually so that they can verify that no errors were made that might increase their cost of credit
          - Explain why it is important to check the accuracy of the information recorded on a credit report and know what steps to take to correct errors on credit reports
      - Financial Investing 
        - The real return on a financial investment is the nominal return minus the rate of inflation
          - Calculate real rates of return earned on a bond given interest rates and inflation rates
        - Federal, state, and local tax rates vary on different types of investments and affect the after-tax rate of return of an investment
          - Given tax rates and inflation rates, calculate the real, after-tax rates of return for groups of stocks and bonds
        - Expenses of buying, selling, and holding financial assets decrease the rate of return from an investment
          - Identify and compare the administrative costs of several mutual funds and estimate the differences in the total amount accumulated after 10 years for each mutual fund, assuming identical market performance
        - Buyers and sellers in financial markets determine prices of financial assets and therefore influence the rates of return on those assets
          - Predict what will happen to the price and rate of return on a bond if buyers believe that the bond has increased in risk
        - An investment with greater risk than another investment will commonly have a lower market price, and therefore a higher rate of return, than the other investment
          - Explain why the expected rate of return on a “bluechip” stock is likely to be lower than that of an Internet start-up company
        - Shorter-term investments will likely have lower rates of return than longer-term investments
          - Explain how markets will determine the rates of return for two bonds if one is a long-term bond and the other a short-term bond, assuming each bond pays the same rate of interest
        - Diversification by investing in different types of financial assets can lower investment risk
          - Compare the risk faced by two investors, both of whom own two businesses on a beach. One investor owns a suntan lotion business and a rain umbrella business. The other investor owns two suntan lotion businesses. Explain why a financial advisor might encourage a client to include stocks, bonds, and real estate assets in his or her portfolio
        - Financial markets adjust to new financial news. Prices in those markets reflect what is known about those financial assets
          - Explain how prices of financial investments can adjust when given specific news about a company’s or industry’s future profitability
        - The prices of financial assets are affected by interest rates. The prices of financial assets are also affected by changes in domestic and international economic conditions, monetary policy, and fiscal policy
          - Give an example of a change in interest rates affecting the current value of a financial asset that pays returns in the future. Explain why the current value increases when interest rates fall. Explain how a change in economic growth might change the value of a stock held by an investor
        - Investors should be aware of tendencies that people have that may result in poor choices. These include avoiding selling assets at a loss because they weigh losses more than they weigh gains and investing in financial assets with which they are familiar, such as their own employer’s stock or domestic rather than international stocks
          - Explain why investors may sell stocks that have gained in value, but hold ones that have lost value. Explain why this may not make sense. Identify an example of why an investor may have a bias toward familiar investments and why this may or may not be a rational decision
        - People vary in their willingness to take risks. The willingness to take risks depends on factors such as personality, income, and family situation
          - Explain how the portfolio of a retiree might differ from that of a young, single person
        - An economic role for governments exists if individuals do not have complete information about the nature of alternative investments or access to competitive financial markets
          - Explain why it is important for individuals to have accurate information about a company’s sales and profits when investing in that company
        - The Securities and Exchange Commission (SEC), the Federal Reserve, and other government agencies regulate financial markets
          - Conduct research to learn about the SEC or the Federal Reserve and identify their roles in regulating financial markets
      - Protecting and Insuring
        - Probability quantifies the likelihood that a specific event will occur, usually expressed as the ratio of the number of actual occurrences to the number of possible occurrences
          - Use the concept of probability to describe the likelihood of a bad event that could pose risk for a person
        - Individuals vary with respect to their willingness to accept risk. Most people are willing to pay a small cost now if it means they can avoid a possible larger loss later
          - Discuss whether or not a premium paid to insure against an accident that never happens is wasted
        - Judgment regarding risky events is subject to errors because people tend to overestimate the probability of infrequent events, often because they’ve heard of or seen a recent example
          - Discuss how an extended warranty on a consumer product is like insurance. Evaluate the costeffectiveness of extended warranties on three consumer products: a new automobile, a smart phone, and a dishwasher, considering the likelihood that the product will fail, the cost of replacing the item, and the price of the warranty
        - People choose different amounts of insurance coverage based on their willingness to accept risk, as well as their occupation, lifestyle, age, financial profile, and the price of insurance
          - Given hypothetical profiles for three types of individuals who differ with respect to occupation, age, lifestyle, marital status, and financial profile, assess the types and levels of personal financial risk faced by each and make recommendations for appropriate insurance
        - People may be required by governments or by certain types of contracts (e.g., home mortgages) to purchase some types of insurance
          - Explain why homeowners insurance is required by a lender when a homeowner takes out a mortgage. Investigate their state’s regulations regarding the amount of auto insurance that drivers are required to purchase
        - An insurance contract can increase the probability or size of a potential loss because having the insurance results in the person taking more risks.
