Policy features such as deductibles and copayments are cost-sharing features that encourage the policyholder to take steps to reduce the potential size of a loss (claim)
          - Given an accident scenario, calculate the amount that would be paid on an insurance claim after applying exclusions and deductibles. Explain why automobile owners with a low deductible on their automobile insurance may be less likely to lock their cars
        - People can lower insurance premiums by behaving in ways that show they pose a lower risk
          - Explain why taking a safe-driving course can lower an auto insurance premium and why not smoking can lower a health insurance premium
        - Health insurance provides funds to pay for health care in the event of illness and may also pay for the cost of preventive care. Large health insurance companies can often negotiate with doctors, hospitals, and other health care providers to obtain lower health care prices for their policyholders
          - Compare the coverage and costs of hypothetical plans for a set of health care scenarios. Using sample policyholder benefit statements, identify any negotiated discounts for health care services and determine the cost of health care in the absence of insurance
        - Disability insurance is income insurance that provides funds to replace income lost while an individual is ill or injured and unable to work
          - Given a potential disability scenario for an individual, assess the extent of personal financial risk and determine whether disability insurance is appropriate for that individual
        - Property and casualty insurance (including renters insurance) pays for damage or loss to the insured’s property and often includes liability coverage for actions of the insured that cause harm to other people or their property
          - Contact an insurance company for an auto insurance quote on a given automobile (make, model, and year) and explain the different categories and levels of coverage in the policy
        - Life insurance benefits are paid to the insured’s beneficiaries in the event of the policyholder’s death. These payments can be used to replace wages lost when the insured person dies
          - Create hypothetical profiles for three individuals who differ with respect to age, marital status, dependents, and occupation. Identify their vulnerability to income loss and make recommendations for life insurance coverage for each
        - In addition to privately purchased insurance, some government benefit programs provide a social safety net to protect individuals from economic hardship created by unexpected events
          - Describe examples of government transfer programs that compensate for unexpected losses, including Social Security Disability benefits, Medicare, Medicaid, unemployment insurance, and workers’ compensation
        - Loss of assets, wealth, and future opportunities can occur if an individual’s personal information is obtained by others through identity theft and then used fraudulently. By managing their personal information and choosing the environment in which it is revealed, individuals can accept, reduce, and insure against the risk of loss due to identity theft
          - Describe problems that can occur when an individual is a victim of identity theft. Give specific examples of how online transactions, online banking, email scams, and telemarketing calls can make consumers vulnerable to identity theft. Describe the conditions under which individuals should and should not disclose their Social Security number, account numbers, or other sensitive personal information
        - Federal and state regulations provide some remedies and assistance for victims of identity theft
          - Recommend actions a victim of identity theft should take to limit losses and restore personal security
  - [NFEC](https://www.financialeducatorscouncil.org/wp-content/uploads/Kids_Financial_Literacy_Standards_PK%E2%80%938th_Grade.pdf)
    - PreK-Kindergarten
      - Financial Psychology
        - Identify needs vs. wants, and that both cost money
        - Use numbers (1–10) with dollar bills
      - Income, Careers, Business, & Entrepreneurship
        - Recognize that people earn money by working
        - Understand jobs and chores as sources of money
      - Accounts, Savings, Budgeting, & Investment
        - Recognize coins and bills
        - Understand the purpose of saving money
      - Credit, Debt, & Loans
        - Understand borrowing means you must pay back
        - Recognize sharing and lending among peers
      - Risk Management & Insurance
        - Learn about keeping money and belongings safe
    - 1st Grade
      - Financial Psychology
        - Recognize some needs/wants are more important
        - Distinguish between children’s and adults’ needs/wants
        - Identify bills ($1, $5, $10, $20) and their values
      - Income, Careers, Business, & Entrepreneurship
        - Understand different jobs and what they provide
        - Recognize the concept of earning and spending
      - Accounts, Savings, Budgeting, & Investment
        - Learn to count and sort coins
        - Begin to understand saving for goals
      - Credit, Debt, & Loans
        - Learn about owing and paying back
        - Recognize the importance of returning borrowed items
      - Risk Management & Insurance
        - Understand basic safety for money and possessions
    - 2nd Grade
      - Financial Psychology
        - Identify different lifestyles
        - Understand goal-setting for future planning
        - Distinguish dollars and cents; write money values
      - Income, Careers, Business, & Entrepreneurship
        - Explore jobs, skills, and interests
        - Understand that different jobs earn different amounts
      - Accounts, Savings, Budgeting, & Investment
        - Practice saving for short- and long-term goals
        - Recognize different types of savings containers/accounts
      - Credit, Debt, & Loans
        - Understand borrowing for needs/wants
        - Learn about consequences of not paying back
      - Risk Management & Insurance
        - Learn about protecting money (piggy banks, safe places)
    - 3rd Grade
      - Financial Psychology
        - Explain that choices have outcomes
        - Set short- and long-term goals
        - Understand saving helps achieve goals
      - Income, Careers, Business, & Entrepreneurship
        - Identify skills needed for various jobs
        - Explore entrepreneurship basics (e.g., lemonade stand)
      - Accounts, Savings, Budgeting, & Investment
        - Make a simple budget
        - Track spending and saving
      - Credit, Debt, & Loans
        - Understand the cost of borrowing
        - Learn about interest in simple terms
      - Risk Management & Insurance
        - Identify ways to protect money and belongings
    - 4th Grade
      - Financial Psychology
        - Analyze how values influence spending
        - Prioritize financial goals
      - Income, Careers, Business, & Entrepreneurship
        - Explore career paths and education needed
        - Understand earning and spending trade-offs
      - Accounts, Savings, Budgeting, & Investment
        - Create and adjust a basic budget
        - Compare savings options
      - Credit, Debt, & Loans
        - Learn about credit and its responsible use
        - Understand consequences of debt
      - Risk Management & Insurance
        - Discuss basic insurance concepts (health, property)
    - 5th Grade
      - Financial Psychology
        - Evaluate financial decisions and their impact
        - Reflect on personal money habits
      - Income, Careers, Business, & Entrepreneurship
        - Research careers and potential earnings
        - Understand taxes and deductions
      - Accounts, Savings, Budgeting, & Investment
        - Set financial goals and plan steps to achieve them
        - Compare banks and savings products
      - Credit, Debt, & Loans
        - Understand credit scores and borrowing responsibly
      - Risk Management & Insurance
        - Identify types of insurance and their purposes
    - 6th Grade
      - Financial Psychology
        - Examine influences on financial decisions (peers, media)
        - Develop strategies for self-control and delayed gratification
      - Income, Careers, Business, & Entrepreneurship
        - Investigate career clusters and entrepreneurship
        - Understand paychecks and deductions
      - Accounts, Savings, Budgeting, & Investment
        - Create a detailed budget including income and expenses
        - Analyze interest and investment basics
      - Credit, Debt, & Loans
        - Understand loan terms and interest
        - Evaluate borrowing options
      - Risk Management & Insurance
        - Explore real-life insurance scenarios
    - 7th Grade 
      - Financial Psychology
        - Analyze advertising and its effect on spending
        - Develop a personal financial plan
      - Income, Careers, Business, & Entrepreneurship
        - Explore education’s impact on income
        - Develop a simple business plan
      - Accounts, Savings, Budgeting, & Investment
        - Use budgeting tools and apps
        - Compare investment options
      - Credit, Debt, & Loans
        - Understand credit reports and how to build credit
      - Risk Management & Insurance
        - Assess risks and insurance needs for teens
    - 8th Grade
      - Financial Psychology
        - Reflect on financial values and long-term goals.
        - Plan for major life expenses (education, transportation).
      - Income, Careers, Business, & Entrepreneurship
        - Prepare for first jobs (applications, interviews).
        - Explore entrepreneurship in depth.
      - Accounts, Savings, Budgeting, & Investment
        - Manage a simulated bank account.
        - Evaluate investment risks and returns.
      - Credit, Debt, & Loans
        - Learn about credit cards, loans, and managing debt.
      - Risk Management & Insurance
        - Understand insurance policies and claims.
  - [Jump$tart](https://www.jumpstart.org/wp-content/uploads/2023/04/2021_Natl_Standards_Downloadable_final.pdf) National Standards for Personal Financial Education
    - Goal = provide a framework for a complete curriculum that prepares K-12 students for lives as smart consumers
    - Organized by grade level benchmarks (4th, 8th, 12th) across 6 core topics
    - 1. Earning Income: Wages, salaries, taxes, and the relationship between education/skills and earnings.
      - Grade 4: Focuses on the existence of different job types, the link between skills and jobs, and payment methods (wages vs. salaries). Students learn that taxes pay for government goods and services.
      - Grade 8: Expands to the concept of human capital, teaching that education and training increase income potential but carry opportunity costs (time, money). Students differentiate between net and gross income and learn about Social Security and entrepreneurship risks.
      - Grade 12: Focuses on complex career decisions, including:
        - Evaluating employee benefit packages (health insurance, retirement) alongside salary.
        - Understanding labor market changes due to technology and economic downturns.
        - Analyzing the tax system (W-2 vs. 1099 forms, progressive tax rates, and filing taxes).
        - Investigating the "gig" economy and small business ownership.
    - 2. Spending: Budgeting, decision-making, and consumer protection.
      - Grade 4: Introduces the concepts of scarcity, needs vs. wants, and opportunity costs. Students identify how peer pressure and advertising influence choices.
      - Grade 8: Students learn to create a budget and critically evaluate product claims and information sources. They compare payment methods (debit vs. credit cards) and their associated protections.
      - Grade 12: Advanced decision-making involving:
        - Housing: Comparing the costs and benefits of renting vs. buying a home and understanding lease terms.
        - Philanthropy: Discussing motivations for charitable giving and researching non-profits.
        - Consumer Protection: Investigating fraud, scams, and the role of agencies like the CFPB and FTC.
        - Tracking: Developing systems for financial record-keeping.
    - 3. Saving: Setting aside income for future use, interest rates, and financial institutions.
      - Grade 4: Defines saving as choosing not to spend today to buy later. Students learn that banks are safe places to keep money and pay interest.
      - Grade 8: Introduces the mathematics of saving.
        - Compound Interest: Differentiating between simple and compound interest and using the "Rule of 72".
        - Safety: Understanding federal deposit insurance (FDIC/NCUA).
      - Grade 12: Deals with strategic saving and external factors.
        - Inflation: How inflation erodes purchasing power if interest rates are too low.
        - Account Types: Comparing CDs, money market accounts, and traditional savings.
        - Incentives: Understanding tax-advantaged saving (Roth vs. Traditional IRAs, 401ks) and employer matches.
        - Behavior: Strategies to overcome psychological obstacles to saving (e.g., automation).
    - 4. Investing: Using money to achieve long-term goals through riskier assets.
      - Grade 4: Basic distinction: Savings accounts are for short-term/emergencies; investing is for long-term growth but carries risk.
      - Grade 8: Introduction to asset classes.
        - Assets: Defining stocks (ownership), bonds (lending), and mutual funds.
        - Diversification: How pooling investments reduces risk.
        - Compounding: The benefit of starting to invest early.
      - Grade 12: Advanced portfolio management.
        - Risk Tolerance: Assessing personal risk profiles based on age and financial resources.
        - Market Mechanics: Nominal vs. real returns, and why riskier assets (like crypto or collectibles) are speculative.
        - Regulation: The role of the SEC and prohibitions against insider trading.
        - Fintech: Using robo-advisors and automated platforms.
    - 5. Managing Credit: Borrowing, interest, credit scores, and debt management.
      - Grade 4: The basic concept that credit allows immediate use of goods in exchange for future repayment with interest.
      - Grade 8: The cost of credit.
        - APR: Understanding advertised rates and introductory offers.
        - Credit History: How a borrower's past behavior influences their interest rate.
        - Good vs. Bad Debt: Financing education/housing vs. consumer purchases.
      - Grade 12: Comprehensive debt management.
        - Mortgages: Fixed vs. adjustable rates and down payments.
        - Student Loans: FAFSA, federal vs. private loans, and repayment plans.
        - Credit Reporting: Deep dive into credit bureaus, credit score calculations, and disputing errors.
        - Bankruptcy & Protection: Understanding bankruptcy laws and alternative financial services (payday loans).
    - 6. Managing Risk: Insurance, identity theft, and risk transfer strategies.
      - Grade 4: Recognizing risk in daily life and the concept of insurance to limit large financial losses.
      - Grade 8: Mechanics of insurance.
        - Key Terms: Premiums, deductibles, copayments, and coinsurance.
        - Risk Management: Avoiding, reducing, retaining, or transferring risk.
      - Grade 12: Purchasing specific protections.
        - Types of Insurance: Detailed analysis of health, auto (liability vs. comprehensive), renters/homeowners, life, and disability insurance.
        - Identity Theft: Proactive steps to safeguard personal data and reactive steps to take if identity is stolen.
        - Warranties: Evaluating extended warranties vs. the cost of replacement
    - Analysis of Standards/Organization:
      - Helpful to break down all of FinLit into 6 main categories (Aurum does this with the 5 classes of financial experience)
      - Benchmarks are a good starting point but this lacks the insights that a fully AI-equipped FinLit system would have - it vaguely tracks knowledge through large periods of time rather than specifically mapping out what is necessary before progressing to other skills
  - Consumer Financial Protection Bureau (CFPB)
    - Young Children (ages 3-5)
      - Too young for abstract concepts, but parents can have simple conversations to help build foundations for their children
      - At this age, your child can develop these skills:
        - Ability to focus on what he has to do
        - Persistence to keep going even if it’s not easy
        - Ability to recognize trade-offs when it comes to things he cares about, like time, money, treats, or belongings
      - Your child is also working on:
        - Understanding what’s important about keeping and using things like time, money, treats, or belongings
        - Basic math skills, like counting and sorting
        - Abstract ideas like money and making trades or exchanges
      - Behaviors to look for
        - On track:
          - Show patience
          - Take their time
          - Try again and again
        - Need help:
          - Show frustration or temper
          - Fail to pay attention
    - School-age children to preteens (ages 6-12)
      - Can help children absorb rules to live by and day-to-day habits that shape how they earn, save, and shop.
      - At this age, your child can develop these skills:
        - Positive attitude about saving, being frugal, and self-control
        - Ability to plan ahead or save up for what she wants
        - Ability to make money choices that align with her own goals and values
        - Self-confidence in starting an unfamiliar money task
      - Your child is also working on:
        - Planning ahead and waiting for what she wants
        - Keeping future events in mind
        - Understanding basic financial processes and concepts, like banking and earning money
        - Successfully managing money, or other resources like time or belongings, to reach a goal
      - Behaviors to look for
        - On track:
          - Keep important things in a safe place
          - Make plans ahead of time
          - Keep track of assignments
          - Are dependable
        - Need help:
          - Regret how he or she spent money, time, or other valuable resources
          - Let others down
          - Feel fearful of hard tasks
    - Teens and young adults
      - Teens and young adults generally start to earn money and make decisions on their own. Adult supervision, guidance, and feedback can help them navigate successfully.
      - At this age, your child can develop these skills:
        - Ability to manage money or other resources to reach a goal
        - Understanding money concepts like long-term savings, taxes, invoices, or bank statements
        - Identifying reliable information sources and how to sort through information to get answers
      - Your child is also working on:
        - Critical thinking skills
        - Focusing on the future instead of just right now
        - Planning ahead and waiting for what she wants
        - Keeping a positive attitude about saving, being frugal, planning, and self-control
        - Building good money habits and good ways to make choices
        - Spending and saving based on her own goals and values
      - Behaviors to look for
        - On track:
          - Take steps to reach a goal, whether or not they actually achieve it
          - Resist things that seem too good to be true
          - Ask questions to understand money
        - Need help:
          - Give up when a money topic or goal seems too hard
          - Make a choice based on unreliable information
          - Regret choices that don’t align with their original intentions
- Current Problems with FinLit/EdTech
  - **Failed/Insufficient FinLit Programs**
    - Mandell and Klein Study: FinLit course = Ineffective
      - High school students "who had taken a high school class in personal finance or money management are **no more financially literate than those who have not**" (Mandell, 2009) [sample size = 79]. The course lasted a full semester and was "highly regarded," covering the topics that were *thought to be relevant to students.*
        - Possible errors with this program: Not engaging (no indication of what specifically makes this course innovative/memorable, likely lecture-based, students do not see relevance or care, based on what administrators thought students should know instead of student input). Small sample size. Outdated (published 2009).
        - Mandell and Klein have shown that "low levels of financial literacy are partly due to a lack of motivation on the part of the students" [here](https://www.jstor.org/stable/43861592)
        - Mandell and Klein ([2007](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-981-10-0360-8_32?fromPaywallRec=false#ref-CR40)) concluded that the lack of motivation was primarily responsible for such an outcome due to the fact that the content of the **courses did not have direct relevance** to financial decisions made by the students in real life focusing instead on** investment strategies and retirement planning**. [here](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-981-10-0360-8_32?fromPaywallRec=false)
    - "Even large interventions incorporating 24 or more hours of instruction have practically no effect if measured 20 or more months after the intervention. Thus, the empirical research literature suggests that increasing financial training and education is not a very effective method for changing actual economic behavior" [here](https://www.jstor.org/stable/resrep22790.5)
    - Some research suggests that financial education does not have a significant effect on improving financial knowledge scores of HS students in the US ([Mandell 2005](https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/j.1745-6606.2010.01170.x#b58)). [Willis (2008)](https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/j.1745-6606.2010.01170.x#b91) contends that the costs of financial education programs outweigh potential benefits.
    - Many early childhood educators use play money, cash registers, and opportunities for buying things in a play store to tangentially teach about financial literacy. **This unstructured way of teaching financial literacy concepts might be counterproductive** in engaging with the children in a purposive manner to help them understand, internalize, and use the financial literacy concepts. [possibly good as a short introduction to buying/selling but not substantive beyond that, probably something that toddlers would understand] [here](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s10643-024-01740-7)
    - BI Program: improved financial understanding but no better than traditional program [here](https://www.academia.edu/125924558/A_behaviorally_informed_financial_education_program_for_the_financially_vulnerable_Design_and_effectiveness)
      - Main finding: the BI program had a positive impact on self-reported financial skills and knowledge and financial behavior but not on financial well-being and financial situation. The BI program was not more effective than the traditional program.
  - **Issues With EdTech Apps**
    - Why Most Education Apps Fail (Hendrick)
      - Source: Substack - [The Learning Dispatch](https://carlhendrick.substack.com/p/why-most-education-apps-fail?utm_source=share&utm_medium=android&shareImageVariant=overlay&r=53462b&triedRedirect=true)
      - Core issue = superficial appearance of learning ignoring the mechanics of how learning actually works in the brain (instructional invariants)
      - Invariant = a condition that must remain true for a system to function correctly
        - Most educational apps violate these invariants because compliance with these invariants conflicts with the metrics that drive their business models: session time, completion rates, user satisfaction, and the holy grail of engagement.
          - Adds to idea of misaligned incentives within financial education (though this is applying that to all education - the EdTech apps flaunt superficial markers of learning instead of designing around what actually matters, many committing the gamification fallacy in the process as well)
      - Invariant 1: The Target Skill Must Be the Only Path to Success
        - Example: seeing pic of elephant while learner trying to read word elephant, learner recognizes pic and gets question right, taking a shortcut and appearing to learn successfully while not actually learning at all
          - Duolingo violates this invariant all the time 
      - Instructional Invariant 2: Mastery Before Progress
        - Learners who advance with gaps will struggle with everything built upon those gaps.
          - Importance of dependency architecture - Math Academy way with DAG that formalizes all dependencies/relationships between different topics, shows learning engine that user must master topic A before moving to topic B so that learning gaps are closed before more complex learning is attempted. 
        - Why apps violate this: mastery gates cause friction, users get stuck, parents complain, completion rates drop 
          - Business model depends on "smooth progression, on the feeling of advancement, on the dopamine hit of moving to the next level" - white hat drives. Real mastery learning requirements cause frustration, and frustration causes churn 
            - Frustration is the result of excessive black hat drives (scarcity, unpredictability, avoidance)
          - The desirable difficulties that research has identified, the spacing, interleaving, and retrieval practice that produce durable learning, are difficult precisely because they demand cognitive effort. 
            - Desirable difficulty comes from a delicate balance of black hat and white hat drives -> this article suggests that traditional EdTech platforms overcompensate for the negative feelings experienced in traditional classrooms using white hat (making users feel happy and motivated without giving them the real challenges that yield mastery). 
      - Instructional Invariant 3: Active Response Required
        - EdTech apps love passive passive formats because they are low friction (users do not get frustrated, session times stay high, paying parents see their children “engaged")
          - But learning requires retrieval, production, generation. Information that washes over a passive learner leaves almost no trace in long-term memory.
      - The Business Model Problem
        - Compliance with the invariants conflicts with every metric they optimize for
          - Requiring the target skill as the only path to success makes tasks harder and failure more common. 
          - Enforcing mastery before progress creates bottlenecks and frustration.
          - Demanding active production rather than passive consumption increases cognitive load and reduces session duration.
        - The incentive structure is almost perfectly misaligned with the conditions for learning.
          - Apps are optimized for engagement, personalization, smooth user experience, motivation, and time on task. 
            - These are not bad things in themselves, but they are not learning.
      - Learning is a constrained process
        - It occurs only when certain conditions (involving the memory, attention, and cognition)
        - The apps that will actually work (that will produce genuine learning at scale) will be those that take these invariants as their starting point rather than as obstacles to user experience.
  - **Demographic & Practical Issues - Problems With Traditional Classroom FinLit**
    - Minority who have taken a FinLit course -> low average score
      - From 2020-2024, the percentage of questions Americans answered correctly on the P-Fin Index dropped by 4% [here](https://www.weforum.org/stories/2024/04/financial-literacy-money-education/)
      - Only 17% of American adults surveyed had taken a personal finance course in high school, 26 states in the US require students to take a course to graduate [here](https://www.ramseysolutions.com/financial-literacy/financial-literacy-crisis-in-america#:~:text=REPORT%20SUMMARY,felt%20stress%20because%20of%20money)
      - Avg score on the Jump$tart FinLit test was <50%, there was also no indication that students who had taken a FL course performed better. 
      - "Americans with financial education appear to know they need to save but do not know how to do so." (pg 113 [here](https://www.jstor.org/stable/48738996))
    - Limited resources: many calculations and complex applications. Teachers often have to rely on theoretical examples or word problems to demonstrate concepts which is boring to the students. 
    - Teachers must be knowledgable themselves (already somewhat rare) and able to keep up with the evolving financial sphere
    - Limited risk assessment + false confidence
      - Respondents also had a false sense of confidence about their FL, on average giving themselves a score of 5.1/7. For all generations between the Silent Generation and Generation Z, risk assessment is the lowest scoring factor on the P-Fin Index, even compared to the other measured groups: borrowing, saving, consuming, earning, go-to info sources, investing, and insuring. Denmark and the UK are making FL education mandatory in schools, boosting their rankings for FL fluency [here](https://www.weforum.org/stories/2024/04/financial-literacy-money-education/)
      - "The results indicate that those that claim to have strong financial education or have had a required course in financial education are ill-equipped to understand basic financial concepts" [here](https://www.jstor.org/stable/48738996)
      - Foltice and Langer (2018): significant % do not understand compound interest well. >25% who had debt believed that it rose linearly, not exponentially. (pg 106 [here](https://www.jstor.org/stable/48738996))
    - Evidence that FinLit courses do not help
      - Avg score on the Jump$tart FL test was <50%, there was also no indication that students who had taken a FL course performed better. 
      - "As of now, our financial education only goes partway. Participants in this large, cross-sectional study understand where they should, at least regarding recognition and saving for retirement. However, education does not complete the pathway of how to get there. Despite having had a financial education course, most do not understand mortgages, compound interest, diversification." (pg 113 [here](https://www.jstor.org/stable/48738996))
    - Most students do not understand stocks, bonds, how to save/budget, 401k's or taxes - all of these concepts can be easily broken down in game platform
    - There is a global lack of understanding of credit cards across all ages
    - "Just in time" education is favored (able to help people at most relevant time - when they "need it most", buying a house/car, planning retirement, doing taxes) but tends to be insufficient if not undergirded by education in the earlier years.
    - "Just in time" programs further the problem of fragmentation within the financial EdTech/FinTech sector.
  - **Problems With Alternative FinLit Education Methods**
    - Lack of AI + Data
    - Actual games have no built-in curriculum, "games" with curriculum are module-based and not fun (think Duolingo - gamified but not a *game*)
      - If financial education is everywhere (YouTube, TikTok, schools, peers/family, online modules) why are we failing so badly?
      - Not lack of data, but a fundamental failure of pedagogy and engagement
      - System focused on educational depth over behavioral change
        - Core conflict: traditional education wants you to understand concept intellectually, but financial health requires discipline. All the knowledge in the world about financial concepts is useless if users don't pay attention or build actual habits
          - Behavioral science failure, not information failure
      - Have something deep but boring or something super engaging but have little/nothing to do with actual curriculum
    - No FinLit game has endogenous assessment
    - FinLit is an afterthought in mainstream schools (but that is changing - slowly and inconsistently)
    - Fragmentation - gap in the market 
    - Cognitive overload: Traditional financial literacy methods often lead to cognitive overload, making it difficult for individuals to understand and apply complex financial concepts.
    - Inefficiencies in financial education: Current financial education methods are often ineffective, leading to poor decision-making and missed opportunities.
    - Lack of engagement: Financial literacy education is often boring and unengaging, failing to motivate individuals to learn and take control of their financial lives.
      - Insufficient emphasis on cognitive drives and biases: Financial literacy education often fails to account for the cognitive drives and biases that influence financial decision-making.
    - Inadequate preparation for real-world financial decisions: Traditional financial education methods do not adequately prepare individuals for the complex financial decisions they will face in real life.
    - Failure to provide a lifelong financial education: Current financial education methods often focus on a specific age group or stage of life, rather than providing a comprehensive and ongoing financial education.
    - Inability to balance freedom and structure: Financial literacy education often fails to strike a balance between providing structure and allowing individuals the freedom to make choices and take control of their financial lives.
  - **Summarized Info**
    - Top-line summary
      - FinLit at the population level remains low and largely stagnant (roughly ~40–55% correct on standard P-Fin measures in recent years), with young adults often scoring worst [here](https://www.weforum.org/stories/2024/04/financial-literacy-money-education/?utm_)
      - Financial education can produce short-term gains in knowledge, but effects often fade without reinforcement, and evidence about durable behavior change is mixed. Rigorous meta-analyses show positive but modest and heterogeneous impacts [here](https://www.nber.org/system/files/working_papers/w27057/w27057.pdf?utm_)
      - Implementation is wildly uneven: curricula, timing (when in life it’s offered), teacher preparation, and state/district mandates vary — so many students never get high-quality, appropriately timed instruction [here](https://www.nefe.org/impact/nefe-annual-report/annual-report-2023.aspx?utm_)
        - Fragmentation issue Aurum
      - EdTech promises scale and personalization but runs into infrastructure, adoption, equity, data/privacy, and design-fit problems that blunt impact in practice
    - Explicit Pain Points
      - 1) Population-level problems (what people actually don’t know / behavior)
        - Low baseline knowledge, persistent over time. Large surveys (P-Fin, OECD) show national averages that haven’t meaningfully improved and major generation gaps (Gen Z often lowest). This limits the floor any intervention starts from [here](https://www.weforum.org/stories/2024/04/financial-literacy-money-education/?utm_)
        - Low confidence & behavioral barriers. Shame, anxiety, procrastination, and low financial self-efficacy reduce help-seeking and behavior change even when information is available. (Surveys highlight worry and avoidance alongside knowledge gaps.) [here](https://www.investopedia.com/her-money-mindset-survey-8647284?utm_)
        - Complex products & misinformation environment. Proliferation of fintech, crypto, buy-now-pay-later, and targeted marketing/scams increases cognitive load - people may learn the wrong practices from influencers or predatory ads
      - 2) Financial education (programmatic) problems
        - Heterogeneous mandates and timing. Some states/classes require personal finance, others don’t; even where courses exist they vary in depth and timing (too late or too early). This inconsistency reduces system-level impact [here](https://www.nefe.org/impact/nefe-annual-report/annual-report-2023.aspx?utm_)
        - Weak long-term retention and behavior change. Randomized trials/meta-analyses show short-term knowledge gains are common but fade, and behavior changes are smaller and inconsistent — suggesting single-shot courses aren’t sufficient [here](https://www.nber.org/system/files/working_papers/w27057/w27057.pdf?utm_)
        - Teachers underprepared / curriculum gaps. Many teachers lack confidence or training to teach personal finance; curricula often focus on knowledge rather than decision-making skills, behavioral design, or context [here](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC12411838/?utm_)
        - Competing priorities / schedule pressure. Schools struggle to fit stand-alone financial literacy into already full schedules; districts prioritize tested subjects [here](https://medium.com/future-of-school/should-schools-teach-financial-literacy-critical-need-despite-implementation-challenges-in-2025-ae6f6f3849f8)
      - 3) EdTech / product problems (FinEd tech-specific)
        - Digital divide & infrastructure limits. EdTech assumes device/connectivity and basic digital literacy — not true for many low-income or rural learners; this widens disparities [here](https://files.eric.ed.gov/fulltext/EJ1453738.pdf?utm_)
        - Adoption friction among educators. Teachers and districts can resist new tech due to workload, training needs, lack of alignment with standards, and unclear evidence of efficacy [here](https://michiganvirtual.org/research/publications/breaking-barriers-a-meta-analysis-of-educator-acceptance-of-ai-technology-in-education/?utm_source=chatgpt.com)
        - Measurement & evidence gaps. EdTech products often lack rigorous, independent evaluations (RCTs or long-term follow-up). Without that, schools hesitate to adopt, and products can’t iterate on what truly moves behavior [here](https://www.nber.org/system/files/working_papers/w27057/w27057.pdf?utm_)
        - Data privacy, commercialization and bias. FinEd tools sometimes collect sensitive financial data or have monetization ties (affiliates, product placement); conflicts of interest and privacy concerns erode trust. (Policy reports highlight careful stewardship needs.) [here](https://www.mymoney.gov/system/files/2024-12/Fiscal-Year-2023-2024-Financial-Literacy-and-Education-Commission-Annual-Report-to-Congress.pdf?utm_source=chatgpt.com)
    - Why These Problems Persist
      - Misalignment of incentives. 
        - Schools measured on tested subjects (FinLit is more applied than tested)
        - Universities/EdTech companies measured on growth or engagement, not long-term financial outcomes
      - Complexity of financial behavior - Knowledge alone rarely changes habits; structural constraints (income volatility, access to products) limit options.
      - Research-practice gap. Good evidence exists but is patchy; translating RCT findings into scalable curricula and tech that fit classroom constraints is hard [here](https://www.nber.org/system/files/working_papers/w27057/w27057.pdf?utm_)
    - Practical Priorities
      - Move from one-off courses → longitudinal, staged learning. Teach concepts at multiple “teachable moments” (e.g., getting first job, opening bank account, first credit card, college financial decisions). This addresses fading effects [here](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S2214804325000679?utm_)
        - Aurum's 3 stages to combat fragmentation 
      - Rigorous evaluation as a product feature. Build RCTs or strong quasi-experimental evaluations into pilots; measure both knowledge and real behaviors (savings, debt levels, product uptake) over time [here](https://www.nber.org/system/files/working_papers/w27057/w27057.pdf?utm_)
        - Aurum's proprietary endogenous assessment algorithm
      - Design for equity and low-bandwidth contexts. Offer offline modes, low-literacy language variants, and culturally relevant scenarios; subsidize devices/connectivity where needed [here](https://files.eric.ed.gov/fulltext/EJ1453738.pdf?utm_)
        - Relevant to what Carolyn Driscoll mentioned about making Aurum accessible to underpriveledged/low socioeconomic backgrounds - they are the ones who could benefit from FinLit the most and who are the least likely to have access to it
      - Teacher enablement, not replacement. Provide concise professional development, turnkey lesson bundles aligned to standards, and classroom management tools — reduce onboarding friction [here](https://michiganvirtual.org/research/publications/breaking-barriers-a-meta-analysis-of-educator-acceptance-of-ai-technology-in-education/?utm_source=chatgpt.com)
        - Like Alpha's model with the teacher shifting roles from underappreciated grunt-work to guides
        - For further consideration - what intentional/explicit teacher tools can be added? Floated idea of Aurum classroom edition vs Aurum public/recreational
          - Classroom edition = more CompSim-like features and customizable settings for educators to provide more structured "lessons"
          - Recreational edition = more OWG-like features, no teacher involvement, feels like a true simulator game
      - Behavioral design + nudges + product integration. Combine learning with product features that nudge real behavior (automated savings, reminders, default options), and integrate with financial products only under strict privacy/conflict rules [here](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC9798916/?utm_)
        - Basic gamification features (nudges, streaks, etc) - borders on superficial gamification with points, badges, leaderboards. Aurum seeks to extend beyond these "gamified" features adopted by FinLit games 
      - Data privacy & trust guardrails. Transparent data policies, independent audits, and non-commercial content help build trust with schools and families [here](https://www.mymoney.gov/system/files/2024-12/Fiscal-Year-2023-2024-Financial-Literacy-and-Education-Commission-Annual-Report-to-Congress.pdf?utm_source=chatgpt.com)
        - Relevant to what Carolyn Driscoll mentioned about regulatory/legal and disclaimers, being very careful about getting trapped in 
  - **Problematic Financial Influencers/Authors**
    - Dave Ramsey
      - Bad Philosophy:
        - Dave: save $1000 as your starter emergency fund
          - Rebuttal: $1000 buffer is not enough, instead build a 3 month emergency fund before even paying off credit card debt
        - Dave: All debt is bad debt
          - Not always true
            - Student loans > credit cards
              - Student loans have a fixed and lower interest rate (credit cards have compounding and higher rate)
            - Instead 7% rule: if interest rate on debt is greater than 7%, pay it off ASAP (since avg return on stock market is 7%)
              - Idea based on opportunity cost - what has the highest payoff
        - Dave: If you can afford a house, don't get a 30 year mortgage and don't put down less than 20%
          - Not realistic, too rigid and if it doesn't work for you, it's your fault
          - Like dieting, budgets need breathing room - using shame/rigidity doesn't work long term
        - Dave: you don't need a credit score, get rid of credit cards
          - If you want a house/car/apartment, you need a credit score
          - As you progress through the Ramsey method, he will teach you how to start/run a business
            - Getting a credit card is one of the first things you do when starting a business 
    - Robert Kiyosaki
      - The fictional Rich Dad
        - In his books, Kiyosaki presents "Rich Dad" as a real person (his best friend's father). However, investigative journalists have never been able to locate or identify this person.
        - The Contradiction: After years of dodging the question, Kiyosaki admitted in a 2003 interview with SmartMoney magazine: "Is Harry Potter real? Why don’t you let Rich Dad be a myth, like Harry Potter?"
        - The Risk: He effectively admitted the foundation of his memoir is a parable, not a biography. Critics argue this undermines the "real world advice" claim.
      - The Bankruptcy (Practicing what he preaches but in a bad way)
        - Kiyosaki often teaches that the rich use corporations to protect their money. He proved this in a way that garnered bad press.
        - The Event: In 2012, one of his companies, Rich Global LLC, filed for Chapter 7 bankruptcy.
        - The Cause: He was sued by *The Learning Annex*, a company that helped launch his speaking career. The court ordered Kiyosaki to pay them nearly $24 million in unpaid royalties.
        - The Move: Instead of paying, he bankrupted the company. While this is technically "legal strategy" (which he teaches), it contradicts the image of limitless wealth and ethical business partnership.
      - The "Seminar" Scams: Kiyosaki licensed his brand to high-priced seminar companies that have been the subject of class-action lawsuits and investigative exposes (such as by CBC’s *Marketplace*).
        - The Model: Attendees attend a free seminar, get pressured to buy a $495 course, then get pressured to buy a $45,000 "mentorship."
        - The Reality: Investigations showed these courses often provided little value and were focused entirely on upselling.
        - Why this matters for Aurum:  Kiyosaki’s seminar business is the poster child for misaligned incentives (one of the largest problems in FinLit) — profit came from upselling, not student success.
      - MLM (multi-level marketing)
        - Kiyosaki argues that MLM is the best way for the average person to transition from "Employee" to "Business Owner."
        - The Argument: He claims the product doesn't matter; the real value is the education. He views MLMs as a "real-world business school" that teaches rejection handling, sales, and leadership for a low entry fee.
        - The Promise: He tells followers that joining an MLM is a way to build a "passive income asset" rather than just working for a wage.
        - His rhetoric is considered dangerous because it rebrands financial loss as "tuition."
          - The Mathematical Reality: According to the FTC and industry studies, 99% of MLM participants lose money. By calling it a "business school," Kiyosaki justifies these losses as the cost of education, keeping people trapped in schemes that are mathematically designed to fail for the bottom tier.
          - The "Business Owner" Lie: He tells vulnerable people they are "entrepreneurs." In reality, they are unpaid commission-only salespeople with zero control over the product, pricing, or supply chain. If the MLM shuts down, they lose everything (unlike a true business owner who owns their assets)
          - Predatory Targeting: He targets people desperate for financial freedom (the very people we are trying to help with Aurum) and funnels them into systems that extract their remaining capital.
- Current Curricula In States with FinLit Courses (data for knowledge graph)
  - NGPF Live Dashboard: States with FinLit Included in Public School Curriculum
    - [NGPF](https://www.ngpf.org/live-us-dashboard/) - Shows which states currently (or are in the process of) implementing a standalone Personal Finance course for all high schoolers
    - States with Standalone Required FinLit Courses (As of Dec 2025)
      - Alabama
        - Core Mandate
          - Requirement: One-semester (0.5 credit) course for all students entering 9th grade starting 2024–2025 (graduation requirement for class of 2028+).
        - Implementation & Resources
          - No State-Mandated Program: Districts select materials aligned to standards. Common adoptions include:
            - EverFi (digital modules)
            - Dave Ramsey's Foundations in Personal Finance (62% of AL districts)
            - NGPF (Next Gen Personal Finance) free resources
          - Teacher Flexibility: Lessons must cover required standards but can use:
            - Simulations (e.g., STAX investment game)
            - Case studies (e.g., student loan debt scenarios)
            - Real-world tools (IRS Form 1040, FAFSA worksheets)
          - Assessments: District-developed (no standardized state test). Common formats:
            - Budgeting projects
            - Credit report analysis exercises
            - Loan amortization calculations
        - Integration with Other Subjects
          - Digital Literacy (2025 Standards): Teaches online banking security, AI-powered financial tools, and cryptocurrency risks.
          - Mathematics: Applied algebra for interest calculations.
        - Key Deadlines
          - Voluntary Implementation: 2024–2025
          - Full Requirement: 2025–2026 for all public high schools.
        - 1. Practical Skills
          - Sunk Cost Fallacy: Explicitly listed under Standard 3 (Financial Goals) as a required concept.
          - 50/30/20 Rule: Explicitly listed as a recommended "Practical Skill" for budgeting.
          - Phishing Scams: Explicitly listed under Identity Theft protection.
        - 2. The Granular "Required Standards" (The Deep Dive)
          - The Official Course of Study (Statute 16-40-12) requires instruction in the following specific atomic concepts. If you are building a curriculum for Alabama, you must cover these specific "niche" topics that other states often skip:
            - Domain: Money Management & Income
              - The "Payroll Cards" Requirement: You must teach "payroll cards" alongside cash/check/direct deposit as a payment method.
              - Layaway Plans: Specifically listed as a payment method to evaluate.
              - Net vs. Gross: You must teach the calculation of disposable income after "government transfer payments" (e.g., SNAP, Medicaid) are accounted for.
            - Domain: Credit & Borrowing (The "Predatory" Clause)
              - Alabama is unique in explicitly naming *illegal* or predatory lending types in the standards. You must define and analyze:
              - Loan-Sharking: Explicitly required terminology.
              - Rent-to-Own Agreements: Must be analyzed for total cost vs. cash price.
              - Payday Loans: Must be taught as a "high interest rate" risk.
              - Bankruptcy: You must assess the specific "implications of bankruptcy" (Chapter 7 vs 13 is often implied here).
            - Domain: Saving & Investing
              - Rule of 72: The standard explicitly names the "Rule of 72" as the required method for teaching compound interest doubling time.
              - Specific Vehicles: You are required to evaluate: Mutual Funds, ETFs, Stocks, Bonds, CDs, Real Estate, and *Commodities* (Gold/Oil).
            - Domain: Consumer Protection (The Legal Layer)
              - Contract Law: You must teach "Simple Contracts" and binding agreements (specifically referencing college and car loans).
              - Consumer Laws: You must cover state and federal consumer protection laws (e.g., Truth in Lending Act, Fair Credit Reporting Act).
        - Summary of "Must-Haves" for Alabama Compliance
          - If you are building a "gold standard" Alabama curriculum, it is not enough to just teach "Budgeting." You must specifically teach:
            - Rule of 72 (Math)
            - Sunk Cost Fallacy (Behavior)
            - Loan-Sharking & Payday Loans (Protection)
            - Payroll Cards & Layaway (Mechanics)
            - 50/30/20 Rule (Application)
        - FinLit Knowledge Graph Nodes Yielded (Doug suggestion)
          - Aligns fully to national standards with emphasis on career prep integration.
            - Earning Income
              - DOK1 Fact: Most people earn wage and salary income in return for working, and they can also earn income from interest, dividends, rents, entrepreneurship, business profits, or increases in the value of investments.
                - DOK2 Summary: Explain how diversifying income sources like adding entrepreneurship to a salary job enhances financial stability during economic downturns by reducing reliance on a single paycheck.
              - DOK1 Fact: Employers generally pay higher wages and salaries to more educated, skilled, and productive workers.
                - DOK2 Summary: Classify job opportunities by skill requirements to prioritize education investments that yield higher lifetime earnings.
            - Spending
              - DOK1 Fact: A budget is a plan for allocating a person’s spendable income to necessary and desired goods and services.
                - DOK2 Summary: Describe how creating a weekly budget helps prioritize essential expenses like housing over discretionary ones like dining out.
              - DOK1 Fact: Individual spending decisions may be influenced by financial constraints, personal preferences, unique needs, peers, and advertising.
                - DOK2 Summary: Analyze an impulse purchase influenced by ads to determine if it aligns with personal financial goals.
            - Saving
              - DOK1 Fact: People who have sufficient income can choose to save some of it for future uses such as emergencies or later purchases.
                - DOK2 Summary: Organize a monthly savings plan by calculating 10% of income for emergencies to build a buffer against unexpected car repairs.
              - DOK1 Fact: Inflation can erode the value of savings if the interest rate earned on a savings account is less than the inflation rate.
                - DOK2 Summary: Compare bank account rates to current inflation to select options that preserve purchasing power for future goals.
            - Investing
              - DOK1 Fact: Investors receive a return on their investment in the form of income and/or growth in value of their investment over time.
                - DOK2 Summary: Predict how reinvesting stock dividends annually compounds returns, accelerating growth toward retirement savings.
              - DOK1 Fact: Riskier investments tend to earn higher long-run rates of return than lower-risk investments.
                - DOK2 Summary: Evaluate a portfolio mix by balancing high-risk stocks with bonds to match personal tolerance for volatility.
            - Managing Credit and Debt
              - DOK1 Fact: Credit allows consumers to buy now and pay later, but interest and fees add to the amount owed.
                - DOK2 Summary: Calculate total cost of a credit card purchase with 18% interest to decide between paying in full or saving up.
              - DOK1 Fact: Lenders use credit history and scores to decide whether to lend money and at what interest rate.
                - DOK2 Summary: Review a sample credit report to identify actions like on-time payments that improve scores for better loan terms.
            - Managing Risk
              - DOK1 Fact: Insurance protects consumers from the financial costs of certain risks by providing reimbursement for covered losses.
                - DOK2 Summary: Compare auto insurance quotes to select coverage that reimburses accident damages without excessive premiums.
              - DOK1 Fact: People can reduce the financial risk of job loss, illness, or death by purchasing insurance and building savings.
                - DOK2 Summary: Plan a response to job loss by combining emergency savings with unemployment insurance to cover 3-6 months of expenses.
      - California (New - Passed 2024)
        - Core Mandate
          - Requirement: One-semester (0.5 credit) standalone course in personal finance.
          - Target Audience: All high school students graduating in the 2030–31 school year and beyond.
          - The "Economics" Swap: Uniquely, students who complete this course may elect to be exempt from the existing one-semester *Economics* graduation requirement.
        - Implementation & Resources
          - Timeline for Schools: Every public high school (including charters) must *offer* the course starting in the 2027–2028 school year.
          - Curriculum Development: The State Board of Education (SBE) is legally required to adopt a specific curriculum guide by May 31, 2026.
            - *Status:* The Instructional Quality Commission (IQC) is currently drafting this guide.
          - Teacher Credentialing: Authorized for teachers holding single-subject credentials in Social Science, Business, Mathematics, or Home Economics.
          - Funding: The "Arts, Music, & Instructional Materials Block Grant" (approx. $3.36B) specifically lists Financial Literacy as a priority funding area for materials and professional development.
        - 1. Practical Skills
          - Tax Forms: Explicit requirement to understand the tax system, including "how to read tax forms and pay stubs".
          - College Value Analysis: Unlike states that just focus on FAFSA, California requires identifying "low-cost community college options" and "workforce education" pathways.
          - Scam Detection: Enhancing consumer protection skills by raising awareness of "common scams and frauds".
        - 2. The Granular "Required Standards" (The Deep Dive): AB 2927 and Education Code 51284.5 explicitly list 12 required topics. If you are building for California compliance, you cannot skip these unique legislative "must-haves":
          - Domain: Psychology & Behavior (Unique to CA)
            - Behavioral Finance: You are legally required to teach "Understanding how psychology can impact one's financial well-being." This is rare in state mandates and points to a focus on *spending behavior* and *marketing influence* rather than just math.
            - Charitable Giving: Explicitly listed as a required topic. You must cover the role of philanthropy in personal finance.
          - Domain: Income & Employment
            - Net Income Factors: You must cover "Employment and understanding factors that affect net income," referencing Labor Code 49110.5 (which deals with minors' work permits and hours), implying a focus on youth employment rights.
            - Independent Living: Budgeting principles must be specifically framed around "independent living" (preparing for rent/utilities in a high-cost state).
          - Domain: Credit & Debt
            - Credit Scores: Explicit instruction on "managing credit scores" is required.
            - Student Loan Policy: Beyond just "loans," you must cover "policies that provide student loan forgiveness," reflecting a legislative desire to ensure students know their relief options.
          - Domain: Investing
            - Wealth Building: The standard requires teaching "Principles of investing and building wealth," specifically naming:
              - 401(k) plans and Pensions
              - IRAs (Individual Retirement Accounts)
              - Stocks, Bonds, Mutual Funds, and Index Funds.
        - Summary of "Must-Haves" for California ComplianceIf you are building a "gold standard" California curriculum, you must specifically include:
          - Behavioral Psychology (How emotions drive spending)
          - Charitable Giving (Philanthropy)
          - Student Loan Forgiveness (Specific policy awareness)
          - Community College/Vocational Pathways (Cost-benefit vs. University)
          - Tax Form Literacy (Reading forms/stubs)
        - FinLit Knowledge Graph Nodes Yielded (Doug suggestion)
          - Aligns to national with added digital banking focus (Class of 2031); NGPF crosswalk available.
            - Earning Income
              - DOK1 Fact: Spendable income is lower than gross income due to taxes assessed on income by federal, state, and local governments.
                - DOK2 Summary: Adjust a paycheck stub for California state taxes to forecast net pay for accurate budgeting.
              - DOK1 Fact: Interest, dividends, and capital appreciation are examples of unearned income derived from financial investments.
                - DOK2 Summary: Track unearned income from a robo-advisor app to integrate it into a diversified income strategy.
            - Spending
              - DOK1 Fact: People can often improve their financial well-being by making well-informed spending decisions, which includes critical evaluation of price, quality, product information, and method of payment.
                - DOK2 Summary: Use a shopping app to compare prices and reviews, selecting cash over credit to avoid fees.
              - DOK1 Fact: Housing decisions depend on individual preferences, circumstances, and costs, and can impact personal satisfaction and financial well-being.
                - DOK2 Summary: Weigh San Francisco rent vs. buying costs in a spreadsheet to align with long-term relocation plans.
            - Saving
              - DOK1 Fact: Funds needed for transactions, bill-paying, or purchases, are commonly held in federally insured checking or savings accounts at financial institutions because these accounts offer easy access to their money and low risk.
                - DOK2 Summary: Set up direct deposit to a high-yield savings app for automatic transfers, ensuring quick access for emergencies.
              - DOK1 Fact: Tax policies that allow people to save pretax earnings or to reduce or defer taxes on interest earned provide incentives for people to save.
                - DOK2 Summary: Contribute to a 529 plan for education savings to leverage state tax deductions on contributions.
            - Investing
              - DOK1 Fact: People can more easily achieve their financial goals by investing steadily over many years, reinvesting dividends, and capital gains to compound their returns.
                - DOK2 Summary: Automate $50 monthly into an index fund via a digital platform to demonstrate compounding over 10 years.
              - DOK1 Fact: Investors select investments that are consistent with their risk tolerance, and they diversify across a number of different investment choices to reduce investment risk.
                - DOK2 Summary: Build a sample ETF portfolio diversified across tech and bonds to mitigate market downturns.
            - Managing Credit and Debt
              - DOK1 Fact: Overuse of credit cards can lead to unmanageable debt and negatively affect credit ratings.
                - DOK2 Summary: Track credit utilization on a statement to keep it under 30%, improving approval odds for future loans.
              - DOK1 Fact: Bankruptcy is a legal process that allows consumers to discharge or reorganize debt, but it has long-lasting effects on creditworthiness.
                - DOK2 Summary: Outline steps to avoid bankruptcy by prioritizing high-interest debt repayment in a debt snowball plan.
            - Managing Risk
              - DOK1 Fact: People should evaluate and compare insurance coverage, deductibles, and premiums of various policies to choose coverage that best matches their needs and financial capacity.
                - DOK2 Summary: Adjust health insurance deductibles based on income to balance premiums with out-of-pocket risks.
              - DOK1 Fact: Identity theft occurs when someone uses personal information without authorization, and it can have serious financial consequences.
                - DOK2 Summary: Set up credit monitoring alerts to detect and freeze accounts quickly after a data breach.
      - Colorado (New - Passed 2025)
        - Core Mandate
          - Requirement: One-semester (0.5 credit) personal financial literacy course (or equivalent instruction) required for graduation.
          - Target Audience: Applies to graduating classes starting in 2028 or 2029 (entering 9th graders in 2025-2026), depending on the specific district roll-out aligned with state legislation.
          - The "District Choice" Model: Colorado is strictly "Local Control." The state mandates *that* students demonstrate competence, but districts decide *how* (standalone course, embedded in Economics/Math, or capstone project). However, the standalone course is the preferred "Gold Standard" implementation under new funding models.
        - Implementation & Resources
          - No State-Mandated Program: Districts select their own materials but must align with the Colorado Academic Standards (CAS) for Personal Financial Literacy (PFL).
          - Common Adoptions:
            - NGPF (Next Gen Personal Finance): Widely used for its alignment with CAS standards.
            - Economic Literacy Colorado (ELC): A critical state-specific resource providing teacher training and materials tailored to Colorado's unique economic landscape (e.g., TABOR amendment context).
            - Junior Achievement: Frequently used partner for district implementation.
          - Funding: The Financial Literacy Cash Fund provides grants specifically to "small and rural school districts" to adopt curriculum and train teachers.
        - 1. Practical Skills
          - FAFSA & CASFA: Explicit requirement to understand the Free Application for Federal Student Aid (FAFSA) and the Colorado Application for State Financial Aid (CASFA).
          - Career ROI: Calculating the "Return on Investment" for various career paths, specifically comparing "college vs. trade school vs. workforce".
          - Cost of Living Analysis: Creating budgets based on *local* Colorado housing and cost-of-living data.
        - 2. The Granular "Required Standards" (The Deep Dive): Colorado’s 2020/2022 Academic Standards (PFL) mandate instruction in 4 distinct Standards. To be compliance-grade, you must cover these specific nuances that go beyond generic finance:
          - Standard 1: Financial Planning (Decision Making)
            - Behavioral Economics: You must analyze how "psychology, marketing, and external forces" influence spending decisions (similar to California, Colorado requires teaching the *why* behind spending).
            - Fiduciary Duty: Uniquely, Colorado requires students to understand "how to select financial advisors" and the concept of fiduciary responsibility.
          - Standard 2: Earning Income (Career & Taxes)
            - The "Gig Economy": Explicitly requires analyzing "entrepreneurship and non-traditional income sources" (freelancing, gig work) alongside traditional W-2 employment.
            - Tax Exemptions: You must teach how to read a pay stub with specific attention to "tax exemptions, deductions, and withholding".
          - Standard 3: Saving & Investing (Wealth)
            - Risk Management: Differentiating between "insurable" (health, auto) and "non-insurable" risks.
            - Diversification: Analyzing how diversification reduces risk in investment portfolios.
          - Standard 4: Credit & Debt (Borrowing)
            - The "3 Cs of Credit": Explicitly references teaching Capacity, Character, and Collateral as the framework lenders use.
            - Predatory Lending: You must define and analyze "predatory lending practices" (specifically payday loans and high-interest alternative credit).
            - Bankruptcy: Assessing the long-term legal and financial implications of filing for bankruptcy.
        - Summary of "Must-Haves" for Colorado ComplianceIf you are building a "gold standard" Colorado curriculum, you must specifically include:
          - Fiduciary Standards (How to hire help)
          - Behavioral Economics (Psychology)
          - The "Gig Economy" (Freelancing/1099 work)
          - CASFA Awareness (State financial aid)
          - The "3 Cs" of Credit (Lender perspective)
        - FinLit Knowledge Graph Nodes Yielded (Doug suggestion)
          - Strong emphasis on investing/risk; standards since 2008; NGPF crosswalk.
            - Earning Income
              - DOK1 Fact: Compensation for a job or career can be in the form of wages, salaries, commissions, tips, or bonuses, and may also include contributions to employee benefits.
                - DOK2 Summary: Compare commission-based sales jobs to salaried roles, factoring in benefits for total compensation.
              - DOK1 Fact: Changes in economic conditions, technology, or the labor market can cause changes in income, career opportunities, or employment status.
                - DOK2 Summary: Update a resume with tech skills during AI shifts to secure higher-paying remote opportunities.
            - Spending
              - DOK1 Fact: When there is sufficient money in their budget, people may decide to give money to others, save, or invest to achieve future goals.
                - DOK2 Summary: Allocate 5% of a budget to local charities after essentials, tracking impact on community support.
              - DOK1 Fact: Consumer decisions are influenced by the price of products or services, the price of alternatives, the consumer’s budget and preferences, and potential impact on the environment, society, and economy.
                - DOK2 Summary: Choose sustainable Colorado-sourced products by comparing costs and environmental labels.
            - Saving
              - DOK1 Fact: Interest rates, fees, and other account features vary by type of account and among financial institutions, with higher rates resulting in greater compound interest earned by savers.
                - DOK2 Summary: Switch to a credit union for lower fees, calculating annual interest gains on $5,000 saved.
              - DOK1 Fact: Employer defined contribution retirement plans and health savings accounts can provide incentives for employees to save.
                - DOK2 Summary: Maximize 401(k) matches to effectively double contributions, projecting retirement balance.
            - Investing
              - DOK1 Fact: Investors have many choices of investments that differ in expected rates of return and risk.
                - DOK2 Summary: Categorize mutual funds by risk level to select ones aligning with a 5-year home-buying goal.
              - DOK1 Fact: A person’s investment risk tolerance depends on factors such as personality, financial resources, investment experiences, and life circumstances.
                - DOK2 Summary: Adjust allocations post-life event like marriage to lower risk for family stability.
            - Managing Credit and Debt
              - DOK1 Fact: Interest is the price of borrowing money, and it is determined by the relationship between the amount borrowed and the time allowed for repayment.
                - DOK2 Summary: Use a loan calculator to compare 3% vs. 5% rates on a $10,000 auto loan over 5 years.
              - DOK1 Fact: Responsible credit management, such as paying bills on time and keeping debt levels low, helps build credit history and increase credit scores.
                - DOK2 Summary: Set calendar reminders for payments to maintain a score above 700 for favorable mortgage rates.
            - Managing Risk
              - DOK1 Fact: People make choices to obtain insurance because the cost of premiums is lower than the financial loss that could occur from the risk.
                - DOK2 Summary: Budget for renters insurance at $15/month to cover $20,000 in belongings against theft.
              - DOK1 Fact: Estate planning is the process of preparing for the management and disposal of assets after death.
                - DOK2 Summary: Draft a basic will template to designate beneficiaries, avoiding probate delays.
      - Connecticut
        - Core Mandate
          - Requirement: One-semester (0.5 credit) course in personal financial management and financial literacy.
          - Target Audience: Mandatory starting with the graduating class of 2027 (current high school sophomores/juniors). This is one of the most aggressive implementation timelines in the country.
          - The "Humanities" Swap: Uniquely, Connecticut allows this course to count toward the 9 required Humanities credits (which includes Civics and Arts) or as an elective. This gives districts significant flexibility in staffing it outside of the Math department.
        - Implementation & Resources
          - Local Control with State Standards: While there is no single mandated textbook, the Connecticut State Board of Education (SBE) formally adopted the MBA Research Standards (National Business Administration Standards) in 2022 to govern the content.
          - Common Adoptions:
            - NGPF (Next Gen Personal Finance): The Connecticut State Department of Education (CSDE) specifically partnered with NGPF to build the "Model Financial Literacy Course" for grades 9-12. If a district wants a "turn-key" compliant solution, this is the state-endorsed path.
            - MBA Research: Districts often use the "High School of Business" curriculum which aligns directly with the adopted standards.
          - Teacher Credentialing: Because of the "Humanities" classification, this course can often be taught by Social Studies, Business, or Family Consumer Science certified teachers, provided they cover the specific standards.
        - 1. Practical Skills
          - Tax Forms: Explicit requirement to "Interpret a pay stub" and differentiate between "gross, net, and taxable income".
          - Benefit Analysis: Uniquely, students must analyze "contributory vs. non-contributory employee benefits" (understanding what you pay for vs. what the employer pays for).
          - Online Commerce: The curriculum guidance specifically highlights "online commerce" and digital banking safety as key practical skills.
        - 2. The Granular "Required Standards" (The Deep Dive): Connecticut’s compliance framework is defined by the MBA Research Performance Indicators (adopted May 2022). To be compliance-grade, you must cover these specific atomic concepts:
          - Domain: Earning Income (Labor & Taxes)
            - The "Gig Economy" & 1099: You must explain why "some income is reported on IRS Form W-2 and some on Form 1099." This specifically targets freelancing and gig work tax liability.
            - Intangible Benefits: Students must evaluate "intangible job benefits" (working conditions, flexible hours, telecommuting) as part of total compensation—a very modern standard compared to other states.
            - Pay Gaps: The standards explicitly require discussing "possible explanations for the persistence of race and gender pay gaps".
          - Domain: Banking & Payment Systems
            - Digital Risks: Instruction must cover the "impact of using credit cards and debit cards" with a specific focus on online security and fraud.
            - Banking Tools: Students must demonstrate the ability to "reconcile a bank statement" (though often digital now, the *reconciliation* skill is mandated).
          - Domain: Credit & Debt
            - 5 Cs of Credit: While standard in many places, CT's aligned standards explicitly name the "5 Cs of Credit" (Character, Capacity, Capital, Collateral, Conditions) as the required framework for analyzing loan eligibility.
            - Rent vs. Buy: Detailed comparison of "short-term and long-term costs/benefits of renting versus buying a home," including defining lease terms and eviction consequences.
          - Domain: Investing
            - Capital Gains Taxes: You cannot just teach "stocks go up." You must explain the *tax difference* between "earned income" (wages) and "unearned income" (capital gains/interest).
            - Business Cycles: Students must "determine the impact of business cycles" (recession vs. expansion) on personal financial activities.
        - Summary of "Must-Haves" for Connecticut ComplianceIf you are building a "gold standard" Connecticut curriculum, you must specifically include:
          - Form 1099 vs. W-2 (Gig Economy Taxes)
          - Intangible Job Benefits (Remote work/Flexibility analysis)
          - Race & Gender Pay Gaps (Structural economic analysis)
          - The 5 Cs of Credit (Lender's rubric)
          - Capital Gains Tax Rates (Investing taxes)
        - FinLit Knowledge Graph Nodes Yielded (Doug suggestion)
          - Half-credit course (Class of 2027); aligns to national standards via MBA Research and CSDE model curricula; emphasis on FAFSA, scholarships, insurance.
            - Earning Income
              - DOK1 Fact: Spendable income is lower than gross income due to taxes assessed on income by federal, state, and local governments.
                - DOK2 Summary: Use a paycheck stub to calculate Connecticut state withholding, adjusting for net pay to plan monthly bills.
              - DOK1 Fact: Interest, dividends, and capital appreciation are examples of unearned income derived from financial investments.
                - DOK2 Summary: Track dividends from a sample portfolio to offset salary shortfalls during seasonal unemployment.
            - Spending
              - DOK1 Fact: A budget is a plan for allocating a person’s spendable income to necessary and desired goods and services.
                - DOK2 Summary: Create a student budget prioritizing tuition over entertainment, using apps to track variances.
              - DOK1 Fact: Individual spending decisions may be influenced by financial constraints, personal preferences, unique needs, peers, and advertising.
                - DOK2 Summary: Evaluate peer-influenced purchases like gadgets against long-term goals like college savings.
            - Saving
              - DOK1 Fact: People who have sufficient income can choose to save some of it for future uses such as emergencies or later purchases.
                - DOK2 Summary: Automate 20% of part-time earnings into a high-yield account for a 6-month emergency fund.
              - DOK1 Fact: Inflation can erode the value of savings if the interest rate earned on a savings account is less than the inflation rate.
                - DOK2 Summary: Compare historical inflation to CD rates, selecting inflation-beating options for college funds.
            - Investing
              - DOK1 Fact: Investors receive a return on their investment in the form of income and/or growth in value of their investment over time.
                - DOK2 Summary: Simulate Roth IRA contributions to project growth, adjusting for post-graduation tax benefits.
              - DOK1 Fact: Riskier investments tend to earn higher long-run rates of return than lower-risk investments.
                - DOK2 Summary: Diversify a mock portfolio with bonds and stocks to balance risk for a 10-year horizon.
            - Managing Credit and Debt
              - DOK1 Fact: Credit allows consumers to buy now and pay later, but interest and fees add to the amount owed.
                - DOK2 Summary: Compare student loan deferment vs. immediate repayment to minimize total interest paid.
              - DOK1 Fact: Lenders use credit history and scores to decide whether to lend money and at what interest rate.
                - DOK2 Summary: Review a credit report simulation to prioritize payments improving scores for auto loans.
            - Managing Risk
              - DOK1 Fact: Insurance protects consumers from the financial costs of certain risks by providing reimbursement for covered losses.
                - DOK2 Summary: Select renter's insurance deductibles based on emergency savings to cover theft of valuables.
              - DOK1 Fact: People can reduce the financial risk of job loss, illness, or death by purchasing insurance and building savings.
                - DOK2 Summary: Plan for internship gaps with unemployment simulations and disability coverage reviews.
            - State Note: Integrates FAFSA and grants; all nodes via half-credit standalone.
      - Delaware (New - Passed Oct 2025)
        - Core Mandate
