          - Requirement: A minimum half-credit (0.5) standalone course in Financial Literacy.
          - Target Audience: Mandatory starting with students entering 9th grade in the 2026–2027 school year, making it a graduation requirement for the Class of 2030.
          - The "Social Studies" Housing: Uniquely, the legislation explicitly classifies Financial Literacy as a "content area within Social Studies." This dictates that the credit will likely count toward Social Studies graduation requirements or be taught by certified Social Studies educators, though the DOE is finalizing specific certification rules.
        - Implementation & Resources
          - State-Mandated Standards: Unlike "local control" states that leave topics vague, HB 203 wrote the specific required topics directly into state law. All districts must verify their curriculum covers the 11 statutory domains listed in the bill.
          - Funding & PD:
            - Teacher Stipends: A unique public-private partnership between Next Gen Personal Finance (NGPF) and YourMoney101 is funding $500 stipends for Delaware teachers who complete 20 hours of professional development to prepare for this new mandate.
            - Reimbursement Rights: The DOE is authorized to seek reimbursement from districts if they fill these positions with unlicensed staff, emphasizing strict adherence to the new certification standards.
          - Curriculum Flexibility: While the *topics* are mandated, districts can choose their own materials (e.g., NGPF, Banzai, Ramsey) as long as they align with the Delaware Financial Literacy Standards.
        - 1. Practical Skills
          - Fiduciary Analysis: Uniquely, students must learn to distinguish between "fiduciary and non-fiduciary advisors." This is a specific legal standard of care that most state curricula ignore.
          - System Comparison: Students must "Compare financial systems, including what works and what does not and why," implying a broader economic systems analysis (Capitalism vs. others) rare in personal finance courses.
          - Philanthropy: Explicitly required to understand "philanthropy" and its role in financial planning.
        - 2. The Granular "Required Standards" (The Deep Dive): HB 203 explicitly codifies 11 Required Topics that must be taught. To be compliance-grade in Delaware, you cannot teach a generic course; you must cover these specific statutory "niche" concepts:
          - Domain: Behavioral Economics (Legally Mandated)
            - Biases & Habits: You must teach "Introduction to behavioral economics," specifically focusing on "the impact of life experiences and biases on personal money management decisions".
          - Domain: Retirement & State Programs
            - Delaware EARNS Program: You are legally required to explain the Delaware EARNS Program (the state's secure choice retirement savings program). This is a hyper-local requirement found in no other state.
            - Self-Sufficient Retirement: Instruction must cover risk assessment strategies that lead specifically to a "self-sufficient retirement".
          - Domain: Credit & Debt (The "Predatory" List)
            - Specific Products: The law mandates teaching not just "credit," but specifically: "payday loans and title loans" alongside auto/student loans.
            - Rule of 72: Like Alabama, Delaware explicitly codifies the "Rule of 72" as a required math concept for debt and investing.
          - Domain: Income & Careers
            - Trade vs. College: You must compare "college vs. trade or technical school" *and* "entrepreneurship vs. employment," reflecting a legislative focus on vocational pathways.
        - Summary of "Must-Haves" for Delaware ComplianceIf you are building a "gold standard" Delaware curriculum, you must specifically include:
          - The Delaware EARNS Program (State-specific retirement)
          - Fiduciary vs. Non-Fiduciary (Advisor standards)
          - Title Loans & Payday Loans (Specific predatory definitions)
          - Behavioral Economics (Biases and Life Experience)
          - Rule of 72 (Specific Math)
        - FinLit Knowledge Graph Nodes Yielded (Doug suggestion)
          - Standalone half-credit course (Class of 2030); K-12 standards by grade bands; local tax and UD CEEE emphasis.
            - Earning Income
              - DOK1 Fact: Compensation for a job or career can be in the form of wages, salaries, commissions, tips, or bonuses, and may also include contributions to employee benefits.
                - DOK2 Summary: Calculate total compensation for a Delaware retail job, including 401(k) matches and health premiums.
              - DOK1 Fact: Changes in economic conditions, technology, or the labor market can cause changes in income, career opportunities, or employment status.
                - DOK2 Summary: Research AI impacts on local jobs, updating skills for higher-wage tech roles.
            - Spending
              - DOK1 Fact: When there is sufficient money in their budget, people may decide to give money to others, save, or invest to achieve future goals.
                - DOK2 Summary: Allocate budget surplus to Delaware charities, tracking tax deductions for next filing.
              - DOK1 Fact: Consumer decisions are influenced by the price of products or services, the price of alternatives, the consumer’s budget and preferences, and potential impact on the environment, society, and economy.
                - DOK2 Summary: Compare eco-friendly vs. budget groceries, factoring in long-term health costs.
            - Saving
              - DOK1 Fact: Interest rates, fees, and other account features vary by type of account and among financial institutions, with higher rates resulting in greater compound interest earned by savers.
                - DOK2 Summary: Shop Delaware banks for APY, projecting $1,000 savings growth over 5 years.

                - DOK2 Summary: Adjust allocations after family changes, like adding a sibling, to conservative bonds.
            - Managing Credit and Debt
              - DOK1 Fact: Interest is the price of borrowing money, and it is determined by the relationship between the amount borrowed and the time allowed for repayment.
                - DOK2 Summary: Use a calculator for Delaware credit union loans vs. national banks, minimizing APR.

                - DOK2 Summary: Simulate on-time payments to boost scores for apartment approvals.

                - DOK2 Summary: Draft a simple will using online tools, designating guardians and beneficiaries.
            - State Note: Grade-band K-12 standards; adds local taxes; all nodes.
      - Florida
        - Core Mandate
          - Requirement: One-semester (0.5 credit) standalone course in Personal Financial Literacy and Money Management.
          - Target Audience: Mandatory for all students entering 9th grade in the 2023–2024 school year (the graduating Class of 2027).
          - The "Elective Swap": To make room for this course without extending the school day, the legislature officially reduced the required "Elective" credits for graduation from 8 to 7.5. This is a critical structural detail that prevents the course from competing with Arts or CTE for space.
        - Implementation & Resources
          - State-Adopted Materials: Florida has a strict instructional materials adoption list. As of the 2024–2025 adoption cycle, the following providers are officially approved for district purchase:
            - Ramsey Education (Foundations in Personal Finance): Widely adopted; the bill’s namesake, Senator Hukill, was a known advocate of the Ramsey method.
            - Budget Challenge: A simulation-based curriculum explicitly approved on the state list.
            - eDynamic Learning & McGraw Hill: Approved for standard and honors tracks.
          - Standards: All courses must align with the Next Generation Sunshine State Standards (NGSSS) for Social Studies, specifically the "Financial Literacy Strand" (Standard SS.912.FL).
        - 1. Practical Skills
          - Balancing a Checkbook: Unlike most modern standards that focus on "monitoring accounts," Florida Statute 1003.4282 legally mandates instruction on "balancing a checkbook," keeping this manual skill as a requirement.
          - Dispute Resolution: Students must identify "state and federal agencies that provide consumer protection" and learn the specific process for filing complaints against financial institutions.
          - Tax Filing: Practical application of completing tax forms, explicitly naming: Form 1040, W-4, W-2, and 1099.
        - 2. The Granular "Required Standards" (The Deep Dive): SB 1054 and the CPALMS Course Description (#2102371) mandate instruction in specific atomic concepts. To be compliance-grade, you must cover these specific statutory requirements:
          - Domain: Banking & Money Management
            - Bank Quality Assessment: You are required to teach students not just how to open an account, but how to "assess the quality of a depository institution’s services." This implies a comparative analysis of banks vs. credit unions.
            - Contributory vs. Non-Contributory Benefits: You must distinguish between benefits the employee pays for (401k match) vs. what the employer fully funds.
          - Domain: Credit & Debt
            - Loan Applications: Students must "simulate the loan application process," specifically comparing consumer loans (auto/student) vs. predatory options (payday).
            - Credit Repair: Uniquely, the standards require identifying "credible sources to assist with credit problems," effectively teaching students how to avoid credit repair scams.
          - Domain: Taxes & Government
            - Entitlements: You must differentiate between "government entitlements" (Social Security, Medicare) and "employer benefits," a distinction rarely made explicit in other states.
            - Progressive vs. Regressive Tax: The standards explicitly require explaining how "federal tax brackets illustrate a progressive tax" while "sales tax is viewed as a regressive tax".
          - Domain: Contracts & Consumer Law
            - Contract Validity: You must teach "what makes a contract valid" and analyze specific terms in leases and warranties.
            - Inheritance: Unlike many states that skip estate planning, Florida explicitly requires understanding "Wills" and how assets are transferred upon death.
        - Summary of "Must-Haves" for Florida ComplianceIf you are building a "gold standard" Florida curriculum, you must specifically include:
          - Balancing a Checkbook (Statutory Requirement)
          - Regressive vs. Progressive Taxes (Specific Tax Theory)
          - Assessing Bank Quality (Vendor Selection)
          - Wills & Estate Planning (Inheritance)
          - Government Entitlements (Social Security Mechanics)
        - FinLit Knowledge Graph Nodes Yielded (Doug suggestion)
          - Half-credit standalone (Class of 2026); integrated with economics; covers banking, budgeting, taxes, insurance.
            - Earning Income
              - DOK1 Fact: Most people earn wage and salary income in return for working, and they can also earn income from interest, dividends, rents, entrepreneurship, business profits, or increases in the value of investments.
                - DOK2 Summary: Diversify Florida gig work with dividend stocks for stable summer income.
              - DOK1 Fact: Employers generally pay higher wages and salaries to more educated, skilled, and productive workers.
                - DOK2 Summary: Map education paths to tourism jobs, prioritizing certifications for tips.
            - Spending
              - DOK1 Fact: People can often improve their financial well-being by making well-informed spending decisions, which includes critical evaluation of price, quality, product information, and method of payment.
                - DOK2 Summary: Compare hurricane supplies prices online vs. local, using cash for no-fee buys.
              - DOK1 Fact: Housing decisions depend on individual preferences, circumstances, and costs, and can impact personal satisfaction and financial well-being.
                - DOK2 Summary: Weigh Miami condo vs. rural rent in a cost spreadsheet for family growth.
            - Saving
              - DOK1 Fact: Funds needed for transactions, bill-paying, or purchases, are commonly held in federally insured checking or savings accounts at financial institutions because these accounts offer easy access to their money and low risk.
                - DOK2 Summary: Open a Florida Prepaid account for auto-transfers toward college emergencies.
              - DOK1 Fact: Tax policies that allow people to save pretax earnings or to reduce or defer taxes on interest earned provide incentives for people to save.
                - DOK2 Summary: Use 529 deductions on state taxes for education savings projections.
            - Investing
              - DOK1 Fact: People can more easily achieve their financial goals by investing steadily over many years, reinvesting dividends, and capital gains to compound their returns.
                - DOK2 Summary: Automate $100/month into S&P 500 via app, tracking 20-year growth.
              - DOK1 Fact: Investors select investments that are consistent with their risk tolerance, and they diversify across a number of different investment choices to reduce investment risk.
                - DOK2 Summary: Build a Florida real estate ETF mix to hedge tourism volatility.
            - Managing Credit and Debt
              - DOK1 Fact: Overuse of credit cards can lead to unmanageable debt and negatively affect credit ratings.
                - DOK2 Summary: Limit card use to 25% utilization for spring break trips without fees.
              - DOK1 Fact: Bankruptcy is a legal process that allows consumers to discharge or reorganize debt, but it has long-lasting effects on creditworthiness.
                - DOK2 Summary: Avoid via avalanche method on sample student loans post-grad.
            - Managing Risk
              - DOK1 Fact: People should evaluate and compare insurance coverage, deductibles, and premiums of various policies to choose coverage that best matches their needs and financial capacity.
                - DOK2 Summary: Shop Florida auto policies for flood add-ons, balancing premiums.
              - DOK1 Fact: Identity theft occurs when someone uses personal information without authorization, and it can have serious financial consequences.
                - DOK2 Summary: Freeze credit after breaches, monitoring for unauthorized charges.
            - State Note: SB 1054 topics mapped; all nodes in half-credit.
      - Georgia
        - Core Mandate
          - Requirement: A minimum half-credit (0.5) standalone course in Financial Literacy.
          - Target Audience: Uniquely, Georgia law mandates this course be taken specifically during the 11th or 12th grade (Junior or Senior year). This differs from states that allow it in 9th/10th, reflecting a legislative intent for "Just-in-Time" education before adulthood.
          - Effective Date: The requirement is effective starting with the 2024–2025 school year. This means the Class of 2025 is the first cohort legally required to complete this for graduation.
        - Implementation & Resources
          - Standards: The course is governed by the Georgia Standards of Excellence (GSE) for *Personal Finance and Economics* (Course 45.0610). While traditionally combined with Economics, SB 220 codified a distinct minimum instruction set.
          - Common Adoptions:
            - Budget Challenge: A major simulation-based vendor that has gained significant traction in Georgia due to its specific alignment guide for SB 220 standards.
            - EverFi: widely used across Georgia districts for its "Financial Literacy for High School" digital course.
            - Ramsey Education: Frequently adopted in rural districts, aligning with the state's focus on debt avoidance.
          - No State Funding: Unlike CA or FL, the legislation (SB 220) did not attach a specific fiscal note or grant fund for curriculum purchase, leaving implementation costs to local districts.
        - 1. Practical Skills
          - The HOPE Scholarship: You must teach the specific eligibility and maintenance requirements for Georgia’s HOPE Scholarship and Zell Miller Scholarship. This is a non-negotiable, state-specific "Practical Skill".
          - Title Pawns: Beyond generic predatory lending, students must explicitly compare interest rates of "title-pawn companies," reflecting Georgia’s specific regulatory environment regarding title lending.
          - Tax Filing: "Computing federal income taxes" is a required skill, moving beyond just "understanding" taxes to actually calculating them.
        - 2. The Granular "Required Standards" (The Deep Dive): The Georgia Standards of Excellence (SSEPF1-6) mandate instruction in very specific economic concepts. To be compliance-grade, you must cover these specific "niche" topics:
          - Domain: Earning & Human Capital
            - Human Capital Theory: You must teach how "investment in human capital" (education/training) directly leads to a higher standard of living. This is an Economics standard applied to personal finance.
            - Voluntary vs. Involuntary Deductions: You must distinguish between federal/state taxes (involuntary) and "elective deductions" like 401(k) and Life Insurance.
          - Domain: Credit & Debt (The "Predatory" List)
            - Specific Scams: You are legally required to teach specific fraud types: "Ponzi schemes, pump and dumps, and 'advance fee' scams." Most states just say "fraud"; Georgia lists the specific schemes.
            - Bankruptcy: You must explain the "causes and effects of personal bankruptcy," specifically framing it as a legal process.
          - Domain: Investing
            - 529 Plans: The standards explicitly list 529 accounts as a required investment vehicle to evaluate, linking back to the "Post-High School" planning standard.
            - Mutual Funds vs. Index Funds: You must explain the importance of diversification through these specific vehicles.
        - Summary of "Must-Haves" for Georgia ComplianceIf you are building a "gold standard" Georgia curriculum, you must specifically include:
          - HOPE & Zell Miller Scholarships (State Aid Rules)
          - Title-Pawn Companies (Specific Predatory Lender)
          - Pump and Dump / Ponzi Schemes (Specific Scams)
          - 11th/12th Grade Delivery (Just-in-Time Mandate)
          - Computing Federal Taxes (Manual Calculation)
        - FinLit Knowledge Graph Nodes Yielded (Doug suggestion)
          - Half-credit standalone (Class of 2028); integrated with economics; emphasis on budgeting, credit.
            - Earning Income
              - DOK1 Fact: Spendable income is lower than gross income due to taxes assessed on income by federal, state, and local governments.
                - DOK2 Summary: Adjust Atlanta paycheck for state taxes, forecasting take-home for rent.
              - DOK1 Fact: Interest, dividends, and capital appreciation are examples of unearned income derived from financial investments.
                - DOK2 Summary: Add dividend yields to freelance income for tax planning.
            - Spending
              - DOK1 Fact: A budget is a plan for allocating a person’s spendable income to necessary and desired goods and services.
                - DOK2 Summary: Build a zero-based budget app for college commuters.
              - DOK1 Fact: Individual spending decisions may be influenced by financial constraints, personal preferences, unique needs, peers, and advertising.
                - DOK2 Summary: Resist ads for fast fashion, prioritizing needs like textbooks.
            - Saving
              - DOK1 Fact: People who have sufficient income can choose to save some of it for future uses such as emergencies or later purchases.
                - DOK2 Summary: Set 50/30/20 rule for part-time job earnings toward car fund.
              - DOK1 Fact: Inflation can erode the value of savings if the interest rate earned on a savings account is less than the inflation rate.
                - DOK2 Summary: Choose high-yield over traditional savings amid 3% inflation.
            - Investing
              - DOK1 Fact: Investors receive a return on their investment in the form of income and/or growth in value of their investment over time.
                - DOK2 Summary: Reinvest dividends in a Roth IRA simulation for retirement.
              - DOK1 Fact: Riskier investments tend to earn higher long-run rates of return than lower-risk investments.
                - DOK2 Summary: Balance high-growth stocks with CDs for moderate risk.
            - Managing Credit and Debt
              - DOK1 Fact: Credit allows consumers to buy now and pay later, but interest and fees add to the amount owed.
                - DOK2 Summary: Pay off promo APR balances before rates spike on purchases.
              - DOK1 Fact: Lenders use credit history and scores to decide whether to lend money and at what interest rate.
                - DOK2 Summary: Build history with secured cards for apartment applications.
            - Managing Risk
              - DOK1 Fact: Insurance protects consumers from the financial costs of certain risks by providing reimbursement for covered losses.
                - DOK2 Summary: Add riders for Georgia storm damage to renter's policy.
              - DOK1 Fact: People can reduce the financial risk of job loss, illness, or death by purchasing insurance and building savings.
                - DOK2 Summary: Layer emergency fund with short-term disability for gaps.
            - State Note: SB 220; all nodes in half-credit.
      - Indiana
        - Core Mandate
          - Requirement: One-semester (1 credit) standalone course in Personal Financial Responsibility.
          - Target Audience: Mandatory for all students expected to graduate in 2028 or thereafter (entering freshmen in 2024–2025).
          - The "Separate Subject" Clause: Uniquely, the law (IC 20-30-5-19) explicitly forbids burying this content in other classes; it states schools *must* provide instruction "as a separate subject," closing the loophole of embedding it into Economics.
        - Implementation & Resources
          - Standards: All courses must align with the Indiana Academic Standards for Personal Financial Responsibility (Course 4540). The Department of Education (IDOE) revised these standards in 2023/2024 to align with the new legislation.
          - Common Adoptions:
            - Junior Achievement (JA) Financial Literacy: JA of Northern Indiana created a specific "Course for Indiana" that is widely adopted because it maps directly to SEA 35’s requirements.
            - Banzai: Widely used in Indiana due to local credit union sponsorships that make it free for districts.
            - Reality Stores: A popular, state-endorsed implementation model where students are assigned a salary and must visit stations (housing, utilities, childcare) to purchase necessities, often used as the "capstone" for the course.
        - 1. Practical Skills
          - Digital Recordkeeping: The standards explicitly require students to "demonstrate recordkeeping that utilizes digital financial management systems" (apps/spreadsheets), moving beyond paper ledgers.
          - Inheritance: Uniquely, Indiana law (IC 20-30-5-19) mandates instruction on "receiving an inheritance and related implications." This is extremely rare in state mandates and requires specific instruction on estate taxes and sudden wealth management.
          - Bank Quality Assessment: You cannot just teach *how* to open an account; you must teach students to "assess the quality of a depository institution’s services," implying a competitive analysis of fees and customer service.
        - 2. The Granular "Required Standards" (The Deep Dive): SEA 35 and Indiana Code 20-30-5-19 mandate instruction in specific atomic concepts. To be compliance-grade, you must cover these specific statutory requirements:
          - Domain: Taxes & Government
            - Local Tax Assessments: While most states focus on Federal/State income tax, Indiana explicitly requires teaching "local tax assessments" (property taxes, county option income taxes), reflecting the state's specific tax structure.
            - 21st Century Scholars: You must investigate and demonstrate the ability to apply for state-specific aid, explicitly naming the 21st Century Scholars program alongside FAFSA.
          - Domain: Credit & Contracts
            - Simple Contracts: The law lists "Simple Contracts" as a required topic. You must teach the essential elements (offer, acceptance, consideration) of a contract between individuals.
            - Debt Rights: You must describe "debtors’ and creditors’ rights related to debt that is not paid," which requires explaining the legal collections process, not just the "ethical" obligation to pay.
          - Domain: Career & Income
            - Partnership Finances: A unique standard requires students to "compare and contrast the benefits of sharing financial goals... with a potential partner," legally mandating instruction on marriage/relationship finances.
            - Intangible Benefits: Students must evaluate "intangible job benefits" (culture, flexibility) as a formal part of compensation analysis.
        - Summary of "Must-Haves" for Indiana ComplianceIf you are building a "gold standard" Indiana curriculum, you must specifically include:
          - Receiving an Inheritance (Statutory Requirement)
          - Local Tax Assessments (County/Property Tax)
          - Simple Contracts (Legal Elements)
          - Relationship Finances (Sharing goals with partners)
          - 21st Century Scholars Program (State Aid)
        - FinLit Knowledge Graph Nodes Yielded (Doug suggestion)
          - Half-credit standalone (Class of 2028); aligns to NLPS competencies; entrepreneurship focus.
            - Earning Income
              - DOK1 Fact: Compensation for a job or career can be in the form of wages, salaries, commissions, tips, or bonuses, and may also include contributions to employee benefits.
                - DOK2 Summary: Negotiate Indiana internship pay, including benefits value.
              - DOK1 Fact: Changes in economic conditions, technology, or the labor market can cause changes in income, career opportunities, or employment status.
                - DOK2 Summary: Upskill for manufacturing automation to boost wages.
            - Spending
              - DOK1 Fact: When there is sufficient money in their budget, people may decide to give money to others, save, or invest to achieve future goals.
                - DOK2 Summary: Donate 5% post-essentials, tracking for tax credits.
              - DOK1 Fact: Consumer decisions are influenced by the price of products or services, the price of alternatives, the consumer’s budget and preferences, and potential impact on the environment, society, and economy.
                - DOK2 Summary: Choose sustainable Indiana products via cost-benefit analysis.
            - Saving
              - DOK1 Fact: Interest rates, fees, and other account features vary by type of account and among financial institutions, with higher rates resulting in greater compound interest earned by savers.
                - DOK2 Summary: Compare credit unions for fees on $2,000 emergency savings.
              - DOK1 Fact: Employer defined contribution retirement plans and health savings accounts can provide incentives for employees to save.
                - DOK2 Summary: Enroll in 401(k) match to double teen job contributions.

              - DOK1 Fact: Investors have many choices of investments that differ in expected rates of return and risk.
                - DOK2 Summary: Select low-fee ETFs for post-grad home down payment.
              - DOK1 Fact: A person’s investment risk tolerance depends on factors such as personality, financial resources, investment experiences, and life circumstances.
                - DOK2 Summary: Shift to bonds after college debt payoff for stability.
            - Managing Credit and Debt
              - DOK1 Fact: Interest is the price of borrowing money, and it is determined by the relationship between the amount borrowed and the time allowed for repayment.
                - DOK2 Summary: Amortize a $5,000 car loan at varying terms for total cost.
              - DOK1 Fact: Responsible credit management, such as paying bills on time and keeping debt levels low, helps build credit history and increase credit scores.
                - DOK2 Summary: Use alerts for due dates to qualify for student apartments.
            - Managing Risk
              - DOK1 Fact: People make choices to obtain insurance because the cost of premiums is lower than the financial loss that could occur from the risk.
                - DOK2 Summary: Insure laptops for theft in dorms at low annual cost.
              - DOK1 Fact: Estate planning is the process of preparing for the management and disposal of assets after death.
                - DOK2 Summary: Update beneficiary forms for family changes post-move.

      - Iowa
        - Core Mandate
          - Requirement: One-half unit (0.5 credit) of Personal Financial Literacy.
          - Target Audience: Mandatory for all students as a condition of graduation, fully effective for the graduating class of 2023 and beyond.
          - The "21st Century" Housing: Uniquely, Iowa law places this requirement within the "21st Century Skills" core curriculum (alongside Employability and Civic Literacy) rather than strictly Math or Social Studies. This allows flexibility: it can be a standalone course or integrated, provided it meets the specific financial literacy standards.
        - Implementation & Resources
          - Standards: All instruction must align with the Iowa Core 21st Century Skills: Financial Literacy (Grades 9-12), which focuses on "financial capability" rather than just knowledge.
          - Assessment Requirement: Under recent legislative updates (referenced in HF 2263 context), students in the graduating class of 2023+ must not only take the course but attain a passing score on an assessment of financial literacy.
          - Common Adoptions:
            - TS Institute (TS Bank): A massive, Iowa-specific philanthropic arm that provides free financial literacy curriculum to nearly half the schools in the state.
            - Iowa Bankers Association: Provides significant "turn-key" resources for districts, specifically focusing on career readiness and banking skills.
            - Junior Achievement of Central Iowa: Widely used for its "Financial Literacy" semester course which is explicitly aligned to the Iowa Core.
        - 1. Practical Skills
          - 529 Plan Literacy: Uniquely, Iowa heavily emphasizes its state-sponsored ISave 529 (formerly College Savings Iowa). Curriculum often includes calculating the specific state tax deduction benefits for Iowa residents contributing to these plans.
          - Filing Complaints: Students must learn how to "file a consumer complaint" specifically with the Iowa Attorney General’s Consumer Protection Division, a localized legal skill.
          - Career ROI: The standards require using the Iowa Student Loan "ROCI" tool (Return on College Investment) to compare starting salaries vs. student loan debt for specific Iowa colleges.
        - 2. The Granular "Required Standards" (The Deep Dive): The Iowa Core 21st Century Skills (Financial Literacy) mandates instruction in atomic concepts that go beyond the basics. To be compliance-grade, you must cover these nuances:
          - Domain: Financial Responsibility & Decision Making
            - Opportunity Cost: You must explicitly teach "opportunity cost" as a decision-making framework for all financial choices, not just a vocabulary word.
            - Marketing Awareness: The standards require students to "analyze consumer awareness of the power of marketing on buying decisions," linking back to behavioral economics.
          - Domain: Planning & Money Management
            - Estate Planning: Students must "evaluate the role of estate planning (wills, trusts, life insurance)" in protecting wealth. This is a Tier 1 requirement often skipped in other states.
            - Automated Savings: Instruction must cover "automated savings plans" (direct deposit splits) as a specific wealth-building tool.
          - Domain: Credit & Debt
            - Predatory Lending: You must analyze "predatory lending practices," with specific focus often given to payday loans and rent-to-own centers prevalent in Iowa.
            - Credit Reports: Students must explain "how to obtain and correct a credit report," moving beyond just reading it to the *dispute* process.
          - Domain: Risk Management (Insurance)
            - Insurable vs. Non-Insurable: You must distinguish between risks that can be insured (health, auto) and those that cannot (market volatility), and how to manage both.
            - Identity Theft: Specific instruction on "detecting and resolving identity theft," often linking to the "FoolProof" curriculum used by the Attorney General.
        - Summary of "Must-Haves" for Iowa ComplianceIf you are building a "gold standard" Iowa curriculum, you must specifically include:
          - ISave 529 Plans (State Tax Benefits)
          - Estate Planning (Wills & Trusts)
          - The "ROCI" Tool (Iowa Student Loan calculator)
          - Consumer Complaint Filing (AG Office)
          - Marketing Power (Behavioral Influence)

                - DOK2 Summary: Blend Iowa farm salary with rental income for stability.

      - Kansas
        - Core Mandate
          - Requirement: A minimum half-unit (0.5 credit) course in Financial Literacy.
          - Target Audience: Mandatory for all students in the Graduating Class of 2027 and beyond (freshmen entering in 2023–2024).
          - The "FAFSA" Bundle: Uniquely, the new regulation couples the course requirement with a mandatory FAFSA Filing Requirement. To graduate, a student must either file the FAFSA or complete a formal opt-out waiver (making Kansas one of the few "Universal FAFSA" states).
        - Implementation & Resources
          - The "Local Control" Paradox: While the *credit* is state-mandated, the *delivery* is strictly local control. Districts can teach this as a standalone course, or "embed" it, BUT the new regulation (K.A.R. 91-31-35) specifies that if it is embedded, the district must demonstrate that the content is equivalent to a 0.5 unit course, effectively forcing most districts toward a standalone model to ensure compliance.
          - Standards: Districts typically align with the Kansas Social Studies Standards (Economics Strand) or Mathematics Standards, but the State Board has endorsed the National Standards for Personal Financial Education as the quality benchmark.
          - Common Adoptions:
            - Kansas Jump$tart Coalition: A dominant force in the state, providing the primary "Clearinghouse" of approved resources for districts.
            - Junior Achievement of Kansas: Their "JA Financial Literacy" course is a widely adopted turnkey solution because it specifically maps to the state's "Individual Plan of Study" (IPS) framework.
            - Catholic Charities of Northeast Kansas: Uniquely, they provide specialized curriculum for high-needs districts focusing on "predatory lending elimination" and debt reduction.
        - 1. Practical Skills
          - Individual Plan of Study (IPS): Kansas is unique in requiring financial literacy to be integrated with the student's IPS. This means budgeting isn't taught in a vacuum; students must build a budget *specifically* for the career path they chose in their IPS.
          - Kansas Career Work-Study: Unlike generic "work-study," curriculum often covers the Kansas Career Work-Study program, which requires the job to be related to the student's major/IPS (unlike Federal work-study).
          - FAFSA Opt-Out: Because of the mandate, students are practically taught the specific legal process of *waiving* rights to federal aid if they choose not to file, a procedural skill unique to Universal FAFSA states.
        - 2. The Granular "Required Standards" (The Deep Dive): Because Kansas relies on "Local Control" guided by state regulations, the compliance "must-haves" come from the Kansas State Department of Education (KSDE) Guidance and KBOR (Regents) expectations:
          - Domain: Post-Secondary Funding (The "Universal FAFSA" Layer)
            - Kansas Promise Scholarship: You must teach the specific eligibility for the Kansas Promise Scholarship, a "last dollar" service scholarship for high-demand trades (IT, Healthcare, Manufacturing).
            - SAI (Student Aid Index): Updated curriculum must teach the calculation of the SAI (replacing the old EFC) as part of the new FAFSA Simplification Act, explicitly required for the Class of 2027.
          - Domain: Credit & Predatory Lending
            - Title Loans: While the legislative bill failed, the Kansas Office of the State Bank Commissioner provides resources explicitly targeting "Title Loans" and "Payday Loans" as key risks for young Kansas adults, making this a "de facto" standard for quality curricula.
            - The "Unbanked": Curriculum is encouraged to address "financial inclusion" for low-to-moderate income individuals, specifically targeting the "unbanked" population (using resources from the Kansas Loan Pool Project).
          - Domain: Earning & Employment
            - Kansas Career Technical Education (CTE): Financial literacy must often be cross-referenced with Senate Bill 155 (tuition-free technical education), forcing students to calculate the "opportunity cost" of a 4-year degree vs. free technical certification.
        - Summary of "Must-Haves" for Kansas ComplianceIf you are building a "gold standard" Kansas curriculum, you must specifically include:
          - FAFSA Filing (The Mandate)
          - Individual Plan of Study (IPS) (Integration with Career)
          - Kansas Promise Scholarship (Trade School Funding)
          - Kansas Career Work-Study (State-specific rules)
          - Title & Payday Loans (Predatory Risks)
        - FinLit Knowledge Graph Nodes Yielded (Doug suggestion)

      - Kentucky (New - Passed 2025)
        - Core Mandate
          - Requirement: A minimum one-credit standalone course in Financial Literacy.
          - Target Audience: Mandatory for all students entering 9th grade in the 2025–2026 school year (the graduating Class of 2029).
          - The "Triple Play" Credit: Uniquely, HB 342 allows this course to satisfy a graduation credit in Mathematics, Social Studies, or as an Elective, giving principals massive scheduling flexibility.
        - Implementation & Resources
          - Standards: All instruction must align with the Kentucky Academic Standards for Career Studies and Financial Literacy, specifically the high school (9-12) financial literacy domain.
          - Consulting Body: Districts are explicitly authorized to consult with the Kentucky Financial Empowerment Commission (KFEC), a state body created to ensure curriculum quality.
          - Common Adoptions:
            - Kentucky Financial Empowerment Commission (Money Academy): A state-specific, KFEC-supported program tailored to Kentucky schools.
            - Ramsey Education: Heavily used in Kentucky due to the regional preference for debt-aversion curricula.
            - NGPF (Next Gen Personal Finance): The primary advocate behind HB 342, with curriculum mapped directly to the new standards.
        - 1. Practical Skills
          - KEES Scholarship Literacy: You must teach the mechanics of the Kentucky Educational Excellence Scholarship (KEES). Students must understand how their high school GPA (2.5 minimum) directly translates to specific dollar amounts for college.
          - Cursive Signatures: In a highly unique statutory requirement, HB 342 explicitly mandates that students demonstrate "the ability to provide a signature in cursive" as part of the document review process.
          - Individual Learning Plan (ILP): The course cannot be generic; it must align with the student’s Individual Learning Plan (ILP), meaning a student tracking toward "Welding" should see different financial examples than one tracking toward "Pre-Med".
        - 2. The Granular "Required Standards" (The Deep Dive): HB 342 and KRS 158.1411 mandate instruction in specific atomic concepts. To be compliance-grade, you must cover these specific statutory requirements:
          - Domain: Critical Document Review (Legally Mandated)
            - Contract Literacy: You are legally required to teach "the necessity of critical review and understanding of documents prior to signing." This is a defense against predatory contracts (e.g., cell phone plans, leases).
          - Domain: Credit & Debt
            - Predatory Lending: The standards require analyzing "predatory lending practices," often with a specific focus on Payday Lenders which are prevalent in Kentucky regulatory discussions.
            - Bankruptcy Implications: Students must understand the long-term impact of filing for bankruptcy, specifically Chapter 7 vs. Chapter 13.
          - Domain: Earning & Benefits
            - Total Compensation: Instruction must cover "benefits and incentives" (like 401k match and health insurance) as part of evaluating a job offer, not just the hourly wage.
            - Workplace Ethics: Linked to KRS 158.1413, financial literacy is often paired with "Essential Workplace Ethics" instruction (reliability, drug-free workplace).
        - Summary of "Must-Haves" for Kentucky ComplianceIf you are building a "gold standard" Kentucky curriculum, you must specifically include:
          - Cursive Signature Proficiency (Statutory Requirement)
          - KEES Scholarship Mechanics (State Aid)
          - Critical Document Review (Contract Law)
          - Predatory Lending Analysis (Payday/Title Loans)
          - ILP Alignment (Career-Specific Budgeting)

      - Louisiana
        - Core Mandate
          - Requirement: A full One Unit (1.0 Credit) standalone course in Financial Literacy.
            - *Note:* This is distinct from most states that only require a half-credit (0.5). Louisiana allocates a full Carnegie Unit to this subject.
          - Target Audience: Mandatory for all students entering 9th grade in the 2024–2025 school year and beyond (the graduating Class of 2028).
          - The "TOPS" Integration: Crucially, this course is now a mandatory component of the TOPS (Taylor Opportunity Program for Students) Core Curriculum. A student cannot qualify for the TOPS Opportunity, Performance, or Honors scholarships without specifically passing this course, giving it massive financial stakes for families.
          - Math Substitution: The course can count as one of the required Mathematics units for the TOPS Tech Award and the Career Diploma, effectively replacing "Business Math" or "Math Essentials" in the sequence.
        - Implementation & Resources
          - Clearinghouse Model: The Louisiana Department of Education (LDOE) is legally required to maintain a "Financial Literacy Clearinghouse" of approved instructional materials.
          - Common Adoptions:
            - Junior Achievement (JA): The "JA Financial Literacy for Louisiana" curriculum is a dominant provider, specifically designed as a year-long program to meet the 1-credit requirement.
            - Money Vehicle: A popular provider that aligns directly with the state's new standards and offers turn-key compliance.
            - PragerU: Uniquely, Louisiana lists PragerU Kids resources in its official alignment guide for specific topics like "The Impact of Policy & Markets".
        - 1. Practical Skills
          - Contesting Bills: You must specifically teach the process of "contesting an incorrect billing statement." This is a rare, highly practical consumer protection skill mandated by law.
          - Balancing a Checkbook: Despite the digital age, the statute (R.S. 17:270) explicitly mandates instruction on "Balancing a checkbook"—not just "monitoring accounts," but the physical act of balancing.
          - Assessing Bank Quality: Students must learn to "assess the quality of a depository institution’s services," implying a comparative analysis of fees, customer service, and location rather than just opening an account blindly.
        - 2. The Granular "Required Standards" (The Deep Dive): Act 267 (R.S. 17:270) lists 13 specific "must-cover" topics. To be compliance-grade, you must ensure your curriculum hits these statutory niche requirements:
          - Domain: Wealth Preservation (The "Inheritance" Clause)
            - Receiving an Inheritance: Louisiana is one of the only states to legally mandate instruction on "Receiving an inheritance and related implications." This reflects the state's focus on intergenerational wealth transfer and likely touches on Louisiana’s unique "forced heirship" civil code history.
          - Domain: Taxation
            - Local Tax Assessments: You cannot just teach federal taxes; you must teach "Local tax assessments" (parish-level property and sales taxes), which are critical in Louisiana's decentralized tax structure.
            - Computing Taxes: The standard is not "understanding" taxes, but "Computing federal income taxes," implying a manual calculation requirement.
          - Domain: Legal & Contracts
            - Simple Contracts: You must teach "Simple contracts," covering the basics of what makes an agreement legally binding.
            - State vs. Federal Law: Curriculum must cover "State and federal laws concerning finance," requiring a distinction between Louisiana Civil Code (Napoleonic) and federal statutes where applicable to finance.
          - Domain: Post-Secondary Debt
            - Default Consequences: You must explicitly teach the "responsibilities, including repayment and default" of student loans. The law requires focusing on the *consequences* of not paying, not just the ability to borrow.
        - Summary of "Must-Haves" for Louisiana ComplianceIf you are building a "gold standard" Louisiana curriculum, you must specifically include:
          - Receiving an Inheritance (Statutory Requirement)
          - Contesting Billing Statements (Consumer Protection)
          - Local Tax Assessments (Parish Taxes)
          - Balancing a Checkbook (Manual Skill)
          - TOPS Scholarship Requirements (Financial Aid)
        - FinLit Knowledge Graph Nodes Yielded (Doug suggestion)
          - Semester standalone (Class of 2027); one-unit required; topics: banking, debt, taxes, insurance.
            - Earning Income
              - DOK1 Fact: Compensation for a job or career can be in the form of wages, salaries, commissions, tips, or bonuses, and may also include contributions to employee benefits.
                - DOK2 Summary: Factor oil rig bonuses into Louisiana coastal pay.
              - DOK1 Fact: Changes in economic conditions, technology, or the labor market can cause changes in income, career opportunities, or employment status.
                - DOK2 Summary: Retrain for green energy amid fossil fuel shifts.
            - Spending
              - DOK1 Fact: When there is sufficient money in their budget, people may decide to give money to others, save, or invest to achieve future goals.
                - DOK2 Summary: Budget Mardi Gras fun within NOLA living costs.
              - DOK1 Fact: Consumer decisions are influenced by the price of products or services, the price of alternatives, the consumer’s budget and preferences, and potential impact on the environment, society, and economy.
                - DOK2 Summary: Choose local seafood for sustainable spending.
            - Saving
              - DOK1 Fact: Interest rates, fees, and other account features vary by type of account and among financial institutions, with higher rates resulting in greater compound interest earned by savers.
                - DOK2 Summary: Maximize CD ladders for hurricane reserves.
              - DOK1 Fact: Employer defined contribution retirement plans and health savings accounts can provide incentives for employees to save.
                - DOK2 Summary: Use HSA for medical in high-cost Baton Rouge.
            - Investing
              - DOK1 Fact: Investors have many choices of investments that differ in expected rates of return and risk.
                - DOK2 Summary: Diversify port with energy stocks for volatility.
              - DOK1 Fact: A person’s investment risk tolerance depends on factors such as personality, financial resources, investment experiences, and life circumstances.
                - DOK2 Summary: Conservative mix post-family expansion.
            - Managing Credit and Debt
              - DOK1 Fact: Interest is the price of borrowing money, and it is determined by the relationship between the amount borrowed and the time allowed for repayment.
                - DOK2 Summary: Refi student debt at low Louisiana rates.
              - DOK1 Fact: Responsible credit management, such as paying bills on time and keeping debt levels low, helps build credit history and increase credit scores.
                - DOK2 Summary: Prioritize payments for bayou home approval.
            - Managing Risk
              - DOK1 Fact: People make choices to obtain insurance because the cost of premiums is lower than the financial loss that could occur from the risk.
                - DOK2 Summary: Evacuation insurance for Gulf hurricanes.
              - DOK1 Fact: Estate planning is the process of preparing for the management and disposal of assets after death.
                - DOK2 Summary: Wills for family amid flood risks.
            - State Note: R.S. 17:270; all nodes in one-unit.
      - Michigan
        - Core Mandate
          - Requirement: A minimum half-credit (0.5) standalone course in Personal Finance.
          - Target Audience: Mandatory for all students entering 8th grade in 2023 (which corresponds to the high school entering class of 2024). This makes it a graduation requirement for the Class of 2028 and beyond.
          - The "Math Swap": Uniquely, Michigan law explicitly allows this 0.5 credit course to replace 0.5 credits of the 4-credit Mathematics requirement (specifically the "4th Math" credit usually taken in senior year), or it can replace 0.5 credits of Visual, Performing, and Applied Arts, or World Language. This flexibility helps districts fit it into tight schedules without expanding the total credit load.
        - Implementation & Resources
          - Standards: All courses must align with the Michigan Department of Education (MDE) Personal Finance Content Expectations, organized into 7 distinct strands (PF1–PF7).
          - Teacher Credentialing: Because the course can count as Math, Social Studies, or Arts depending on the substitution, it can be taught by teachers with endorsements in Mathematics (EX), Social Studies (RX), Family and Consumer Sciences (KH), or Business (GQ), provided they meet the content proficiency.
          - Common Adoptions:
            - MI Money Matters: The official state platform provided by the Michigan Department of Treasury, offering free curricular tools and the "Financial Passport" certification.
            - Michigan Schools & Government Credit Union (MSGCU): A massive provider of "classroom presentations" and "Reality Fairs" that schools use to meet the "simulation" components of the curriculum.
            - Banzai: Heavily used in Michigan due to alignment with the new "Merit Curriculum" standards and sponsorship by local credit unions.
        - 1. Practical Skills
          - FAFSA Proficiency: The standards (PF 4.1) explicitly require students to understand the Free Application for Federal Student Aid (FAFSA), separating it from general loans as a specific tool to master.
          - Risk Transfer: You must teach not just "what is insurance," but the specific concept of "transferring risk" (paying a fee now to avoid a larger loss later), a technical definition often missed in simpler curricula.
          - Non-Income Career Factors: Uniquely, the standards require analyzing "non-income factors" (stress, location, work-life balance) that influence career choice, mandating a holistic view of "wealth" beyond just salary.
        - 2. The Granular "Required Standards" (The Deep Dive): The Michigan Merit Curriculum (PF Standards) mandates instruction in 7 specific domains. To be compliance-grade, you must cover these specific atomic concepts:
          - Domain: Earning Income (PF1)
            - Technical Skills ROI: You must research the "costs and benefits of obtaining necessary education or technical skills," explicitly validating trade/vocational paths alongside college.
            - Tax Impact: Instruction must cover how taxes affect "take-home pay" (Net Pay), requiring students to calculate the difference between Gross and Net.
          - Domain: Buying Goods & Services (PF2)
            - Role of Government: You must teach the "role of government in obtaining information" for consumers. This refers to using government databases (like the CFPB or CPSC) to research product safety and scams before buying.
          - Domain: Using Credit (PF4)
            - Credit Scoring Mechanics: Students must "evaluate the benefits, costs, and potential impacts" of using credit, which implies a deep dive into how credit scores are calculated and damaged.
            - Alternative Funding: Beyond loans, you must evaluate "Work Study" and "Grants" as specific non-repayable funding sources for education.
          - Domain: Financial Investing (PF5)
            - Government Agency Role: A unique standard requires analyzing the "role of government agencies" in investing (e.g., SEC, FDIC, SIPC), ensuring students understand who protects their assets.
            - Tax Benefits: You must analyze the "tax benefits" of different investment vehicles (Traditional vs. Roth), linking back to the "Paying Taxes" strand.
        - Summary of "Must-Haves" for Michigan ComplianceIf you are building a "gold standard" Michigan curriculum, you must specifically include:
          - The "Math/Arts/Language" Swap (Counseling Logic)
          - Role of Gov't in Investing (SEC/FDIC)
          - Work-Study & Grants (Specific Aid Types)
          - Risk Transfer (Insurance Definition)
          - Non-Income Career Factors (Quality of Life)
        - FinLit Knowledge Graph Nodes Yielded (Doug suggestion)
          - Half-credit required (Class of 2028); content expectations; digital literacy added.
            - Earning Income
              - DOK1 Fact: Spendable income is lower than gross income due to taxes assessed on income by federal, state, and local governments.
                - DOK2 Summary: Adjust Detroit auto pay for city income tax.
              - DOK1 Fact: Interest, dividends, and capital appreciation are examples of unearned income derived from financial investments.
                - DOK2 Summary: Use app dividends for EV charging costs.
            - Spending
              - DOK1 Fact: A budget is a plan for allocating a person’s spendable income to necessary and desired goods and services.
                - DOK2 Summary: Budget winter heating in UP homes.
              - DOK1 Fact: Individual spending decisions may be influenced by financial constraints, personal preferences, unique needs, peers, and advertising.
                - DOK2 Summary: Evaluate ad-driven gadget buys vs. needs.
            - Saving
              - DOK1 Fact: People who have sufficient income can choose to save some of it for future uses such as emergencies or later purchases.
                - DOK2 Summary: Save for Great Lakes boating emergencies.
              - DOK1 Fact: Inflation can erode the value of savings if the interest rate earned on a savings account is less than the inflation rate.
                - DOK2 Summary: Hedge with I-bonds for auto inflation.

                - DOK2 Summary: Balance crypto with index for volatility.

                - DOK2 Summary: Avoid high-interest for student EVs.

      - Minnesota
        - Core Mandate
          - Requirement: A minimum half-credit (0.5) standalone course in Personal Finance.
          - Target Audience: Mandatory for all students entering 9th grade in the 2024–2025 school year, making it a graduation requirement for the Class of 2028 and beyond.
          - Grade Level Restriction: Uniquely, the statute (Minn. Stat. § 120B.024) explicitly restricts when this course can be taken. Students must complete it during Grade 10, 11, or 12. A freshman (9th grade) course does *not* satisfy the state requirement, forcing districts to move it later in the high school career.
        - Implementation & Resources
          - Teacher Licensure: To solve staffing shortages, the law allows a broad range of licensure areas to teach this course. Teachers with field licenses in Agricultural Education, Business, Family and Consumer Science (FACS), Social Studies, or Mathematics are all legally authorized.
          - Standards: While the course is standalone, it must encompass the financial literacy standards found within the Minnesota K-12 Academic Standards in Social Studies (specifically the Economics strand), which were significantly revised in the 2021–2024 cycle.
          - Common Adoptions:
            - BestPrep: A dominant Minnesota-specific nonprofit that provides the "Minnesota Business Venture" and "Financial Matters" curriculum widely used across the state.
            - Junior Achievement North: The "JA Personal Finance" curriculum is heavily utilized in the Twin Cities metro area for its alignment with state standards.
            - Banzai: Widely adopted in rural Minnesota districts due to sponsorship by local community banks and credit unions.
        - 1. Practical Skills
          - Tenant Rights: Uniquely, Minnesota standards emphasize "Landlord/Tenant Law." Students must analyze lease agreements and understand their specific rights under Minnesota law (e.g., Cold Weather Rule, security deposit timelines).
          - Payroll Cards: You must specifically teach "Payroll Cards" as a compensation method, detailing the fees and regulations (Regulation E) associated with them, which is a specific focus of Minnesota labor law education.
          - Check 21 Act: The standards require technical knowledge of the "Check Clearing for the 21st Century Act (Check 21)," forcing students to understand digital check processing and why "float" time no longer exists.
        - 2. The Granular "Required Standards" (The Deep Dive): The Minnesota Academic Standards (Economics Strand) mandate instruction in specific atomic concepts. To be compliance-grade, you must cover these specific statutory/regulatory requirements:
          - Domain: Income & Taxes
            - FICA Breakdown: You cannot just teach "taxes." You must explicitly differentiate between FICA (Social Security/Medicare) and state/federal income tax withholding.
            - Tax Additions/Subtractions: Curriculum must cover "Common Tax Additions and Deductions," implying a need to understand Minnesota-specific K-12 Education Credits or subtraction for 529 contributions.
          - Domain: Consumer Protection (The "Regulation" Clause)
            - Regulation E: You are explicitly required to teach "Regulation E" (Electronic Fund Transfer Act), which governs errors and liability for debit cards and ATMs.
            - Consumer Agencies: Students must identify "state and federal policies... providing consumer protection," specifically referencing the Minnesota Attorney General’s Office and the CFPB.
          - Domain: Credit & Debt
            - Secured vs. Unsecured: You must distinguish between "Secured and Unsecured" credit, specifically using auto loans and credit cards as the primary examples.
            - Balloon Payments: Uniquely, the standards list "Balloon Payments" as a specific loan term to define and analyze, often in the context of auto or home financing risks.
        - Summary of "Must-Haves" for Minnesota ComplianceIf you are building a "gold standard" Minnesota curriculum, you must specifically include:
          - Landlord/Tenant Laws (Lease analysis)
          - Regulation E (Debit Card Rights)
          - Payroll Cards (Fees and usage)
          - Balloon Payments (Loan structures)
          - Grade 10-12 Requirement (Scheduling restriction)
        - FinLit Knowledge Graph Nodes Yielded (Doug suggestion)

              - DOK1 Fact: Most people earn wage and salary income in return for working, and they can also earn income from interest, dividends, rents, entrepreneurship, business profits, or increases in the value of investments.
                - DOK2 Summary: Blend Minneapolis salary with Airbnb for goals.
              - DOK1 Fact: Employers generally pay higher wages and salaries to more educated, skilled, and productive workers.
                - DOK2 Summary: Certify in med-tech for wage jump.

      - Mississippi *("College & Career Readiness" course)*
        - Core Mandate
          - Requirement: A full 1.0 Carnegie Unit course titled "College and Career Readiness" (CCR).
            - *Note:* While the course is 1 year long, state policy explicitly dictates that the financial literacy component must be equivalent to one semester (0.5 units) of instruction.
          - Target Audience: Mandatory for all students as a requirement for graduation. This was fully implemented starting with the Class of 2022, meaning it is now the standard for all current high schoolers.
          - The "Senior Year" Logic: While it can be taken earlier, the Mississippi Department of Education (MDE) recommends taking it in Junior or Senior year (or a 0.5 split between them) to ensure the financial aid modules align with actual college application timelines.
        - Implementation & Resources
          - Standards: Instruction is governed by the Mississippi College- and Career-Readiness Standards (MCCRS), specifically Unit 6 (Financial Literacy) and Unit 4 (Applying for Financial Aid).
          - Teacher Credentialing: A unique "Master Teacher" model. To teach this course effectively, educators often complete the Master Teacher of College and Career Readiness (MTCCR) certification provided by the Mississippi Council on Economic Education (MCEE).
          - Common Adoptions:
            - Get2College: A massive, state-specific nonprofit partner. Their curriculum is arguably more dominant than any textbook because it integrates directly with the state's scholarship applications.
            - Sallie Mae & Federal Reserve Bank of St. Louis: Official partners for the financial literacy modules.
        - 1. Practical Skills
          - The Student Portfolio: Uniquely, Mississippi requires every student to build a "Senior Portfolio" as a graduation exit requirement. This portfolio must contain evidence of financial literacy, a completed FAFSA (or opt-out), and college applications.
          - State Aid Application: Beyond Federal aid, students must be taught how to apply for Mississippi State Financial Aid (MAAPP), specifically distinguishing between the MTAG, MESG, and HELP grants.
          - Resumes: The standards require the creation of an "Academic Résumé" (distinct from a job resume) designed specifically for scholarship applications.
        - 2. The Granular "Required Standards" (The Deep Dive): The MCCRS (CCR Standards) mandate instruction in specific atomic concepts. To be compliance-grade, you must cover these specific statutory/regulatory requirements:
          - Domain: Financial Aid (The "Gold Standard" Model)
            - 4 Sources of Aid: You are legally required to teach the "Four Sources of Financial Aid" framework: Federal, State (Mississippi), Institutional, and Private.
            - Net Price Calculators: Students must demonstrate the ability to use a "Net Price Calculator" on a university website to determine the true cost of attendance after aid.
          - Domain: Post-Secondary Strategy
            - Degree ROI: The curriculum requires students to "develop a college financial plan" that projects the sustainability of funding for *all years* of the degree, not just the freshman year — a safeguard against dropout due to running out of funds.
            - 21st Century Skills: Uniquely, the financial literacy unit is paired with a "Soft Skills" requirement (communication, collaboration) as defined by the state's "Profile of a Graduate".
          - Domain: Banking & Wealth
            - The "Unbanked" Risk: While standard banking is taught, the MCEE curriculum heavily emphasizes the high cost of being "unbanked" (check cashers, payday lenders), reflecting the state's focus on economic inclusion.
        - Summary of "Must-Haves" for Mississippi ComplianceIf you are building a "gold standard" Mississippi curriculum, you must specifically include:
          - MTAG, MESG, & HELP Grants (State Aid Rules)
          - The Senior Portfolio (Graduation Exit Project)
          - Net Price Calculators (True Cost Analysis)
          - Sustainable Funding Plans (Multi-year budgeting)
          - The "Unbanked" Cost Analysis (Predatory Alternative Services)
        - FinLit Knowledge Graph Nodes Yielded (Doug suggestion)

                - DOK2 Summary: Use port dividends for stability.

      - Missouri
        - Core Mandate
          - Requirement: A minimum half-unit (0.5 credit) standalone course in Personal Finance.
          - Target Audience: Mandatory for all students entering 9th grade in the 2023–2024 school year and beyond, meaning it is a hard graduation requirement for the Class of 2027.
          - The "Test-Out" Competency Model: Uniquely, Missouri is one of the only states that explicitly codified a "Competency-Based" waiver. A student can "test out" of the course requirement if they attain a score of 90% or higher on the state’s online Personal Finance Assessment, effectively bypassing the seat-time requirement.
        - Implementation & Resources
          - Standards: Instruction must align with the Missouri Learning Standards (MLS) for Personal Finance, which are organized into five distinct "Competencies".
          - The "Fed" Partnership: The Federal Reserve Bank of St. Louis is the *de facto* curriculum partner for the state. They produced the official "Tools for Teaching the Missouri Personal Finance Competencies" guide, which most districts use as their primary textbook.
          - Assessment Requirement:
            - For Embedded courses (taught within Social Studies/FACS): Students MUST take the state's online End-of-Course (EOC) assessment.
            - For Standalone courses: The EOC is optional but highly recommended to validate curriculum quality.
        - 1. Practical Skills
          - I-9 & W-4 Proficiency: Missouri standards specifically require students to complete both the IRS Form W-4 *and* the Form I-9 (Employment Eligibility Verification), linking financial literacy directly to workforce onboarding.
          - The "Wealth Game": The state-endorsed Federal Reserve curriculum utilizes specific simulations like "The Wealth Game" (determinants of success) and "The Inventory Game" (Net Worth/Cash Flow) as practical application tools.
          - Check Writing: Despite the digital age, the standards explicitly require students to "demonstrate... writing checks," ensuring this manual skill is preserved.
        - 2. The Granular "Required Standards" (The Deep Dive): The Missouri Personal Finance Competencies mandate instruction in specific atomic concepts. To be compliance-grade, you must cover these statutory/regulatory requirements:
          - Domain: Income & Government (Uniquely Detailed)
            - Government Transfer Payments: You must define and analyze "Government Transfer Payments" (e.g., Social Security, Welfare, Unemployment) as a specific source of income distinct from wages. This is a standard rarely seen in other states.
            - Disposable Income Math: Students must calculate "Disposable Income" by specifically subtracting taxes *and* adding transfer payments, a precise economic formula mandated by the standards.
          - Domain: Investing & Math
            - Rule of 72: The standards explicitly name the "Rule of 72" as the required method for teaching the time value of money and compound interest doubling times.
            - Advisor Vetting: You are required to teach students how to "evaluate advisors' credentials and how to select professional advisors," moving beyond DIY investing to consumer protection.
          - Domain: Credit & Federal Reserve
            - Money Supply: Uniquely, students must analyze "the role of the Federal Reserve in controlling the money supply" within the Personal Finance course, bridging the gap between Macroeconomics and personal banking.
            - Creditworthiness Factors: The curriculum must cover the specific "factors that affect creditworthiness" (Capacity, Character, Collateral) before discussing loan products.
        - Summary of "Must-Haves" for Missouri ComplianceIf you are building a "gold standard" Missouri curriculum, you must specifically include:
          - Government Transfer Payments (Income Source Analysis)
          - Rule of 72 (Specific Math)
          - Form I-9 & W-4 (Employment Documents)
          - Role of the Federal Reserve (Money Supply)
          - Advisor Credential Evaluation (Fiduciary Vetting)
        - FinLit Knowledge Graph Nodes Yielded (Doug suggestion)
          - Half-credit elective (Class of 2010); national standards; end-of-course assessment.

                - DOK2 Summary: Include Show-Me benefits in St. Louis total comp.

                - DOK2 Summary: Retrain for ag-tech in rural MO.
            - Spending

                - DOK2 Summary: Donate post-essentials to KC food banks.
              - DOK1 Fact: Consumer decisions are influenced by the price of products or services, the price of alternatives, the consumer’s budget and preferences, and potential impact on the environment, society, and economy.
                - DOK2 Summary: Sustainable Ozark crafts vs. imports.

                - DOK2 Summary: Choose low-fee for flood reserves.

      - Nebraska
        - Core Mandate
          - Requirement: A minimum five-credit (0.5 unit) high school course in Personal Finance or Financial Literacy.
          - Target Audience: Mandatory for all students graduating in the 2023–2024 school year and beyond (meaning the Class of 2024 was the first cohort required to comply).
          - The "Universal FAFSA" Add-On: Separate from the course, LB 705 (passed 2023) mandates that starting with the Class of 2025, every public high school student must complete the FAFSA or sign a specific opt-out waiver to graduate.
        - Implementation & Resources
          - Embedding Flexibility: While a "course" is required, Nebraska Statute 79-729 allows this to be a standalone course *or* embedded within another subject (typically Economics or Ag Business), provided the district can verify it meets the 5-credit hour definition and covers all statutory standards.
          - Accountability: Uniquely, Statute 79-3004 requires every school district to present an "Annual Financial Literacy Status Report" to their local school board. This report must detail student progress and curriculum compliance, creating a localized "public shaming" mechanism if the district fails to teach it.
          - Common Adoptions:
            - Nebraska Council on Economic Education (NCEE): The dominant resource provider, offering teacher training and the "Nebraska Economics and Financial Literacy" guide.
            - EverFi & Banzai: Explicitly listed as recommended instructional materials in the Department of Education guidance.
            - In-House Ag Programs: In rural districts, this requirement is frequently met through Agricultural Education courses (e.g., "Ag Business"), which are approved to count for the credit if aligned.
        - 1. Practical Skills
          - Financial Record Keeping: The statute explicitly distinguishes between "budgeting" and "financial record keeping." Students must demonstrate the ability to organize and maintain financial documents (digital or physical).
          - FAFSA Waiver Process: Because of the new mandate, students are practically taught the legal implications of "Opting Out"—understanding what aid they are forfeiting by signing the waiver.
          - Risk Management: Unlike states that just say "Insurance," Nebraska law requires teaching "Risk Management" as a broader concept—analyzing how to mitigate risk *before* insurance is even involved.
        - 2. The Granular "Required Standards" (The Deep Dive): Statute 79-3002 and the Nebraska Social Studies Standards (Economics Strand) mandate instruction in specific atomic concepts. To be compliance-grade, you must cover these specific statutory requirements:
          - Domain: Fiduciary & Advisory (The "Choice" Clause)
            - Selecting Professionals: You are required to teach students how to "select financial professionals" (tax preparers, insurance agents, bankers). This moves beyond *doing* it yourself to *hiring* help correctly.
          - Domain: Credit & Debt
            - Monitoring Credit: The law explicitly requires "Monitoring Credit"—not just building it. This implies instruction on reading credit reports for errors and monitoring for identity theft.
            - Leverage: The standards often reference the use of debt as "leverage" (borrowing to invest/grow, particularly in Ag/Business contexts) rather than just "debt is bad," reflecting the state's agricultural business focus.
          - Domain: Saving & Investing
            - Nebraska NEST 529: While not in the statute, NDE guidance heavily emphasizes awareness of the Nebraska NEST 529 Direct College Savings Plan, including the specific state income tax deductions available to residents.
            - Economic Factors: You must connect personal finance to "Economic Indicators" (inflation, unemployment rates), bridging the gap between the mandatory Economics standards and personal money management.
        - Summary of "Must-Haves" for Nebraska ComplianceIf you are building a "gold standard" Nebraska curriculum, you must specifically include:
          - Financial Record Keeping (Statutory Phrase)
          - Annual Board Report Data (District Compliance)
          - Risk Management vs. Insurance (Distinction)
          - The FAFSA Waiver (Legal Opt-Out)
          - Ag Business Integration (Rural Context)
        - FinLit Knowledge Graph Nodes Yielded (Doug suggestion)
          - Standalone half-credit (Class of 2024); social studies integration; LB 452.
            - Earning Income
              - DOK1 Fact: Spendable income is lower than gross income due to taxes assessed on income by federal, state, and local governments.
                - DOK2 Summary: Adjust Omaha ag pay for property taxes.
              - DOK1 Fact: Interest, dividends, and capital appreciation are examples of unearned income derived from financial investments.
                - DOK2 Summary: Corn futures dividends for stability.
            - Spending
              - DOK1 Fact: A budget is a plan for allocating a person’s spendable income to necessary and desired goods and services.
                - DOK2 Summary: Rural vs. Lincoln cost budget.
              - DOK1 Fact: Individual spending decisions may be influenced by financial constraints, personal preferences, unique needs, peers, and advertising.
                - DOK2 Summary: Farm co-op buys vs. ads.
            - Saving
              - DOK1 Fact: People who have sufficient income can choose to save some of it for future uses such as emergencies or later purchases.
                - DOK2 Summary: Tornado fund automation.
              - DOK1 Fact: Inflation can erode the value of savings if the interest rate earned on a savings account is less than the inflation rate.
                - DOK2 Summary: Grain inflation hedge.
            - Investing
              - DOK1 Fact: Investors receive a return on their investment in the form of income and/or growth in value of their investment over time.
                - DOK2 Summary: Reinvest in ethanol plants.
              - DOK1 Fact: Riskier investments tend to earn higher long-run rates of return than lower-risk investments.
                - DOK2 Summary: Balance with treasury for risk.
            - Managing Credit and Debt
              - DOK1 Fact: Credit allows consumers to buy now and pay later, but interest and fees add to the amount owed.
                - DOK2 Summary: Co-op loans for equipment.
              - DOK1 Fact: Lenders use credit history and scores to decide whether to lend money and at what interest rate.
                - DOK2 Summary: Scores for silo financing.
            - Managing Risk
              - DOK1 Fact: Insurance protects consumers from the financial costs of certain risks by providing reimbursement for covered losses.
                - DOK2 Summary: Hail on crops.
              - DOK1 Fact: People can reduce the financial risk of job loss, illness, or death by purchasing insurance and building savings.
                - DOK2 Summary: Savings for harvest delays.
