      - New Hampshire
        - Core Mandate
          - Requirement: A minimum half-credit (0.5) standalone course in Financial Literacy.
          - Timeline & Target Audience:
            - Transitional Period (Now – 2025): Districts are currently operating under the "instructional" mandate of HB 1263/1671, where content can still be embedded in Economics or Social Studies.
            - Full Mandate (2026–2027): Starting in the 2026–2027 school year, the new Ed 306 Minimum Standards take full effect, requiring all high schools to offer a distinct, standalone 0.5 credit course for graduation.
          - The "Learn Everywhere" Loophole: Uniquely, New Hampshire allows students to satisfy this graduation requirement *outside* of the classroom through the "Learn Everywhere" program. This allows approved third-party vendors (like PragerU) to grant high school credit directly, bypassing district curriculum disputes.
        - Implementation & Resources
          - Local Control Competencies: Unlike states with rigid statutory topic lists, New Hampshire follows a "Competency-Based Education" (CBE) model. Ed 306.28 directs each district to establish its own "competencies and curriculum" for personal finance. There is no single statewide "checklist" of standards, giving districts massive flexibility— or liability — to define what "literate" means.
          - Common Adoptions:
            - PragerU (Cash Course): In a controversial but legally significant move, the NH State Board of Education approved PragerU’s "Cash Course" as an official "Learn Everywhere" provider. This means a student can take this specific online module and legally force their district to accept it for the 0.5 credit requirement.
            - NH Jump$tart Coalition: The primary non-profit partner for most public schools, providing the "FinLit300" championship and teacher training.
            - Banzai: Widely used for its alignment with the existing Social Studies frameworks.
        - 1. Practical Skills
          - Digital Portfolios: Under Ed 306.42, students are required to maintain digital portfolios to demonstrate ICT (Information & Communication Technologies) competency. Financial literacy artifacts (e.g., a digital budget spreadsheet or investment simulation report) are frequently used to satisfy this dual requirement.
          - Debt Management: Through the state-approved "Cash Course" framework, students must specifically master "Debt Management," explicitly covering the mechanics of reducing overall expenses and managing student loan debt.
          - The "FinLit300" Model: Practical application is often measured through the FinLit300, a state-sponsored tournament where students apply classroom knowledge in a competitive quiz-bowl format, effectively acting as a state "capstone" for high achievers.
        - 2. The Granular "Required Standards" (The Deep Dive): Because New Hampshire relies on District Competencies rather than a state statute list, the "Must-Haves" are defined by the Ed 306 Minimum Standards and the National Standards for Personal Financial Education (which the state implicitly endorses via its partners).
          - Domain: Earning & Reporting (The "Cash Course" Standard)
            - Pay Stubs & Taxes: If utilizing the state-approved "Learn Everywhere" pathway, students must demonstrate mastery of "Pay Stubs and Taxes"—specifically reading withholdings and understanding net vs. gross pay.
            - Employment forms: While not statutory, the accepted competencies often require understanding the difference between W-2 and 1099 employment, reflecting the state's focus on entrepreneurship and the "Live Free or Die" ethos of self-employment.
          - Domain: Wealth & Investing
            - Interest & Compounding: Instruction must cover "Interest and Insurance," specifically how compound interest works for (investing) and against (debt) the consumer.
            - Scam Avoidance: The NH Banking Department emphasizes resources on "avoiding scams and fraud," specifically targeting virtual currencies and investment fraud, which districts are encouraged to include.
          - Domain: Credit & Borrowing
            - Credit vs. Debit: Students must articulate the specific liability differences between credit cards and debit cards (e.g., fraud protection limits), a core competency in the state-endorsed materials.
        - Summary of "Must-Haves" for New Hampshire ComplianceIf you are building a "gold standard" New Hampshire curriculum, you must specifically include:
          - The "Learn Everywhere" Alignment (PragerU/Cash Course Option)
          - Digital Portfolio Integration (Ed 306.42)
          - Virtual Currency Scams (Banking Dept. Emphasis)
          - 2026-2027 Effective Date (The precise timeline for standalone compliance)
          - Competency-Based Assessment (Focus on mastery, not seat time)

      - North Carolina
        - Core Mandate
          - Requirement: A full One Unit (1.0 Credit) standalone course titled "Economics and Personal Finance" (EPF).
            - *Note:* Unlike states that require a 0.5 semester course, North Carolina mandates a full-year course that splits time between Economic theory (Micro/Macro) and Personal Finance.
          - Target Audience: Mandatory for all students who entered high school in the 2020–2021 school year or later. This means it is currently a graduation requirement for the Class of 2024 and all future cohorts.
          - The "Civics" Separation: Previously, finance was embedded in "Civics and Economics." HB 924 split them, creating this standalone EPF course and a separate "Founding Principles of the USA and NC: Civic Literacy" course, ensuring finance is no longer rushed at the end of a history class.
        - Implementation & Resources
          - Legislated Teacher Training: North Carolina is unique in *mandating* who trains the teachers. HB 924 explicitly names the North Carolina Council on Economic Education (NCCEE) as the required provider of the 40-hour professional development course for EPF teachers.
          - Standards: The course is governed by the North Carolina Standard Course of Study for Economics and Personal Finance, which is organized into five strands: Economics (E), Income & Education (IE), Money Management (MCM), Financial Planning (FP), and Critical Consumerism (CC).
          - Common Adoptions:
            - NCCEE: Because they are the legislated training partner, their resources (and the Council for Economic Education materials) are the *de facto* state curriculum.
            - Banzai: Widely used across NC districts as it aligns directly with the state's specific "Financial Planning" strand.
            - EverFi: Frequently used for the digital components of the "Money Management" strand.
        - 1. Practical Skills
          - Payment Apps: The standards (MCM.2.3) explicitly require comparing "costs and benefits of... payment applications" (e.g., Venmo, CashApp) alongside traditional cash and debit, acknowledging the rise of P2P finance.
          - True Cost of Credit: The statute (HB 924) legally mandates instruction on "The true cost of credit," requiring students to calculate total interest paid over the life of a loan rather than just looking at monthly payments.
          - Philanthropy: Uniquely, the standards (FP.1.3) require summarizing the "role of philanthropy, volunteer services, businesses, and nonprofits" in community development, treating giving as a core financial skill.
        - 2. The Granular "Required Standards" (The Deep Dive): House Bill 924 and the DPI EPF Standards mandate instruction in specific atomic concepts. To be compliance-grade, you must cover these statutory and regulatory requirements:
          - Domain: Income & Education (The "ROI" Strand)
            - Minimizing College Costs: You cannot just teach "how to apply" for aid; Standard IE.1.4 requires comparing "strategies which can minimize the costs of postsecondary education" (e.g., community college transfer, dual enrollment), focusing on ROI.
            - Post-Secondary Lifestyle: Students must "differentiate career... options after high school in terms of desired lifestyle," explicitly linking income levels to the standard of living they want (Standard IE.1.2).
          - Domain: Critical Consumerism (Consumer Protection)
            - Fraudulent Solicitation: You are required to teach "various types of fraudulent solicitation and business practices" (Standard CC.2.2), which covers everything from phishing to predatory door-to-door sales.
            - Consumer Empowerment: Instruction must explain how "consumer protection laws contribute to the empowerment of the individual," focusing on the *rights* of the buyer (Standard CC.2.1).
          - Domain: Money Management & Housing
            - Mortgage Comparison: The statute explicitly lists "Home mortgages" as a required topic. The standards expand this (MCM.1.4) to require *comparing* "various types of mortgages" (Fixed vs. ARM), not just defining what a mortgage is.
            - Leasing vs. Buying: You must compare the costs/benefits of "renting, leasing, or owning" for both housing and vehicles (MCM.1.3).
          - Domain: Economics (The "E" in EPF)
            - *Note:* Because this is an *Economics* course too, you must teach:
              - Market Structures: Perfect competition, monopoly, oligopoly (E.1.2).
              - Business Cycles: Analyzing macroeconomic indicators (GDP, CPI) over the business cycle (E.2.1).
              - Supply & Demand: Determining equilibrium price (E.1.3).
        - Summary of "Must-Haves" for North Carolina ComplianceIf you are building a "gold standard" North Carolina curriculum, you must specifically include:
          - Payment Apps (Venmo/CashApp) (Standard MCM.2.3)
          - Philanthropy & Voluntarism (Standard FP.1.3)
          - Fraudulent Solicitation (Scams)
          - True Cost of Credit (Statutory Requirement)
          - Market Structures & Business Cycles (The Economics Layer)
        - FinLit Knowledge Graph Nodes Yielded (Doug suggestion)

      - Ohio
        - Core Mandate
          - Requirement: A minimum half-unit (0.5 credit) standalone course in Financial Literacy.
          - Target Audience: Mandatory for all students entering 9th grade on or after July 1, 2022, which corresponds to the graduating Class of 2026 and beyond.
          - The "Math Swap" Nuance: Uniquely, Ohio law allows this 0.5 credit course to count toward graduation as either an Elective OR a Mathematics credit.
            - *Constraint:* It cannot replace Algebra 1, Geometry, or Algebra 2. It generally serves as the "4th Math" credit.
            - *Conflict Rule:* If a student is already using "Advanced Computer Science" to replace Algebra 2, they *cannot* also use Financial Literacy as a math credit; it must count as an elective.
        - Implementation & Resources
          - Teacher "Validation" License: Starting with the 2024–2025 school year, teachers must hold a specific "Financial Literacy Validation" on their license to teach this course, *unless* they already hold a license in Social Studies (9-12), Business Education, or Family and Consumer Sciences.
            - *Reimbursement:* The state explicitly provides a reimbursement (up to $500) for teachers to take the required assessment (OAE 059) to get this validation.
          - Standards: Instruction must align with Ohio's Learning Standards for Financial Literacy, which were significantly updated in 2024 to include statutory "Free Market Capitalism" requirements.
          - Common Adoptions:
            - Federal Reserve Bank of Cleveland: They provide a massive, 18-week "High School Financial Literacy Curriculum" that is specifically scaffolded to meet Ohio's standards and is free for districts.
            - Budget Challenge: A simulation-based provider widely used because it aligns directly with Ohio’s "real-world application" focus.
            - Banzai: Frequently adopted by Ohio credit unions to provide free materials to local schools.
        - 1. Practical Skills
          - Reconciliation: The standards specifically require students to "reconcile a checking and savings account balance," explicitly mandating the skill of balancing a register against a bank statement (Standard 2).
          - Tax Professional Vetting: Beyond just filing, students must learn "when to hire a tax professional" and how to evaluate their credentials, recognizing that not all tax situations are DIY.
          - Fraud Recompense: You must teach the specific "process for consumers who believe they are victims of fraud to seek recompense," moving beyond prevention to the actual recovery/reporting steps.
        - 2. The Granular "Required Standards" (The Deep Dive): Senate Bill 1 and the 2024 Model Curriculum Update mandate instruction in specific atomic concepts. To be compliance-grade, you must cover these specific statutory/regulatory requirements:
          - Domain: Free Market Capitalism (The "2024 Mandate")
            - Statutory Requirement: As of June 2024, you are legally required to teach 10 specific concepts related to capitalism, including:
              - "Raw materials, labor, and capital are privately owned."
              - "Market prices are the only way to convey information about supply and demand."
              - "Political features of the free market" (property rights, enforceable contracts).
          - Domain: Income & Taxes
            - Net Pay Calculation: Students must calculate the difference between Gross and Net Pay, specifically identifying "voluntary" (401k) vs. "involuntary" (tax) deductions.
            - Tax Additions: Unique to Ohio standards is the requirement to teach "Common Tax Additions" (income sources that increase tax liability) alongside deductions.
          - Domain: Credit & Debt
            - Debt Management Services: You must analyze "Debt Management Services" (credit counseling vs. debt settlement), evaluating which are legitimate and which are predatory.
            - Good vs. Bad Debt: The curriculum uses the explicit framework of "Good Debt vs. Bad Debt" to categorize borrowing decisions (e.g., mortgage vs. high-interest consumer debt).
        - Summary of "Must-Haves" for Ohio ComplianceIf you are building a "gold standard" Ohio curriculum, you must specifically include:
          - The "Free Market Capitalism" Module (10 Statutory Concepts)
          - Teacher Validation License (Legal staffing requirement)
          - Reconciling Accounts (Manual/Digital Skill)
          - Hiring Tax Professionals (Service Selection)
          - The "Math Swap" Rules (Counseling/Credit Logic)
        - FinLit Knowledge Graph Nodes Yielded (Doug suggestion)
          - Semester standalone (Class of 2026); model curriculum; six topics.
            - Earning Income
              - DOK1 Fact: Most people earn wage and salary income in return for working, and they can also earn income from interest, dividends, rents, entrepreneurship, business profits, or increases in the value of investments.
                - DOK2 Summary: Cleveland manufacturing plus rentals.
              - DOK1 Fact: Employers generally pay higher wages and salaries to more educated, skilled, and productive workers.
                - DOK2 Summary: Certs for auto wage increase.
            - Spending
              - DOK1 Fact: A budget is a plan for allocating a person’s spendable income to necessary and desired goods and services.
                - DOK2 Summary: Buckeye game budget.
              - DOK1 Fact: Individual spending decisions may be influenced by financial constraints, personal preferences, unique needs, peers, and advertising.
                - DOK2 Summary: Resist OSU merch ads.
            - Saving
              - DOK1 Fact: People who have sufficient income can choose to save some of it for future uses such as emergencies or later purchases.
                - DOK2 Summary: Lake Erie storm fund.
              - DOK1 Fact: Inflation can erode the value of savings if the interest rate earned on a savings account is less than the inflation rate.
                - DOK2 Summary: Manufacturing cost hedge.
            - Investing
              - DOK1 Fact: Investors receive a return on their investment in the form of income and/or growth in value of their investment over time.
                - DOK2 Summary: Rust Belt revitalization stocks.
              - DOK1 Fact: Riskier investments tend to earn higher long-run rates of return than lower-risk investments.
                - DOK2 Summary: Balance with munis.
            - Managing Credit and Debt
              - DOK1 Fact: Credit allows consumers to buy now and pay later, but interest and fees add to the amount owed.
                - DOK2 Summary: Student loans avalanche.

                - DOK2 Summary: Scores for factory homes.

      - Oklahoma
        - Core Mandate
          - Requirement: Completion of the "Passport to Financial Literacy," which consists of demonstrating competency in 14 specific areas of instruction.
          - Target Audience & Timeline:
            - Classes of 2025–2028: Students can fulfill the "Passport" requirement through instruction embedded in other courses (e.g., Math, Social Studies) during Grades 7–12.
            - Class of 2029+ (Freshmen in 2025–2026): A significant new shift requires these students to complete the requirement specifically through a standalone half-unit (0.5 credit) course or distinct coursework completed in Grades 10, 11, or 12.
          - The "Passport" Transcript: Uniquely, Oklahoma requires schools to maintain a separate "Personal Financial Literacy Passport" for every student — a cumulative record that tracks when they mastered each of the 14 standards, which follows them if they transfer districts.
        - Implementation & Resources
          - Standards: Instruction must cover the 14 Areas of Instruction mandated by Oklahoma Statute 70 O.S. § 11-103.6h.
          - State Resources: The State Department of Education (SDE) provides a free, standardized curriculum. A major partner is Oklahoma Money Matters (OKMM), an initiative of the State Regents for Higher Education, which provides the "Your Money Matters" guides used by many districts.
          - Teacher Credentialing: Because the Passport can be embedded (until the new mandate kicks in), it can be taught by teachers certified in Social Studies, Mathematics, Business Education, or Family and Consumer Sciences (FACS).
          - Common Adoptions:
            - Oklahoma Council on Economic Education (OCEE): A dominant provider of teacher training and curriculum resources.
            - FoolProof: Specifically endorsed by the Oklahoma Northern Bankruptcy Court and frequently used for the "Consumer Protection" modules.
        - 1. Practical Skills
          - Gambling Consequences: Oklahoma is one of the few states that legally mandates instruction on the "financial impact and consequences of gambling," reflecting the state's specific gaming economy.
          - Online Commerce: The standards explicitly bundle "Online Commerce" with credit card debt, requiring students to understand the specific risks of digital purchasing (e.g., "Buy Now, Pay Later" apps).
          - Check Endorsement: Students must demonstrate the physical ability to "correctly write, endorse, and deposit checks," preserving a manual banking skill often lost in modern curricula.
        - 2. The Granular "Required Standards" (The Deep Dive): The Passport to Financial Literacy Act (70 O.S. § 11-103.6h) explicitly lists 14 Areas of Instruction. To be compliance-grade, you cannot skip these statutory niches:
          - Domain: Consumer Risks (The "Predatory" List)
            - Gambling: As noted, you must cover the financial risks of gambling. This is Area of Instruction #12.
            - Bankruptcy: You must teach the specific "consequences of bankruptcy" (Area #13), often using resources provided by the Oklahoma bankruptcy courts.
            - Identity Theft: Instruction must cover "Identity fraud and theft" (Area #9), specifically focusing on prevention and resolution.
          - Domain: Earning & Taxes
            - Pyramid Schemes: Updated standards require understanding the "financial impact and consequences of multi-level marketing (MLM) businesses and pyramid schemes," a specific warning rarely codified in other states.
            - FICA vs. Income Tax: Students must distinguish between Federal/State income tax and FICA (Social Security/Medicare) withholdings (Area #2).
          - Domain: Housing & Living
            - Renting vs. Buying: You must cover the "Rights and responsibilities of renting or buying a home" (Area #10), which includes analyzing lease agreements and mortgage terms.
            - Charitable Giving: Area #11 explicitly mandates instruction on "Charitable giving," requiring students to budget for philanthropy.
        - Summary of "Must-Haves" for Oklahoma ComplianceIf you are building a "gold standard" Oklahoma curriculum, you must specifically include:
          - Gambling Consequences (Statutory Requirement)
          - The "Passport" Cumulative Record (Tracking Mechanism)
          - Multi-Level Marketing (MLM) Risks (Pyramid Schemes)
          - Online Commerce (Digital spending risks)
          - Bankruptcy (Legal consequences)
      - Oregon
        - Core Mandate
          - Requirement: A minimum half-credit (0.5) in Personal Financial Education AND a separate half-credit (0.5) in Higher Education and Career Path Skills.
            - *Note:* Unlike most states that combine these, Oregon legally mandates them as two distinct 0.5 requirements, totaling 1.0 credit of "Future Planning" instruction.
          - Target Audience: Mandatory for all students receiving a diploma on or after January 1, 2027 (the graduating Class of 2027 and beyond).
          - The "Standalone" Victory: In June 2024, the State Board of Education formally adopted rules requiring these to be standalone courses. While early discussions allowed embedding, the final rules effectively closed that loophole to ensure "dedicated time" for the material, though limited waivers exist for districts unable to staff it immediately.
        - Implementation & Resources
          - Standards: Instruction must align with the Oregon High School Personal Financial Education Standards (HS.PFE), adopted June 2024. These are organized into 5 Domains (A–E).
          - Common Adoptions:
            - Financial Beginnings Oregon: The dominant non-profit partner. Their "Pathways" and "Financial Foundations" curricula are mapped directly to Oregon’s standards and are free for schools.
            - Oregon Open Learning Hub: The Department of Education (ODE) maintains a curated group of Open Educational Resources (OER) specifically for PFE and HECPS (Higher Ed/Career).
            - Credit Unions: The GoWest Credit Union Association was a primary lobbyist for the bill; local credit unions frequently provide "guest speaker" modules that meet specific technical standards.
        - 1. Practical Skills
          - OregonSaves Awareness: Unique to Oregon, you must teach the specific mechanics of "OregonSaves" (the state-mandated auto-IRA for private sector workers). Students entering the workforce must know they will be *automatically enrolled* unless they opt out.
          - The "Career" Half: Because of the paired Higher Education & Career Path Skills (HECPS) credit, students must actively practice "Interview Skills" and create a "Post-High School Plan" (apprenticeship, military, or college) as a graduation requirement.
          - Tax Cycles: You must teach not just "taxes" but "Tax Cycles"—specifically when forms arrive (Jan), when filings are due (April), and when the fiscal year impacts planning.
        - 2. The Granular "Required Standards" (The Deep Dive): Senate Bill 3 and the HS.PFE Standards mandate instruction in specific atomic concepts. To be compliance-grade, you must cover these specific statutory/regulatory requirements:
          - Domain: Personal Income, Taxes & Services (HS.PFE.D)
            - Form W-4 vs. W-2 vs. 1099: You are explicitly required to teach the purpose and connection between these three forms. It is not enough to just "mention" taxes; students must trace the data flow from W-4 to Paycheck to W-2.
            - Tax Credits vs. Deductions: The standards require differentiating between a "Tax Credit" (dollar-for-dollar reduction) and a "Tax Deduction" (taxable income reduction), a nuance often missed in basic courses.
          - Domain: Banking & Investing (HS.PFE.B)
            - Oregon 529 Network: Instruction should cover the Oregon College Savings Plan (529) and its specific state tax advantages, linking the Finance credit to the Career credit.
            - Compound Interest: You must teach "Compound Interest" as a double-edged sword: how it builds wealth in savings and how it destroys wealth in debt.
          - Domain: Credit & Debt (HS.PFE.A)
            - Social Security Trace: Uniquely, the standards often link "Credit Reports" back to "Social Security Numbers," teaching students how their SSN is the primary key for their financial identity and how to protect it.
            - Rental Costs: You must explicitly cover "Common costs associated with rentals" (security deposits, first/last month rent, renter's insurance), acknowledging that most graduates will rent before owning.
        - Summary of "Must-Haves" for Oregon ComplianceIf you are building a "gold standard" Oregon curriculum, you must specifically include:
          - OregonSaves (Auto-IRA Law)
          - Paired Career Credit (HECPS requirement)
          - Tax Credits vs. Deductions (Specific Definitions)
          - Rental Costs (First/Last/Deposit)
          - Class of 2027 Mandate (Strict effective date)
        - FinLit Knowledge Graph Nodes Yielded (Doug suggestion)
          - Half-credit required (Class of 2027); SB 3; six areas with tech influence.
            - Earning Income
              - DOK1 Fact: Spendable income is lower than gross income due to taxes assessed on income by federal, state, and local governments.
                - DOK2 Summary: Portland tech pay minus no-sales-tax.
              - DOK1 Fact: Interest, dividends, and capital appreciation are examples of unearned income derived from financial investments.
                - DOK2 Summary: Silicon Forest dividends.
            - Spending
              - DOK1 Fact: A budget is a plan for allocating a person’s spendable income to necessary and desired goods and services.
                - DOK2 Summary: Rainy day Portland budget.
              - DOK1 Fact: Individual spending decisions may be influenced by financial constraints, personal preferences, unique needs, peers, and advertising.
                - DOK2 Summary: Eco-ads vs. needs.
            - Saving
              - DOK1 Fact: People who have sufficient income can choose to save some of it for future uses such as emergencies or later purchases.
                - DOK2 Summary: Wildfire evac fund.
              - DOK1 Fact: Inflation can erode the value of savings if the interest rate earned on a savings account is less than the inflation rate.
                - DOK2 Summary: Housing inflation hedge.
            - Investing
              - DOK1 Fact: Investors receive a return on their investment in the form of income and/or growth in value of their investment over time.
                - DOK2 Summary: Green tech compounding.
              - DOK1 Fact: Riskier investments tend to earn higher long-run rates of return than lower-risk investments.
                - DOK2 Summary: Cannabis stocks balanced.

                - DOK2 Summary: Student debt for Reed College.
              - DOK1 Fact: Lenders use credit history and scores to decide whether to lend money and at what interest rate.
                - DOK2 Summary: Scores for Portland lofts.

                - DOK2 Summary: Earthquake coverage.
              - DOK1 Fact: People can reduce the financial risk of job loss, illness, or death by purchasing insurance and building savings.
                - DOK2 Summary: Savings for timber layoffs.

      - Pennsylvania
        - Core Mandate
          - Requirement: A minimum half-credit (0.5) standalone course in Personal Finance.
          - Target Audience: Mandatory for all students in the graduating class of 2027 and beyond (meaning the requirement takes full effect starting with the 2026–2027 school year).
          - The "Standalone" Victory: Act 35 explicitly closes the "embedded" loophole. Schools can no longer just hide financial literacy inside an Economics or Math class; they must provide a distinct course with a specific course code (PIMS) dedicated to this subject.
        - Implementation & Resources
          - Standards: Instruction must align with the newly adopted Pennsylvania Academic Standards for Personal Finance (Chapter 4), which are organized into six specific domains (17.1 through 17.6).
          - Teacher Credentialing: The Department of Education (PDE) allows the course to be taught by educators holding certifications in Business, Computer and Information Technology (BCIT), Family and Consumer Sciences (FACS), Mathematics, or Social Studies, provided they use the state-aligned curriculum.
          - Common Adoptions:
            - Pennsylvania Council on Financial Literacy (PennCFL): A dominant non-profit partner that runs the statewide "Stock Market Challenge" and "Personal Finance Challenge," often used as the capstone for the course.
            - NGPF (Next Gen Personal Finance): Widely used for its alignment with the new Act 35 standards, particularly for the "Behavioral Economics" modules.
            - Federal Reserve Bank of Philadelphia: Provides specific regional economic data and curriculum modules widely adopted in the eastern part of the state.
        - 1. Practical Skills
          - Gig Economy & 1099: The standards explicitly require analyzing "income earned through entrepreneurship and the gig economy," ensuring students understand the tax implications of freelancing (Side hustles/DoorDash) vs. W-2 employment.
          - Evaluating Advisors: Uniquely, students must learn the criteria for "selecting financial institutions and professionals" (Standard 17.1.9-12.H), which implies vetting advisors for fiduciary status rather than just opening an account.
          - Digital Record Keeping: You must teach students to "develop a system for documenting and organizing personal financial records," specifically citing both paper and *electronic* methods (Standard 17.1.9-12.I).
        - 2. The Granular "Required Standards" (The Deep Dive): Act 35 and the PA Academic Standards (Chapter 4) mandate instruction in specific atomic concepts. To be compliance-grade, you must cover these specific statutory/regulatory requirements:
          - Domain: Behavioral Economics (The "Psychology" Mandate)
            - Biases: Pennsylvania is one of the few states to explicitly codify Behavioral Economics in its standards. You must teach specific biases, including "Anchoring Bias," "Loss Aversion," and "Mental Accounting" (Standard 17.1.9-12.D).
            - Family Influence: Students must analyze "how friends and family can impact a person's attitudes toward money," requiring a sociological look at financial habits (Standard 17.1.3-5.C).
          - Domain: Spending & Consumerism
            - Payment Methods: You must compare the "effects of using various payment methods" (Standard 17.3.9-12.G), specifically analyzing digital wallets vs. credit vs. debit.
            - Charitable Giving: The standards explicitly list "Charitable Giving" as a required sub-strand (17.3.K-2.M), requiring instruction on why and how people donate to causes.
          - Domain: Risk & Insurance
            - Risk Transfer: You must teach the concept of "Transferring Risk" (Standard 17.5.9-12.B), explaining insurance not just as a product but as a strategic financial move to shift liability.
        - Summary of "Must-Haves" for Pennsylvania ComplianceIf you are building a "gold standard" Pennsylvania curriculum, you must specifically include:
          - Behavioral Biases (Anchoring/Loss Aversion)
          - Evaluating Financial Advisors (Vetting Professionals)
          - Gig Economy Taxation (1099 vs W-2)
          - Charitable Giving (Philanthropy)
          - Risk Transfer (Insurance Theory)
        - FinLit Knowledge Graph Nodes Yielded (Doug suggestion)

      - Rhode Island
        - Core Mandate
          - Requirement: Students must demonstrate proficiency in consumer education/financial literacy prior to graduation.
            - *Note:* Unlike states that mandate strictly "one course," Rhode Island law (RIGL § 16-22-13) provides flexibility. Districts can meet this via a standalone course, a state-aligned project, an assessment, or another council-approved demonstration.
          - Target Audience: Mandatory for all students in the Graduating Class of 2024 and beyond. This requirement is fully active right now.
          - The "Proficiency" Model: While many districts have adopted standalone courses (like Cranston High School West and Central High), the law uniquely allows for a "Project" option — often used by CTE or Art pathways — where a student can manage a budget for a senior capstone to prove competency without sitting in a dedicated finance class.
        - Implementation & Resources
          - Standards: Instruction must align with the Rhode Island Financial Literacy Standards, adopted by the Council on Elementary and Secondary Education in December 2021. These standards are a hybrid of the CEE and Jump$tart national frameworks.
          - Teacher Credentialing: The law requires that if the "Project" option is chosen, it must be completed "under the supervision of a certified educator," though it does not mandate a specific Business/Math endorsement for that supervisor, allowing significant flexibility.
          - Common Adoptions:
            - NGPF (Next Gen Personal Finance): The RIDE implementation guide explicitly lists NGPF as a primary resource, and the state has funded cohorts of teacher professional development specifically for this curriculum.
            - EverFi: Widely used in districts like Westerly High School, where it is embedded into CTE courses (e.g., Cosmetology) to meet the requirement.
            - Credit Abuse Resistance Education (C.A.R.E.): The Rhode Island Bankruptcy Court heavily promotes this program, which brings bankruptcy judges and trustees into schools to teach about debt consequences.
        - 1. Practical Skills
          - Comparison of Prices: The statute (RIGL § 16-22-13) explicitly mandates instruction on "Comparison of prices," a specific consumer skill that goes beyond just "budgeting" to include unit price analysis and vendor vetting.
          - Installment Purchasing: You must teach "Installment purchasing" (Buy Now, Pay Later / Layaway). This is a statutory requirement, reflecting a focus on preventing young adults from falling into retail debt traps.
          - Rights in the Marketplace: Students must understand their "Rights and responsibilities in the marketplace," which implies a legal focus on consumer protection laws (Lemon Laws, Return Policies) rather than just math.
        - 2. The Granular "Required Standards" (The Deep Dive): RIGL § 16-22-13 and the 2021 Adopted Standards mandate instruction in specific atomic concepts. To be compliance-grade, you must cover these statutory requirements:
          - Domain: Credit & The Law (Statutory Mandate)
            - Credit & The Law: The statute specifically pairs "Credit and the Law." You cannot just teach interest rates; you must teach the *legal* aspects of borrowing, including truth-in-lending disclosures and binding repayment obligations.
            - Bankruptcy: Supported by the RIDE resource list and local court programs, instruction must cover the reality of Chapter 7 vs. Chapter 13 bankruptcy, often framing it as a "last resort" with long-term consequences.
          - Domain: Asset Protection
            - Protecting & Insuring Assets: The law mandates instruction on "Protecting and insuring assets." This phrasing requires a broader view than just "buying insurance"—it encompasses concepts like warranty protection, security systems, and digital asset security.
          - Domain: Employment & Income
            - NIL (Name, Image, Likeness): While primarily targeting post-secondary, 2021 legislation regarding student athletes created a unique environment where financial literacy modules often include "NIL" compensation rules for high-performing student athletes.
        - Summary of "Must-Haves" for Rhode Island ComplianceIf you are building a "gold standard" Rhode Island curriculum, you must specifically include:
          - "Credit and the Law" (Legal liability of debt)
          - Installment Purchasing (BNPL/Retail credit)
          - Comparison of Prices (Consumer Unit Pricing)
          - Protecting Assets (Warranties/Insurance)
          - Project-Based Option (Curriculum must support independent projects)
        - FinLit Knowledge Graph Nodes Yielded (Doug suggestion)
          - Integrated with economics (Class of 2024); SB 349 standards.

                - DOK2 Summary: Yacht club dividends.

      - South Carolina
        - Core Mandate
          - Requirement: A minimum half-credit (0.5) standalone course in Personal Finance.
          - Target Audience: Mandatory for all students entering 9th grade in the 2023–2024 school year and beyond, making it a graduation requirement for the Class of 2027.
          - The "Economics" Split: Previously, financial literacy was a small unit within the required Economics course. This new mandate explicitly separates it. Students must now take 0.5 credit of Economics AND 0.5 credit of Personal Finance, effectively dedicating a full Carnegie Unit to financial/economic education.
        - Implementation & Resources
          - Standards: Instruction must align with the South Carolina College- and Career-Ready Standards for Personal Finance, specifically utilizing course codes 5131 (Advanced Personal Finance) or 5141 (Personal Finance).
          - Common Adoptions:
            - VirtualSC: The state's virtual school curriculum is the "gold standard" for scope and sequence, ensuring all 8 required units are covered.
            - EverFi: A dominant partner in SC; over 60% of high schools use their resources, and the SC Department of Education (SCDE) formally collaborated with them to align their suite with the new mandate.
            - Junior Achievement (JA): Their "JA Financial Literacy" course is officially approved as a "100% aligned" solution for the code 5131 requirement.
            - MoneySKILL: The American Financial Services Association (AFSA) provides this free, web-based curriculum which is widely cited in state guidance.
        - 1. Practical Skills
          - Cybersecurity & Internet Safety: Uniquely, the legislation (Section 59-29-17) places a massive emphasis on "Internet safety and cyber security" specifically in a financial context. You must teach students how to safeguard financial data against technology-based attacks.
          - Computing Local Taxes: Beyond just federal income tax, the standards (Standard G) explicitly require students to "Compute local taxes on products and services" (sales tax/excise tax), ensuring they understand the specific tax burdens of their county/municipality.
          - Career Inventory Analysis: Students are required to "Complete and analyze the results of a career inventory," directly linking their financial future to their specific personality traits and skills (Standard G).
        - 2. The Granular "Required Standards" (The Deep Dive): Regulation 43-234 and the SCDE Course Standards (5131) mandate instruction in specific atomic concepts. To be compliance-grade, you must cover these specific statutory/regulatory requirements:
          - Domain: Income & Employment (The "Benefit" Focus)
            - Fringe Benefit Packages: You must teach students to analyze "fringe benefit packages" (health, vision, dental, 401k match) as a core component of compensation, differentiating them from base salary.
            - Voluntary vs. Mandatory Withholding: Students must "Differentiate mandatory and voluntary withholdings," specifically explaining why a 401(k) contribution is voluntary while FICA is not.
          - Domain: Credit & Consumer Law
            - Consumer Organizations: You are required to identify major consumer protection organizations, specifically citing the "Better Business Bureau (BBB)" and "Consumer Product Safety Commission (CPSC)" alongside the CFPB.
            - Cost of Credit Calculation: It is not enough to explain interest; students must "Calculate the cost of credit" for various loan products, requiring specific math competency.
          - Domain: Financial Planning & Giving
            - Philanthropy: The standards (Standard 3.6) and state guidance align with the concept of "Philanthropy" as a budgeting category, teaching students to allocate resources for charitable giving.
            - Estate Planning: Uniquely, the VirtualSC syllabus and state standards include "Inheritance and Estate Planning" as a required unit (Unit 7/8), ensuring students understand what happens to assets after death.
        - Summary of "Must-Haves" for South Carolina Compliance - If you are building a "gold standard" South Carolina curriculum, you must specifically include:
          - Cyber Security (Financial Data Protection)
          - Computing Local Taxes (Sales/Excise Math)
          - Fringe Benefit Analysis (Total Compensation)
          - Estate Planning/Inheritance (Wills & Trusts)
          - Consumer Protection Agencies (BBB/CPSC)
        - FinLit Knowledge Graph Nodes Yielded (Doug suggestion)

                - DOK2 Summary: Charleston tourism plus Airbnbs.

      - Tennessee
        - Core Mandate
          - Requirement: A minimum half-credit (0.5) standalone course in Personal Finance.
          - Target Audience: Mandatory for all high school students as a requirement for graduation. Unlike states with future effective dates, this is a current requirement for all cohorts.
          - The "JROTC" Substitution: Uniquely, Tennessee law allows students to substitute three years of JROTC for the 0.5 Personal Finance credit, *provided* the JROTC instructor attends the state-mandated personal finance training. This is a common compliance pathway for cadets.
          - Course Code: The specific state course code is 5901. It is technically housed within the CTE (Career & Technical Education) department but is a universal graduation requirement.
        - Implementation & Resources
          - Standards: Instruction must align with the Tennessee Academic Standards for Personal Finance, specifically standards 1 through 17+. These standards are heavily performance-based (e.g., "Write a check," "Fill out a form").
          - Teacher Credentialing: The course can be taught by teachers endorsed in Business, Marketing, Family and Consumer Sciences (FACS), or Economics.
            - *The "14-Hour" Rule:* Teachers with *other* endorsements (e.g., Math or Social Studies) can only teach this course if they complete a state-approved 14-hour training workshop.
          - Common Adoptions:
            - Ramsey Education: Extremely dominant in Tennessee (headquartered in Franklin, TN). Many districts adopt it as the default because it aligns culturally and educationally with the state's focus on debt avoidance.
            - Tennessee Financial Literacy Commission: Provides supplemental resources and teacher training workshops to meet the 14-hour requirement.
            - Banzai: Used in over 140 Tennessee schools due to sponsorship by local banks.
        - 1. Practical Skills
          - Tennessee Promise Requirements: You cannot teach financial aid in a vacuum. You must cover the Tennessee Promise scholarship, which requires students to not only complete the FAFSA but also perform 8 hours of community service per term. Curricula often integrate tracking these service hours.
          - Writing a Check: Standard 11 explicitly mandates that students demonstrate the ability to "Write a check," preserving this manual skill alongside digital reconciliation.
          - The "Argue" Standard: Uniquely, the standards require students to "craft an argumentative essay" regarding their choice of financial institution or student loan usage, linking financial literacy to English Language Arts (ELA) writing standards.
        - 2. The Granular "Required Standards" (The Deep Dive): The TN Personal Finance Standards mandate instruction in specific atomic concepts. To be compliance-grade, you must cover these statutory/regulatory requirements:
          - Domain: Credit & Predatory Lending (The "Flex Loan" Clause)
            - Flex Loans: Tennessee is one of the few states where "Flex Loans" (an open-end line of credit with high interest, authorized under Title 45) are common. Standard 12 explicitly lists "flex loans" alongside title and payday loans as products students must evaluate.
            - Negotiation Factors: You are required to teach the specific factors involved in "negotiating" an auto purchase (e.g., trade-in value vs. cash price), rather than just calculating the monthly payment.
          - Domain: Taxes & Employment Forms
            - Form W-4 & W-2: You must do more than "explain" taxes. Standard 6 requires students to "Complete... a W-4" and "analyze the W-2," focusing on the mechanical connection between the two forms.
            - 1040 Filing: The standard explicitly requires completing a federal income tax return. (Note: The text references "1040EZ," but compliance now implies the standard 1040 due to IRS changes).
          - Domain: Consumer Protection
            - Service Contracts vs. Warranties: You must evaluate the costs and benefits of "service contracts" (extended warranties) versus manufacturer warranties, a specific consumer protection niche often skipped in general courses.
        - Summary of "Must-Haves" for Tennessee ComplianceIf you are building a "gold standard" Tennessee curriculum, you must specifically include:
          - "Flex Loans" (Specific high-interest product)
          - Tennessee Promise (Community Service + FAFSA rules)
          - JROTC Substitution Rules (Administrative pathway)
          - Argumentative Essay Writing (ELA Integration)
          - Service Contracts vs. Warranties (Consumer Analysis)
        - FinLit Knowledge Graph Nodes Yielded (Doug suggestion)

                - DOK2 Summary: Resist concert ads.

                - DOK2 Summary: Music inflation hedge.

      - Texas (New - Updated to Standalone 2025)
        - Core Mandate: The "Two Regimes"
          - Current Reality (2025–2026 School Year):
            - Most high schools satisfy the state economics requirement using the "Personal Financial Literacy and Economics" (PFLE) course (TEKS §113.76). This is a 0.5 credit course that combines 70% personal finance with 30% economic theory.
            - *Note:* This course replaced the old "General Economics" for many students, but the shift created a demand for more finance time.
          - The New Mandate (House Bill 27):
            - Requirement: A dedicated, standalone half-credit (0.5) course in Personal Financial Literacy.
            - Target Audience: Mandatory for all students entering 9th grade in the 2026–2027 school year and beyond (Class of 2030).
            - The Split: Under HB 27, Financial Literacy is decoupled from Economics. Students will eventually need to take *both* a PFL course AND an Economics course to graduate, ending the "two-for-one" hybrid model.
        - Implementation & Resources
          - Standards (TEKS):
            - For PFLE (Current): You must align with TEKS Chapter 113.76 (Personal Financial Literacy and Economics).
            - For Standalone (Future): Instruction will likely align with TEKS Chapter 113.49 (Personal Financial Literacy), which is the existing elective standard likely to be updated for the new mandate.
          - The "Free Enterprise" Constraint: Texas law is explicit that instruction must emphasize the "Free Enterprise System." You cannot teach neutral economics; you must specifically teach the *benefits* of the U.S. free enterprise system (private property, price system, incentives) as superior to other systems.
          - Instructional Materials (Proclamation 2024): The State Board of Education (SBOE) recently adopted materials for the PFLE course under Proclamation 2024. If you are a vendor, your materials are likely vetted against the 113.76 standards.
            - *Approved Vendors:* Ramsey Education, McGraw-Hill, and Decker & Associates were all approved 100% compliant lists for the 2024 cycle.
        - 1. Practical Skills
          - "Money Personality": A unique Texas TEKS requirement (113.76.c.1.B) is investigating the student's "Money Personality," specifically analyzing their spending and saving propensity. This is a behavioral psychology standard codified into law.
          - FAFSA & TASFA: You must teach not just the FAFSA but the TASFA (Texas Application for State Financial Aid), which is crucial for non-citizen residents eligible for state aid under Texas House Bill 1403.
          - Benefit Analysis: Students must calculate and compare "Fringe Benefits" (Health Savings Accounts, Flexible Spending Accounts, 401k match) as part of total compensation, distinguishing them from gross pay.
        - 2. The Granular "Required Standards" (The Deep Dive): The TEKS §113.76 (PFLE) standards mandate instruction in specific atomic concepts. To be compliance-grade for the current market, you must cover these nuances:
          - Domain: The Free Enterprise System (Legal Mandate)
            - Scarcity & Choice: You must teach how "scarcity forces individuals and businesses to make choices," explicitly using the Production Possibilities Curve (PPC) model.
            - Property Rights: Instruction must cover "the right to own, control, and dispose of private property" as a central tenet of wealth building in Texas.
          - Domain: Entrepreneurship
            - Business Structures: You must compare the liability and tax implications of Sole Proprietorships, Partnerships, and Corporations.
            - Gig Economy: The standards require comparing "total compensation... as an independent contractor vs. an employee," covering self-employment tax obligations.
          - Domain: Investing & Retirement
            - Simple vs. Compound: You must teach the power of "Compound Growth" on investments versus "Compound Interest" on debt, framing one as a wealth builder and the other as a wealth destroyer.
            - Retirement Vehicles: Specific instruction on 401(k), 403(b), and IRAs (Roth vs. Traditional) is required, focusing on the tax treatment of each.
        - Summary of "Must-Haves" for Texas Compliance - If you are building a "gold standard" Texas curriculum (targeting the current PFLE market and future HB 27 mandate), you must specifically include:
          - TASFA (Texas Financial Aid)
          - The Free Enterprise System (Ideological/Economic Mandate)
          - "Money Personality" (Behavioral Analysis)
          - Fringe Benefits Calculation (HSA/FSA/Match)
          - Independent Contractor vs. Employee (Tax rules)
        - FinLit Knowledge Graph Nodes Yielded (Doug suggestion)
          - Half-credit standalone (Class of 2030); TEKS §113.49; replaces economics.
            - Earning Income
              - DOK1 Fact: Most people earn wage and salary income in return for working, and they can also earn income from interest, dividends, rents, entrepreneurship, business profits, or increases in the value of investments.
                - DOK2 Summary: Oil salary plus Austin rentals.
              - DOK1 Fact: Employers generally pay higher wages and salaries to more educated, skilled, and productive workers.
                - DOK2 Summary: Tech certs for Houston boost.
            - Spending
              - DOK1 Fact: A budget is a plan for allocating a person’s spendable income to necessary and desired goods and services.
                - DOK2 Summary: BBQ festival budget.
              - DOK1 Fact: Individual spending decisions may be influenced by financial constraints, personal preferences, unique needs, peers, and advertising.
                - DOK2 Summary: Rodeo ads vs. needs.
            - Saving
              - DOK1 Fact: People who have sufficient income can choose to save some of it for future uses such as emergencies or later purchases.
                - DOK2 Summary: Hurricane fund.
              - DOK1 Fact: Inflation can erode the value of savings if the interest rate earned on a savings account is less than the inflation rate.
                - DOK2 Summary: Energy inflation hedge.
            - Investing
              - DOK1 Fact: Investors receive a return on their investment in the form of income and/or growth in value of their investment over time.
                - DOK2 Summary: Shale reinvestment.
              - DOK1 Fact: Riskier investments tend to earn higher long-run rates of return than lower-risk investments.
                - DOK2 Summary: Balance oil futures.
            - Managing Credit and Debt
              - DOK1 Fact: Credit allows consumers to buy now and pay later, but interest and fees add to the amount owed.
                - DOK2 Summary: Student loans for UT.
              - DOK1 Fact: Lenders use credit history and scores to decide whether to lend money and at what interest rate.
                - DOK2 Summary: Scores for ranch loans.
            - Managing Risk
              - DOK1 Fact: Insurance protects consumers from the financial costs of certain risks by providing reimbursement for covered losses.
                - DOK2 Summary: Tornado in Panhandle.
              - DOK1 Fact: People can reduce the financial risk of job loss, illness, or death by purchasing insurance and building savings.
                - DOK2 Summary: Savings for oil busts.
            - State Note: HB 27; TEKS; all nodes.
      - Utah
        - Core Mandate
          - Requirement: A minimum half-credit (0.5) standalone course titled "General Financial Literacy" (GFL).
          - Target Audience: Mandatory for all students as a graduation requirement. This has been fully effective since the Class of 2008, meaning the infrastructure is mature and strictly enforced.
          - The "Assessment" Gatekeeper: Unlike most states, Utah law (SB 40) mandates that every student enrolled in the GFL course must take a state-administered End-of-Course Assessment (typically provided by YouScience). While passing the exam is not strictly required for graduation (local district discretion), the data is reported to the state legislature to grade school performance.
        - Implementation & Resources
          - Teacher Endorsement: Utah is highly restrictive on *who* can teach this course. Instructors must hold a specific "General Financial Literacy Endorsement" on their license. A standard Math or Social Studies license is not sufficient without this specific add-on, which requires passing a state exam or completing specific coursework.
          - Standards: Instruction must align with the General Financial Literacy Strands and Standards (revised 2019/2023), organized into 5 Strands covering Economic Concepts, Financial Priorities, Saving/Investing, Money Management, and Credit.
          - Common Adoptions:
            - Finance in the Classroom: The official state repository of approved lesson plans and resources.
            - Banzai: Widely used across Utah districts due to its direct mapping to the state's required Strands.
            - YouScience (Precision Exams): The vendor for the mandatory end-of-course assessment, heavily influencing how teachers pace their curriculum to match the test blueprint.
        - 1. Practical Skills
          - Tax Forms: You must explicitly teach the difference between the W-2, W-4, and I-9 forms. Uniquely, the standards also require instruction on the Utah State Tax Form TC-40, ensuring students know how to file state taxes specifically.
          - Charitable Giving: Utah standards (Strand 2, Standard 1) explicitly require discussing the "pros and cons of charitable giving," specifically listing "religious worship" alongside "tax benefits" as a motivation — a reflection of the state's unique cultural demographics.
          - Bankruptcy Societal Impact: Beyond just personal credit damage, the curriculum must cover the "societal effects of bankruptcy" (e.g., higher costs for everyone else), framing it as a community issue rather than just a personal failure.
        - 2. The Granular "Required Standards" (The Deep Dive): Utah Code 53E-3-505 and the GFL Standards mandate instruction in specific atomic concepts. To be compliance-grade, you must cover these specific statutory requirements:
          - Domain: Economic Systems (The "Socialism" Mandate)
            - Capitalism vs. Socialism: As of HB 286 (2019), the GFL course must analyze "different economic systems" including "Capitalism, Socialism, and Communism." The law explicitly requires discussing "historic and current examples" of how these systems affect economic growth and freedom, preventing a purely neutral stance.
          - Domain: The "Success Sequence" (Legislative Push)
            - Success Sequence: Following S.J.R. 3 (2024), there is a strong legislative push (and taskforce encouragement) to teach the "Success Sequence" (Education -> Full-Time Job -> Marriage -> Children) as a proven pathway to avoid poverty. While "soft" law, it is a key differentiator for alignment with legislative intent.
          - Domain: Behavioral Finance
            - Money Scripts: The standards require students to "evaluate the role of emotions, attitudes, and habits (Money Scripts)" in financial decision-making, acknowledging the psychological barriers to wealth.
            - Cultural Values: You must analyze how "culture and values" influence financial goals, which links back to the "Charitable Giving/Religious Worship" standard.
        - Summary of "Must-Haves" for Utah Compliance - If you are building a "gold standard" Utah curriculum, you must specifically include:
          - Capitalism vs. Socialism/Communism (HB 286 Mandate)
          - Utah Form TC-40 (State Tax Filing)
          - "Success Sequence" (Poverty Avoidance Framework)
          - Religious Worship & Giving (Charitable Motivations)
          - Societal Effects of Bankruptcy (Community Impact)
          - End-of-Course Assessment Prep (YouScience Alignment)
        - FinLit Knowledge Graph Nodes Yielded (Doug suggestion)
          - Half-credit GFL (Class of 2008); strands on economic reasoning.
          - Earning Income
            - DOK1 Fact: Spendable income is lower than gross income due to taxes assessed on income by federal, state, and local governments.
              - DOK2 Summary: SLC tech tax deductions.
            - DOK1 Fact: Interest, dividends, and capital appreciation are examples of unearned income derived from financial investments.
              - DOK2 Summary: Ski resort dividends.
          - Spending

              - DOK2 Summary: Pioneer Day budget.
            - DOK1 Fact: Individual spending decisions may be influenced by financial constraints, personal preferences, unique needs, peers, and advertising.
              - DOK2 Summary: Temple ads vs. needs.

              - DOK2 Summary: Quake reserves.

              - DOK2 Summary: Housing inflation.
          - Investing

      - Virginia
        - Core Mandate
          - Requirement: A full One Unit (1.0 Credit) course in Economics and Personal Finance.
            - *Note:* Unlike states that separate them, Virginia usually bundles these into a single year-long course, though the law allows for two semester courses (0.5 Finance + 0.5 Economics) to satisfy the requirement.
          - Target Audience: Mandatory for all students (Standard and Advanced Studies Diplomas) as a graduation requirement. This has been effective since the Class of 2015, meaning it is a deeply entrenched requirement.
          - The "Virtual" Double-Dip: Uniquely, because many districts use the Virtual Virginia platform or online modules to teach this course, it is frequently used to simultaneously satisfy the state’s "Virtual Course" graduation requirement.
        - Implementation & Resources
          - The W!SE Certification Engine: While the *course* is the mandate, the W!SE Financial Literacy Certification is the *de facto* exam.
            - *Why?* Virginia requires students on the Standard Diploma track to earn a "Career and Technical Education (CTE) Credential." Passing the W!SE exam counts as this credential.
            - *Impact:* This makes the W!SE test "high stakes" for schools, as they compete for "Blue Star School" status based on pass rates.
          - Standards: Instruction must align with the 2019 Economics and Personal Finance Standards of Learning (SOLs). These are distinct from the History/Social Science SOLs and are managed by the CTE department.
          - Common Adoptions:
            - Virtual Virginia: The state-run online course is the benchmark curriculum used by rural and small districts.
            - Khan Academy: Officially recommended by W!SE and aligned specifically to the Virginia EPF standards.
            - VCEE (Virginia Council on Economic Education): The primary provider of teacher training and "Reading Makes Cents" programs.
        - 1. Practical Skills
          - The "Gig Economy" Law: In 2021, the General Assembly amended Code § 22.1-200.03 to *explicitly* require instruction on "Employment arrangements," specifically defining: "gig work, piece work, contingent work, day labor work, freelance work, and 1099 work." You must teach the tax and stability implications of these specific arrangements.
          - School Banking: The Code explicitly encourages the establishment of "School Banks" (student-run branches) and requires guidelines for their operation, making practical banking experience a preferred instructional method.
          - Quality of Institutions: It is not enough to open an account; the law mandates teaching students how to "judge the quality of a financial institution’s services," implying a comparative analysis of fees, APY, and customer service.
        - 2. The Granular "Required Standards" (The Deep Dive): Code of Virginia § 22.1-200.03 and the 2019 EPF SOLs mandate instruction in specific atomic concepts. To be compliance-grade, you must cover these statutory requirements:
          - Domain: Employment & Income (The "1099" Update)
            - Employment Forms: You must teach the specific differences between W-2, W-4, and 1099 forms. The 2021 legislation makes the "1099" (Independent Contractor) focus legally mandatory.
            - Human Capital: The standards require students to "demonstrate that their own human capital (knowledge and skills) is their most valuable resource," linking education directly to lifetime earnings.
          - Domain: Credit & Consumer Protection
            - Predatory Lending: You must analyze "Predatory Lending" practices, specifically identifying payday lenders and title loans (common in Virginia regulatory discussions).
            - Bankruptcy Consequences: Instruction must cover the long-term impact of Bankruptcy on credit scores and future employment.
          - Domain: Planning & Insurance
            - Inheritance: Uniquely, the standards often list "Inheritance" as a topic for wealth transfer, requiring instruction on wills and estate planning basics.
            - Virginia 529: While generic college savings is taught, best-practice curricula in Virginia explicitly cover the Virginia529 (Invest529) plan and its state tax deduction benefits.
        - Summary of "Must-Haves" for Virginia Compliance - If you are building a "gold standard" Virginia curriculum, you must specifically include:
          - Gig Economy & 1099 Work (2021 Statutory Update)
          - W!SE Certification Prep (The "High Stakes" Test)
          - Judging Financial Institutions (Comparative Analysis)
          - Virginia529 Plans (State Specifics)
          - School Banking Guidelines (Practical Application)
        - FinLit Knowledge Graph Nodes Yielded (Doug suggestion)
          - Integrated into economics (Class of 2015); full-credit; objectives correlated.

      - West Virginia
        - Core Mandate
          - Requirement: A minimum half-credit (0.5) standalone course in Personal Finance.
          - Target Audience: Mandatory for all students entering 9th grade in the 2024–2025 school year and thereafter. This means the requirement is fully effective for the Class of 2028 and beyond.
          - The "Civics Divorce": Previously, financial literacy was embedded within the "Civics" course. Policy 2510 explicitly states that for the 2024–2025 cohort, Personal Finance is removed from Civics to become a separate, distinct graduation requirement.
          - Grade Level Flexibility: While originally targeted at 11th/12th graders, Senate Bill 283 (passed 2025) expanded the window. Students can now satisfy this requirement in Grades 8, 9, 10, 11, or 12, providing districts massive scheduling flexibility. Crucially, this applies retroactively, so a student who took a qualifying course in 8th grade has already met the high school requirement.
        - Implementation & Resources
          - Standards: Instruction must align with the West Virginia College- and Career-Readiness Standards for Personal Finance, which focus on practical money management and wealth building.
          - State Treasurer’s Programs: The West Virginia State Treasurer’s Office is a uniquely active partner in curriculum.
            - "Get a Life": A state-sponsored budget simulation program used in over 140 schools. It assigns students a random career/salary and forces them to manage monthly expenses, often serving as the "capstone" for the middle or high school unit.
            - Bank at School: A program connecting schools with financial institutions to open actual student savings accounts.
        - 1. Practical Skills
          - The PROMISE Scholarship: You must teach the specific eligibility for the PROMISE Scholarship, West Virginia’s merit-based financial aid. This includes the specific 3.0 GPA (core and overall) and ACT/SAT thresholds. It is a critical financial planning tool for WV families.
          - Trade Savings (Jumpstart): Unlike most states that focus solely on college 529s, West Virginia requires awareness of the Jumpstart Savings Program. This is a tax-advantaged savings account specifically for skilled trades, allowing students to save for tools, equipment, and certifications tax-free.
          - Hope Scholarship Awareness: With the expansion of the Hope Scholarship (Education Savings Accounts), financial literacy curricula in WV increasingly cover how families can use these funds for individualized learning, viewing education funding as a "consumer choice".
        - 2. The Granular "Required Standards" (The Deep Dive): House Bill 3113 and Policy 2510 mandate instruction in specific atomic concepts. To be compliance-grade, you must cover these statutory/regulatory requirements:
          - Domain: Earning & Career Pathways
            - In-State ROI: You must analyze the Return on Investment (ROI) for West Virginia-specific institutions, often using data from the Higher Education Policy Commission to compare tuition at WVU/Marshall vs. community colleges.
            - Fringe Benefits: Instruction must cover "fringe benefits" (PEIA insurance, retirement match) as part of total compensation, a key concept in a state with significant public sector employment.
          - Domain: Savings & Wealth
            - SMART529: You must specifically teach the benefits of the SMART529 plan (West Virginia’s college savings plan), including the specific state income tax deductions available to parents/grandparents.
            - Emergency Fund Liquidity: The curriculum emphasizes the difference between "savings" and "investing," specifically highlighting the need for liquid emergency funds to handle unexpected economic shocks (a focus of the Treasurer’s "Get a Life" module).
        - Summary of "Must-Haves" for West Virginia Compliance - If you are building a "gold standard" West Virginia curriculum, you must specifically include:
          - Jumpstart Savings Program (Trades/Vocational Savings)
          - PROMISE Scholarship Rules (3.0 GPA/Test Scores)
          - "Get a Life" Simulation (Budgeting Capstone)
          - SMART529 Tax Benefits (State Deduction)
          - SB 283 Grade Flexibility (8th Grade Option)
        - FinLit Knowledge Graph Nodes Yielded (Doug suggestion)
          - Half-credit (Class of 2028); HB 3113; social studies integration.
            - Earning Income
              - DOK1 Fact: Spendable income is lower than gross income due to taxes assessed on income by federal, state, and local governments.
                - DOK2 Summary: Coal county tax deductions.
              - DOK1 Fact: Interest, dividends, and capital appreciation are examples of unearned income derived from financial investments.
                - DOK2 Summary: Timber dividends.
            - Spending
              - DOK1 Fact: A budget is a plan for allocating a person’s spendable income to necessary and desired goods and services.
                - DOK2 Summary: Appalachian trail budget.
              - DOK1 Fact: Individual spending decisions may be influenced by financial constraints, personal preferences, unique needs, peers, and advertising.
                - DOK2 Summary: Resist mine tour ads.
            - Saving

                - DOK2 Summary: Flood reserves.

                - DOK2 Summary: Energy inflation.
            - Investing
              - DOK1 Fact: Investors receive a return on their investment in the form of income and/or growth in value of their investment over time.
                - DOK2 Summary: Shale compounding.

                - DOK2 Summary: Balance nat gas.

                - DOK2 Summary: Student debt for WVU.

                - DOK2 Summary: Scores for mountain homes.

      - Wisconsin
        - Core Mandate
          - Requirement: A minimum half-credit (0.5) standalone course in Personal Financial Literacy.
          - Target Audience: Mandatory for all students graduating in 2028 and beyond (meaning the freshmen entering in the 2024–2025 school year are the first cohort on the clock).
          - The "Standalone" Shift: Prior to Act 60, Wisconsin had a strong *standard* but no *course* mandate (districts could embed it). Act 60 explicitly requires a dedicated 0.5 credit, forcing many districts to pull this content out of Social Studies or Business embedding and create a specific class.
        - Implementation & Resources
          - Standards: Instruction must align with the Wisconsin Standards for Personal Financial Literacy (revised 2020). These are distinct because they include a unique sixth strand: "Financial Mindset".
          - Common Adoptions:
            - Money Path: A massive, Wisconsin-specific non-profit (formerly SecureFutures) that is arguably the dominant provider. Their "Money Path" app is a simulation tool heavily subsidized for Wisconsin schools and maps directly to the Act 60 mandates.
            - Edvest 529: The state's official college savings plan provides specific curriculum modules that many districts use to satisfy the investing standards.
            - Junior Achievement of Wisconsin: Their "JA Personal Finance" curriculum is widely used in Milwaukee and Madison districts for its alignment with the "Financial Mindset" strand.
        - 1. Practical Skills
          - The "Financial Mindset" Strand: Unique to Wisconsin, you must teach the psychological aspect of money. Standard FM1 requires students to "analyze how... values, emotions, attitudes, and personality influence financial decisions." This effectively mandates a Behavioral Economics module.
          - Edvest 529 Awareness: You must specifically teach the benefits of the Edvest 529 plan (Wisconsin's state-sponsored plan), including the specific state income tax deduction eligibility for contributors.
          - Filing a Complaint: The standards require students to demonstrate how to "file a consumer complaint" specifically with the Wisconsin Department of Agriculture, Trade and Consumer Protection (DATCP), the state's primary consumer watchdog.
        - 2. The Granular "Required Standards" (The Deep Dive): Act 60 and the 2020 Standards mandate instruction in specific atomic concepts. To be compliance-grade, you must cover these statutory/regulatory requirements:
          - Domain: Credit & Debt (The "Predatory" List)
            - Instant Tax Refunds: You are explicitly required to analyze "Instant Tax Refunds" (Refund Anticipation Loans) as a predatory lending practice, a specific product warning rarely listed in other states.
            - Payday & Title Loans: Instruction must cover "payday loans" and "auto title loans," specifically calculating the APR of these products compared to traditional financing.
          - Domain: Risk Management
            - Liability Insurance: The standards require differentiating between "Liability" and "Full Coverage" auto insurance, ensuring students understand what is legally required in Wisconsin vs. what protects their own asset.
            - Identity Theft Recovery: You must teach not just prevention, but the "steps to recovery" if identity theft occurs (e.g., freezing credit at the three bureaus).
          - Domain: Money Management
            - Digital Awareness: Under the Financial Mindset strand, you must teach "Digital Awareness," which covers the specific psychological pressure of social media influencers and digital marketing on spending habits.
        - Summary of "Must-Haves" for Wisconsin ComplianceIf you are building a "gold standard" Wisconsin curriculum, you must specifically include:
          - "Financial Mindset" Module (Behavioral Psychology)
          - Instant Tax Refunds (Predatory Product)
          - Edvest 529 Plan (State Tax Rules)
          - Filing DATCP Complaints (Consumer Protection)
          - Digital Marketing Influence (Social Media Pressure)

    - Embedded FinLit within Other Courses (As of Dec 2025)
      - Note: Many states in this category (like Texas used to be) are actively moving toward the standalone requirement
      - Arizona, Idaho, Illinois, Maine, Maryland, Montana, Nevada, New Mexico, North Dakota, South Dakota
    - No Mandate (As of Dec 2025)
      - Alaska, Arkansas, Hawaii, Massachusetts, New Jersey, New York, Vermont, Washington, Wyoming
    - Texas 
      - The Old Way: Previously, Texas allowed students to take a hybrid course called "Personal Financial Literacy and Economics" to satisfy their Economics credit.
      - The New Mandate: [HB 27](https://www.texaspolicyresearch.com/bills/89th-legislature-hb-27/#:~:text=Instead%2C%20it%20requires%20students%20to,world%20geography%2C%20or%20world%20history.) splits these subjects. It creates a standalone half-credit (one semester) Personal Financial Literacy course that every student must pass to graduate.
      - Economics is No Longer "Strictly" Required: In a major shift, the new law replaces the old specific mandate for Economics. Students must now complete one credit chosen from a list that includes Economics, World Geography, or World History, but they must take the Personal Financial Literacy course
      - Who it affects:
        - Start Date: The requirement applies to students entering 9th Grade in the 2026-2027 school year.
        - First Graduating Class: The Class of 2030 will be the first group that cannot graduate without this specific credit.
      - General curriculum standards:
        - The course is not just "math." Under the new TEKS (Texas Essential Knowledge and Skills) standards, the standalone course must cover:
        - Post-Secondary Funding: Completing the FAFSA/TASFA and understanding student loans.
        - Tax Wedge: The difference between Gross vs. Net pay and how payroll taxes work.
        - Credit & Debt: Managing credit scores and understanding interest rates
      - Curriculum specifics - "home rule" philosophy:
        - Texas doesn't force every school to use the same book/curriculum, instead they issue TEKS (the standards) and the schools pick
        - Districts then choose their path: Buy the "State Adopted" materials (paid) or use "Open Education Resources" (free)
        - Specific Curricula (from HB 27 mandate)
          - Most Common
            - Ramsey Education (Foundations in Personal Finance)
              - Type: Paid / State Adopted.
              - The Vibe: This is the "Dave Ramsey" curriculum. It is heavy on behavioral psychology ("debt is bad," "cash is king").
              - Status: It was officially adopted under Proclamation 2024 and is fully aligned with Texas standards. Many districts (like McKinney ISD) have already signed multi-year contracts for this.
              - Format: Video-based lessons, printed workbooks, and a very structured "baby steps" approach.
            - Next Gen Personal Finance (NGPF)
              - Type: Free / Open Source (OER).
              - The Vibe: Modern, neutral, and highly digital. It focuses on current trends like "Gig Economy," "Crypto," and "Behavioral Economics" without the strict "no credit card" dogma of Ramsey.
              - Status: While not a "textbook" you buy, it is the most popular free curriculum in the US. NGPF has released specific "Texas TEKS Alignment" maps so teachers can use it to satisfy the law.
              - Format: Google Docs, online arcade games, and "move-based" activities.
          - Gamified & Digital Providers
            - Banzai!
              - The Vibe: "Choose Your Own Adventure." It uses a simulation where students have a job and bills, and they have to survive the month.
              - Texas Status: They have released specific mapping for the new HB 27 mandate. It is often sponsored by local banks (like TexasBank), making it free for schools.
            - EverFi
              - The Vibe: Corporate-sponsored digital modules. Very slick, high production value, but often shorter.
              - Texas Status: Fully aligned with TEKS and free for K-12 educators.
          - The Official "Proclamation 2024" List
            - Ramsey Education (Foundations in Personal Finance)
            - eDynamic Learning (Digital CTE courses)
            - Goodheart-Willcox (Publisher of standard textbooks)
            - iCEV (A major Texas-based CTE provider)
- Financial Knowledge Representation & Mapping (citations from Doug)
  - Knowledge Graph For Finance
    - Meegle (2025) [Knowledge Graphs](https://www.meegle.com/en_us/topics/knowledge-graphs/knowledge-graph-for-finance)
    - What is a Knowledge Graph for Finance?
      - A knowledge graph for finance is a structured representation of financial data that connects disparate data points through relationships. Unlike traditional databases, which store data in isolated tables, a knowledge graph organizes information in a network of nodes (entities) and edges (relationships).
