        - A node could represent a company, while an edge could signify its relationship with a supplier, investor, or regulatory body. This interconnected structure allows for a more intuitive and dynamic exploration of data.
      - Key Components of a Knowledge Graph for Finance
        - Entities: These are the core data points, such as companies, individuals, financial instruments, or transactions.
        - Relationships: The connections between entities, such as ownership, partnerships, or market influence.
        - Attributes: Additional information about entities or relationships, like a company's revenue or the terms of a financial contract.
        - Ontology: The schema or framework that defines how entities and relationships are categorized and connected.
        - Data Sources: The raw data feeding into the graph, which can include structured databases, unstructured text, and real-time feeds.
        - Graph Database: The underlying technology that stores and queries the knowledge graph, such as Neo4j or Amazon Neptune.
    - Benefits of Implementing Knowledge Graphs for Finance
      - Enhanced Data Connectivity
        - Knowledge graphs can connect disparate data sources
          - In finance, data often resides in silos — spread across departments, systems, and even external organizations. 
          - For instance, a financial institution can link customer transaction data with credit scores, social media activity, and market trends to gain a 360-degree view of customer behavior.
      - Improved Decision-Making
        - Knowledge graphs empower financial professionals to make better decisions by providing deeper insights into complex data.
          - For example, a portfolio manager can use a knowledge graph to analyze the interconnected risks of various investments, such as how a downturn in one industry might ripple through related sectors.
        - Knowledge graphs support advanced analytics and machine learning
          - By feeding structured, interconnected data into predictive models, organizations can forecast market trends, detect anomalies, and optimize strategies with greater accuracy.
    - How to Build a Robust Knowledge Graph for Finance
      - Tools and Technologies
        - Graph Databases: Neo4j, Amazon Neptune, and TigerGraph are popular choices for storing and querying knowledge graphs.
        - Data Integration Tools: Apache Kafka and Talend help aggregate data from multiple sources.
        - Natural Language Processing (NLP): Tools like spaCy and BERT extract entities and relationships from unstructured text, such as news articles or earnings reports.
        - Visualization Platforms: Tools like Graphistry and Cytoscape enable users to explore and analyze the graph visually.
      - Step-by-Step Guide to Knowledge Graph Creation
        - Define Objectives: Identify the specific problems you aim to solve, such as fraud detection or portfolio optimization.
        - Gather Data: Collect data from relevant sources, including internal databases, public datasets, and third-party providers.
        - Design Ontology: Create a schema that defines the types of entities, relationships, and attributes in your graph.
        - Build the Graph: Use a graph database to construct the knowledge graph, populating it with nodes, edges, and attributes.
        - Integrate Analytics: Incorporate machine learning models and analytics tools to extract insights from the graph.
        - Test and Iterate: Validate the graph's accuracy and usefulness, making adjustments as needed.
        - Deploy and Monitor: Implement the knowledge graph in your workflows and continuously monitor its performance.
      - Common Challenges in Knowledge Graph Development
        - Scalability Issues
          - Large graphs with millions of nodes and edges require robust infrastructure and efficient algorithms to maintain performance. 
            - Solutions include distributed graph databases and cloud-based platforms that can handle high computational loads.
        - Data Integration Problems
          - Financial data often comes in various formats and may contain inconsistencies or errors. 
            - Addressing these issues requires robust data cleaning and transformation processes, as well as tools that can handle both structured and unstructured data.
      - Industry-Specific Use Cases
        - Banking: Knowledge graphs are used for fraud detection by identifying unusual patterns in transaction networks.
        - Investment Management: Portfolio managers use knowledge graphs to analyze the interconnected risks and opportunities of various assets.
        - Regulatory Compliance: Financial institutions leverage knowledge graphs to track relationships and ensure compliance with anti-money laundering (AML) regulations.
      - Success Stories and Case Studies
        - JP Morgan Chase: The bank uses knowledge graphs to enhance its fraud detection capabilities, reducing false positives and improving detection rates.
        - BlackRock: The investment firm employs knowledge graphs to optimize portfolio management by analyzing market trends and asset relationships.
        - RegTech Startups: Companies like ComplyAdvantage use knowledge graphs to streamline regulatory compliance, saving time and reducing costs.
    - Future Trends in Knowledge Graphs for Finance
      - Emerging Technologies Impacting Knowledge Graphs
        - AI and Machine Learning: Advanced algorithms are making it easier to extract entities and relationships from unstructured data.
        - Blockchain: Decentralized ledgers can serve as a reliable data source for knowledge graphs, enhancing transparency and trust.
        - Quantum Computing: Although still in its infancy, quantum computing holds the potential to revolutionize graph analytics by solving complex problems faster.
      - Predictions for Knowledge Graph Evolution
        - Increased Adoption: As tools become more user-friendly, knowledge graphs will see broader adoption across the financial sector.
        - Real-Time Analytics: Future knowledge graphs will increasingly support real-time data analysis, enabling faster decision-making.
        - Integration with IoT: The Internet of Things (IoT) will provide new data streams for knowledge graphs, particularly in areas like supply chain finance.
    - FAQs About Knowledge Graphs for Finance
      - What industries benefit the most from knowledge graphs for finance?
        - Banking, investment management, and insurance benefit significantly from knowledge graphs due to their complex data ecosystems and need for advanced analytics.
      - How does a knowledge graph improve data management?
        - By integrating disparate data sources into a unified framework, making it easier to analyze and extract insights.
      - What are the best tools for building a knowledge graph for finance?
        - Neo4j, Amazon Neptune, and TigerGraph for graph databases, as well as NLP tools like spaCy for extracting entities and relationships.
      - Can small businesses use knowledge graphs effectively?
        - Yes - gain insights into customer behavior, optimize operations, and identify growth opportunities, often using cloud-based solutions to minimize costs.
      - What are the ethical considerations in knowledge graph development?
        - Data privacy, bias in data sources, and the potential misuse of insights for unethical purposes. Organizations must ensure compliance with regulations like GDPR and adopt transparent practices.
  - Major Points of the Financial Literacy Map
    - Committee for the Promotion of Financial Education (2015) [Central Council for Financial Services Information](https://www.shiruporuto.jp/e/consumer/pdf/major_points_of_the_financial_literacy_map.pdf)
    - Areas of Development for FinLit
      - Independence and Family Budget Management - goal evolves from establishing basic foundations to managing full household finances:
        - Students: Primary students focus on appreciating money and spending on needs vs. wants. Secondary and high school students learn to manage income/expenditure and make decisions based on a budget plan.
        - Adults: The focus shifts to managing household assets and liabilities, covering living expenses, and planning for retirement.
      - Life Planning - emphasizes the connection between work, earnings, and life goals:
        - Students: encouraged to understand the importance of working, envision their future occupation, and understand the funds required for major life events.
        - Adults: Adults engage in reviewing life plans regarding pensions and retirement, while senior citizens focus on effective use of leisure time and contributing to society
      - Financial Knowledge and Safety - covers transaction mechanics and consumer protection:
        - Scams and Contracts: avoiding trouble and scams, moving toward understanding contract laws and consumer responsibilities.
        - Economic Context: roles of money and finance, including the impact of inflation, deflation, and exchange rates.
      - Financial Products and Services - specific knowledge regarding financial tools:
        - Insurance:
          - Risk Management: students predict risks and avoid accidents, while adults focus on understanding social vs. private insurance and reviewing coverage based on family changes.
          - Coverage: highlights understanding compensation for accidents and the benefits of insurance for the elderly.
        - Loans and Credit:
          - Borrowing: younger students taught to avoid borrowing from friends, while older students learn about student loans and repayment mechanisms.
          - Credit Management: adults must understand housing loans, credit cards, and the consequences of payment delays on their credit information records.
        - Wealth Building:
          - Investment: covers the relationship between risk and return, the benefits of compound interest, and the importance of long-term, diversified investment.
          - Asset Management: adults encouraged to build wealth as part of their life plan and manage portfolios based on their age and risk tolerance.
      - Use of Outside Expertise - underscores the importance of seeking professional help:
        - Consultation: Learners taught to consult reliable adults or experts when in trouble and to identify appropriate organizations for advice when purchasing financial products.
  - Money Maps Cement Financial Statement Training
    - Business Literacy Institute (2024) [Customized Financial Training](https://www.business-literacy.com/bli-products-services/money-maps/)
    - >65% of population = visual learners, conceptual maps are powerful
    - Concepts Found in Our Money Maps
      - Financial concepts. The difference between cash and profit, the flow of the income statement, the money-making process, the sources and uses of cash, and the movement of investor dollars.
      - Life cycles. Customer life cycle, a day in the life of an employee, and ongoing manager responsibilities.
      - Business processes. The manufacturing process, service process, departmental process, product development, workflow, products and target customers, and pricing impact.
      - Big picture. The organization’s strategy, departmental relationships, products and services, and competitive and sales issues.
    - Standard Money Maps
      - Roads to Profitability Map
        - The Roads to Profitability map illustrates all the typical expenses that come out of revenue before profit is determined. Our research has shown that most people believe that a company retains 50% to 80% of revenues as profit.  
          - One glance at this map will show them the true picture. Your employees will immediately comprehend where the money comes from, where it goes, and how much is left over.
      - The Difference Between Profit and Cash Map
        - One of the problems with running a successful business is that most expenses have to be paid before cash comes in from the sale. Cash may not be received from customers for 60-90 days after a sale has been made, but many cash outflows need to be immediate (try telling an employee that they’ll get their paycheck in 90 days).
          - This is why cash is king – a concept that is well understood by those who pay the bills. But those who don’t see the cash inflows and outflows often don’t understand the difference between profit and cash. This map shows clearly how cash flows out weeks and months before any cash is received.
    - Note: this doesn't seem to be a reputable source for help building a financial literacy knowledge graph, as it is simply describing specific types of money maps businesses might use for their individual affairs, not a true map of interconnected financial topics. The source is trying to sell the viewer something (and it is something too niche).
  - Financial Life Mind Map: A Visual Approach to Financial Clarity
    - Schlotterbeck [Waterstreet Wealth Management](https://www.waterstreetwealth.com/post/financial-life-mind-map)
    - "I use what I call a Financial Life Mind Map. This tool isn't just a visual representation of your finances — it’s a powerful starting point for meaningful conversations and smarter decisions."
    - What Is a Financial Life Mind Map?
      - A snapshot of your entire financial household. The Financial Life Mind Map organizes all the major components of your financial life (assets, liabilities, income sources, insurance policies, goals, and more) into a simple, intuitive visual.
        - 
      - Instead of sifting through disconnected statements and documents, we can sit down and see everything in context. This clarity helps you understand where you are today and what steps you need to take to achieve your goals.
    - Building Your Mind Map: Using Asset-Map
      - Using Asset-Map, I can:
        - Quickly spot gaps in your plan.
        - Identify areas where tax or estate planning may need attention.
        - Track progress over time.
        - Have more productive and personalized conversations with you.
    - Note: not strictly a knowledge graph in the same way Aurum is, but this mind map is a helpful visual for individuals to see their finances in one place (the same goal the holistic predictive dashboard and A2/A3 have)
  - Financial Industry Business Ontology
    - What is FIBO [EDM Association](https://edmcouncil.org/financial-industry-business-ontology/)
    - Definition: FIBO is the "sets of things that are of interest in financial business applications" and the ways those things relate to one another.
    - Purpose: Its primary goal is to "give meaning to any data" (such as spreadsheets, relational databases, or XML documents) that describe the business of finance. It ensures that concepts are framed in a way that is "unambiguous and that is readable both by humans and machines".
    - Format: It is developed as an ontology in the Web Ontology Language (OWL) and is standardized by the Object Management Group (OMG).
    - Structure: The ontology is modularized into domains including Business Entities (legal persons, ownership), Securities (equities, bonds), Loans (contracts, credit, risk), and Derivatives.
    - Governance: It is authored and stewarded by the Enterprise Data Management (EDM) Council and reviewed by member firms to represent a consensus of the industry.
  - Conceptual Design of Financial Ontology
    - Dudycz (2015) [Proceedings of the Federated Conference on Computer Science and Information Systems](https://annals-csis.org/Volume_5/pliks/162.pdf)
    - Research-backed approach to creating a structured financial knowledge model (an ontology). The goal is to support Intelligent Dashboards for managers of Small and Medium Enterprises (SMEs) by enabling better data analysis, semantic search, and decision-making support.
    - The Problem: Why SMEs Need This
      - Lack of Resources: Unlike big companies, SMEs often lack access to strategic consultation or expensive financial expertise.
      - Higher Risk: SMEs operate in a more dynamic and risky environment where market changes have a heavier impact.
      - Data Overload: Managers need to analyze data from various systems (ERP, external sources), but standard Business Intelligence (BI) tools mostly offer static reports without explaining the "why" or the relationships between indicators
    - The Solution: Financial Ontology
      - Use an ontology (a semantic network of financial concepts) to upgrade standard BI systems.
      - Definition: The ontology acts as a "semantic layer" that describes relations between different economic topics, helping managers interpret financial indicators rather than just viewing raw numbers.
      - Six Key Areas: The knowledge model is split into six specific domains relevant to SME management:
        - Cash Flow at Risk
        - Comprehensive Risk Measurement
        - Early Warning Models
        - Credit Scoring
        - Financial Market
        - General Financial Knowledge
    - The Design Process
      - 5-stage method for building this financial ontology:
        - Goal Definition: Defining scope and constraints with SME managers.
        - Conceptualization: The most critical stage. It involves identifying concepts (topics), defining hierarchies (taxonomy), and modeling relationships (e.g., "Aggregate-Member") without automated tools.
        - Verification: Experts check the ontology for formal correctness and content accuracy.
        - Encoding: The model is coded into a formal language/editor (creating the digital version).
        - Validation & Evaluation: Testing if the ontology meets manager needs through use cases
    - Case Study: Return on Sales (ROS)
      - Return on Sales (ROS) indicator shows how the ontology works in practice.
      - Taxonomy: They classify ROS under "Profitability evaluation indicators," which is a subclass of Indicators.
      - Relationships: They map the formula ROS = Net Profit / Revenues from Sales. This creates a semantic link where Net Profit and Revenues are "members" that determine the aggregate ROS
      - Usage Scenario:
        - A manager sees ROS is down.
        - Using the semantic network, they trace the cause to Net Profit.
        - They dig deeper into Net Profit to find General Costs are too high.
        - The system helps them navigate from the high-level problem to the specific root cause using the ontology.
    - Functional Architecture
      - The proposed system integrates the ontology into a standard BI architecture:
        - Data Sources: ERP systems, internal databases, and external data feed into a Data Warehouse.
        - Semantic Layer: The "Financial Ontology" module sits alongside data exploration tools, allowing managers to view "focus and context" simultaneously.
        - Visual Interface: The interface uses Topic Maps to visualize connections, enabling managers to interactively browse financial knowledge.
  - Financial Knowledge Graph Dataset
    - (2025) [Emergent Mind](https://www.emergentmind.com/topics/financial-knowledge-graph-dataset) 
    - Financial Knowledge Graph Datasets are structured collections of entities, relationships, and metadata that model complex financial systems for applications like AML and fraud detection.
    - They integrate diverse data sources including transaction ledgers, corporate filings, and financial news, using advanced extraction and graph neural network techniques.
    - Benchmarks such as the Elliptic and DGraph datasets demonstrate efficacy in categorizing transactions and evaluating anomaly detection, guiding regulatory and risk assessments.
    - Source Overview 
      - This page is an aggregator and overview of datasets that model financial systems as networks of connected entities (like companies, transactions, and people). 
        - It defines these datasets as critical tools for tasks like fraud detection, anti-money laundering (AML), and risk assessment. The page highlights several specific datasets that serve as industry benchmarks:
          - Elliptic Data Set: A map of the Bitcoin payment network used to identify illicit activity. It consists of over 200,000 nodes (transactions) and edges (payment flows) to train AI to spot money laundering.
          - FinDKG (Financial Dynamic Knowledge Graph): A dataset built from financial news that tracks how relationships between entities change over time (e.g., "Company A sues Company B"). It is used to detect global market trends.
          - DGraph: A massive dynamic graph containing millions of nodes used to study fraudster behaviors in social and financial networks.
    - Note: seems vague and irrelevant for Aurum's specific knowledge graph
  - The Power of Ontologies and Knowledge Graphs: Practical Examples from the Financial Industry
    - Popov (2024) [GraphWise](https://graphwise.ai/blog/the-power-of-ontologies-and-knowledge-graphs-practical-examples-from-the-financial-industry/)
    - Why financial institutions use Ontologies (formal rulebooks for data) to power Knowledge Graphs (connected networks of facts).
      - Source argues that ontologies are superior to simple lists or taxonomies because they can define complex relationships and rules
      - Better Knowledge Representation:
        - Financial data is complex. An ontology can distinguish between different "legal entities" (a Partnership vs. a Public Corp) and map their relationships (JPMorgan -> regulated by -> Federal Reserve).
        - This is critical for "Company 360" views and risk analysis.
      - Data Interoperability:
        - Ontologies provide a common vocabulary that allows different banks and regulators to share data without confusion.
        - Example: The Bank of England and FCA use these standards to streamline reporting and ensure everyone defines "data" the same way.
      - Inference and Reasoning:
        - Ontology-powered systems can "think" (reason) to find hidden connections.
        - Example: A system can automatically infer that if Company A owns Company B, and Company B is sanctioned, then Company A poses a reputation risk, even if no human explicitly wrote that down.
      - Improved Search (Semantic Search):
        - Instead of keyword matching, users can ask complex questions.
        - Example: An investment advisor can search for optimal investment targets based on deep, connected criteria rather than just filtering a spreadsheet.
    - Usefulness for Aurum
      - Reasoning for Personalized Learning
        - Industry Use: If A owns B, and B is bad, then A is risky.
        - Literacy Use: If User fails "Interest Rates" quiz, and "Mortgages" depends on "Interest Rates," then "Mortgages" is currently too advanced.
        - Application: Your graph can automatically lock advanced topics or suggest prerequisites based on the relationships you defined, creating a smart tutor.
      - Abstractions Alignment for Diverse Users
        - Industry Use: Aligning technical data with business reports.
        - Literacy Use: Aligning content for different age groups.
        - Application: You can map a single concept to different labels in the graph:
          - Child View: "Borrowing Money"
          - Adult View: "Debt Instrument"
          - Retiree View: "Liability"
          - Your graph acts as a translator, serving the right language to the right user while keeping the underlying logic consistent.
      - Semantic Search for Life Events
        - Industry Use: Find companies with high ESG scores and low risk.
        - Literacy Use: Find advice for "I just lost my job."
        - Application: Users rarely search for technical terms like "Emergency Fund Allocation." They search for life problems. 
          - If the graph connects "Job Loss" -> requires -> "Emergency Fund," a user searching for their problem will automatically discover the solution, even if they didn't know the financial term for it.
  - Financial Report Knowledge Graphs (helpful)
    - Hoffman (2023) [Creative Commons](https://xbrlsite.azurewebsites.net/2021/Library/FinancialReportKnowledgeGraphs.pdf)
    - Financial Reports = Logical Systems: A financial report isn't just a PDF of numbers - it is a logical system made of "Terms" (Assets), "Structures" (Balance Sheet), "Associations" (Assets = Liabilities + Equity), and "Facts" (Cash is $5,000).
    - The DIKW Pyramid: The document distinguishes between Data (raw numbers), Information (numbers in context), and Knowledge (interpreting that information).
      - Helpful hierarchy for Aurum FLKG mastery levels
    - Graph Theory Basics:
      - Trees: Simple hierarchies (like a folder structure). Safe but limited because items only have one parent.
      - Directed Acyclic Graphs (DAGs): More powerful structures where items can have relationships to multiple things without creating infinite loops. The author argues financial reports are best represented this way.
      - The 7 Levels of Digital Reports: a maturity model for financial data:
        - Level 0: Paper/Clay Tablet (Not machine readable).
        - Level 1: PDF/HTML (Presentation only).
        - Level 3-4: XBRL with Taxonomies (Structured data).
        - Level 5-7: Fully verified, logic-enforced systems with blockchain anchoring.
    - Advice for Aurum
      - Adopt the "DAG" Structure over the "Tree" - Most financial literacy curriculums are "Trees" (Chapter 1: Banking -> Topic: Checking). This document argues that finance is actually a Directed Acyclic Graph (DAG).
        - A concept like "Mortgage" shouldn't just sit under "Debt." It is connected to "Housing" (Asset), "Interest Rates" (Economy), and "Credit Score" (Personal History).
        - Action: Build the graph so that a single node (like "Student Loan") connects to multiple parents ("Liabilities," "Education," "Government"). 
          - This helps users see the interconnectedness of finance.
      - Use the Logical Theory to Define Concepts - The document breaks down a financial model into five specific parts:
        - Terms: The vocabulary (e.g., "Compound Interest").
        - Structures: Where it fits (e.g., "Part of Retirement Planning").
        - Associations: How it relates (e.g., "Compound Interest" *increases* "Savings" but *increases* "Debt Cost").
        - Rules: The logic (e.g., "IF Credit Score < 600 THEN Interest Rate = High").
        - Facts: Real-world examples (e.g., "Average Credit Card APR is 24%").
      - The "Levels" as a User Progression System:
        - Level 0 (Chaos): User has receipts in a shoebox (Data).
        - Level 1 (Awareness): User tracks spending in a list (Information).
        - Level 3 (Logic): User understands "Assets = Liabilities + Equity" (Knowledge).
        - Level 5 (System): User has automated rules for savings/investing (Wisdom/System).
- Case Studies & Scientific Reviews
  - ==PRACTICAL IMPLEMENTATIONS/CURRENT FINLIT PROGRAMS==
    - JA Finance Park (==Carlin==)
      - **Carlin and Robinson (2012) [The Journal of Economic Education](https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/00220485.2012.686385?scroll=top&needAccess=true)**
      - Simulated range of expenses, how actions change based on prior FL exposure - **19 hr in-class instruction + 17 simulation kiosks** in the park where financial decisions are made
      - FLAW IN EXPERIMENT: no interest rate associated with savings, so students felt they were saving by paying off loans quickly (yielding higher monthly expenses). Also no control group that went to the park twice without treatment
      - Qualitative Findings
        - "**After receiving the classroom training, the fraction that students saved was nearly four times larger than their pre-treatment amount**. Post-treatment students paid off their home improvement faster and spent significantly less on clothing...They also had important **risk** differences. In the case of home improvement, there was no uncertainty surrounding future payments. In the case of health insurance, students were exposing themselves to potential income volatility by adopting percentage co-payment plans."
          - VIRTUAL JA FINANCE PARK SIMULATION [here](https://jausa.ja.org/programs/ja-finance-park-virtual)
            - 
        - Treated group was able to turn in a balanced budget in the timeframe more often than untreated group (difference = large (35%))
        - Treatment group spent more to pay off debt (home improvement), ate out less and bought clothing less
        - Components of JA Online Simulation
          - Select their own avatar and interests.
          - Review information about career clusters to select their career path and education.
          - Set up to three savings goals for future items such as homeownership or dream car.
          - Research, shop, and pay for various categories.
          - Make financial decisions across multiple life stages.
          - Choose their own future as it relates to marriage, children, and pets.
          - Track progress of their budgeting, spending, and saving through a financial health meter.
      - Summary of JA programs [here](https://jausa.ja.org/dA/14f80dc524/scopeandsequencefile/2023%20JA%20Scope%20and%20Sequence%20Real%20Life%20Immersion%20Learning%20Experiences.pdf?language_id=1) and [here](https://jausa.ja.org/dA/761f776dd3/scopeandsequencefile/2023%20JA%20Scope%20and%20Sequence%20K-5%20Offerings.pdf?language_id=1)
        - JA finance park entry level (grades 7-9, but could possibly be younger, curriculum does not look advanced) [here](https://jausa.ja.org/programs/ja-finance-park-entry-level)
        - JA Ourselves (Kinder) [here](https://jausa.ja.org/programs/ja-ourselves)
        - JA Our City 1.0 (3rd grade) [here](https://jausa.ja.org/programs/ja-our-city)
        - JA Hand In Hand Video Series (elementary) [here](https://jausa.ja.org/programs/supplements/ja-hand-in-hand-video-series)
        - JA $ave USA (lesson plans for all ages) [here](https://jausa.ja.org/programs/supplements/junior-achievement-save-usa)
      - Personal Thoughts/Judgements/Opinions:
        - Seems to be a VERY good approach because
          - Situated learning theory
          - Beautiful interface
          - Character customization
          - Large amounts of info presented in a way that doesn't overwhelm student
          - Research component + future planning
          - Comprehensive financial health tracking (possibly similar to Aurum's holistic predictive dashboard)
        - Questions/reservations about this platform - possible ways Aurum will be better
          - Multiplayer components?
          - Endogenous/stealth assessment?
          - AI predictive power?
          - Realism/depth of simulation?
            - Especially with regard to how concepts are taught and what financial calculators are used
            - Doesn't seem to teach basic taxes (filing, how they work), let alone tax optimization (deductions, strategic tax planning) 
          - Engagement components (like persistent world)?
          - Likely doesn't have financial psychology profiling, continuous novelty, tie-ins, protege effect (through multiplayer system or AI NPCs), or family financial socialization
    - Researching + Presenting (==Ehrlich==)
      - Ehrlich (2017) [Journal of Financial Education](https://www.jstor.org/stable/26573525)
      - Evaluation of a new 3-week-long FinLit curriculum (voluntary, fun, hands-on, intro, soft skills of original research and presentations) is more of a **creative/researching** and less of a consumer approach. Students tasked with **researching whether a given company was a good investment**.
      - Instruction provided in 
        - (1) Identifying needed info 
        - (2) Locating appropriate info sources 
        - (3) Critically evaluating sources and info 
        - (4) Synthesizing + integrating + applying info to research question 
        - (5) Presenting findings 
      - Results: students more confident, **scores rose by avg of 15.8%**. Effective in improving FinLit, but scores still low (likely because of difficult population and short class). Private school students outperformed public school students and had a larger improvement (likely due to additional parental involvement).
      - Personal Thoughts/Judgements/Opinions:
        - Researching, while still being extrinsically motivated, gives students the opportunity to exercise autonomy and explore 
        - Evaluating possible assessments causes students to use critical thinking
        - Shows how parental involvement is helpful for increasing scores
        - Criticisms/study limitations:
          - Not intrinsically motivating
          - Limited to researching a possible investment, not other areas of FinLit
          - No goal-setting/long-term thinking or planning
    - Flipped classroom model, final project = personal finance plan, visitor speaker = wealth manager. (==Martinez + Koh==)
      - Martinez (2016) [Journal of Financial Education](https://www.jstor.org/stable/90001154)
      - Cheng (2018) [Educational Technology Research and Development](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s11423-018-9633-7)
        - Meta-analysis of the flipped classroom model: **positive albeit small effect of flipped classroom.**
        - Asynchronous video lectures and practice problems as homework, and active, group-based problem-solving activities in the classroom
        - The effect sizes were not significantly moderated by student level, study duration, or publication type, but were significantly moderated by subject area
      - As Ellie (learning specialist phone call) said, children's attention span correlates to their age. Therefore, the flipped classroom model would likely only work with older students (7th-8th grade)
      - Financial literacy programs can especially benefit from implementing flipped classroom pedagogical approaches (Lage et al. [2000](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-981-10-0360-8_32?fromPaywallRec=false#ref-CR33)) where students engage in collaborative work with multimedia content before being taught in the classroom.
      - Results: **scores rose by avg of 10%**
    - SaveWise: design of FL education in the Netherlands (==Amagir==)
      - Amagir, Groot, Brink, Wilschut (2019) [Citizenship, Social and Economics Education](https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/epdf/10.1177/2047173419870053?src=getftr&utm_source=tfo&getft_integrator=tfo)
      - Program designed around goal setting theory, experiential learning, behavioral economics (pg 105): DOES LAY OUT INSTRUCTIONAL METHODS, testing different curriculum prototypes. 
      - Qualitative study, low-income students (8th and 9th grade)
      - Setting a single savings goal seems to be more effective than setting multiple goals (Soman and Zhao, 2011)
        - Setting promotional goals (e.g. saving for a certain purchase) seems to be more motivating than prevention-oriented goals (e.g. falling into debt) (Riitsalu, 2018)
      - The expert found prototype IV (most interactive and efficient) to be very innovating. He noted that the emphasis on achieving dreams by **setting a savings goal could be motivating, not only for high school students, but also for primary school children**. However, he emphasized that teachers should give students enough time to discuss money issues. We developed prototype V. The financial education program **SaveWise consists of 8 consecutive weekly lessons, each lasting 50 minutes. **
      - Students found the use of mood boards very helpful and interesting for making a savings goal, also developed autonomy
        - Students indicated that they learned how to set up a personal budget, construct a savings plan, and about different ways to spend less and earn an income.
      - Student participation encouraged because of activities and developing autonomy. Autonomy -> intrinsic motivation. Autonomy is also one of the three basic needs in Deci and Ryan’s (1991) self-determination theory of motivation (SDT)
      - Animated instructional videos helped: seen as relevant and easy to digest
      - Personal Thoughts/Judgements/Opinions:
        - Goal-setting is great for students' emotional involvement with material, long-term thinking, and intrinsic motivation (especially with regard to autonomy)
        - 50 minutes/week is a short enough time to retain engagement
        - Criticisms:
          - Seems to be limited to classroom, with the slight personal (out-of-classroom) involvement with goals
    - MoneyWise and Self-Efficacy: important psychological factors in learning (==Schaff==)
      - Schaff, Silva, et al (2020) [Journal of Financial Education](https://www.jstor.org/stable/48646932)
      - All 4 variables (financial literacy, acting behaviors, self-efficacy, planning behaviors) increased to a statistically significant degree. (pg 233). 
      - According to Bandura (1977), completing activities that are "subjectively threatening but in fact relatively safe" helps one's self-efficacy, an important part of their identity and willingness to learn/try new difficult things.
        - Efficacy comes from 4 main sources: 
          - (1) performance accomplishments [enactive]
          - (2) vicarious experience [vicarious]
          - (3) verbal persuasion [exhortive]
          - (4) emotional arousal [emotive]
        - The process of developing self-efficacy is similar to scaffolded learning because "as treatment progresses, the supplementary aids are withdrawn so the clients cope effectively unassisted. Self-directed mastery experiences are then arranged to reinforce a sense of personal efficacy" (197) [here](https://educational-innovation.sydney.edu.au/news/pdfs/Bandura%201977.pdf)
        - Vicarious experience = watching others accomplish something, thinking you could also accomplish it (not one of the strongest contributors to self-efficacy)
        - Verbal persuasion = persuaded through suggestion/placebo. Not as strong of a contributor to SE (self-efficacy) as personal accomplishments. Doesn't necessarily raise raise SE, but can help with outcome expectations.
        - Performance successes -> lower negative emotional arousal. Those who have a high perceived self-efficacy (ability to perform well) are less likely to feel anxiety.
      - MoneyWise case study shows students successfully improved their financial behaviors, and did not forget newly learned skills/behaviors 4-6 months after education. Participants were adults. 
    - FFFL (financial fitness for life) vs MCE (my classroom economy) [==multiple==]
      - Evaluation of MCE [here](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0272775718303054#sec0010)
      - FFFL (financial fitness for life) vs MCE = lecture-based financial literacy program in elementary schools
        - About the same time to implement and designed to complement core subjects instead of being a stand-alone FL class
        - Both are effective for improving measured knowledge for elementary-age students
      - There are other forms of experiential financial learning in schools, such as savings programs or student-run banks or credit unions (Batty, Collins, Odders-White, 2015, Choi, 2009, Johnson, Sherraden, 2007). 
      - FFFL
        - Curriculum found students significantly gained large positive effects **(gain of 11%)** in financial knowledge (d = 0.77), even after a year. MCE is imbedded in other classroom activities, and therefore requires no formal instruction time. [here](https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/full/10.1177/2047173417719555)
        - Material = future oriented, content based on national and state standards regarding economics, personal finance, mathematics, and language arts. Instructional materials range from K-12. Valid tests developed to measure students’ knowledge and understanding of personal finance concepts, but doesn't assess financial attitudes and behavior. [here](https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/epdf/10.1177/2047173417719555?src=getftr&utm_source=tfo&getft_integrator=tfo)
      - FFFL shows strong financial knowledge gains (0.77) and measured after 1 year, but reported no financial behavior changes. MCE also reports financial knowledge gains (not quantified), while also reporting financial behavior gains, [pg 66](https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/epdf/10.1177/2047173417719555?src=getftr&utm_source=tfo&getft_integrator=tfo)
        - Students who participated in the **MyCE had 6% higher scores** in financial knowledge than the control group. Another program that uses hands-on activities to reinforce students’ understanding through application and practice, and addresses concepts in a developmental appropriate manner is the FFFL curriculum
        - FFFL program comes with a parent guide for each grade level
      -  A combination of playing the SMG (stock market game) with content lessons has proved to be effective (d = 0.79) in further deepening the financial knowledge (Harter and Harter, 2010) [pg 73](https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/epdf/10.1177/2047173417719555?src=getftr&utm_source=tfo&getft_integrator=tfo)
      - Personal Thoughts/Judgements/Opinions:
        - Both appear to be great improvements for experiential learning in traditional classrooms
        - Time-efficient
        - Seems to help with intrinsic motivation
        - Criticisms/limitations:
          - Lecture-based
          - Unable to assess financial attitudes/behavior
    - Review of different FL programs for children: very good broad comparison of several FL programs and efficacy (==Amagir==)
      - Amagir, Groot, Brink, Wilschut (2018) [SageJournals](https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/epdf/10.1177/2047173417719555?src=getftr&utm_source=tfo&getft_integrator=tfo)
      - This study focuses on 4 categories: 
        - (1) making thoughtful, well-informed financial decisions in long + short run 
        - (2) reinforce students' transferrable skills
        - (3) invest in one's human capital [earn/manage/save] 
        - (4) problem-solving skills
      - **FFFL** helps with (1) (4), the FFFL test does not assess financial attitudes and behavior differences were large with a program that used 17 lessons [after year 1 (d = 0.505) and year 2 (d = 0.561)]
        - Compared to effect sizes in a shorter intervention (8 lessons) to teach the FFFL curriculum were small (d = 0.221; 0.267) 
        - In contrast, an intensive program in Brazil (between 72 and 144 hours in 1.5 years) resulted in a significant but small improvement of students’ knowledge levels (d = 0.25). 
      - **Aflatoun Program**: combines a savings program with social education components for children aged 3–18 years executed in 116 countries
      - **Honest Money Box (HMB)** continues as savings clubs after completion of the curriculum
      - Milwaukee Urban League Academy of Business and Economics (**MULABE**) focuses on (4) MULABE schools offer a customized economic and personal finance curriculum (~1/3 of the school curriculum) with the aim of teaching students basic skills such as banking, personal budgeting, and problem-solving, as well as more advanced business and finance skills
        - Long and intensive, resulted in considerable effects on basic economic knowledge (d = 0.981) and personal finance (d = 1.020) (Schug and Hagedorn, 2004). 
      - Differences seen in effect sizes of playing the Stock Market Game, with or without content lessons: small effects in students’ financial knowledge without lessons, ranging from 0.39 to 0.45 (Hinojosa et al., 2010),and a larger effect (d = 0.79) when combined with seven content lessons
      - For intrinsic motivation: (1) “relatedness” (the need to have a close and friendly relationship with others), (2) “autonomy,” and (3) “competence.” These three basic needs are part of the Self-Determination Theory (SDT)
      - Elementary school = hands-on pedagogy, grade 7-12 = relevance of the topic
      - "Another limitation is that the design principles of programs do not receive sufficient attention. Specifically, several studies do not explicitly describe the teaching methods." 
      - Personal Thoughts/Judgements/Opinions:
        - Good for emphasis on intrinsic motivation and SDT
        - Shows how long/intensive programs like MULABE are very beneficial
        - Criticisms/limitations:
          - "Another limitation is that the design principles of programs do not receive sufficient attention. Specifically, several studies do not explicitly describe the teaching methods." 
    - Five Steps Program - 5 Basic Financial Planning Concepts Related to Retirement (==Heinberg==)
      - Heinberg (2015) [Oxford Review of Economic Policy](https://academic.oup.com/oxrep/article/30/4/697/360418?login=true) 
        - Interesting references
      - Quantifying the relative impact of delivering the program through video and narrative formats
      - Focused on (i) compound interest; (ii) inflation; (iii) risk diversification; (iv) tax treatment of retirement savings vehicles; and (v) employer matches of defined contribution savings plans
      - Designing content (increased accessibility and self-efficacy)
        - The 5 financial concepts were embedded in 5 simple, short **stories** that describe the concepts verbally and present the benefits of taking action. 
          - Each story explains elements of one of the financial principles, consists of dialogue between two people, and is written to convey information in a relatively lighthearted, positive manner.
      - Some expressed keener interest in the videos as they did not require the work of reading, while others noted that they preferred having access to both formats. **No one argued for written narratives alone**
      - Formats have significant effects on other psychological levers of behavioral change: effects on self-efficacy are significantly higher when videos are used, which ultimately influences knowledge acquisition
      - Personal Thoughts/Judgements/Opinions:
        - Good that program includes some info on taxes
        - Helpful to convey ideas through narrative, as it is approachable + relatable, helps with emotional involvement, also helps that narratives are brief and digestible 
        - Good to use videos, more interesting than lectures (or written narratives)
        - Criticisms/limitations:
          - Seems to lack student involvement, quantitative (and qualitative) improvements and assessment measures 
          - Niche focus on retirement (good for specific audience but not enough FinLit for a comprehensive education)
    - Approaches to Teaching Financial Literacy: Evidence-Based Practices in Singapore Schools (==Koh==) [~] (*)
      - Koh (2016) [International Handbook of Financial Literacy](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-981-10-0360-8_32?fromPaywallRec=false)
      - This chapter describes the evolution of a large-scale financial literacy program in Singapore that has been formulated and implemented by the Financial Literacy Hub for Teachers since 2007
        - No separate subject - The Hub has created and developed numerous **financial literacy **lesson plans **woven into teaching mathematics, social studies or other subjects**
          - A stand-alone program is Breakfast with Dad, which is built upon parent–child bonding and joint budgeting activities
        - Covered topics include budgeting, risk management and insurance, savings and investments, smart shopping, income and careers, and money management
      - Short FL courses have had a high impact (Fox et al. [2005](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-981-10-0360-8_32?fromPaywallRec=false#ref-CR14))
      - Mandell and Klein study (FL = **ineffective**) concluded that this was **due to lack of motivation** (content not immediately relevant to age group: investment + retirement strategies)
      - **When** the topics taught are **adapted to students’ interests**, financial literacy scores show marked **increase** (Bowen and Jones [2006](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-981-10-0360-8_32?fromPaywallRec=false#ref-CR6)). Financial education programs therefore should strive not for maximal syllabus coverage but for helping tackle real challenges in making day-to-day monetary decisions
      - Self-control is a crucial determinant of general success and specific financial attitudes, can be taught from a young age
      - **FL programs can especially benefit from implementing flipped classroom pedagogical approaches** (Lage et al. [2000](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-981-10-0360-8_32?fromPaywallRec=false#ref-CR33)) where students engage in collaborative work with multimedia content **before** being taught in the classroom (but likely less helpful to younger students - need less screen time and have shorter attention span)
        - As FL instruction emphasizes highly contextualized real-life problem solving, digital games are a natural choice for developing the necessary skills and habits in students.
      - For authentic learning in primary schools, the mShopper mobile application (which encourages collaborative learning) was designed by the Hub. The **students learn how to spend within their budget and make prudent consumer choices by not succumbing to common marketing ploys**. Thus, gamification has been successfully used in the Hub’s programs to help students acquire the necessary skills and values in realistic settings.
        - The concept of a mobile learning trail has proven to be very successful, and is now used in programs that target all levels, from primary to secondary, as well as tertiary students. The most **recent development is the SG50 Entrepreneurs Trail** that is designed for primary and secondary school students
      - Personal Thoughts/Judgements/Opinions:
        - Good to include family components (to facilitate FFS)
        - Short course length but high impact
        - Relevant to student interests - very important, scores increase
        - Helpful implementation of an experiential game
      - Criticisms/limitations:
        - Article doesn't specify actual teaching methods
        - FinLit tends to be less effective when integrated within existing subjects instead of being taught as a standalone course
    - Can Students Learn Econ and Personal Finance in a Specialized Elementary School? (==Posnanski==) (~) 
      - Posnanski (2007) [ERIC](https://files.eric.ed.gov/fulltext/EJ782147.pdf#page=3.58)
      - [MULABE PROGRAM = SUCCESS]
      - Examines 3 elementary schools in an urban area. **What differentiates the schools are the emphases each school places on financial and economics education**. Study measured changes in survey responses of 5th/6th grade students, measured over the course of 1 academic year. 
      - General goal of this evaluation was to **examine gains in student scores**/improvements in student knowledge as measured during the course of an academic year
      - Different schools
        - (1) **MULABE** (Milwaukee Urban League Academy of Business and Economics): charter school in 4th year of operation. The general mission of the school is to provide a strong academic program with emphasis on reading, mathematics, science, and social studies. The specialized mission of the school is to **prepare students who have extraordinary knowledge and skills in business, economics, and personal finance**. Students at MULABE experience a customized economic and personal finance curriculum on a daily basis throughout the school year
          - Includes materials published by the National Council on Economic Education (NCEE) (i.e. Master Curriculum Guides and Financial Fitness for Life). In the primary and elementary grades students learn basic skills such as basic banking, personal budgeting, and problem solving. In the upper grades students study more advanced business and finance topics, including basic investing and personal financial planning, economics, finance, and marketing
        - (2) **SCHOOL A**: also has a specialized charter mission, in this case to prepare students in the sciences. **The science focus of the school permeates the curriculum**, with additional emphasis on language arts, mathematics, and social stUdies.Although School A specializes in science, the social studies curriculum used at ,the school is a typical standards-based program. Students at School A receive **roughly two hours per week of instruction in the social studies**.
        - (3) **SCHOOL B**: no specialized content area focus. Academic program stresses language arts, mathematics, science, and social studies. Students at School B also receive **roughly two hours per week of instruction in social studies.**
      - Methodology
        - Pre and post-test for both BET and PFT (basic economics test and personal finance test), 182 5th/6th graders between the 3 schools
      - Results
        - Both MULABE and School B showed significant improvements in BET scores over time. Further, the pairwise comparison revealed MULABE students performed better at both the beginning and end of the school year than did the students in the other schools tested
        - MULABE students ended up performing better over the course of the year; however, they did not perform better than the other schools on the pre-test at the start of the school year.
          - Improvement is VERY significant (from graph)
        - MULABE:
          - BET Post-Test Score: Higher than both School A and School B.
          - PFT Post-Test Score: Higher than both School A and School B.
        - School A:
          - Did not show statistically significant improvements in BET or PFT scores.
        - School B:
          - BET Mean difference: 1.65 points (from pre-test to post-test).
          - PFT Mean difference: 0.714 points (not statistically significant).
      - Personal Thoughts/Judgements/Opinions:
        - Good that MULABE course is challenging, consistent, and customized
        - This study better follows the structure of a controlled experiment than some other studies/papers, with control groups and 
        - Criticisms/limitations:
          - Study doesn't lay out exact MULABE curriculum for personal finance courses
    - An Efficacy Study of the Open Hands Finance Education Program (==Coggin==)
      - Coggin (2023) [Union University ProQuest Dissertations & Theses](https://www.proquest.com/docview/2898764763/fulltextPDF/E3DDD9582FB64CC6PQ/20?accountid=7118&sourcetype=Dissertations%20&%20Theses)
      - Christian program that weaves Biblical parables and wisdom on money-management throughout the curriculum
      - The Open Hands Financial Education Program is a 6-week peer-led curriculum targeted at Christian college juniors and seniors. It covers budgeting, saving, debt, investing, and financial decision-making, integrating Biblical teachings on stewardship, wisdom, and generosity. **Participants receive $100 from donors to open a Roth IRA, emphasizing practical application**
      - A statistically significant improvement in financial literacy scores among participants. The experimental group improved their posttest scores by an average of 2.19 correct answers on a 13-question test compared to the control group (F = 6.073, p = .016).
        - On average, the test scores improved by approximately 16.85%.
      - Peer-led approach: Sessions were conducted in small groups led by trained student leaders, creating a relatable and engaging learning environment. This aligns with Bandura’s social learning theory (SLT), which emphasizes the role of peers in influencing behavior
      - Personal Thoughts/Judgements/Opinions:
        - Peer-led nature of curriculum seems to help engagement, as students see activities as more collaborative, adheres to SLT
        - Good incorporation of incentives: Providing $100 for participants to start a Roth IRA not only reinforced the importance of saving but also offered an immediate opportunity to apply learned concepts, helping with experiential learning and student engagement
        - Significant score increase
        - Criticisms/limitations:
          - Study did not elaborate further on lesson structure/specifics
    - The Impact of Financial Education for Children: Evidence from an Experiment in China (==Zhou==) (*)
      - Zhou (2023) [The Asia-Pacific Education Researcher](https://aflatoun.org/latest/news/the-impact-of-financial-education-for-children-evidence-from-an-experiment-in-china/)
      - This study carries out a paired-design experiment to evaluate the effectiveness of the Aflatoun Child Social & Financial Education program, which is initiated by the International Children’s Savings Foundation and covers more than 100 countries and millions of children and adolescents. The participants are students in grades 4–5 of primary schools in China
      - Finds that the Aflatoun curriculum can significantly improve children’s behaviors in regular saving, rational consumption, and social participation. At the same time, unintended adverse negative effects are spotted in terms of children’s attitudes and personality development
      - Other studies of similar curricula:
        - Harter and Harter (2009) and Chen and Heath (2012) have found significant gains in financial knowledge for elementary students who have received instruction in the “Financial Fitness for Life” curriculum
        - Sherraden et al. (2011) examines a four-year school-based financial education and savings program called “I Can Save” (ICS) and have found that children who participated have a significantly higher level of financial knowledge than those who do not
        - Sari and Fatimah (2017) evaluate the ‘‘Financial Intelligence Curriculum’’ program designed for elementary school students and have found that the treatment group who received financial education have improved financial knowledge compared to the control group. Some studies also show that financial education can be effective in altering children’s attitudes or behaviors related to their consumption and saving patterns.
        - Alan and Ertac (2018) show that a training program of financial awareness and savings aimed at improving the ability to imagine future selves has fostered greater levels of patience among 3rd and 4th graders in Turkey
        - Walstad et al. (2017) state that “experiential learning” is an important component of all student learning, particularly as it relates to financial education.
        - Kaiser and Menkhoff (2022) conduct a randomized experiment to study the effects of two financial education interventions, "active learning" and traditional "lecturing". Their results show that active learning has a positive effect on savings and investment outcomes, in contrast to small or zero effects for lecturing. These findings suggest that delivering approach can play an important role in the effectiveness of financial education programs
      - The main questions in this study are: what impact does Aflatoun intervention have on child development, i.e., their financial behavior, attitudes, and personality development? Is there heterogeneity in these effects by region and gender?
      - This effectiveness could be partly attributed to Aflatoun’s teaching methods, "experiential learning" (i.e., group learning, theme games, Aflatoun clubs, off-campus field study), which have also been proposed as an important component for improving the efficacy of school-based programs for children by Amagir et al. (2018) and Dare et al. (2020)
      - Personal Thoughts/Judgements/Opinions:
        - Shows importance of experiential learning
        - Paired-design experiment is an improvement on unpaired (which is easier to carry out but produces less accurate results)
        - Criticisms/limitations:
          - The Aflatoun intervention has no positive impact on children’s attitudes and personalities, even showing unintended negative effects on their money attitude and emotional stability
          - Doesn't specify percentage score improvements or particular lesson designs for this curriculum
    - Increasing Youth Financial Capability: An Evaluation of the MyPath Savings Initiative (==Loke==) (*) (~)
      - Loke (2015) [Journal of Consumer Affairs](https://www.academia.edu/105786926/Increasing_Youth_Financial_Capability_An_Evaluation_of_the_MyPath_Savings_Initiative)
      - PROGRAM DESIGN:
        - Impact of the MyPath Savings pilot on 275 economically disadvantaged youth participating in a youth development and employment program. MyPath Savings targets youth earning their first paycheck — a critical “teachable moment” to promote savings and connect youth with mainstream financial products. **The results indicate that MyPath Savings is highly relevant to participants’ needs**. In addition, youth experienced significant increases in financial knowledge, financial self-efficacy, and the frequency with which positive financial behaviors were carried out. Participants also saved an average of $507 through MyPath Savings.
          - The research seeks to understand whether the MyPath Savings initiative results in changes in financial knowledge, attitudes, and self-efficacy, as well as behavioral changes related to saving, budgeting, and meeting a financial goal
          - Hands-on, experiential approach, with an emphasis on peer learning and support
      - OTHER PROGRAMS/WHAT IS SUCCESSFUL:
        - Programs offer financial education paired with a savings account in a mainstream financial institution so that participants can apply financial concepts and increase their familiarity with financial institutions. 
          - Example: "bank at school” initiative that provides elementary school-aged youth with classroom-based financial education and the opportunity to open savings accounts and make deposits at school. 
        - Programs such as I Can Save (ICS) have reported promising results using a financial capability model. Children who participated in ICS, which provided financial education, savings accounts, and matches, scored significantly higher on financial fitness tests than comparison groups, regardless of parent education and income (Sherraden et al. 2011)
        - While the research findings on the outcomes of financial education programs have been mixed, programs that have been found to be effective are those which: are** targeted and narrowly focused **(Klinge, Harper, and Vaziri 1974); demonstrate **relevance**, engage participants’ **motivation**, and **capitalize on teachable moments** (Hathaway and Khatiwada 2008; McCormick 2008); engage participants with **real-world financial products and services** (Land and Russell 1996; McCormick 2008); and **leverage incentives** and principles from behavioral economics (Hernandez 2011)
      - MyPath Savings incorporates the following 4 principles in the program design: (1) timely, **relevant**, peer-developed intervention for youth workers; (2) **engagement** with mainstream financial **products** and services; (3) leveraging the principles of **behavioral economics**; and (4) **employing best practices** in youth development.
        - (1) Importance of this **just-in-time education** is underscored by Lewis Mandell, regarded as one of the founders of the modern financial literacy movement, who was quoted in explaining the lack of effectiveness of the mainstream financial literacy paradigm, that “Financial education doesn’t work when it’s given in advance of when the consumer needs it” (Olen 2014).
          - MyPath Savings is layered on existing youth development and employment programs and specifically focuses on participants who are receiving their first paychecks. The program capitalizes on this teachable moment by imparting just-in-time and relevant money management knowledge and skills to prepare youth to manage their new income stream wisely through **three 90-minute financial education workshops**. The workshops were conducted approximately 2 months apart over a 6 month period.
        - (2) Research also suggests that when people have **access to mainstream saving structures**, such as savings accounts, pension plans, and IRAs, they are more likely to save at higher rates than those who lack such access (Sherraden, Schreiner, and Beverly 2003)
          - A partnership with the credit union allows the youth to be sole owners of their savings accounts, without their parents as co-signatories. Youth participants thus learn to manage their accounts in a real-world setting, accessing their money via online banking, ATMs, and in-person transactions, putting into practice the concepts learned through MyPath Savings.
        - (3) **Minimizing barriers**, establishing reasonable defaults, incorporating meaningful incentives
          - For example, the paperwork for opening a savings account at the partner credit union is simplified and included in the enrollment package for the youth employment program; thus, even before the youth have begun to participate in the employment program (and thus MyPath Savings). The $5 minimum deposit also waived. 
        - (4) MyPath Savings provides youth with the opportunity to **exercise autonomy** and build various leadership and communication skills in the development and delivery of the financial education curriculum. Youth also exercise autonomy when they set their own personal savings goal amount and purpose, unlike other youth financial capability programs where the goals and purposes are pre-determined.
      - Methods/demographics:
        - Participants ranged from 14 to 18 years, with the mean age being approximately 15 years old. Approximately 94% of participants were in the 9th or 10th grade
      - Results:
        - The appropriateness of the financial education was assessed by three variables: the extent to which youth found the various workshops to be **relevant**, to be **interesting**, and the extent to which they felt the **learned** from the workshops
        - Participants, on a 3-point scale, generally found that workshops on the benefits of **saving**, **budgeting**, and **goal setting** to be most appropriate to their life stage and current developmental needs. This was followed by the workshops on providing information on managing their savings account, the different types of financial institutions, the power of compound interest, and the workshop on the different types of financial products and services.
        - On average, each youth had accumulated $734 and saved $507 net of savings matches and the initial deposit. Youth also saved 86% of their goal, on average. 46% of youth fully met their savings goal.
        - Through this initiative, youth **participants’ knowledge of various financial topics significantly increased after each financial education workshop**, as measured by their scores on pre- and post-test assessments. At the end of MyPath Savings, participants also expressed a higher level of financial self-efficacy and confidence in their ability to effectively carry out a variety of financial tasks, and reported significant increases in the frequency in which they practiced positive financial behaviors
        - MyPath Savings built on **teachable moments** (earning first paychecks) + helped participants understand why they were saving/budgeting etc. 
        - Having youth peer-trainers lead the sessions, rather than adult teachers, further increases the level of youth engagement. Feedback gathered from focus groups indicated that participants were able to relate better to the peer trainers who were having the same life experiences.
        - Despite being from disadvantaged backgrounds, **participants were able to save $507** on average over a 6-month period. This **compares to an average of $138** saved over the same period by youth at one of the program partner sites of the Savings for Education, Entrepreneurship and Downpayment (SEED) research initiative, who were of similar ages and backgrounds (Mason et al. 2009).
          - Having all participants **open savings accounts** in their names at a financial institution, as well as the use of **direct deposits, match caps, financial incentives, and peer support** may have contributed to the higher savings performance seen in MyPath Savings as compared to other financial capability programs.
        - Unlike in other programs, where the direct deposit feature is optional,** every youth in MyPath Savings determines the amount they would like to direct deposit** at each pay period during one of the earlier financial education workshops, providing yet another teachable moment. (percentage of paycheck immediately goes to savings)
        - In MyPath Savings, participants are provided a 2-to-1 savings match on the first $30 saved for their first four deposits of the initiative. (given twice as much as they put into savings)
        - Youth in MyPath Savings **freely shared their savings goals** as well as their progress toward achieving their goals with each other in their group meetings.
        - Significant score improvement of 24% (43% -> 62%) on average
          - While there was a 24-point gain in financial literacy after the session on goal-setting and budgeting, the sessions on financial products and institutions saw more modest gains (only 8 points)
        - Savings Matches May Not Need to Be Generous:
          - Surprising Insight: While the 2-to-1 match was well-received, participants indicated they would have been just as motivated with a lower match rate if the savings cap were higher or extended longer.
          - "Participants were unanimous in stating that this savings match was the biggest factor in encouraging them to start saving." [here](https://www.academia.edu/105786938/My_Path_An_Innovative_Initiative_to_Increase_Financial_Capability_Among_Economically_Vulnerable_Youth)
      - Personal Thoughts/Judgements/Opinions:
        - Very helpful to focus on teachable moments for maximized personal relevance to the student
        - Comprehensive goal: important to leverage students' psychology to best teach them, good that program is targeted, demonstrates relevance, motivating, prioritizes teachable moments, is connected to the outside world (through real financial products and services), and leverages incentives
        - Good to minimize barriers and maximize autonomy (both contribute to self-efficacy)
        - Goal-setting very good for practicing delayed gratification
        - Great for peers to share their goals, helps with communal aspect
        - Criticisms/limitations:
          - 90 minute workshops are too long and too far spread out
    - Financial Capability in Children: Effects of Participation in a School-Based Financial Education and Savings Program: ICS (==Sharraden==) (~)
      - Sherraden (2011) [Day Care and Early Education](https://www.academia.edu/4541879/Financial_Capability_in_Children_Effects_of_Participation_in_a_School_Based_Financial_Education_and_Savings_Program)
      - This study examines ICS (I Can Save), an innovative four-year school-based financial education and savings program
        - ICS was created to explore the effects of matched savings and financial education on elementary school students.
        - ICS children included classroom-based curricula and a once-a-week voluntary after-school ‘‘I Can Save’’ Club during the academic year. The goals for financial education were to 
          - (a) increase knowledge of basic financial and economic principles
          - (b) learn how to earn, manage, and save money
          - (c) build aspirations and expectations for post-secondary education and training
          - In-school financial education included one half-hour lesson per week from Financial Fitness for Life or Wise Pockets World
        - ICS Club met for 1h after school most weeks throughout the 4 years. Club activities included games, refreshments, and, beginning in year 2, monthly ﬁeld trips to deposit savings in the bank. Children received one dollar for attending each weekly ICS Club session.
        - By elementary school age, children are able to grasp basic economic concepts such as scarcity, production, specialization, consumption, saving, distribution, demand/supply, business, money, and bartering
      - RESULTS:
        - Elementary school children who participated in ICS scored signiﬁcantly higher on a ﬁnancial literacy test taken in 4th grade than comparison group students in the same school, regardless of parent education and income.
          - Those not involved in the study had an average score of 47.8%.** Those participating in ICS (60.4%) scored signiﬁcantly higher than the comparison group (49.9%)**
          - The correlation between ICS Club participation (deﬁned as number of sessions attended across 4 years) and FFFL test scores is not significant.
          - When researchers asked which ICS activities students liked best, 2nd graders reported that their favorite activities were **games** (40%), followed by **saving** (28%), snacks (26%), and bank visits (11%). 
          - By 4th grade, students said they liked **saving** the best (48%), followed by **games** (28%), snacks (24%), and bank visits (14%).
      - OTHER PROGRAMS:
        - Programs like Kinder-economy (Kourilsky 1977): 5 and 6 year olds in four classrooms who participated in Kinder-Economy, shows significantly greater mastery in a post-assessment of basic economic concepts, compared to students in 4 control classrooms
        - Reading story books: positive impact on the 15 treatment children’s financial literacy from reading storybooks about money compared to 16 controls (Grody et al. 2008).
        - Money Savvy Kids: Each child received a piggy bank with four slots (saving, spending, investing, and donating) and eight ﬁnancial lessons. Analysis of pre- and post-tests shows statistically signiﬁcant improvements in knowledge and attitudes (Schug and Hagedorn 2005)
      - According to students, the lessons in ICS Club were more ‘‘fun’’ (experiential) than classroom lessons. **Children expressed a clear preference for experiential learning and, as other studies also show, it may have increased learning and retention among ICS children** (Laney 1989; Kourilsky 1977; Kourilsky and Carlson 1996)
      - Children may have been especially motivated to learn because of the savings account and visits to the bank. **Unlike most school-based financial education, which uses games and simulations, children in ICS had access to real financial services**. They opened a savings account, visited the bank regularly, made savings deposits, and monitored their savings.
      - Personal Thoughts/Judgements/Opinions:
        - Significant score improvement
        - Experiential + fun, increased student enjoyment and performance
        - Very good that students had access to real financial services 
        - At a young age, students gained understanding of more complex financial concepts 
        - Criticisms/limitations:
          - Voluntary after-school clubs shown to not be as effective
    - Changes in Learning Outcomes of Students Participating in Problem-Based Learning for the First Time: A Case Study of a Financial Management Course (==Lee==)
      - Lee (2024) [Asia-Pacific Education Researcher](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s40299-024-00873-y)
      - Explore how first time PBL students can improve their learning outcomes
      - Results
        - Students with higher academic performance improved their critical thinking/problem solving skills
        - Students with lower academic performance = PBL less effective
      - Van Berkel and Schmidt ([2000](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s40299-024-00873-y#ref-CR58)) pointed out that students with less knowledge are more inclined to actively participate in group meetings to compensate for their lack of knowledge.
      - Study participants were undergraduate students
      - Problem quality was assessed using 8 items, including whether the problem was clearly stated, stimulated interest in learning, was conducive to exploratory learning, facilitated in-depth discussions, had educational value, and contributed to the development of critical thinking
      - Personal Thoughts/Judgements/Opinions:
        - Problems seemed to facilitate increased student involvement (as opposed to lecturing and worksheets)
        - Criticisms/limitations:
          - Mixed results - helpful for high-performance students and less effective for low performers
    - Understanding Taxes: Lesson 4B (==FRE==)
      - Lesson plan for 9th-12th grade from [Federal Reserve Education](https://www.federalreserveeducation.org/teaching-resources/personal-finance/earning-income/understanding-taxes-lesson-4b)
      - More detailed description [here](https://www.federalreserveeducation.org/resources/lessons/lesson--4b-understanding-taxes.pdf)
      - Students will be able to
        - Define and use a simple tax formula (tax base × tax rate) to determine the amount of taxes paid
        - Explain and recognize tax structures
        - Describe the most common types of taxes — income, payroll, sales, and property
        - Calculate a household’s total taxes and net income based on that household’s gross income and expenditures
      - Covered topics
        - Gross income
        - Net income (disposable income)
        - Tax base
        - Tax rate
        - Tax structures (progressive, proportional, and regressive)
        - Types of taxes (income, payroll, sales, and property)
      - Personal Thoughts/Judgements/Opinions:
        - Good intro to taxes, very basic concepts that some people have to learn while they file taxes the first time - good to familiarize before then so they are less confused/frustrated
        - Criticisms/limitations:
          - Lesson plan is an example of teaching tax compliance over tax strategy
            - Focuses on tax terminology, types of taxes, tax structures, and calculating taxes owed. 
            - Very surface level, learning what taxes are, not how to optimize them. 
              - Omits strategic decision-making, tax planning, simulation and behavioral economics components
            - Could likely be understood by a middle school student (if not younger, like a 5th-6th grader)
    - FinAI: Gamified Finance Learning Platform (==Deshmukh==)
      - Deshmukh (2025) [Journal of Information Systems Engineering and Management](https://jisem-journal.com/index.php/journal/article/view/685/235)
      - Intro
        - FinAI = a software platform designed to simplify complex financial concepts through gamification
        - Tech stack/features
          - Frontend: React.js
          - Backend: Node.js
          - Database: Neon DB
          - Hosting: Vercel
          - Mobile Interface: Developed using Android Studio, enabling access on both web and mobile platforms
        - Gamified elements
          - The platform's user interface is likened to the Duolingo mobile application's home page. Learning is structured through a gamified system:
          - Courses: Users can select from various financial knowledge courses, including Share Market, Mutual Funds, Bonds, Life Insurance, and Health Insurance . * Learning Path: Each course is structured into levels and a learning path, for example, the Share Markets course is divided into five chapters.
          - Progression: Users progress by expending Energy Points and completing levels to earn Experience Points (XP).
          - Incentives: The platform generates revenue through the option to purchase additional energy points.
          - Leaderboard: Accumulated XP allows users to ascend a leaderboard and compete within leagues, fostering competition and motivation
        - AI and Data Features
