          - Finbot Chatbot: A financial assistant named "Finbot" is powered by the Bard API (Application Programming Interface). It provides comprehensive responses to user queries. To address discrepancies in stock price accuracy from the Bard API, a separate API using a Rule-based Information Extraction Technique was developed to fetch live stock data from Finhub . * Stock Prediction Model: The platform offers a stock prediction model to provide insights into future stock prices. It uses live data from yfinance and incorporates and compares two machine learning models:
            - LSTM (Long Short-Term Memory): Used for capturing temporal patterns.
            - ARIMA (Auto-Regressive Integrated Moving Average): Used for historical price analysis and forecasting
          - News Feed: A feature that uses the News API to aggregate live updates on trending news headlines relevant to investments, allowing users to stay informed about market developments
    - KhanAcademy Personal Finance & Financial Literacy Summary
      - 1. Foundational Money Management
        - This area is given the highest priority as the starting point for financial health.
        - Budgeting and Saving: Focuses on tracking income and expenses, creating a budget (like the 50/30/20 rule), reducing expenses, and the importance of paying yourself first. It stresses the Emergency Fund as separate from general savings.
        - Financial Goals: Covers setting SMART goals and understanding concepts like Net Worth and your "money personality."
      - 2. Debt and Credit Mechanics
        - Consumer Credit: Details how credit scores are calculated and affected by different factors, and explains the use of credit cards and various payment methods.
        - Loans and Debt: Breaks down types of loans (secured vs. unsecured, revolving vs. installment), interest rates (APR), predatory lending, debt repayment strategies, and even the process of personal bankruptcy (as a last resort).
      - 3. Long-Term Wealth and Risk
        - Investments and Retirement: Covers the basics of saving versus investing, the relationship between risk and return, and retirement accounts like 401(k)s, IRAs, and Social Security. It promotes long-term planning.
        - Insurance: Explains the different types of insurance (health, property, life, car) and the basics of estate planning.
        - Scams & Fraud: Dedicated lessons on identity theft, common scams, and consumer protection agencies, emphasizing financial security.
      - 4. Real-World Financial Decisions
        - Taxes and Tax Forms: Teaches how income is taxed, the progressive tax system, and the purpose of key forms like the W-2, 1099, and 1098.
        - Employment: Details how to evaluate job offers, understand paycheck deductions, benefits, and total compensation.
        - Housing: Compares renting vs. owning, explains lease agreements, and covers the basics of mortgages.
        - Car Buying: Provides detailed guidance on financing a car, comparing leasing versus buying, additional ownership costs, and dealer strategies.
    - FinMaster: A Holistic Benchmark for Mastering Full-Pipeline Financial Workflows with LLMs (==Jiang==)
      - Jiang (2025) [The Hong Kong Polytechnic University](https://arxiv.org/html/2505.13533v1)
      - The Core Problem FinMaster Addresses
        - Financial operations (like accounting, auditing, and consulting) are highly sensitive, labor-intensive, and require complex, multi-step reasoning with zero error tolerance.
        - Current LLM benchmarks for finance typically focus on basic Natural Language Processing (NLP) or isolated financial metrics, failing to assess the full chain of required professional tasks, which leads to unpredictable and unreliable LLM outputs in real-world scenarios.
      - FinMaster's Three Modular Components
        - FinSim (Financial Simulator):
          - Function: Automatically generates synthetic, privacy-compliant financial datasets, including detailed transaction records and interconnected financial statements (Income Statement, Balance Sheet, Cash Flow).
          - Benefit: Solves the perennial problem of lacking real-world financial data due to privacy concerns, allowing researchers to generate infinite, realistic datasets for various company types and market dynamics.
        - FinSuite (Task Suite):
          - Function: Provides a comprehensive set of tasks covering core financial domains: Financial Literacy, Accounting, Auditing, and Consulting. It spans 183 distinct tasks of varying types and difficulty.
          - Focus: It emphasizes full-pipeline tasks, forcing the LLM to execute a chain of operations, from recording a transaction to performing financial diagnostics.
        - FinEval (Evaluation Framework):
          - Function: Offers a streamlined and unified interface for systematic evaluation across all tasks, ensuring consistent and rigorous assessment.
      - Key Findings and Capability Gaps
        - Accuracy Cliff: While top LLMs achieve over 90% accuracy on basic, isolated tasks, their performance drops drastically to merely 40% when tackling complex scenarios that require multi-step financial reasoning.
        - Error Propagation: The study highlights how computational errors propagate through the workflow. Tasks involving a single metric calculation showed about 58% accuracy, but when integrated into a multi-metric scenario (where one error affects the next calculation), accuracy degraded further to just 37%.
        - Lack of Domain Knowledge: General-purpose LLMs frequently produce hallucinated conclusions or statistically invalid outputs when faced with problems requiring professional financial judgment, underscoring their deficiency in domain-specific expertise.
      - Conclusion
        - The paper concludes that FinMaster is the first benchmark to comprehensively simulate multi-step, full-pipeline financial workflows with realistic and challenging tasks. 
        - Even the most advanced LLMs are not yet reliable for end-to-end automation in crucial financial sectors due to deep-seated reasoning and error propagation issues.

  - ==THEORETICAL FINLIT==
    - Financial Parenting (==Serido + Jambunathan==)
      - Serido (2016) [Handbook of Consumer Finance Research](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-3-319-28887-1_24#Abs1)
        - Two objectives: 
          - Describe the ways that financial parenting promotes the acquisition of financial knowledge and skills
          - Propose how financial parenting promotes or constrains financial self-reliance 
        - In one survey study, adolescents who reported more parental warmth, were more likely to save for their education, especially if parents spoke to them about the uses of money, for example, the importance of donating to charity (financial values) and saving for school (goal-striving and planning) (Kim, LaTaillade, & Kim, [2011](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-3-319-28887-1_24#ref-CR27)).
        - Studies among young children demonstrate that those with experience handling money (like an allowance) show a greater understanding about pricing knowledge, cash, and credit transactions compared to children who lack that experience (Abramovitch, Freedman, & Pliner, [1991](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-3-319-28887-1_24#ref-CR1))
      - Not having a clear understanding of what to teach, how to teach it, and how to engage parents might be a significant cause of teachers’ lack of clarity in engaging with parents to teach the financial concepts at home. ([Jambunathan](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s10643-024-01740-7) 2024)
      - How family socialization affects financial literacy/behavior Khawar (2021) [Future Business Journal](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1186/s43093-021-00064-x?fromPaywallRec=false)
        - (1) No distinction in the financial conduct of representatives from various socioeconomics gatherings. Financial education has a critical positive relationship with financial behavior. 
        - (2) Family financial socialization additionally shows a huge positive relationship with financial education and financial behavior. 
        - (3) Financial education demonstrated a remarkable abnormal impact on financial behavior through family financial socialization.
      - Personal Thoughts/Judgements/Opinions:
        - Good emphasis on family component, especially considering parental "warmth" -> unique insight that's definitely relevant, FFS is a big component of learning financial literacy that sometimes gets overlooked
        - Identifies problems teachers face in feeling unprepared to teach FinLit (common problem for FinLit teachers is feeling unprepared)
        - Criticisms/limitations:
          - How parental warmth is measured?
          - Doesn't specify how exactly teachers can be made more prepared to teach FinLit
    - Combining FL education with a larger economics education? (==Berti==) (*)
      - Berti (2016) [International Handbook of Financial Literacy](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-981-10-0360-8_33)
      - "Financial concepts should be taught as components of a more extensive learning progression in economics"
      - Series describing what children understand about money/economies from preschool and beyond, explaining what could be taught at each age
      - explore references
    - I Wish: Multigenerational Regrets and Reflections on Teaching Children About Money (==LeBaron==)
      - LeBaron (2017) [Journal of Family and Economic Issues](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s10834-017-9556-1?fromPaywallRec=false)
      - This qualitative, multigenerational study explored what Millennials and their parents and grandparents (*N* = 153) wish they had been taught about finances by their parents, as well as what parents and grandparents wish they had taught their children
      - Gives firsthand examples of what adults wish they were taught as kids
      - Never taught how to budget/not many opportunities to budget their own money
        - Exactly how to pay a bill or setup autopay
        - Saving
        - Stocks
        - Debt/loans
        - Working harder/getting a job
      - Teaching more practical knowledge, giving more financial stewardship/responsibility, and communicating more openly about finances with them
      - Personal Thoughts/Judgements/Opinions:
        - Good to have feedback on what various FinLit courses don't (and should) include, very relevant to content list for Aurum
        - Stresses open communication between parents and their children (FFS)
        - Criticisms/limitations:
          - Doesn't speak much to what *was *covered in FinLit courses
          - Small sample size
    - How Can Configurators Enhance FinLit (offering personal financial services)? (==Shafiee==) (*)
      - Shafiee (2023) [Journal of the Knowledge Economy](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s13132-023-01651-9)
      - The argument for personalized FinLit education through configurators
        - By bridging the gap between technology and finance, a personalized financial configurator can address contemporary challenges faced by traditional financial advisory services
        - Through its integration of advanced technologies such as AI and data analytics, the **configurator** can **process extensive financial data**, providing tailored insights and real-time recommendations that cater to each individual's unique circumstances.
      - The available solutions (for increasing FinLit) are often generic by providing standard results, instead of personalized ones
      - Cost and inefficacy of traditional financial education (e.g., high school courses and seminars) call for alternative solutions, such as mobile banking, personal finance blogs, robo-advice, and media (Goyal & Kumar, [2021](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s13132-023-01651-9#ref-CR17))
      - Cole et al. ([2011](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s13132-023-01651-9#ref-CR10)) demonstrated that a carefully designed and targeted financial literacy program that is more cost-effective than a large-scale effort may serve as a valuable complement to such digital financial reform.
      - Classroom-based financial education generally yields weaker results, compared with implementing higher-intensity, personalized educational treatments (Carpena et al., [2017](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s13132-023-01651-9#ref-CR8)).
      - Current financial systems = designed for a single purpose (mortgage loans), and are not developed to provide sufficient advice for personalized financial decisions (Tian & Shao, [2023](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s13132-023-01651-9#ref-CR50))
      - Financial configurator:
        - Synthesizes diverse information sources into a coherent and user-friendly format. By doing so, it helps bridge the gap between financial literacy and effective decision-making
        - Personalized configurator to be developed should act as a knowledge management system by including a simple and user-friendly interface to allow users to get a fast and easy overview of their financial situations. A knowledge management system refers to a platform that not only aggregates and presents financial data but also translates this data into actionable insights tailored to the user’s unique circumstances.
        - Configurator needs to 
          - (1) provide real-time, higher-quality financial data
          - (2) reduce the complexity of pension services provided
          - (3) enhance financial literacy
          - (4) generate pension-related outputs based on different input alternatives
      - Personal Thoughts/Judgements/Opinions:
        - Stresses importance of education tailored to individual using data analytics and AI (very relevant to Aurum's emphasis on endogenous assessment, the holistic predictive dashboard, etc, as we plan to use extensive data collection techniques and analysis to provide personalized feedback and training to each student)
        - Identifies problems with traditional FinLit education methods
        - Emphasizes the need to not overwhelm the user with excessive complexity (as the financial configurator will be highly involved), good to reduce complexity
        - Shows need for providing real-time data (something the Aurum's internal system will need to do)
        - Criticisms/limitations:
          - Doesn't specify actionable steps for what a financial configurator could look like, how it would be integrated into course/game
    - Developing a Human-Centered AI Environment to Enhance Financial Literacy of College Students: A Systematic Review (==Xie==) (*) (~)
      - Xie (2024) [Cross-Cultural Design](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-3-031-60913-8_25?fromPaywallRec=false)
      - Comparing the findings of the previous studies based on a human-centered AI environment toward promoting the financial literacy of college students.
        - Examining the findings on the factors associated with college students’ financial decision-making behavior
          - Decision-making highly influenced by peer pressure, financial literacy, socioeconomic background, and personal psychological characteristics
        - Reviewing previous study findings focused on **personalization strategies** and recommendation of system algorithms
          - Chang et al. argued that personalized strategies in recommendation systems are not just about providing accurate suggestions but also **fostering long-term user engagement**. Systems should be designed to learn and adapt to user preferences over time, ensuring a sustained and positive user experience.
          - Bernacki and Greene revealed that the interaction that recommendation system algorithms and personalized techniques have with human-centered AI settings is an important part of building systems that put user experience and engagement first.
        - Factors associated with the **effectiveness of human-centered AI Environment** in improving the financial literacy among college students
          - **Neural collaborative filtering**, in particular, is a deep learning technique that has shown greater performance in capturing complex user-item interactions, **resulting in suggestions that are more precise and tailored to the individual.**
            - Madinga, N.W., Maziriri, E.T., Chuchu, T., Magoda, Z.: An investigation of the impact of financial literacy and financial socialization on financial satisfaction: mediating role of financial risk attitude. Glob. J. Emerg. Mark. Econ. 14(1), 60–75 (2022)
            - Personalized learning solutions have become more popular in educational environments [[13](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-3-031-60913-8_25?fromPaywallRec=false#ref-CR13)]. 
              - Giannakos, M.N., Sharma, K., Pappas, I.O., Kostakos, V., Velloso, E.: Multimodal data as a means to understand the learning experience. Int. J. Inf. Manage. **48**, 108–119 (2019)
            - These techniques use recommendation systems to personalize instructional information according to each learner’s preferences, style, and level of performance [[3](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-3-031-60913-8_25?fromPaywallRec=false#ref-CR3)]. **Using real-time data, adaptive learning solutions modify examinations, content, and pacing to maximize every student’s learning experience.**
              - Bernacki, M.L., Greene, M.J., Lobczowski, N.G.: A systematic review of research on personalized learning: personalized by whom, to what, how, and for what purpose (s)? Educ. Psychol. Rev. **33**(4), 1675–1715 (2021)
        - Key financial literacy challenges faced by college students.
          - Students lack the information and abilities needed to properly build and manage a financial budget
          - Many college-bound kids have only a cursory knowledge of basic financial concepts like managing **spending, budgeting, and saving.** Their inability to make wise financial decisions as a result of this ignorance may put them in financial jeopardy both during and after college.
          - Katarachia and Konstantinidis [[19](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-3-031-60913-8_25?fromPaywallRec=false#ref-CR19)]: using AI technologies for personalized financial education can give students specialized advice and assistance in improving their financial literacy. In the context of human-centered AI, a major financial literacy difficulty faced by college students is** limited awareness of financial products and services** [[12](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-3-031-60913-8_25?fromPaywallRec=false#ref-CR12)].
            - Such limited awareness could be the reason for great success with the FL program where students researched and presented findings on a certain investment (therefore, researching different financial products/services was an integral part of their FL education)
            - Katarachia, A., Konstantinidis, A.: Financial education and decision making processes. Proc. Econom. Finance **9**, 142–152 (2014)
            - Eberhardt, W., Bruine de Bruin, W., Strough, J.: Age differences in financial decision making: T he benefits of more experience and less negative emotions. J. Behav. Decision Making **32**(1), 79–93 (2019)
          - Users may want to customize their preferences, adjust the level of personalization, or even opt out of certain recommendation features [[3](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-3-031-60913-8_25?fromPaywallRec=false#ref-CR3)]. Recommendation algorithms should be flexible enough to accommodate these user choices [[11](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-3-031-60913-8_25?fromPaywallRec=false#ref-CR11)].
            - Bernacki, M.L., Greene, M.J., Lobczowski, N.G.: A systematic review of research on personalized learning: personalized by whom, to what, how, and for what purpose (s)? Educ. Psychol. Rev. **33**(4), 1675–1715 (2021)
            - Du, X., Yang, J., Hung, J.L., Shelton, B.: Educational data mining: a systematic review of research and emerging trends. Inform. Disc. Delivery **48**(4), 225–236 (2020)
      - Personal Thoughts/Judgements/Opinions:
        - Good that article specifies decision-making components
        - NCF (neural collaborative filtering) mentioned as a technique to tailor learning experience and increase engagement 
        - Mentions several related studies to things brought up
        - Good that article recognizes issues faced by college students (although this is older than Aurum's target age group, it is still very useful for understanding what to prepare kids for before they are college-age)
        - Criticisms/limitations:
          - Doesn't specify psychological factors that influence decision-making (would involve cognitive biases and the 8 core drives)
    - Mobile App to Promote Young People’s Digital Financial Literacy (==Zhang==) (*) (~)
      - Zhang (2021) [Design, User Experience, and Usability](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-3-030-78227-6_10?fromPaywallRec=false)
      - Paper discusses types of nudges + engagement strategies
        - Research questions: **What knowledge do young people need to promote their digital financial literacy? What are the potential content design strategies to fill in the knowledge gaps?**
      - DFS = Digital Financial Services
      - Paper proposes the **design of a mobile app** to promote **young people’s digital financial literacy**. Specifically, it focuses on a set of content design and delivery design strategies.
      - Consumer info:
        - The design of this app was intended for younger adults (18-40) with difficulties involving crypto, crowdfunding, blockchain etc
        - In terms of the content, they displayed more interest and motivation to learn the mechanism and potential risks of DFS (see Table [5](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-3-030-78227-6_10?fromPaywallRec=false#Tab5)).
        - “The financial marketplace is much more complex for the average consumers. **Consumers have more opportunities to manage their finances**, but **increased complexity makes them more vulnerable to financial fraud and more prone to unwise financial decisions**” (IOSCO & OECD [2018](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-3-030-78227-6_10?fromPaywallRec=false#ref-CR22), p. 16).
        - The financial marketplace is rather complicated for the average consumers, due to the **evolving technology** and **sophisticated products **(G20 [2017](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-3-030-78227-6_10?fromPaywallRec=false#ref-CR13); OECD [2019](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-3-030-78227-6_10?fromPaywallRec=false#ref-CR33)). This adds an extra layer of complexity for people’s **working memory capacity**, which means people can only “hold in mind, attend, or maintain a small amount of information in a rapidly accessible state at one time” (Cowan [2016](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-3-030-78227-6_10?fromPaywallRec=false#ref-CR4), p. 1)
      - Digital nudges in an effort to reduce cognitive load and sustain user engagement. The nudging strategies include: 
        - **Facilitator nudges** (keep learning chunks small/easy to understand), implemented in the delivery design, aiming to reduce users’ cognitive load in their mobile learning
          - Specifically, the design efforts involved keeping information short and small-chunked, sorting jargon by their relevance, using infographics, and underlining keywords 
        - Some users mentioned not being interested in learning digital financial literacy (majority = experienced investors, leading to overconfidence) **Spark nudges** used to **increase users’ motivation** before they embark on the learning journey. 
          - An onboarding survey *Knowing yourself Better* might be a good fit, nudging users into assessing their risk tolerance level and digital financial literacy status
        - **Signal nudges** are self-regulation learning reminders, keeping users informed of their learning progress, used to navigate the entire learning journey
        - **Inquiry nudges**: provide scaffolding anywhere, anytime
      - The currently available learning materials fail to present DFS information in an accessible and salient manner (G20 [2017](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-3-030-78227-6_10?fromPaywallRec=false#ref-CR13)). The vast majority of them are filled with **jargon**, resulting in **consumers being overwhelmed** by the sophisticated DFS features. Especially for young people who have been targeted by many Fintech companies, this issue might cause more concerns. Young generations tend to make quick judgments without fully understanding the terms and conditions, due to their low tolerance to redundant and complicated information. As a result, they “more easily fall prey to personal bias such as overconfidence in their own digital capabilities to manage DFS risks” (G20 [2017](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-3-030-78227-6_10?fromPaywallRec=false#ref-CR13), p. 42)
      - As Palalas ([2018](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-3-030-78227-6_10?fromPaywallRec=false#ref-CR34), p. 19) pointed out, “Cognitive load, which has been identified as a key issue in successful instructional design, is yet another aspect that is impacted by the content, delivery, and setting of mobile learning”.
      - Caraban et al. ([2019](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-3-030-78227-6_10?fromPaywallRec=false#ref-CR2)) summarized 23 mechanisms of digital nudging, and further clustered them into three overall categories: facilitator nudges, spark nudges, and signal nudges.
      - The design adopted the five-phases process of DT (design thinking) (Stanford [2010](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-3-030-78227-6_10?fromPaywallRec=false#ref-CR38)), including empathy (data collection based on user research), defining (data synthesis to frame the problem statements), ideation (generating design ideas), prototyping (developing a tangible solution), and testing (with potential users).
      - Results:
        - Ease of learning/use was rated most highly by consumers, but participants perceived that the app failed to help them balance the risks and rewards of DFS to some degree
        - App’s favorable features included: 
          - 1) the onboarding test to assess their risk tolerance
          - 2) the approaches to organizing the content
          - 3) the quick quizzes after learning
          - 4) the flashcards for learning DFS jargon
        - Testing results suggested the **prototype failed to help the participants balance the risks and rewards of DFS** to some degree
      - Key concerns for a mobile app are **cognitive overload** and **continuous user engagement**
        - “The ruthless management of cognitive load” (Dirksen [2016](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-3-030-78227-6_10?fromPaywallRec=false#ref-CR5), p. 167) has become a top priority in the context of digital financial literacy promotion.
        - Digital nudges to help?
          - **Facilitator nudges: keep learning chunks small and easy-to-understand**
            - Infographic, plain and simple language, and small-chunked information to mitigate the concerns of cognitive overload. (Facilitator nudges home to overcome (1) Limitations of short-term memory capacity and (2) Limitations of display size in mobile devices
            - Similar to scaffolding/CLT (cognitive learning theory)
          - **Spark nudges: enhance learning motivation**
            - Onboarding test. "More options for spark nudges could be further explored, yet the motivational trait is at the core." (Duolingo? Using notifications/coins/gamification/streaks/competition to motivate young students?)  COULD further explore the psychology of what makes games *addictive* (best gamification strategies) to foster engagement in learning (when it is most fun)
          - **Signal nudges: navigate the learning journey**
            - Contest for attention in the digital world, easy to be distracted. This situation calls for signal nudges working as self-regulation reminders, which not only inform them of learning progress, but also provide immediate feedback on the tasks they have completed.
        - Self-scaffolding?
          - Testing results also indicated that self-scaffolding was a compelling need of young people, yet the current version of the app didn’t fulfill this need. 
          - They highly rely on the search function as just-in-time learning support to “provide scaffolding WHEN and WHERE learners need it” (Martin and Ertzberger [2013](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-3-030-78227-6_10?fromPaywallRec=false#ref-CR28), p. 78). 
            - Search function like ChatGPT to augment the regular curriculum/gamification/simulation? "In this sense, a robust search capability would be an inquiry nudge to make the design engaging at delivering content, as well as effective at enhancing user learning."
            - Last nudge to add: inquiry nudges to provide the scaffolding anywhere anytime.
      - NUDGING (from [article](https://dl.acm.org/doi/pdf/10.1145/3290605.3300733) and [video](https://dl.acm.org/doi/abs/10.1145/3290605.3300733))
        - Nudge Definition: Introduced a decade ago, it refers to aspects of choice architecture that can modify behaviors predictably without restricting options or altering incentives.
        - Example: Supermarkets have successfully nudged healthy eating by relocating unhealthy snacks to less accessible areas near checkout counters.
        - Educational effects were noted to diminish over time; nudges may not sustain behavior changes without continuous reinforcement.
      - Personal Thoughts/Judgements/Opinions:
        - Nudges are a helpful design tool, especially as seen within the context of motivations 
        - Correct that increasing complexity in the financial world means increased confusion for  consumers (overloading working memory)
        - Good that app included onboarding test
        - Self-scaffolding is not broadly talked about, but could be a helpful component that could increase autonomy (and thereby self-efficacy) while also decreasing dependency on guides or other students 
        - Criticisms/limitations:
          - Could give more examples as to what the nudges look like in a broader context or more specific applications
    - Determinants of Financial Literacy: a Systematic Review and Future Research Directions (==Rheman==)
      - Rheman (2024) [Future Business Journal](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1186/s43093-024-00365-x#Abs1)
      - Theory of consumer socialization
        - Children acquire financial knowledge and skills from a variety of sources, including peers, parents, schools, and media [[38](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1186/s43093-024-00365-x#ref-CR38)]. The theory emphasizes that within the context of family traditions and culture, family members play a crucial role as socialization facilitators. Youths exposed to financial education at home demonstrate higher financial literacy 
      - Self-efficacy theory
        - Bandura highlighted that vicarious experience, verbal persuasion, and physiological and emotive state are all forms of mastery experience that influence self-efficacy. 
      - Paper discusses impact of demographic, socio-economic, psychological, financial, societal, and technological factors on financial literacy
      - Shows most influential papers and authors in the context of financial literacy
      - Personal Thoughts/Judgements/Opinions:
        - Emphasis on family financial socialization and development of self-efficacy, both of which are crucial to effectiveness in FinLit education
        - Mentions the contribution of background factors on an individual's FinLit level
        - Criticisms/limitations:
          - Doesn't give actionable solutions
    - What Determines Financial Literacy in Japan? (==Kadoya==)
      - Kadoya (2019) [Journal of Pension Economics and Finance](https://www.cambridge.org/core/journals/journal-of-pension-economics-and-finance/article/what-determines-financial-literacy-in-japan/F4D9851FF30450317147A7E8CDB58F99#)
      - Financial Socialization:
        - The theory of consumer socialization argues that children learn the consumption-related knowledge and skills required to live capably in society from parents, schools, the mass media, and peers (Ward 1974, Moschis and Churchill 1978). 
        - Some empirical evidence shows an important relationship between financial knowledge and behavior and social learning opportunities (Gutter, Garrison and Copur 2010).
        - Lachance and Choquette-Bernier (2004) found that observation, parental communication, and learning through error contribute to students’ acquisition of financial knowledge.
      - Results suggest that socialization and future orientation have a profound impact on the acquisition of FinLit in Japan
      - Personal Thoughts/Judgements/Opinions:
        - Social component is very relevant for learning -> SCT (social cognitive theory)
        - Highlights family financial socialization as an important factor for learning 
        - Criticisms/limitations:
          - Doesn't clearly specify what "future orientation" is
          - More qualitative than quantitative
    - Learning by doing: the role of financial experience in financial literacy (==Frijns==)
      - Frijns (2013) [Journal of Public Policy](https://www.cambridge.org/core/journals/journal-of-public-policy/article/learning-by-doing-the-role-of-financial-experience-in-financial-literacy/C23B4B230D8599499A40AD9E47443194)
      - Paper aims to address the **causality problem in the relationship between financial literacy and experience**
      - Results indicate that the design of general financial education programs may need to be re-evaluated and perhaps **include experiential components**, such as stock market games
      - Financial education programs have been provided in many settings, such as inclusion in high school curricula. To date, however, evaluations of these programs have generally shown little success in improving financial literacy and changing financial behavior 
      - While the efficacy of financial education programs in improving financial literacy is limited, several studies find that financial experience can make people more receptive to financial education
        - Bradley et al. find that the major source of learning of their participants was a difficult financial experience. **Mandell finds that participation in a stock market game results in a 6–8% improvement in financial literacy among respondents**. These studies suggest that **financial experiences make people more receptive to financial education programs, and improve financial literacy and financial behavior.**
      - We assess the relationship between financial experience and literacy by conducting a survey on 338 first-year business students. 
        - The survey contains 19 questions covering three areas: understanding of financial concepts, financial activity and experience, and demographics. 
        - Understanding of financial concepts is tested in 8 questions covering time value of money, compounding interest, diversification, real versus nominal interest rates, risk, market effects of interest rate changes, how loans function and financial planning
      - Results corroborate the findings of Dvorak and Hanley and have important consequences for the design of policies to improve financial literacy. 
      - **"We further find support for Mandell, who concludes that education programs in high school have a very limited impact on financial literacy"**
      - Education programs should rely more on experiential learning. Mandell, for instance, concludes that high school financial literacy programs that incorporate stock market games result in marked improvements in financial literacy scores.
      - **CONCLUSION**:
        - Cannot infer causality between financial experience and financial literacy, as either one could cause the other
        - Financial experiences seem to make people more open to financial education, and experiential learning (e.g. the stock market game) is helpful in improving financial literacy
      - Personal Thoughts/Judgements/Opinions:
        - Highlights importance of experiential aspect to FinLit education
        - Finds that traditional programs fail especially with regard to changing financial behaviors (congruent with Mandell & Klein study on the ineffectiveness of FinLit, but that study attributes the failure to lack of motivation and relevance)
        - Criticisms/limitations:
          - Study focuses on college students, while Aurum's target audience is K-8
    - Impact of the FutureSmart Online Financial Education Course on Financial Knowledge of Middle School Students (==Johnson==) (*) (~)
      - Johnson (2021) [Journal of Financial Counseling and Planning](https://connect.springerpub.com/highwire_display/entity_view/node/155690/full)
      - Overview of several of the most influential studies/articles + reviews FutureSmart
      - FutureSmart:
        - Free, co-curricular, online financial education course using a quasi-experimental design with a diverse sample of middle school students nationwide. The study assessed the impact of the course on students' financial knowledge, attitudes, and behaviors, and explored the association of program implementation factors with changes in student outcomes. **Financial knowledge gains were significant, substantial, and consistent**
          - **Gains in financial attitudes and behaviors**—specifically, financial confidence, engagement with parents about financial issues, current engagement with financial products, and intended future engagement with financial products—**were not significant**
        - The course consists of seven modules: financial values and goal setting, budgeting and opportunity cost, saving and investing, payment types, banking, risk versus return, and planning for the future. Each **FutureSmart module utilizes a “story-based narrative**” in which students role play as the mayor of a fictional town and help local citizens make real-life financial decisions ([EVERFI, 2018a](https://connect.springerpub.com/highwire_display/entity_view/node/155690/full#ref14)). Individual modules take approximately 30 minutes to complete and include optional supplemental learning activities provided to the teacher. 5 of the modules include pre- and post-assessments of student knowledge.
        - FutureSmart was designed to be administered flexibly, allowing students to progress through the modules at their own pace. Since the course is web-based, participants can access the modules from anywhere
        -  [Kulo and Bodzin (2013)](https://connect.springerpub.com/highwire_display/entity_view/node/155690/full#ref23) found a significant increase in content knowledge and large effect sizes for all ability levels for students who used web-enhanced science curricula in middle school classes. Similarly, [Lynch et al. (2008)](https://connect.springerpub.com/highwire_display/entity_view/node/155690/full#ref25) found that a web-based program utilizing video segments, quizzes, and interactive games and activities to increase food-safety knowledge among middle school students, had a statistically significant, moderate effect size between pre- and post-test.
        - Students who participated in FutureSmart exhibited increases in financial knowledge that were statistically significant and educationally meaningful. However, the results of this study did not support our hypotheses that the FutureSmart intervention has an effect on the four financial attitude and behavior outcomes—financial confidence, communication with parents, and current and intended future engagement with financial products. 
      - Other Studies:
        -  [Varcoe et al. (2005)](https://connect.springerpub.com/highwire_display/entity_view/node/155690/full#ref38), using difference of means tests, assessed whether financial knowledge changed significantly from pre- to post-test among high school students who participated in the “Money Talks” curriculum. They found an increase in both financial knowledge and financial behaviors such as shopping choices and attitudes toward saving.
      - 10 studies examined students in Grades 6–8, exploring the impacts of the following programs: 
        - **FFFL** (which consists of five weekly classroom lessons of approximately 45 minutes) 
        - Milwaukee Urban League Academy of Business and Economics (**MULABE**), (a charter school implementing lessons focused on economics and personal finance) 
        - **Stock Market Game** (a 10- to 15-week activity)
        - **YEA**! (a free 3-week program offered to income-eligible middle school students in Boston)
      - Personal Thoughts/Judgements/Opinions:
        - Paper gives good description of course details and implementation methods
        - Consistent with other studies finding that financial knowledge gains were consistent, while behaviors and attitudes did not change 
        - Good that FutureSmart utilizes narrative as an engagement strategy
        - Web-based course good for flexibility and increased engagement 
        - Connects to other relevant studies
        - Criticisms/limitations:
          - Student roleplaying not very direct (they act as the mayor in a town and not a citizen, limiting their involvement and decision-making)
          - Modular implementation (not continual simulation)
          - Assessment is exogenous (pre- and post-tests)
    - The Features and Effectiveness of the Keys to Financial Success Curriculum (==Hill==) (*)
      - Hill (2014) [International Review of Economics Education](https://www.academia.edu/75413976/The_features_and_effectiveness_of_the_Keys_to_Financial_Success_curriculum)
      - This paper explains the features of a successful and unique high school personal finance curriculum, Keys to Financial Success
      - The survey results indicate that the training of teachers in the Keys curriculum, and the implementation of a one-semester Keys course, significantly improve the average personal finance knowledge of students in each of the standards and concept areas of the FFFL-HS Test.
      - Students in classrooms with teachers who attended a FFFL workshop scored significantly higher on the required end-of-course test than students taught by teachers who did not attend the workshop.
      - The Keys course consists of 52 lessons built around 9 themes: 
        - Goals and decision-making
        - Careers and planning
        - Budgeting
        - Saving and investing
        - Credit
        - Banking services
        - Transportation issues
        - Housing issues
        - Risk protection
      - The results from the pre-and posttests show that students whose teachers are trained in the Keys curriculum, and who participate in a one-semester Keys course, exhibit a **statistically significant increase in their overall personal finance knowledge**. This finding is also true at the standard and concept specific levels.
        - The largest knowledge improvements took place in the areas dealing with credit history and records, and the rights and responsibilities of buyers, sellers and creditors. Coincidentally, these two areas where identified as the **most challenging for students in the pretest. **
      - Personal Thoughts/Judgements/Opinions:
        - Study highlights the component of teacher training, which tends to be overlooked in many newly implemented courses. Other studies mention teachers feeling unprepared to teach FinLit, so this is a good solution. 
        - Good that lessons categorized by theme
        - Effective in meeting students where they are - largest improvement areas were the most challenging topics before the course
        - Criticisms/limitations:
          - Student improvements measured by internal test, not a standardized assessment like the P-Fin Index
    - A behaviorally informed financial education program for the financially vulnerable: Design and effectiveness (==Antonides==)
      - Antonides (2022) [Frontiers in Psychology](https://www.academia.edu/125924558/A_behaviorally_informed_financial_education_program_for_the_financially_vulnerable_Design_and_effectiveness)
      - The purpose of our study is to investigate the impact of a **behaviorally informed** financial education program (BI program) targeting financially vulnerable consumers in comparison to both a control group and a traditional ﬁnancial education program (traditional program).
        - Research question: Does the BI program improve financial outcomes, both in comparison with no program and a traditional financial education programs?
        - Main finding: the **BI program** had a **positive impact** on self-reported financial **skills** and **knowledge** and **financial behavior** 
          - But **not on financial well-being and financial situation**. The **BI program was not more effective than the traditional program.**
      - PROGRAM DETAILS:
        - Consists of 5-6 weekly sessions of 2.5h resulting in a total of 12.5–15h of financial education.
        - In the first session, the trainer presents potential topics to discuss around four modules: 
          - (1) income
          - (2) financial administration
          - (3) making ends meet (now)
          - (4) making ends meet (later)
          - Thereafter, participants choose which topics they will discuss during the other sessions. The trainer designs the rest of the program accordingly.
        - Several studies suggest that **effective financial education programs require a combination of transfer of knowledge, skill-building, and increasing motivation** to create the desired changes in behavior (Hilgert et al., 2003; Peeters et al., 2018). The traditional program primarily focuses on the transfer of knowledge and skill-building. In designing the BI program, we supplemented these elements of the traditional program with three elements focusing on motivation and practicing. 
          - Program contains a **need-driven and adaptive approach** in which participants decide which topics will be discussed. 
          - Trainers apply insights from autonomy-supportive teaching, the stages-of-change model, and interviewing techniques to enhance **intrinsic motivation**, solve ambivalence, and support behavioral change.
          - Program contains implementation assignments to enhance practicing and behavioral change.
        - Trainers instructed to apply elements of autonomy-supportive teaching derived from **self-determination theory** (Kusurkar et al., 2011)
      - Each program session ends with making a goal-action plan and creating a rule of thumb. In a goal-action plan, participants define a session-related goal, describe the planned activities to achieve that goal, describe what and who they need for performing the activities, and set a deadline. These plans may help participants to develop concrete and actionable goals and to apply them in practice.
      - The participants’ main chosen topics were budgeting, book accounting, ﬁnancial administration, and resisting temptations.
      - Reason there were positive eﬀects on budgeting specifically is that budgeting was among the common activities of the BI program.
      - **Traditional program already obtained high scores **from participants, thus leaving little room for improvement. **Don't know if the new elements contributed to the effectiveness of the BI **program
      - Personal Thoughts/Judgements/Opinions:
        - Has control group and treatment group (traditional and behaviorally informed)
        - Consistent findings to other studies: while financial knowledge may improve, actual financial status/behaviors do not. 
        - Skill building and increasing motivation directly relevant to Aurum, with its emphasis on experiential learning and targeting intrinsic motivations
        - Adaptive approach, intrinsic motivation, and SDT (self-determination theory)
        - Criticisms/limitations:
    - Boosting financial literacy: benefits from learning study (==Pang==)
      - Pang (2010) [Instructional Science](https://www.academia.edu/58686128/Boosting_financial_literacy_benefits_from_learning_study)
      - Students in the learning study group outperformed their counterparts in the lesson study group in all three post-lesson tests, and that the inter-group performance gap was maintained or widened over time
      - Gives several example questions asked of students on the various tests
      - STUDY DESIGN/METHODS
        - Theory-based approach on 12th grade students
        - Variation theory: learners understand a concept not just by learning what it is, but how it differs from what it is not
        - According to variation theory, the likelihood of learning something is a function of the particular pattern of variation and invariance in how learners encounter it. This relationship between learning and the pattern of variation and invariance inherent in the condition of learning has been demonstrated empirically in numerous studies (see, for example, Pang and Marton 2003, 2005; Marton and Pang 2006, 2008; Holmqvist 2006).
          - Variance teaching involved constant features (limited resources/needing to make a decision) and variable features (nature of trade-off/type of cost etc). Used to explain opportunity costs and help students make informed decisions
        - One group followed the lesson study model (lesson study group), and the other followed the learning study model (learning study group). The only critical difference between the groups was that teachers in the learning study group used variation theory
        - 6 months after the post-test, students were given another post-test to assess their continued understanding
      - RESULTS/FINDINGS
        - FinLit is critically low: 12th graders consistently score just above 50% in national financial literacy surveys, and similar trends are observed globally
        - Lack of conceptual depth: Many existing financial education programs focus on practical skills for specific situations but often lack depth in teaching the underlying economic concepts. This limits students' ability to apply knowledge to novel or complex financial situations
        - Concepts essential for FinLit
          - Relations among saving, consumption, and investment
          - Opportunity cost of financial decisions
          - Relations among risk, return, and liquidity
          - Inflation and real value of return
          - Present value and discounting
        - According to Marton and Pang (2006), **powerful ways of acting originate from powerful ways of seeing**. To acquire a more powerful way of seeing, one needs to develop the ability to attend to the critical features of a given phenomenon or situation, and the critical features identified hinge on the lens of the experiencer, which is influenced by his or her own domains of knowledge or understanding. In other words, a domain of knowledge will provide people with a certain lens through which to interpret and analyze the phenomena around them and thus enable them to focus upon different critical aspects of the situation or phenomenon.
      - TAKEAWAYS
        - Focus on core concepts instead of practical tips: games should go beyond teaching the skills themselves (write checks/shop for loans) and instead focus on how to leverage larger economic concepts (opportunity cost, risk/return, present value, etc). These are the threshold concepts that enable flexible, transferrable financial thinking
          - Such core concepts are things the holistic predictive dashboard (see [competitive advantage](https://workflowy.com/#/b0de74f9662d)) would likely not measure (at least at first), as they are more abstract (financial attitudes and behaviors instead of financial knowledge)
        - Use variation + comparison: 
          - Study's success with variation theory suggests that exposing learners to a range of similar and contrasting examples helps them discern critical features of financial concepts. In a game, this could mean presenting scenarios with subtle differences and prompting players to compare outcomes, fostering deeper understanding
        - Scaffold complex decision-making:
          - Financial literacy involves weighing multiple, intertwined factors (risk, liquidity, inflation). Game scenarios should reflect this complexity, requiring players to make trade-offs and consider opportunity costs, rather than focusing on single-variable decisions (holistic predictive dashboard can help with this)
        - Encourage reflection + transfer:
          - Reviewing past decisions, analyzing what went right/wrong, applying lessons to new situations
        - Emphasize long-term retention:
          - Study showed conceptual learning had lasting effects. Game mechanics that revisit and reinforce core concepts over time (e.g., through recurring challenges or narrative arcs) can help ensure long-term retention of financial literacy skills
        - Contextual/IRL relevance:
          - Scenarios should be grounded in realistic, everyday financial situations (like budgeting, investing, responding to inflation), making the learning directly applicable to players’ lives 
            - Connects to SLT (situated learning theory) and endogenous fantasy - experiential learning needs to be relevant and contextual to the real scenarios in which it would be applied 
    - Students and Financial Literacy: What do middle school students know? What do teachers want them to know? (==Connolly==)
      - Connolly (2015) [University of British Columbia](https://www.academia.edu/14197160/Students_and_Financial_Literacy_What_do_middle_school_students_know_What_do_teachers_want_them_to_know)
      - Students in 7th/8th grade
      - Provides a graph with which topics are most/least valuable:
        - (1) BUDGETING (needs vs wants/cost of living)
        - (2) CREDIT CARDS (payments/fees/interest)
        - (3) FORMS OF PAYMENT (cash/debit/credit)
        - (4) PLANNING (goal setting/savings/investing)
        - (5) BANKING (checking/savings accounts)
        - (6) MONEY SCAMS
        - (7) INTEREST (compound interest costs)
        - (8) PLANNING FOR LIFE EVENTS
        - (9) EARNING INTEREST/BENEFITS OF COMPOUNDING
        - (10) HOW BANKS MAKE MONEY
        - (11) CREDIT REPORTS/RANKINGS
        - (12) STOCK MARKET BASICS
    - Exploring Parents’ and Teacher’s Perception on the Effects of Kwartalino Financial Education on Kindergartens’ Financial Literacy (==Curugan==) 
      - Curugan (2019) [The International Journal of Humanities and Social Studies](https://www.academia.edu/50314537/Exploring_Parents_and_Teacher_s_Perception_on_the_Effects_of_Kwartalino_Financial_Education_on_Kindergartens_Financial_Literacy)
      - This study collected and analyzed data using descriptive qualitative method in order to gather parents’ and teacher’s perceptions regarding the effects of the Kwartalino financial education program on kindergarten learners’ financial literacy. Lessons were integrated across subject areas taught in a kinder class
      - PROGRAM DESIGN:
        - An elementary public school was chosen to implement a developed financial education program in one class in kindergarten level with the following objectives: 
          - 1) introduce financial lessons and concepts to young children in fun, developmentally-appropriate and meaningful ways
          - 2) develop the financial knowledge of kindergarten learners
          - 3) develop money-management skills among young learners
          - 4) instill positive financial attitude among pupils
      - KEY TAKEAWAYS/RESULTS (qualitative study)
        - Storytelling, games/role-playing/simulations, piggy banks, etc helpful for kindergarteners
        - Content included saving/goal setting, spending wisely, money management, sharing/giving/charity
        - Not quantitative measurements of financial knowledge progress (children too young to do more complex math/abstract thinking + exploratory nature of study)
    - Financial Education Program for Early Childhood Education (==Curugan==) (*)
      - Curugan (2016) [International Journal of Academic Research in Business and Social Sciences](https://www.academia.edu/54724302/Financial_Education_Program_for_Early_Childhood_Education)
      - Sherraden, Johnson, Guo, and Elliot (2011) **examined an innovative four-year school-based financial education and savings program**. In their study, children who participated in the financial education and savings program scored significantly higher than those who did not.
      - Children should be given access to financial services through a savings account
      - Grody, Grody, Kromann, and Sutliff (2008) conducted pilot study regarding a financial literacy and financial services program for 3rd graders. Their study examined the effectiveness of teaching financial literacy to elementary school children. In their study,** they used a reading book on financial literacy read aloud by a school librarian and had a visit to a bank as part of the financial education program. Result of their study shows that the pilot group had significant improvement in their financial learning. **The researchers suggest on using other materials other than books such as concrete visuals, experiential learning, and workbooks to support financial education programs in schools. 
      - In their article on Financial Education for Preschoolers, Birbili and Kontopoulou (2015) give two of the most effective teaching approaches to finance education. First, **incorporate financial concepts and money management skills into the children’s daily social interactions** with adults and other children, and providing relevant activities that take into consideration their cognitive limitations, and second, **make the experience as closely related to children’s personal experience.**
    - A Holistic Approach to Financial Literacy Education (==Blue==)
      - Blue (2015) [ACRN Journal of Finance and Risk Perspectives](https://www.academia.edu/15063926/A_Holistic_Approach_to_Financial_Literacy_Education)
      - This paper focuses on an alternative approach to FinLit education: one that is focused on a holistic and sustainable approach, underpinned by realistic expectations (unspecified age group)
      - CURRENT FL PROBLEMS (applicable to K-8):
        - Time of implementation: programs = short/fragmented. Little follow-up and implementation, no continued support
      - LIMITATIONS OF TRADITIONAL FINANCIAL EDUCATION
        - Generic content fails - (especially those aimed at low-income adults) rarely lead to meaningful change. They often ignore the participant’s context, foundational skills, and real-life barriers
        - Knowledge alone isn't enough - simply providing information does not reliably change behavior, especially if the program ignores attitudes, beliefs, and social context
        - Risk of blame - programs that promise financial well-being through knowledge alone can inadvertently blame individuals for outcomes that are shaped by systemic issues
      - HOLISTIC FL SUGGESTIONS
        - Tailored to individual goals
        - Critical thinking and reflection (not just memorizing)
        - Sustained access to education over time
        - Support networks with peers
        - Move away from deficit thinking: empower self-efficacy
        - Go beyond budgeting, must also address financial awareness/attitudes, decision-making skills, understanding of behavioral biases, social/cultural dimensions 
    - Personal Finance Education: Effective Practice Guide for Schools (==Spielhofer==) (*)
      - Spielhofer (2010) [National Foundation for Educational Research](https://www.academia.edu/26653641/Personal_Finance_Education_Effective_Practice_Guide_for_Schools)
      - 2 different implementations of FinLit
        - Math-based course:
          - 6 lessons of personal finance education in mathematics lessons over a two-week period for year 11 students
          - Financial topics were also integrated into regular math schemes (e.g., using percentages in real-world financial contexts). Teachers adapted resources for different ability levels and ensured content was relevant to current economic events
        - PSHE course (personal, social, health, economic education):
          - With the help of PFEG, teachers developed 8-12 lessons focusing on a variety of topics, including budgeting, salaries for different careers, different types of bank accounts and the cost of mobile phones. They praised PFEG for providing lots of interactive resources to support the lessons, which also allowed for differentiation across the ability range:
            - The school has used a variety of teaching approaches including group work, class discussions and independent research. As part of one lesson focusing on mobile phones, students worked in groups and then had to present to other students which mobile phone they would choose and why
      - RESULTS:
        - Students in the math-based financial literacy program improved their average scores from 4.125 to 6.5 out of 10, nearly a 50% increase in knowledge
        - Students reported feeling more confident about money management, budgeting, and understanding the risks of borrowing
    - Evaluation of Learning Money Matters (LMM). Final Report (==Spielhofer==)
      - Spielhofer (2009) [Program Evaluation](https://www.academia.edu/26653639/Evaluation_of_Learning_Money_Matters_LMM_Final_Report)
      - Article provides insight into what ages are taught FL, time teaching, what materials were used
      - Target group: high school students (primarily 11-12th grade), special emphasis on low-income/underserved youth
      - Curriculum design:
        - 12 lessons to be delivered in traditional classroom settings
        - Aligned with academic standards in math/language arts for smooth classroom integration
        - Hands-on activities - emphasized IRL applications of skills rather than rote learning
          - Managing mock bank accounts, role-playing financial decision making, using budgeting worksheets
        - Teachers trained on implementing LMM effectively, but fidelity of implementation varied, with some teachers adapting or omitting content
        - Exposure to LMM was greatest among comprehensive schools with students aged 11-16 and 11-18
        - Time spent delivering PFE (personal finance education) varied considerably between schools
          - Min = 3 hours (school allocated a 30 minute lesson to each of 6 financial subjects
          - Max = 25 lesson periods
          - Mean = 6-8 lessons
      - Results:
        - RCT where students assigned either LMM or standard instruction
        - LMM students scored significantly higher on posttests than control, specifically in budgeting, banking, and credit concepts
        - Attitude shifts = positive, toward saving and budgeting
        - Behavioral changes (bank account usage, saving) = modest, likely due to short follow-up time and limited independence of students
      - Implications:
        - Embed financial learning in IRL scenarios
        - Tie to academic standards
        - Focus on conceptual understanding, not just facts (something the holistic predictive dashboard can help with, see DOK 3 insights -> competitive advantage)
        - Prioritize teacher support (specifically training for teachers on how to teach FinLit
        - Track attitudes + behaviors, not just scores (via decision logs, journal entries, or in-game goals)
        - Consistent engagement (inconsistent use of LMM reduced its effectiveness)
      - Schools emphasized the need for a flexible teaching approach to maintain student interest and engagement in PFE.
        - Internet downloaded materials
        - Resources provided by PFEG (Personal Finance Education Group).
        - School-developed resources, often created with pfeg’s assistance.
        - Money Sense website
        - Bank leaflets explaining how to open accounts
        - Materials from Connexions on the Educational Maintenance Allowance (EMA)
        - Games from PFEG consultants for budgeting skills
      - Teachers' confidence: two-thirds of respondents thought that LMM had helped them become more confident about teaching PFE (personal finance education) than before (15% said that they were confident already before receiving the support)
      - The study suggests that a one-off day focussing on PFE is not enough to have an impact on student’s knowledge and attitudes and that the impact of PFE is the greatest in those schools that dedicate a series of up to 25 lessons per year group to this topic area.
    - Financial Literacy of People Gaining Economic Education in the Context of Acquired Practical Experience (==Kozubik==)
      - Kozubik (2022) [International Scientific Conference](https://www.academia.edu/116513890/Financial_Literacy_of_People_Gaining_Economic_Education_in_the_Context_of_Acquired_Practical_Experience)
      - Target audience: low income individuals, single mothers, unemployed/underemployed, with limited formal education
      - Low baseline financial knowledge: interest accumulation, credit card repayment consequences, budgeting/banking terminology
      - Positive attitudes, suboptimal behaviors:
        - Participants generally wanted to learn and improve
        - Poor behavioral patterns persisted (insufficient budgeting, no emergency savings, irregular bill payment), often due to stress, lack of practice, or resource scarcity
      - Barriers to FinLit learning:
        - Emotional stress, competing responsibilities, low self-confidence
        - Difficulty accessing/trusting financial institutions
        - Skepticism about financial advisors
      - Effective delivery preferences:
        - Participants liked interactive, practical, and visual methods
        - Hands-on methods instead of lecture/reading-heavy formats
        - Topics most relevant to daily life (paying bills, managing a grocery budget)
      - Specifcally, the functional areas of the P-FIN index are determined as:
        - Earnings, determinants of wages and income
        - Consuming, budgeting and spending
        - Saving, comprehension of the accumulation factors
        - Investing, understanding the types and risks of investments
        - Borrowing and debt management
        - Risk management, comprehension of the uncertain outcomes
        - Insurance and the understanding of coverages
        - Accessing and working with information sources
      - Confirmed a higher percentage of students with a greater range of practical experience with financial sector products and services. This difference was reflected in the overall level of financial literacy as measured by the P-FIN index and also in the individual functional areas of financial literacy
      - Findings
        - Financial Knowledge: very low baseline, particularly in credit, loans, and interest calculation
        - Attitudes Toward Learning: positive — most participants wanted to improve
        - Actual Financial Behavior: often inconsistent with goals due to stress, poor planning, or misinformation
        - Preferred Learning Style: practical, role-play based, game-like or visual learning
    - Evaluation of the youth financial literacy trial program (==Russell==)
      - Russell (2006) [RMIT](https://www.academia.edu/25567793/Evaluation_of_the_youth_financial_literacy_trial_program)
      - Pre and post training surveys for the 104 middle school participants
      - Primary aim: increase student knowledge of financial issues with **phones** and **credit cards** 
      - 73.5% had a positive experience with the program
      - 94-95% had increases in knowledge about phones and credit cards
        - Measured by survey (self-assessed/reported), not measured by a comparison of pre/post test performance
      - Suggestions for improvement: 
        - Home/car loans, health insurance (these might not be very relevant to middle school)
        - Speaker from bank/phone company to help students understand better
        - More abt credit cards/phones
        - Info about job earnings
        - Different things for other lessons, not just credit cards
        - Watch video to see how things are in the real world
    - Financial Literacy of Graduate High School Students (==Tsakiridou==)
      - Tsakiridou (2019) [American Journal of Educational Research](https://www.academia.edu/116749437/Financial_Literacy_of_Graduate_High_School_Students)
      - Random sample from a high school in Thessaloniki, Greece
      - Performance among students was roughly the same regardless of sex, native country, intent of continued education, parental education, and ownership of a bank account
      - Performance DID VARY based on family income and work experience
        - Students with work experience showed lower levels of financial literacy than students who have never worked officially. (???)
    - Evidence for Second Language Socialization in Multiplayer Online Game Play (==Shahrokni==)
      - Shahrokni (2021) [The Electronic Journal for ESL](https://www.academia.edu/109721998/Evidence_for_Second_Language_Socialization_in_Multiplayer_Online_Game_Play?nav_from=a872d523-8a3f-47aa-adb7-57a024186270)
      - Qualitative study focuses on the use of MMOGs for international students learning English
      - "Group participation and collaboration, negotiations of actions, feedback and scaffolds, authenticity and connection, and balanced challenges are required to make an engaging learning experience" (Csikszentmihalyi, 1990; Levy & Stockwell, 2006; Egbert & Shahrokni, 2018; Zheng et al., 2009).
      - Role of emotions in group dynamics = likely important (29)
    - Game-based Learning and Intrinsic Motivation (==Mead==) (*)
      - Mead (2011) [Game-based Learning](https://www.academia.edu/1137269/Game_based_Learning_and_Intrinsic_Motivation)
      - Project based learning
      - CLT (cognitive load theory) supports forms of GBL
      - Haphazard interactivity can inhibit learning, it was a distractor (5)
      - Game-based learning
      - MMORPGs
        - Competition = strong intrinsic motivator (7)
      - Lee Sheldon (professor who used XP instead of grading) (7)
      - "Game designers have been successful in creating games that are not only intrinsically motivating, but some are down right addicting (Susaeta et al., 2010)"
    - The development of Kwartalino Financial Education Program for kindergarten learners (==Masnan==)
      - Masnan (2020) [Southeast Asia Early Childhood Journal](https://www.academia.edu/78865805/The_development_of_Kwartalino_Financial_Education_Program_for_kindergarten_learners)
      - 6 FinLit concepts identified by experts (20) [lessons are also included in JA Ourselves program by Junior Achievement]
        - Wants/needs
        - Working to earn money
        - Protecting oneself/possessions
        - Spending money
        - Saving money
        - Donating
      - Table 4 = answers from kindergarten teachers on what elements could be helpful (21)
    - The Importance of Financial Literacy Knowledge For Elementary School Students In 21st Century (==Perdanasari==)
      - Perdanasari (2019) [Efektor](https://www.academia.edu/96983889/The_Importance_of_Financial_Literacy_Knowledge_For_Elementary_School_Students_In_21st_Century)
      - Stages of learning (29)
        - 1) 5-6: students are expected to begin to recognize the concept of money. Money is a means of buying and selling that serves to buy goods or pay for services; 
        - 2) 7-9: students are expected to begin to recognize the culture of saving and begin to instill ways to use wise money, even though the money is self and determines short-term targets
        - 3) 13-15: students are expected to start learning money planning in a simple way and start to know about banking products such as ATMs, debit cards, and debit cards, what their functions are and their differences
    - Financial Literacy Programs Targeted on Preschool Children: Development and Evaluation (==Kalish==)
      - Kalish (2009) [University of Wisconsin-Madison](https://www.academia.edu/20194855/Financial_literacy_programs_targeted_on_pre_school_children_Development_and_evaluation)
      - Theoretical Foundations of Financial Literacy in Children
        - Piagetian Theory (Stage-Based Development) (pg 10-11)
          - Young children (preoperational stage) struggle with abstract concepts like value, interest, and credit
          - Should focus on tangible aspects (size of coins not actual worth)
        - Theory Theory/Core Knowledge Approach (pg 12-13)
          - Children develop theories about the world based on their experiences (connects to Piagetian idea of schemas)
          - FinLit should challenge naïve theories that children form, such as thinking that more coins are better than fewer bills​ (adjusting schemas to fit new information)
          - Financial literacy should be integrated into children's everyday experiences rather than taught as isolated lessons​ (support for a game that kids can play around the clock)
        - Brain Development and Executive Function (pg 14-15)
          - The prefrontal cortex (governs planning, delayed gratification, impulse control) is not fully developed in young children.
          - Financial education programs should take into account that younger children struggle with future-oriented thinking and need strategies to reinforce concepts like saving​
      - Key Financial Concepts for Young Children
        - Numeracy and Basic Money Understanding (pg 16-17)
          - Teaching children about numbers, values of coins and bills, and simple counting strategies
          - Early exposure to savings and spending choices helps in building lifelong financial habits​
        - Concept of Exchange and Value (pg 18-19)
          - Children should engage in transactions like trading and allowance management
          - Experiences with money should reflect real-world exchanges to strengthen understanding
        - Delayed Gratification and Saving (pg 25-26)
          - Young children find it difficult to delay rewards (undeveloped prefrontal cortex)
          - Strategies like visual reminders (goal charts) and labeling future rewards as special occasions can make saving more concrete​
        - Understanding Financial Institutions (pg 23-24)
          - Since children rarely interact with banks, simplified activities like "pretend banking" can help them understand saving accounts and credit​
        - Choice and Decision-Making (pg 27-28)
          - Programs should introduce basic decision-making skills like distinguishing needs vs. wants and making trade-offs​
        - Social Values and Financial Responsibility (pg 29-30)
          - Teaching concepts like generosity, sharing, and community welfare
          - Helping children understand taxes, public goods, and the role of financial institutions​
      - Assessment of Existing Financial Literacy Programs for Children
        - Lack of Consistency Across Programs (pg 7-8)
          - Different programs teach different concepts without a standardized curriculum, and there is no uniform agreement on what key financial concepts should be included​
        - Limited Evaluations of Program Effectiveness (pg 8-9)
          - Most programs for preschoolers rely on parental involvement rather than formal classroom instruction.
          - Few studies rigorously assess the long-term impact of financial education on children's financial behaviors
        - Need for Age-Appropriate Educational Approaches (pg 10-11)
          - Financial concepts must be introduced in ways that align with children’s cognitive abilities (learning science and Piagetian theory)
          - Programs should use storytelling, games, and interactive activities rather than abstract explanations​
        - Parental Involvement is Crucial (pg 12-13)
          - Most young children do not interact with financial institutions independently, so effective financial literacy programs should provide parents with tools to reinforce lessons at home
      - Recommendations for Building a Financial Literacy Curriculum for Children
        - Align Financial Education with Cognitive Development (pg 14-15)
          - Start with concrete financial experiences before introducing abstract concepts
          - Use everyday interactions (shopping, allowances, piggy banks) as learning opportunities​
        - Incorporate Interactive and Play-Based Learning (pg 17-18)
          - Games, role-playing, and hands-on activities can help children grasp complex financial ideas
          - Storytelling with characters making financial choices can reinforce lessons​
        - Teach Core Financial Habits Early (pg 19-20)
          - Introduce the concepts of earning, spending, and saving before kindergarten
          - Help children develop habits like setting goals and planning for purchases​
        - Involve Parents and Caregivers (pg 22-23)
          - Provide resources for parents to teach financial lessons at home
          - Encourage discussions about money in everyday situations
        - Introduce Simple Budgeting and Decision-Making Tools (pg 25-26)
          - Use visual tools like sticker charts for tracking savings
          - Teach simple trade-offs (like choosing between two toys)​
        - Evaluate and Adapt the Curriculum Regularly (pg 28-29)
          - Programs should be tested for effectiveness through behavioral assessments
          - Adjustments should be made based on children’s responses and learning progress
    - Bringing Voluntary Financial Education in Emerging Economy: Role of Financial Socialization During Elementary Years (==Mada==)
      - Mada (2017) [Asia Pacific Edu Res](https://www.academia.edu/40565671/Bringing_Voluntary_Financial_Education_in_Emerging_Economy_Role_of_Financial_Socialization_During_Elementary_Years)
      - Financial Intelligence Curriculum for grades 1-6
        - Designed to be given for 4 weeks, 45 min each
        - Focus: need/want, priority needs, income, spending, saving, and sharing
        - Pre/post tests and treatment/control groups
          - Pre/post tests: 21 MC questions, adjusted to grade (1-4 had more images)
        - Interactive teaching method
        - Each lesson included a short lecture and worksheet, IT-based educational games, and story as a learning medium.
        - Integrated into existing subjects (math/language/arts). No extended school days or classes needed
      - Hypotheses:
        - Hypothesis 1 Financial education for elementary school students has positive impacts on ﬁnancial knowledge (CONFIRMED)
        - Hypothesis 2 After receiving ﬁnancial education, boys have higher score in ﬁnancial knowledge than girls. (REJECTED)
        - Hypothesis 3 Parents’ education and income level have positive impact on ﬁnancial knowledge. (PARTIALLY CONFIRMED: parental education = positive effect, income level = negative effect)
        - Hypothesis 4 GPA has a positive impact on ﬁnancial knowledge. (REJECTED)
    - Shaping Students’ Financial Literacy: The Role of Parents and SocioEconomic Backgrounds (==OECD==)
      - Shows histogram of countries with varying student proficiencies
      - Students who are autonomous in their spending decisions score 30 pts higher (on avg) compared to those who are not.
