    - Financial literacy programs targeted on pre-school children: Development and evaluation (==Kalish==)
      - Kalish (2009)  [University of Wisconsin-Madison](https://www.academia.edu/20194855/Financial_literacy_programs_targeted_on_pre_school_children_Development_and_evaluation)
      - Questions seeking to answer:
        - 1. What are the financial concepts (outcomes) that are the stepping stones to financial decisions making and that young children are able to grasp?
          - Saving/spending
          - Money + its value
          - Delayed gratification
          - Budgeting + planning skills
        - 2. Which programs (interventions) do this and how?
          - Interactive activities, games, storytelling, parental involvement
        - 3. What is the best way (intervention method) to deliver these lessons?
          - Experiential learning: shopping simulations, earning rewards, savings challenges
          - Integration into core subjects?
          - Digital tools (apps + games)
        - 4. What are the practical and legal constraints on and opportunities for the delivery these lessons to young children?
          - Constraints: lack of curriculum standardization, teacher knowledge limitations, time constraints, legal/regulatory
          - Opportunities: support from nonprofits, parental engagement initiatives
        - 5. How can programs be evaluated, including the gathering of data on later outcomes of young children’s early learning?
          - Longitudinal studies: pre/post tests, behavioral assessments, follow-up surveys
        - 6. How should these early childhood education programs targeting financial knowledge be integrated with other education goals for that age group and with financial literacy education at older ages?
          - Article suggests integrating FinLit into a broader life skills education
      - Important conclusion:
        - Financial education programs should focus on high priority issues, which, depending on national circumstances, may include important aspects of financial life planning such as basic savings, private debt management or insurance as well as pre-requisites for financial awareness such as elementary financial mathematics and economics 
      - Core schemes (discussed in cited study)
        - Numeracy, literacy and information skills in the context of financial literacy
        - How to assess, interpret, question and evaluate finances
        - How to understand the nature and use of money
        - The consequences of financial decisions
        - The rights and responsibilities of customers
        - How to weigh risks and benefits 
    - The Impact of Financial Literacy Education on Subsequent Financial Behavior (==Mandell + Klein==)
      - Mandell + Klein (2007) [Association for Financial Counseling and Planning Education](https://files.eric.ed.gov/fulltext/EJ859556.pdf)
      - Each of the schools (experimental and control) offered a highly regarded course in personal financial management. The course lasted a full semester and covered all aspects of personal financial management that were thought to be relevant and important to students.
      - Main finding: those who had taken a personal finance course in high school were no more financially literate than those who had not
        - Students who had taken the course averaged a score of 68.7%, and those who had not averaged a score of 69.9%
      - Evidence suggests that student motivation was a key factor in increasing the financial literacy of participants (motivated adults can benefit from targeted financial education)
    - Financial Literacy and Financial Education: An Overview (==Kaiser==)
      - Kaiser (2024) [IZA Institute of Labor Economics](https://download.ssrn.com/iza/dp16926.pdf?response-content-disposition=inline&X-Amz-Security-Token=IQoJb3JpZ2luX2VjEB0aCXVzLWVhc3QtMSJHMEUCIEdqTqH9q5j68yOtSNvmrsi2gqCVfFCa1HEJR1QT6gzFAiEAl79kD11aAyzDXZ0gi0ZSQd03YdrFSr3usrchgrLNGMIqxwUIlv%2F%2F%2F%2F%2F%2F%2F%2F%2F%2FARAEGgwzMDg0NzUzMDEyNTciDBo16dv9IkhB%2BwB5nSqbBTOZ2Y7FH8HUBrd2fwShDNFKTx%2F8S186kRw8GoYTaV6YwmrlTnZQ%2Bpq59PnnAuC8RJ2WwDUdpIi2jeuy7AFAVGqXDv%2FAcenwnnmhBfib2ifmuCNfY8OUcH8fHBvkp4lJwZIQGudSEGKwL%2FXaqj97efimGbLT2Ym2feaDuAy60SM8kdlIlh7UIyaXs7%2FNMV9JPOYzFGTRoekL9qocorlVuPhP%2Bu%2B5sfHNj7byoyJ6uo92AOK2XJB%2BXDeA5yJiqDrJ8njZ5DogJscyh2p8NF4Y3PmKgrzKcUYb0D8VOfV3WVNPhD7kz3B%2BupnlHv9AiQLNuIvsjKzl3N7o6DwN5RfQc0FUM%2FhiyJgcNwuuc6OoTiMajQTjE4jFO2Ld4UEbFNgPBkWyIxeV8ZrGHUSHdJbdlU6b0vPEQKN8EEDlC%2BNg4zleoN%2FgRxcdWR0wW9hpjFuBz2ZwJJTfFAydwfYHGxobzfuAwT4VdUuWREBQXWu6OjLzPeDK6lS51EFmIe86Heqg81oCCQmYs0U6mg%2BZC%2BRmAOUFnSrsun97Rrpe0kshk1AjCh7nA%2Fo3HIgq1PnQ8G4ssB1cg0ysPw558EzhrD2gab%2BaCXrzEBkOkzb1xneHAoFAf%2BlrQf3hIKyC7sZLtNR3BNNeeGvvMkTRkgKX1Lpg7ebAmU4FRLrRmENOT5zkjrSN1%2Fzpqclmr9jblZTFf1tbklOXPsLTT%2BZCAMVQuQe1RTz%2BYDceUym1rdWElJyxS2kKtr0RV5wRKHRdCfnF4PJRgmm4qTN2OLpc3PBMj3qSo4N7jfEQPX2WVXKzG4gfLUpBdaaRIJ5ceI%2Ff9mIeYl0DJvGCEy2rJuckv3NZrUVay1%2FpIsem4y0bpY8vGUZZyGLNloNgZ042FxNWTA8w%2Fr3bvwY6sQHVfX96i89YmH%2BO5KMGjakO%2Buz6CDzrZFxRo%2BqjEAwqfT6IB5M5cDe86OijOjHDbjg%2FmFJP3AYvrH53gIiU%2FAFaJhApOFyobA3mC3GfnUG6sMMq0r2Z1rQT0OxcVPfAvYsBGbmxqKhu%2FUtnQtJk1DBhD3wyX8YkQ1AF%2F2C733xQrWoqbWvxj5njfcrL5EiuoBxr8W9tMcXAHsoPVIMMVLeCWlCAldKgN%2BEZIk9FsZdAkfA%3D&X-Amz-Algorithm=AWS4-HMAC-SHA256&X-Amz-Date=20250409T212126Z&X-Amz-SignedHeaders=host&X-Amz-Expires=300&X-Amz-Credential=ASIAUPUUPRWEYIZAGPIX%2F20250409%2Fus-east-1%2Fs3%2Faws4_request&X-Amz-Signature=b0308cef5f7fa0f4d9641ad26a8b8869ea11e935f2a2b81a698242d97913cd9a&abstractId=4800263)
      - Article is an overview of literature on financial education
      - Findings
        - Financial education has causal effect on FinLit score - average intervention boosts financial literacy scores by about 15-20% of a SD
        - Interventions change financial behaviors - average effect is estimated to be about 6 to 10% of a SD
        - Treatment effects = highly heterogenous (16)
        - Financial education programs vary in:
          - Intensity/depth
          - Format
          - Participant age
        - More classroom time = bigger impact (Kaiser et al., 2022; Kaiser & Menkhoff, 2020)
        - Light-touch interventions (like just giving info) have much smaller effects (Choi et al., 2010; Goda et al., 2014)
        - Newer research shows classroom-based programs are generally effective
        - Younger students: see bigger gains in financial knowledge
        - Adults: show more changes in financial behavior
        - RCT interventions = low costs and high cost/effectiveness ratio
          - Avg = $60, median = $20
        - "As expected, we found that intensity is positively correlated with larger treatment effect estimates at a rate of about +0.03% for each additional hour of classroom exposure"
      - What works in financial education?
        - Recommendations:
          - (a) Relevant to the life situation of those targeted by the program
          - (b) Accessible, entertaining, and actively engaging
          - (c) Scalable with moderate marginal cost per participant
        - Innovative programs for adults (20)
          - RCTs examined programs that evaluate alternatives to classroom-based settings
          - Approaches include
            - Tailoring interventions to target groups
            - Simplifying curricula by introducing "rules of thumb"
            - Introducing personalized elements like counseling
            - Using mass media to communicate financial info + change attitudes
            - Relying on experiential learning to remove biases
            - Relying on digital delivery + GAMIFICATION (Attanasio 2019; Sconti 2022)
            - Adapting teaching pedagogy to include active learning + group exercises (as opposed to lecture-based formats)
            - Relying on decentralized teaching responsibility
      - Evidence on Causal Mechanisms
        - "While there is now robust evidence that financial education, on average, is successful in changing financial behavior, the causal mechanism translating educational inputs into action is less well understood."
      - Outlook and Research Priorities in FinLit and FinEd
        - On average, individuals with more patience appear to be more financially literate (Bianchi 2018; Oberrauch and Kaiser 2022).
        - Stango and Zinman (2023) and Chapman et al. (2023) have recently studied the dimensionality of behavioral biases and derived a taxonomy of consumer financial decision-making
    - Freeing Financial Education via Tablets: Experimental Evidence from Colombia (==Attanasio==)
      - Attanasio (2019) [NBER Working Paper Series](https://www.nber.org/system/files/working_papers/w25929/w25929.pdf)
      - "Our results show that the intervention had significant positive impacts on financial knowledge, attitudes, practices, and performance, translating into increased financial health 25 months after the end of the intervention"
      - Heterogeneity in impacts:
        - Larger effect on poorer, less educated, younger, more rural groups
      - Tablet-based application - 3 principles
        - No physical presence of a trainer
        - Individually paced
        - Customizable content - users focus on relevant topics
      - Idea: financial education has greater effect in domains where people have more autonomy (budgeting/savings instead of loan default)
      - "Average user engaged with the application for 3-4 hours, though this does not account for the possibility that there were multiple users in the same beneficiary household"
    - Cognitive Development (==Malik==)
      - Malik (2023) [National Library of Medicine](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/books/NBK537095/)
      - Article focuses on theoretical concepts of childhood psychological development
      - Piaget
        - Children learn by *assimilation* + *accommodation*
        - *Equilibration* also plays a role (as described further [here](https://www.earlyyears.tv/piagets-theory-of-cognitive-development/#:~:text=Key%20Features%20of%20Piaget's%20Theory&text=Constructivist%20approach%20to%20learning:%20Piaget,of%20thought%20to%20the%20next.))
          - Balance between assimilation + accommodation 
          - Process of achieving a balance between schemas (existing frameworks of knowledge) and new knowledge
        - Preschool (2-5 years)
          - Magical/wishful thinking, logical solutions sometimes not sufficient
          - Poor concept of causation
          - Egocentric perception (like giving a toy to an upset person)
          - Play stories more detailed (4-5 years)
          - Rules = absolute
        - Older childhood (6-12 years)
          - Understand multiple POVs
          - Rules can change with mutual agreement
          - 4th-5th grade: learning to read --> reading to learn
        - Adolescence (12+)
          - Abstract thinking, logic --> scientific solution
    - Banzai Teachers on Teaching: FinLit and Preparing for the Future Zoom Call
      - Teaching Curriculum - Lessons that Resonate?
        - Differences between debit/credit cards, what are elements of paying credit card, what happens when you don't pay card off, experiment with decisions of paying/not paying in Banzai games, kids love being able to see consequences of actions
        - Teaching 5th grade, do lemonade stand, use coins + money to do decimal operations, many students don't know what coins are, Banzai helps teach them what coins are, don't know it until they have to use it (not actual money but they feel like it is), go through experiment and what result they can get
      - Outside of Grading, how to gauge understanding?
        - High school: using vocab (credit scores, filing taxes), putting things together is a sign that they get it (credit score, deductions, etc)
      - Challenges this Year in Teaching FinLit + Solutions
        - Where teachers work: how to prepare different students differs
        - Finlit tends to cater to those with better financial backgrounds, how to ensure that 
        - Make FinLit a conversation not "this is the only way," understanding what situation is, Banzai tends to make games not judgmental, just seeing outcomes
      - What tools for your own professional development?
        - To become better financial literacy teacher?
        - Staying connected to world and knowing what changes, using tests + curriculum before use, recognizing new financial technologies,

      - Released stock market game at Banzai, trying to be realistic but discouraging the "get rich quick" schemes

    - Financial Literacy, Financial Education, and Downstream Financial Behaviors (==Fernandes==)
      - Fernandes (2014) [Management Science](https://www.researchgate.net/publication/259763070_Financial_Literacy_Financial_Education_and_Downstream_Financial_Behaviors)
      - Interventions to improve financial literacy explain only 0.1% of the variance in financial behaviors (effect even weaker in low-income samples)
      - The impact of financial education diminishes rapidly - even large, multi-hour interventions have negligible effects on behavior after 20 months
      - The evidence suggests that the effectiveness of financial education as currently practiced is extremely limited
      - Authors recommend a reduced role for general financial education, especially if not immediately relevant or reinforced.
        - Instead, they advocate for “just-in-time” financial education (targeted, timely instruction tied to specific financial decisions like receiving mortgage counseling when applying for a loan)
      - The current model of broad, classroom-based financial education is not effective at changing long-term financial behavior
      - The benefits of financial education decay quickly unless the knowledge is applied soon after it is learned
  - ==SCAFFOLDING/GAMIFICATION/LEARNING SCIENCE==
    - Scaffolding
      - Scaffolding education [for language learning] (==Ertugruloglu==)
        - Ertugruloglu et al (2021) [Educational Research Review](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1747938X2300043X#sec2)
        - Paper goal: clarify the types of language scaffolding that are used by researchers to analyze scaffolding in secondary bilingual education contexts 
        - Characteristics of scaffolding (Van de Pol et al. p. 275).
          - **Contingency** ‘the teacher's support must be adapted to the current level of the student's performance and should either be at the same or a slightly higher level.’ Use of diagnostic strategies - teacher determines the student's current level of competence.
          - **Fading** applies ‘when the level and/or the amount of support is decreased over time’ by a teacher.
          - **Transfer of responsibility** responsibility for learning transferred when a student takes increasing learner control
        - Across studies of scaffolding, increased responsibility/autonomy (IRA), contingency, and fading were the most common occurrences. The occurrence of diagnostic strategies was extremely rare. (section 4.1)
      - Teacher scaffolding in game-based learning in primary education (==Sun==) (*)
        - Sun et al (2022) [Educational Research Review](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1747938X23000398)
        - Research questions
          - (1) What games have been used in game-based learning research? 
          - (2) What kinds of scaffolding strategies are used by teachers in teacher–student interactions within game-based learning in primary education? 
          - (3) What empirical evidence exists regarding the influence of the scaffolding strategies used in game-based learning on primary students’ education?
        - Puzzle games (n = 7) were the most popular genre, closely followed by construction games (n = 5), simulation games (n = 2), role-playing games (n = 2), adventure games (n = 2), and strategy games (n = 1), all but one of the games were digital, a majority were collaborative, but 9 studies were individual. in most of the studies, teacher = facilitator
        - Game-based learning (GBL) means (1) using action instead of explanation, (2) creating personal motivation and satisfaction, (3) accommodating multiple learning styles and skills, (4) reinforcing mastery of skills, and (5) providing an interactive and decision-making context
          - A number of studies have identified that game-based learning has many benefits for teaching and learning in primary education, especially because it is able to capture students’ interests and promote their learning of basic knowledge However, concerns have also been raised regarding the negative effects of games on learning ([Van Eck, 2006](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1747938X23000398#bib103); [Wen, 2018](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1747938X23000398#bib110)).
            - Problem with game-based learning: novice or **inexperienced** students find it **difficult to apply prior knowledge to gameplay, which leads to low motivation and engagement **([Chen & Law, 2016](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1747938X23000398#bib21)) - highlights the necessity of integrating teacher scaffolding into game-based learning, particularly in primary education, to help students develop the habit of self-regulation and build connections between game content and subject knowledge 
        - Scaffolding associated with zone of proximal development 
          - According to Vygotsky's (1978) theory, **learning** is considered a **social activity** in which interaction and language play central roles. Moreover, it emphasizes that **learning happens within the ZPD**, which refers to the difference between what a student can do independently and what s/he can do with the help of a more knowledgeable other (scaffolding = support provided within a student's ZPD)
        - How scaffolding in a game-based setting might not work
          - Barzilai and Blau (2014) stated that integrating teacher scaffolding into game-based learning might “negatively impact learners’ perception of learning and enjoyment in the game” (p. 65)
          - However, our findings also indicate that in some studies, the scaffolding provided by the teachers in the gameplay stage had negative influences. Some researchers (e.g., Boticki et al., 2015; Bragg, 2012) have noticed that teacher-led discussion might have passive effects on students' knowledge acquisition because the students might end up spending more time engaging in the discussion than learning through the game. 
            - This likely happens when teachers provide too much support, hindering the transfer of autonomy to the student
        - RESULTS
          - Emphasized that due to their age (6-13 years old), primary school students may benefit more from dynamically generated teacher scaffolding strategies appropriate for game-based learning ([Dukuzumuremyi & Siklander, 2018](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1747938X23000398#bib3), [Sun et al., 2021a](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1747938X23000398#bib4), [Sun et al., 2021b](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1747938X23000398#bib5); [Haataja et al., 2019](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1747938X23000398#bib35)).
          - Notably, many researchers have highlighted the importance of integrating teacher scaffolding into the orientation stage rather than only the gameplay stage ([Sun et al., 2021b](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1747938X23000398#bib5)).
      - Identifying Indicators to Guide Adaptive Scaffolding in Games (==Faber==)
        - Faber et al (2021) [Learning and Instruction](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0959475222000871)
        - 1st vs 2nd order scaffolding
          - 1st = learning of domain-specific skills [immediate, provided by teacher or peer, completing current task, reactive to specific needs]
          - 2nd = self-regulatory skills [prep for future independent tasks, provided by tools, rubrics, frameworks, developing long term autonomy, proactive - anticipating future challenges]
          - Example: when learners cannot complete a task because they don't know how to use a specific tool, we can demonstrate how the tool works, applying 1st order scaffolding; or we can explain where to find information about the tool, applying 2nd order scaffolding and aiming to further the development of problem-solving skills
        - RQ (research question): how indicators of performance and self-regulation relate to overall game performance in a medical emergency simulation game. These indicators have the potential to guide the design of adaptive first-order and second-order scaffolding, respectively. 
          - This paper aims to identify indicators of performance in GBL (game-based learning) that may be used as input for adaptive scaffolding.
        - Important to diagnose SLA (student level of attainment) to correctly adjust (1) CONTINGENCY, (2) FADING, (3) TRANSFER OF RESPONSIBILITY
        - 1st order scaffolding success variables: accuracy, systematicity in approach, thoroughness
          - Systematicity example: If a learner deviates from a correct order, their systematicity in approach becomes lower, suggesting their cognitive strategies for the task are underdeveloped.
        - 2nd order scaffolding success variables: SRL (self-regulated learning)
          - On a global level, SRL can be measured by self-report measures such as the Motivated Strategies for Learning Questionnaire (MSLQ) ([Pintrich et al., 1991](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0959475222000871#bib41)). On a more context-specific level, SRL can be measured using self-regulated learning microanalysis (SRL-MA) by asking questions during a learning activity regarding specific SRL aspects, such as goal-setting or monitoring
          - Students showing lower systematicity profit more from monitoring (i.e. consulting the checklist) than students showing higher systematicity
        - Another study found that more failures before initial success predicted greater learning gains than time on task ([Anderson et al., 2018](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0959475222000871#bib2)).
        - Conclusions
          - Valid indicators of performance and SRL in a medical emergency simulation game. Systematicity in approach and thoroughness of performance can be regarded as valid behavioral indicators of overall performance, and as such can be incorporated into an algorithm to guide 1st order scaffolding. 
          - Different effects of ‘normal play’, intended to perform well at the task, and ‘dark play’, intended to worsen the patient's condition. Both types of play resulted in learning gains. 
      - Computer-Based Scaffolding (==Belland==)
        - Belland (2016) [Instructional Scaffolding in STEM Education](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-3-319-02565-0_3)
        - Covers problem-based learning (PBL), case-based learning, design-based learning, inquiry-based learning (IBL), project-based learning
          - **Project-based learning** is typically more structured than problem-based learning in that its **deliverable is more well-specified**. At the end of project-based learning, students typically produce the target product, and then engage in some sort of reflection, which can include the creation of a portfolio
            - Project-based learning can lead to strong gains in design skills on the part of elementary school students (Barron et al., [1998](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-3-319-02565-0_3#ref-CR9)) 
            - can be very motivating (Blumenfeld et al., [1991](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-3-319-02565-0_3#ref-CR22); Helle, Tynjälä, Olkinuora, & Lonka, [2007](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-3-319-02565-0_3#ref-CR55))
          - That the **effect size** of computer-based scaffolding was **lowest when paired with problem-based learning** may be explained by the **open-ended nature** of problem-based learning. Problem-based learning requires self-directed learning perhaps to the greatest extent among the covered problem-centered instructional approaches
            - Problem-based learning students are responsible not only for defining the problem, but also determining what they need to know to come up with a solution, finding the information, and synthesizing the information to determine a solution 
            - Students have less structure from the inherent nature of problem-based learning than they would have from inquiry-, case- , project- , or design-based learning 
          - IBL: characterized by overt foci on students (a) posing their own questions early in the process (Edelson, Gordin, & Pea, [1999](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-3-319-02565-0_3#ref-CR44)) and (b) designing and carrying out an experimental technique to address the generated questions 
            - The model led to an average effect of 0.5 (a medium-large effect) Notably, the effect sizes in the review were **twice as big when teacher support was highest**. According to another review, it may not be inquiry-based learning per se that leads to strong learning outcomes, but **the extent to which students need to analyze authentic data and generate conclusions**
        - Scaffolding had a statistically significantly greater effect among adult learners than among college learners, high school students, middle level students, and primary students
        - CONCLUSION: this article may help order the different learning methods chronologically in the student's career so they start with greater support and end with PBL (largely unguided learning)
      - Facilitating Diagnostic Competences in Higher Education — a Meta-Analysis in Medical and Teacher Education (==Chernikova==) (*)
        - Chernikova et al (2019) [Educational Psychology Review](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s10648-019-09492-2#Abs1)
        - Scaffolding = process of supporting learners by regulating or limiting intricate factors of the task. This objective is accomplished by six *scaffolding functions*: 
          - (a) sparking situational interest
          - (b) reducing the complexity and difficulty of tasks
          - (c) keeping learners focused on their goal
          - (d) highlighting crucial features of a task
          - (e) motivating disappointed learners
          - (f) providing solutions and models of a task
          - The concept of scaffolding builds on Vygotsky’s ([1978](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s10648-019-09492-2#ref-CR100)) notion of ZPD, which includes challenging tasks a learner can perform successfully with external guidance but would not yet be able to perform independently
        - The ***worked example effect* **and the **underlying cognitive load theory** (Sweller [1994](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s10648-019-09492-2#ref-CR89)) suggest that problem-solving at early stages of knowledge or skill acquisition **without** **scaffolding** can lead to an **excessive amount of information** and an inhibition of **schema acquisition** (Renkl [2014](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s10648-019-09492-2#ref-CR72)). [similar to study saying that unguided education = bad bc of too much load on working memory and not connected to long-term memory]
        - Classifying scaffolding based on the amount and kind of guidance, were introduced by Brush and Saye ([2002](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s10648-019-09492-2#ref-CR14)). These authors suggested a dichotomous categorization into 
          - (a) “soft” scaffolding, focused on fostering meta-cognitive skills and self-regulation, and 
          - (b) “hard” scaffolding, focused on content: conceptual or procedural knowledge required to solve the task, providing learners with full or partial solutions to foster learning
          - Brush and Saye ([2002](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s10648-019-09492-2#ref-CR14)) claim that “hard” scaffolding is beneficial for initial stages of learning, whereas “soft” scaffolding is more beneficial when initial knowledge is already acquired
        - Role-taking (taking an agent’s role during the learning phase) had a significant positive effect on advancing diagnostic competences (*g* = .49, *SE* = .11, *p* < .05).
        - Fig 5 shows that those with high prior knowledge benefit most from reflection, while those with low prior knowledge benefit most from examples (support of scaffolding theory). Roles and prompts are in the middle for both groups
        - As long as learners are able to practice problem-solving, all of the different types of scaffolding are beneficial
      - Scaffolding: Definition, Current Debates, and Future Directions (==Belland==)
        - Belland (2013) [Handbook of Research on Ed Com and Tech](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-1-4614-3185-5_39?fromPaywallRec=false)
        - Scaffolding can help students with strategy, what to consider, how to judge the sufficiency of work, and how to use tools (Hannafin et al., [1999](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-1-4614-3185-5_39?fromPaywallRec=false#ref-CR003938)).
        - Different types of scaffolding: one-to-one, peer, computer/paper-based
        - Jadallah et al. ([2011](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-1-4614-3185-5_39?fromPaywallRec=false#ref-CR003942)) examined the process of one-to-one scaffolding in fourth-grade reading discussions. The most frequent scaffolding moves made by the teacher were “(a) asking for clarification, (b) prompting for evidence, (c) praising the use of evidence, and (d) challenging” (Jadallah et al., [2011](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-1-4614-3185-5_39?fromPaywallRec=false#ref-CR003942), p. 208).
          - As the reading discussions progressed, the teacher (a) prompted for evidence less as students spontaneously used evidence more and (b) increased her praising of students’ use of evidence
        - Students who received self-monitoring prompts were significantly more likely to explain phenomena using principles and evidence than students who received activity prompts (Davis & Linn, [2000](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-1-4614-3185-5_39?fromPaywallRec=false#ref-CR003927)). (???)
        - Intelligent Tutoring Systems can engage in dynamic assessment, and thus dynamic customization, because they are often based on set procedures such as programming a VCR (Kalyuga & Sweller, [2005](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-1-4614-3185-5_39?fromPaywallRec=false#ref-CR003947); Koedinger & Corbett, [2006](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-1-4614-3185-5_39?fromPaywallRec=false#ref-CR003949); VanLehn, [2011](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-1-4614-3185-5_39?fromPaywallRec=false#ref-CR0039104)). The key difference between Intelligent Tutoring Systems and scaffolds to support authentic problem solving—the ill-structuredness of the problem solving they can support—also can explain the difference in the capacity to dynamically customize support.
      - Scaffolding Student Understanding Within Student-Centered Inquiry Learning (==Calder==) (*) (~)
        - Calder (2015) [ZDM](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s11858-015-0734-z)
        - Paper does not show quantitative evidence for/against SCIL (student-centered inquiry learning), instead gives practical examples of how it works and theoretical frameworks 
        - Student-centered inquiry learning: SCIL is a democratic teaching approach that aims to utilize relevant and meaningful contexts. Students investigate questions or inquiries into issues that are of genuine interest to them and curriculum is collaboratively co-constructed (Beane, [1997](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s11858-015-0734-z#ref-CR3); Fraser, [2000](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s11858-015-0734-z#ref-CR18)). 
        - Inquiry-based learning = similar to case study where students researched and presented a possible investment [here](https://www.jstor.org/stable/26573525) 
          - There are similarities between SCIL and IBL (inquiry-based learning) in this regard, with students given primacy in the **posing of research questions and the subsequent research process**, but unlike discovery learning the teacher has a clear role to play in the learning process
        - Scaffolding = teachers asking questions to trigger independent student thinking, transfer of responsibility (fading support)
        - Whole-class scaffolding: Smit and van Eerde’s ([2013](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s11858-015-0734-z#ref-CR37)) extension of the scaffolding process to whole-class situations becomes informative. Their identification of **three features of whole-class scaffolding recognizes the complexity of the interactions** compared with an individual scaffolding situation
        - Is IBL cognitive overload? (related to debate of CLT vs PF, self-efficacy also plays a role) Some think IBL burdens working memory as learners seek to pose, and then research, authentic personal questions without teacher support. IBL and SCIL are not discovery learning though, nor devoid of the teacher’s input or scaffolding (Hmelo-Silver et al., [2007](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s11858-015-0734-z#ref-CR25)). They rebutted Kirschner et al’s ([2006](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s11858-015-0734-z#ref-CR29)) contention, arguing that **scaffolding is a key element of the IBL process**. In the version of **SCIL that this paper draws on, the teacher co-constructs the research question with the learners, scaffolds the learning, and leads ‘needs-based’ just-in-time teaching sessions on both conceptual knowledge and mathematical processes**.
        - This paper examines diagnosis, responsiveness, and transfer of responsibility
        - Earlier versions of the scaffolding metaphor identified **6 tutor actions:** **recruiting** interest in the task; **reducing** the degrees of freedom (simplifying the task); **maintaining** direction towards the goals of the task; **marking** critical features; **controlling** frustration; and **modeling** the preferred procedures by demonstrating (Wood et al., [1976](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s11858-015-0734-z#ref-CR47), [1978](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s11858-015-0734-z#ref-CR48)).
          - The 6 tutor actions are relevant, but **maintaining direction towards the goals of the task and marking critical features are most useful in SCIL situations**, where students take more responsibility for the research and process than in traditional classroom programs. Central to the intentions of this paper is the relationship between these aspects of responsiveness and the shift towards the transfer of responsibility.
        - The research question for this study was: **in what ways might learners be scaffolded in SCIL groups, particularly with regards to responsiveness and transfer of responsibility?**
        - This study took place in a new secondary school (ages 11–17) built specifically to facilitate inquiry-based learning. One secondary school year-10 class of 28 students was involved, and the age range of the class spanned 13–15 years of age.
      - Re-Conceptualizing "Scaffolding" and the Zone of Proximal Development in the Context of Symmetrical Collaborative Learning (==Fernandez==)
        - Fernandez (2001) [The Journal of Classroom Interaction](https://www.jstor.org/stable/23869224)
        - (Maybin, Mercer, & Stierer, 1992; Mercer, 1995): questions, feedback, explanations of the structure of the task
        - Newman, Griffin and Cole (1989) argue for reformulation of the ZPD concept, noting that it should be expanded beyond the asymmetric and individual focus of Vygotsky
        - Intermental Development Zone: Mercer (2000)
        - 3 types of talk: disputational (disagreements and individual decision-making, short claims and counterarguments), cumulative (simple agreement + restating), exploratory (critical + constructive engagement with each other, knowledge + reasoning made publicly available). Study observing 3 children (symmetrical) shows that increased use of exploratory talk expanded their joint ZPD
      - Scaffolding in Teacher–Student Interaction: A Decade of Research (==Van de Pol==) (*)
        - Van de Pol (2010) [Educational Psychology Review](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s10648-010-9127-6)
        - The goal of this review is to provide an **overview** of research on scaffolding in the classroom of the last decade, particularly with regard to its **conceptualization**, **appearances**, and **effectiveness**.
        - Tharp and Gallimore speak of six means of “assisting performance”: modeling, contingency management, feeding back, instructing, questioning, and cognitive structuring. Wood *et al*. speak of six scaffolding functions: recruitment, reduction of degrees of freedom, direction maintenance, marking critical features, frustration control, and demonstration
        - Scaffolding is often presented as an effective instructional method (e.g., Cole [2006](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s10648-010-9127-6#ref-CR10); Hogan and Pressley [1997](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s10648-010-9127-6#ref-CR23); Pawan [2008](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s10648-010-9127-6#ref-CR52))
        - Swanson and Lussier ([2001](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s10648-010-9127-6#ref-CR74)) evaluated the effectiveness of three types of dynamic assessment which strongly relates to contingency: **scaffolding** (testing the limits; scaffolding via prompting and verbal mediation steps), **coaching** (training and intervening), and **strategy training** (modeling and general feedback) for all ages and many different tasks (categorized as visual–spatial or verbal tasks). The effect sizes were found to be **higher for studies on strategy training**, followed by those on scaffolding, followed by those on coaching. The results of this meta-analysis thus showed that scaffolding was not the most effective method of dynamic assessment but also not the least effective method. 
        - Students receiving contingent support (mainly modeling and hints) were able to solve significantly more long-division problems than all of the students in the other conditions both at direct and follow-up measurement.
      - The Architecture of Cognitive Amplification: Enhanced Cognitive Scaffolding as a Resolution to the Comfort-Growth Paradox in Human-AI Cognitive Integration (Riva)
        - Riva (2025) [Catholic University of Sacred Heart](https://arxiv.org/pdf/2507.19483)
        - The Comfort-Growth Paradox
          - AI systems can act as "cognitive extensions," amplifying human problem-solving, creativity, and learning.
          - BUT, to be user-friendly, AI is often designed to be "frictionless," providing immediate answers and removing obstacles. This creates cognitive complacency, where users stagnate because they are no longer challenged to think or grow.
            - Example: AI chatbot affirming and never challenging user's perspective/ideas, just telling them how smart of a plan/thought/theory their idea is without providing any constructive criticism or disagreeing even when there is ample evidence to organize a counterargument.
          - Proposed solution: "Enhanced Cognitive Scaffolding," a framework that shifts AI from a convenient assistant to a dynamic mentor. 
            - This framework ensures AI amplifies human cognition without creating dependency - like Alpha model relying on AI but banning the use of ChatGPT to take shortcuts?
        - Enhanced Cognitive Scaffolding (from ZPD theory)
          - Pillar 1: Progressive Autonomy (The "Fading" Effect) - AI support must be temporary and decrease over time.
            - The AI provides intensive support initially but systematically withdraws it as the user gains competence (this is called fading)
            - Goal: To prevent the user from being "forever coddled" and to force the internalization of skills
            - Mechanism: The AI moves from giving full solutions -> giving specific hints -> giving open-ended prompts -> providing no help at all.
            - Example: In a programming tool (DBox), the AI initially offers detailed code fixes but eventually only asks, "Have you considered X approach?" to prompt the user to solve the bug themselves
          - Pillar 2: Adaptive Personalization (The "Just-Right" Challenge) - AI must tailor the difficulty to the specific user's current ability in real-time.
            - Support is dynamically adjusted based on performance data. If a user struggles, the AI intervenes; if they excel, the AI steps back or increases difficulty.
            - Goal: To keep the user in the "Zone of Proximal Development"—not too easy (stagnation) and not too hard (frustration).
            - Mechanism: The AI monitors metrics (e.g., time taken, error rate) to build a profile of strengths and weaknesses.
            - Example: An AI math tutor might notice a student has mastered linear equations but struggles with quadratics, so it removes hints for the former and increases scaffolding for the latter.
            - WILL be implemented by AI algorithm in Aurum
          - Pillar 3: Cognitive Load Optimization (Balancing Mental Effort) - AI manages the user's mental energy to maximize learning efficiency.
            - The AI minimizes extraneous load (distractions, bad UI, rote memorization) while maximizing germane load (effort used for understanding and schema construction).
            - Goal: To prevent "cognitive overload" (too much info) and "under-stimulation" (boredom).
            - Mechanism: The AI automates tedious tasks (e.g., data entry, formatting) so the user can focus on high-level reasoning. However, it must *not* automate the core learning task, or critical thinking will atrophy.
            - Example: A medical AI might summarize thousands of patient records (reducing extraneous load) but then ask the doctor, "Why do you think this diagnosis fits?" to force active reasoning (increasing germane load).
        - Application for Aurum (possible implementations)
          - Applying Progressive Autonomy
            - Dynamic UI Fading:
              - Early Game: The game interface highlights exactly where to click or which path to take (high support).
                - Tutorial phase several mentors have suggested implementing
              - Mid Game: The "navigation line" disappears, leaving only a compass or landmark hints (medium support).
              - Late Game: The player must navigate purely by reading the map and environment (autonomy).
              - Goal: prevent the player from relying on the UI as a crutch
            - From Execution to Strategy:
              - Early Game: The AI auto-manages complex inventory or resource logistics so the player can learn basic mechanics.
              - Late Game: As the player masters the basics, the AI breaks or hands over these systems, requiring the player to manually manage supply lines to succeed.
          - Applying Adaptive Personalization
            - The "AI Director":
              - Instead of a static Easy/Hard mode selected at the start, the game utilizes an AI Director that monitors player efficacy (accuracy, speed, failure rate).
              - Scenario: If a player is breezing through a chemistry puzzle game, the AI introduces a new volatile element that requires faster reaction times (increasing intrinsic load).
              - Scenario: If a player fails a boss fight three times, the AI doesn't just lower the boss's HP; it might drop a supply crate with a specific tool that hints at the boss's weakness, scaffolding the solution without solving it for them.
            - Personalized Narrative Scaffolding:
              - If the AI detects the player is a visual learner (e.g., they ignore text logs but inspect 3D models), it shifts narrative clues to be environmental (visual storytelling) rather than text-based
          - Applying Cognitive Load Optimization
            - Filtering Grind vs. Skill:
              - Extraneous Load Reduction: The game AI should automate "grinding" tasks (e.g., walking long distances, repeated crafting of basic items) once the player has proven they know how to do it.
                - Sandbox economy games (and other for-fun games) already do this as part of the leveling up game loop.
              - Germane Load Maximization: The AI should stop helping during key decision moments. For example, in a detective learning game, the AI can organize all the clues (reducing clutter), but it must not connect the final two dots. It should prompt the player: "How does Clue A contradict the Suspect's Alibi?".
            - The "Pause for Reflection" Mechanic:
              - To prevent button-mashing (passive consumption), the game can introduce reflection checkpoints. After a major success, the AI character asks, "That strategy worked perfectly, but why did you choose X instead of Y?" 
                - This forces the player to articulate their strategy (metacognition)
          - Risks to Be Aware of
            - The Autopilot Problem: If the game AI assists too much, leads to skill atrophy.
            - Metacognitive Laziness: Players might stop planning their own strategies if they know the AI companion will always yell out "Reload!" or "Duck!" at the right time.
              - Mitigation: The AI companion should become "unsure" in higher levels, saying "I'm not sure if we should go Left or Right..." forcing the player to decide
    - Gamification
      - The RPG Classroom: How Role-Playing Games Have Influenced the Gamification of Education (==Crocco==) (*)(~)!!
        - Crocco (2016) [The Role-Playing Society](https://www.academia.edu/38590437/The_RPG_Classroom_How_Role_Playing_Games_Have_Influenced_the_Gamification_of_Education)
        - This paper examines the gamification of education in order to demonstrate its indebtedness to the design and components of popular RPGs, particularly tabletop RPGs (TRPGs), computer RPGs (CRPGs), and massively multiplayer online RPGS (MMORPGs). Provides examples of different attempts to gamify education that utilize some of these mechanics, specifically **Lee Sheldon’s multiplayer classroom**, the **Quest to Learn schools**, and **Levelfly**, a gamified learning management system (LMS)
        - RPG learning would be different from traditional forms of game-based learning, which focus on the benefits of embedding commercial games or so-called “serious games”—games designed to teach specific content—into learning environments
        - Gamification different from games and serious games
          - Game = “a system in which players engage in an abstract challenge, defined by rules, interactivity, and feedback, that results in a quantifiable outcome often eliciting an emotional reaction” (GOAL = ENTERTAINMENT)
          - Serious game = "construction of games to educate individuals in a specific content domain” (GOAL = EDUCATION)
          - **Gamification = “using game-based mechanics, aesthetics and game thinking to engage people, motivate action, promote learning, and solve problems” (GOAL = embed game design into existing non-game activities)**
        - EXPERT OPINIONS
          - Csikszentmihalyi explains that games produce the perfect conditions for flow because, as highly structured experiences with strict rules and clear goals, they generate the high levels of concentration and engagement necessary for flow.
          - Gaming has also been linked to greater intrinsic motivation. Self-Determination Theory, a concept developed by psychologists Edward L. Deci and Richard M. Ryan over decades of research, identifies three factors that produce intrinsic motivation: autonomy, competency, and relatedness. In later research, Ryan concludes that what makes video games so engaging is that they satisfy these three needs.
          - Game designer Sebastian Deterding argues that games satisfy our innate need for intrinsic motivation and should be used to redesign schooling and workplace training
          - Rajat Paharia identifies ten key game mechanics for gamification: fast feedback, transparency, goals, badges, leveling up, onboarding, competition, collaboration, community, and points.
          - Kevin Werbach and Dan Hunter shaves down the list of key game mechanics to the holy trinity: points, badges, and leaderboards.
          - Karl M. Kapp’s The Gamification of Learning and Instruction (2012) blends general properties of games, such as goals, rules, conflict, feedback, and rewards, with specific game mechanics like levels, storytelling, and time constraints.
          - In a primer for pedagogical gamification, Kevin Yee lists five principles of gamification: display progress, maximize competition, calibrate difficulty, provide diversions, and employ narrative elements; Yee makes reference to several common gamification techniques to achieve these goals, such as using points, badges, and levels to display progress.
          - Hamari, Koivisto, and Sarsa found that the examples of gamification in their study set shared ten key game mechanics: points, leaderboards, achievements/badges, levels, story/theme, clear goals, feedback, rewards, progress, and challenges
          - There is evidence that digital avatars impact memory, emotion, and behavior
        - RPG DESIGN FOR GAMIFICATION OF EDUCATION
          - Including RPG mechanics that translate well into an education setting: substitution of XP for grades and the use of paper or online character sheets to track skills and learning outcomes. But it also involves the explicit use of RPG jargon and aesthetics to make learning more game-like, for instance, by having students adopt avatars, labeling assignments "quests,” and stressing collaborative work in the form of “party” or “guild” activities.
          - Lee Sheldon’s multiplayer classroom exemplifies the work that individual teachers are doing to implement gamification in their classrooms. 
          - Quest to Learn demonstrates what can happen when gamification is implemented at the institutional level.
          - Levelfly represents the growing popularity of gamification for learning management systems, which have the ability to impact learning at a national and international level.
        - THE MULTIPLAYER CLASSROOM - THE LEE SHELDON CLASSROOM GAMIFICATION EXPERIMENT
          - Lee Sheldon: professor of game design at Indiana University
          - In the fall of 2009, Sheldon decided to transform his traditional Theory & Practice of Game Design course into a multiplayer game modeled after commercial MMORPGs like Ultima Online (1997) and World of Warcraft (2004).
          - Replacing grades with XP points In his book, Sheldon argues that almost any class can be turned into a game, and he provides tips for how to do so. He also models this process by describing four iterations of his multiplayer classroom and providing eight case studies of educators who have gamified their courses at other institutions.
          - He began by taking his rather conventional syllabus and re-skinning it using jargon borrowed from commercial MMORPGs. He replaced grades with experience points so that students began the course at “Level 0” with no experience points and gradually leveled up as they earned points for completing assignments.
          - Course assignments consisted of “boss fights” (quizzes or exams), “quests” (solo or group presentations), and “crafting” (writing assignments). 
          - Students created avatars and assumed player names
          - To capture the collaborative element of RPGs, he placed students into “guilds” and had each member specialize by choosing from among key game industry roles (e.g., Designer, Writer, Producer, Tech Lead, Art Lead, and Marketing). 
            - Guilds collaborated throughout the semester on “PvE” quests (i.e., “Player vs. Environment”), which included quizzes, midterms, and the final project—a presentation in which each guild had to pitch an idea for a game. They also competed with other guilds in “PvP” quests (i.e., “Player vs. Player”). To allocate XP more fairly for completed group quests, Sheldon introduced a gamified “Peer Review” process in which guild members voted on how much XP each member should receive based on degree of contribution.
          - “As the semester progressed, I began to see the fun of learning the students were experiencing and how much more fun teaching felt to me. When grades improved and class attendance headed toward a record high, I began to ask myself, ‘Why haven’t we been designing classes as games for a long time now?’
        - QUEST TO LEARN + GAMIFICATION OF SCHOOLING
          - Gamified NYC public school, first opened to 6th grade, now serving 6th-12th grade
          - The Quest schools emphasize a variety of innovative approaches to teaching, many of which overlap with Gees list of key learning principles found in good video games. These include situated learning (embedding learning in real-world contexts that situate and embody meaning), design, inquiry and discovery, problem solving, systems thinking, scaffolding of assignments, embedded assessment, collaboration and sharing, feedback loops, and just-in-time delivery of information.
          - School representative states that "Quest to Learn is not a school of video games or a school where students play video games all day. Games are one important tool in the school, most certainly, but they represent something more than a resource. They are the basis of a theory of learning that is both situated and gamelike. As a result, we have designed the school around an approach to learning that draws from what we know games do best: drop players into inquiry-based, complex problem spaces that are **scaffolded** to deliver just-in-time learning and to use data to help players understand how they are doing, what they need to work on, and where to go next."
          - Schools are not engaging and use outdated methods (lecturing/textbooks and tests) The Quest solution—to develop a school based on the principles of gamelike learning—promises “to mempower and engage students from all walks of life.”
          - The ChicagoQuest website explains that students are immersed “in narratives where they ‘take on’ a role or identity. **role-playing and narrative are HUGE** 
          - Schoolwork is organized into “Discovery Missions,” often called “Missions” for short, which are defined as “questlike challenges that require students to plan, collect data, create theories, test their results, and document outcomes.
            - Missions are further broken down into constitutive parts called “Quests”— “goal -oriented challenges that equip students with necessary data, knowledge, resources, and practices to solve the larger Mission.” Each Mission spans an entire 12-week school term and contains 4-10 Quests. 
            - final two weeks of each Mission are devoted to overcoming a final challenge called a “Boss Level,” a capstone project that requires “research, theory building, hypothesis testing, evaluation, and critique—all followed by a public defense of results.
        - LEVELFLY + GAMIFICATION OF ONLINE LEARNING
          - Current examples of gamified LMSs demonstrate that this technology significantly relies on the adoption of key RPG mechanics, particularly XP, quests, character statistics, and avatars.
          - Many established LMSs, such as Blackboard and Moodle, are developing gamification plugins like badges, leaderboards, and avatars to retain and attract customers
          - 3D Game Lab (use for K-grad school) 
            - Instead of following a standard curriculum with mandatory assignments along a single pathway, teachers who use 3D GameLab devise their courses as a collection of quests from which students can arrange their own pathways to course completion
            - As students complete quests, they earn XP, badges, and awards, and unlock new quests that open up different pathways to course completion. The more quests they complete, the more XP they earn, and the more they level, which improves their final grade.
          - Classcraft (K-12) 
            - Players earn XP and gold for completing quests, and level up along the way, which unlocks new powers and increases their stats (pg 19)
          - Levelfly
            - XP system for the class in which each assignment was a “quest” worth a designated number of XP, and students could choose which quests to tackle in order to earn enough XP to level up and qualify for the final exam, a writing test that determined whether they passed or failed the course.
            - The results were amazing: student absences went down, student participation went up, and, as a result, my pass rate on the final exam improved significantly
            - The final product exhibited many of the hallmark features of RPGs: an XP and level system, quest-based assignments, character sheets with statistics on points and skills, an avatar-based role-playing component
            - Levelfly is an “achievement system” that incorporates common RPG mechanics to improve engagement and provide helpful feedback using XP, badges, and stats on student learning outcomes
            - Just like with achievement systems for video games and MMORPGs, badges in Levelfly are social: they are prominently displayed on a student’s public profile page to mark accomplishment. (pg 23) 
            - provides students with real-time feedback on how they are doing in each learning outcome for the course, information which they can view on their individualized course statistics page. Students can assess their strengths and weaknesses, and also view a list of the top five students for each learning outcome in order to facilitate peer-mentoring opportunities. When the course is over, students automatically receive a gold medal for each high-performing outcome, which is publicly displayed alongside their badges and distinguished by a golden hue
      - Game-Based Learning: A Push for Introducing a C(Classroom)-MORPG (==Nunes==)
        - Nunes (2021) [Advances in Intelligent Systems and Computing](https://www.academia.edu/79916498/Game_Based_Learning_A_Push_for_Introducing_a_C_Classroom_MORPG)
        - With the massive use of gamiﬁed approaches, and tools like **Mentimeter**, **Kahoot!** or **Classcraft**, the look of those who intend to innovate in their practices now turns to other types of tools, namely those that even involve the use of more radical applications, as it is the case with ODEUM platforms (odeum.ai) or EUTOPIA ([www.lanas.unina.it/eutopia/](http://www.lanas.unina.it/eutopia/)), which work according to the assumptions of digital RPG.
        - RPG can be described as any game that allows the player to immerse itself into a character, by introducing him/her into the story as, usually, the main character or hero, and progress through a narrative which includes a main storyline and several smaller branched storylines, in which they can undertake speciﬁc adventures, quests and goals, until the main story is completed. 
        - By creating a structured and logical story behind all of these quests and missions and other tasks we implement to transmit knowledge, exemplify in, practice and test it, it should be possible to maintain engagement from the pupil without a feeling of repetition or meaningless task completions
