      - Learning Through Games: Essential Features of an Educational Game (==Kannan==)
        - Kannan (2012) [Thesis Dissertation](https://www.academia.edu/52499782/Learning_through_games_Essential_features_of_an_educational_game)
        - The experience itself is probably the reward, so to speak. For a creative mind to sustain creativity, intrinsic motivation is very essential. Robert Henri: “intrinsic motivation is at the back of every true work of art”
        - Intrinsic motivation has some specific characteristics (Smith & Ragan, 2004):
          - 1. Competence (For example, challenge of a task)
          - 2. Curiosity (For example, complexity, incongruity, surprises)
          - 3. Autonomy (For example, control of environment and self-determination)
          - 4. Volition (For example, process of internalization)
          - 5. Goal-orientation (For example, orientation towards performance goals, orientation towards learning goals)
        - Learning is generally perceived as exotelic (like securing a good career) and game play is considered as autotelic (end in itself, no other goal for doing it)
        - Jean Piaget and Lev Vygotsky are two of the most acclaimed researchers in the area of child developmental psychology. When scholars talk about games and their influence over humans, they invariably quote from Piaget and Vygotsky’s work. Both strongly believed children acquire knowledge through games (ZPD on pg 35)
        - TYPES OF GAMES
          - Practice games without symbols, make-believe or rules are characteristic of games played by animals.
          - Symbolic games: when a child pushes a box and imagines it as a car, the box is a symbolic representation of the car, and according to child, the box is the car. Between 4 – 7 years a child is able to perceive and imagine intuitively
          - Games with rules: Rules mean how one is conducting himself or herself in a social and inter-individual relationship. Between the ages of 7 – 11 years, a child can form a more accurate representation of their environment.
          - The three categories are indeed three stages of child development: sensory-motor, representational, and reflective (Piaget)
        - HOW GAMES ARE FUN
          - Challenges, fantasy, curiosity
          - Interactive = doing, outcomes/feedback = learning, conflict/competition = exciting
        - Papastergiou (2009) 88 high school students were randomly assigned to two groups: one of which used an educational game (Group A, N = 47) and one that did not (Group B, N = 41). A Computer Memory Knowledge Test was used as the pretest and posttest. Results showed that the gaming approach was both more effective in promoting students' knowledge of computer memory concepts as well as more motivational than the non-gaming approach
        - Endogenous vs exogenous fantasy:
          - Exogenous = “sugar coating” over educational materials, no integration between the game’s storyline and the learning of content. Gaming content superimposed on top of game. Won't trigger a deep investment or an extended engagement
          - Endogenous =  fantasy arises from the game's internal mechanics, rules, and narrative. It is created by the game's design itself, where the fantasy elements are embedded within the learning objectives and gameplay
        - GAME DEVELOPED
          - Humatan is an instructional game where high school students will learn to identify and assemble human skeletal structures.
      - Using Game Theory and Competition-Based Learning to Stimulate Student Motivation and Performance (==Burguillo==)
        - Burguillo (2009) [Computers and Education](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0360131510000527)
        - Article describes how GBL is connected to other educational methods
          - Game-based Learning (GBL) can be combined with similar learning methodologies as Collaborative-based Learning (CBL), Problem-based Learning (PBL) and Project-based Learning (PjBL)
        - CnBL does not introduce any special kind of overload in the lab work from the student or from the teacher point of view. On the one hand, from the student point of view, there is no additional effort compared with any other learning technique, as the student groups have to do their practices collaboratively, and at the end participate in a tournament that takes about half an hour in our case. On the other hand, from the teachers point of view, the teacher has a natural overload the first time that the lab work is designed and implemented; as happens with any new learning technique.
        - % of students passing the course is higher in the 5 years where the Competition-based Learning approach has been used (96.56% in average) than in the other 5 years without CnBL (90% in average)
        - Conclusion: difference between CnBL/no CnBL might NOT be statistically significant, should not run entirety of class off of CnBL, but seems to be a helpful/fun exercise (particularly for student engagement/motivation)
      - Enhancing Student Learning Experience with Technology-Mediated Gamification: An empirical Study (==Tsay==)
        - Tsay (2017) [Computers and Education](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0360131518300095)
        - Student **performance** was **significantly higher among those who participated in the gamified system **than in those who engaged with the non-gamified, traditional delivery, while behavioral engagement in online learning activities was positively related to course performance, after controlling for gender, attendance, and Year 1 PPD performance
        - GAME DESIGN:
          - Variety of game components may provide motivational affordance to student users ([Dicheva et al., 2015](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0360131518300095#bib23)). Design principles: such as **goals**, **competition and cooperation**, **freedom to fail**, and many others: and game mechanics: avatars, badges, boss fights, content unlocking, and others. ([Iosup and Epema, 2014](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0360131518300095#bib40), [Landers and Callan, 2011](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0360131518300095#bib45)).
          - While some game components, such as badges, points, levels, and virtual goods, can act as external rewards, other game components, such as **social graphs, teams, and content unlocking**, can serve as **intrinsic motivators** to students who imbue these components with personally important meanings ([Banfield & Wilkerson, 2014](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0360131518300095#bib9)).
          - Employed **self-determination theory**, difficulty levels of **Bloom's taxonomy**, **freedom to choose** which SL activities to do (+ when and where to do them). **Direct feedback**, **badges** and **leaderboards**
          - Online learning activities were developed with the support of the institution's [VLE](https://www.sciencedirect.com/topics/computer-science/virtual-learning-environments) (i.e., Moodle) and were classified in a two-tiered gamified learning system with 14 Essential Learning (EL) and 37 Super Learning (SL)
            - The IT used for this gamified learning system, i.e., **Moodle**, fulfilled many criteria suggested in the IS success model ([Delone & McLean, 2003](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0360131518300095#bib20)). First, the good system quality of Moodle reflected in that its many activities, including lessons, quizzes, assignments, feedback, forum, and [wikis](https://www.sciencedirect.com/topics/computer-science/wiki), allowed the use of game design principles (e.g. **freedom to fail**,** free of choice**, **competition, cooperation, rapid feedback, social engagement**) and implementation of game mechanics (e.g. **badges**, **content** **unlocking**, **leaderboard**, **time**-**based** activities). 
          - According to the information systems success model ([Delone & McLean, 2003](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0360131518300095#bib20)), the system's success is based on the following dimensions: system quality, information quality, service quality, user satisfaction, and system use.
        - LEARNING FACTORS AND PSYCHOLOGY:
          - **Surface learning** is associated with** limited interest **in a learning task **or memorization** and reproduction of elements. **Deep learning**, on the other hand, is associated with **attempts to understand a topic if it is of real interest** to them or if they can see its **relevance** to their current or future life.
            -  Deep learning is associated with an intrinsic orientation in motivation ([Deci & Ryan, 2002](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0360131518300095#bib19)), motivation to study, and a range of mastery-approach goals ([Baeten et al., 2010](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0360131518300095#bib8)). In contrast, surface learning is associated with an extrinsic orientation in motivation, fear of failure, and performance avoidance goals ([Baeten et al., 2010](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0360131518300095#bib8)). 
          - U.S. Department of Education ([Means, Toyama, Murphy, Bakia, & Jones, 2009](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0360131518300095#bib50)), where the authors found that both online and hybrid courses have a positive impact on learning outcomes, with **hybrid courses having a greater impact.** 
          - 2-year longitudinal study by [Hamari (2015)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0360131518300095#bib31) suggests there are **positive long-term effects in using gamified approaches in education**, as users in the gamified condition are significantly more likely to post trade proposals, carry out transactions, comment on proposals, and generally use the service in an active way ([Hamari, 2015](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0360131518300095#bib31)).
          - Two-academic-term longitudinal study suggested that **novelty effect seems to influence student engagement**, as reflected in the EL completion rate decline as the terms progressed
            - Novelty needs to be consistently introduced? Like updates to a game where they add new objects, tasks, experiences, etc??
          - [Hamari et al. (2014)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0360131518300095#bib33) mentioned that the **nature of the system (e.g., hedonic or utilitarian), user qualities, and the social environment influence the effectiveness of gamification.**
            - [Koivisto and Hamari (2014)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0360131518300095#bib43) and [Venkatesh et al. (2000)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0360131518300095#bib63) showed that women perceived greater social and hedonic benefits from the use of gamification, while men may engage more in a gamified system due to utilitarian benefits.
        - INTRINSIC VS EXTRINSIC MOTIVATION
          - Some research cautions against excessive focus on the achievement aspect of gamification (e.g., visible status and virtual collections), since **learning based on extrinsic rewards is unsustainable in the long run**. [Deci et al. (2001)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0360131518300095#bib18) demonstrated that **all forms of rewards (extrinsic motivation) eventually erode intrinsic motivation**. (Is this *really* true?) This view was supported by the decline of student engagement in our design, especially towards the end of the two-academic-term teaching period. Therefore, designing learning activities in a meaningful way that intrinsically motivates students becomes key for “meaningful gamification” ([Nicholson, 2015](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0360131518300095#bib52)). 
            - Echoing the idea of student-centered learning environments, researchers have suggested that **meaningful gamification can be achieved by inviting users to design their learning goals and assessments.**
              -  [Kofinas (2017)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0360131518300095#bib42) proposed a narrative approach to assessment. He suggested that gamification can help educators develop [narratives](https://www.sciencedirect.com/topics/social-sciences/narrative) where both formative and [summative assessments](https://www.sciencedirect.com/topics/social-sciences/summative-assessment) of the assessment regime can be incorporated as challenges to be overcome. Inviting students to set up challenges for themselves by developing [formative assessments](https://www.sciencedirect.com/topics/social-sciences/formative-assessment) that help with their goal setting can lead to more engaged students who put in extra effort to achieve the learning goals they set.
      - Game design and learning: a conjectural analysis of how massively multiple online role-playing games (MMORPGs) foster intrinsic motivation (==Dickey==) (*)(~)!!!
        - Dickey (2007) [Educational Technology Research and Development](https://www.academia.edu/7848914/Game_design_and_learning_a_conjectural_analysis_of_how_massively_multiple_online_role_playing_games_MMORPGs_foster_intrinsic_motivation)
        - "The theoretical assumption underlying interactive learning environments is that learners construct understandings by interacting with information, tools, and materials as well as by collaborating with other learners. Yet, **these environments must also help scaffold the learning process**"
        - This article presents a conjectural analysis of how the structure in MMORRPGs might inform the design of interactive learning and game-based learning environments by looking at the **elements that support intrinsic motivation**. (Weird because more involved games tend to prioritize external motivation?)
        - How can MMORPG style gameplay (which is proven to be fun/interactive/engaging/addicting) be synthesized with the idea of hedonic/utilitarian game design? (connecting to other aspects of game design research)
        - **Article covers 4 main aspects**
          - (1) 2 primary elements in MMORPG game design: character design + learning environment
            - Character management: players enhance their character's skills, attributes, appearance, etc throughout gameplay + choose small quests, the design of the game is scaffolded
          - (2) Intrinsic motivation in character role-playing
            - No final victory or loss conditions in a MMORPG - game continues to evolve (helping with SpikyPOV about gamification not introducing enough novelty).
            - Players keep evolving their character/make new characters to play --> spend a lot of time investing in their character, interacting in gameplay environment + become attached to it. 
            - Developing a character --> playing a role
            - Resnick and Wilensky (1997) integrated role-playing activities into math/science classes and found that role-playing can help students understand complex systems and relationships (pg 258)
          - (3) Intrinsic motivational supports + cognitive support of the narrative structure of small quests
            - Persistent world: gameplay continues even when a player logs off (might make game addictive)
            - No one single storyline for players to uncover, but it is a series of small quests (short narrative storylines)
            - Players able to collaborate
            - Narrative design fosters exploration, often no "right" way to do something, players must weigh opportunity costs of completing quests and decide which way is the most economical to complete many small quests in the most efficient way. (Budgeting? Opportunity costs?)
            - Many types of quests, Goodwill quest is one: requiring a higher-level player to assist a lower-level player in completing a quest (scaffolding) (pg 263)
          - (4) How narrative structure of MMORPGs might aid in the instructional design of interactive/game-based learning environments
            - Open-ended environment, no penultimate way to end the game that players strive for (might want a final end for FL education game)
            - Malone and Lepper (1987) argue that choice is a significant variable in fostering motivation (when learners given a range of choices + provided with sufficient structure in which to make such choices)
            - Iyengar and Lepper (1999, 2000) examined motivating aspects of choice AND demotivating aspects of choice: when participants provided with too many options of choice (decision fatigue)
            - Malone and Lepper (1987) argue that control is a key aspect of fostering intrinsic motivation
              - MMORPGs balance the amount of choices/control players have, allowing them to choose how to complete quests but not offering a high-level quest to a lower level player (similar to scaffolding?)
            - Malone and Lepper (1987) add that cooperation may foster interpersonal motivation
            - Small quest structure + marking progress --> sense of achievement/self-efficacy/accountability
            - Malone (1981) and Malone and Lepper (1987) argue that challenge + uncertainty foster motivation in gameplay
            - ELEMENTS WITHIN DESIGN OF NARRATIVE ENVIRONMENT THAT SCAFFOLDS PLAYERS IN GAMEPLAY: providing cognitive framework for problem-solving + metacognitive skills
              - Game environment/backstory establishes boundaries + allows players to be experimental (but possible) within environment
              - "Players are put in a position of having to make conjectures about causal relationships based on the type of information they have encountered while exploring and participating in previous small quests"
              - Environment provides choice, control, collaboration, achievement, scaffolding for problem solving
            - Fostering intrinsic motivation (from Malone 1981) = challenge, fantasy, curiosity
              - Malone and Lepper (1987) expanded on this to include choice + control to cause intrinsic motivation (pg 265)
        - OTHER GAMES/LEARNING MODES
          - Bowman applied Csikszentmihalyi and Lawson's (1980) flow state interaction to explain motivation of extrinsic supports in Pac-man
          - Provenzo (1991) used Malone's findings to explain intrinsic motivational support for Super Mario Bros 2
          - Goal-based scenarios and case-based learning are existing learning models which incorporate role-playing, narrative, and tasks similar to small quests. Use small quests and character development to foster intrinsic motivation
        - **4 types of knowledge** (from researchers in cognitive psychology, knowledge management, learning theory, and instructional design): [pg 268]
          - 1. Declarative: facts, data, concepts, and principles (**collection + goodwill quests**)
          - 2. Procedural: how to perform a task, action, or process (**FedEx + messenger quests**)
          - 3. Strategic: awareness of how to apply knowledge, principles, and experiences to various and new situations. Foundation of problem-solving. (**bounty + escort quests**)
          - 4. Metacognitive: reflection + regulation of one's own thinking during an activity (**bounty + escort + goodwill quests**)
      - Escaping from the Traditional Classroom - The 'Escape Room Methodology' in the Foreign Languages Classroom (==Cruz==)
        - The use of dynamics, mechanics and game elements contributes to a state of continuous ﬂow at the time of learning, i.e., an intrinsic motivation as explained by the self-determination theory (cf. Werbach & Hunter, 2012).
          - Also felt within escape rooms, which are seen as "live-action team-based games where players discover clues, solve puzzles, and accomplish tasks in one or more rooms in order to accomplish a speciﬁc goal. usually escaping from the room) in a limited amount of time” (Nicholson, 2016, p. 1).
        - “Helping Gumball to Escape” escape room which was implemented in a Private Catholic school in Vila Nova de Gaia, Portugal, with a total of 43 pupils aged between 8 and 10 years old
        - Escape Room used for language education, probably not as straightforward/effective for teaching FinLit
      - Combining Software Games with Education: Evaluation of its Educational Effectiveness (==Virvou==)
        - Virvou (no date) [Department of Informatics](https://www.academia.edu/9431582/Combining_Software_Games_with_Education_Evaluation_of_its_Educational_Effectiveness)
        - Study of an Intelligent Tutoring System (ITS) that operates as a virtual reality educational game. The results of the evaluation show that educational virtual reality games can be very motivating while retaining or even improving the educational effects on students. Moreover, one important finding of the study was that the educational effectiveness of the game was particularly high for students who used to have poor performance in the domain taught prior to their learning experience with the game
        - Papert (1993) notes that software games teach children that some forms of learning are fast-paced, immensely compelling and rewarding whereas by comparison school strikes many young people as slow and boring.
        - Research shows that games can be highly effective for teaching and fun for students, however they have not been implemented in schools yet, and some parents/educators worry about the effectiveness. The poor implementation of gamification/serious games shows people that games are not fun and the content is not educational
        - VR-ENGAGE teaches students geography
          - Aims at increasing students’ engagement by providing a popular and motivating VR environment. In this way, it aims at being more effective in teaching students than other educational software and traditional media of education.
          - ITS (intelligent tutoring system): 4 components = domain knowledge, student modeling, tutoring, user interface
          - Main similarity of VR-ENGAGE with computer games like DOOM = 3D engine. However, VR-ENGAGE unlike DOOM is not violent at all and is connected to an educational application.
          - Has ultimate goal (unlike MMORPGs), which player gets closer to through an adventure style (dragons guard doors and ask player geography questions, player gets closer to final goal by getting questions right) (negotiation when wrong question pgn 56)
        - STUDY/EXPERIMENTAL DESIGN
          - This article measures the educational effectiveness of an educational VR-game as compared to educational software that does not incorporate the gaming aspect to see if the gaming environment might improve education. Did not conduct a comparison between human teaching and tutoring through the game. A primary aim of the experiment was to reveal the degree of educational effectiveness (if any) for students whose performance was considered good, mediocre or poor respectively by their human teachers. All of the children who participated in all four parts of the experiment were of 9-10 years old
        - RESULTS
          - In the post-test, the players of VR-ENGAGE made 43.15% less mistakes than in the pre-test. The other sub-group of students that had used the simple ITS resulted in 32.48% less mistakes of their answers in total, as compared to the pre-test. Thus the players of VR-ENGAGE resulted in a higher improvement of 10.67% in terms of their mistakes than the users of the simple ITS (no game element)
          - The players of VR-ENGAGE made 909 mistakes in the post-test. This number of mistakes as compared to the 1599 of the pre-test constituted an improvement of 43.15%. The students who had worked with the other educational software made 1112 mistakes, which constituted an improvement of 32.48% in the number of erroneous answers. The users of both educational applications showed an improvement in their post-test performance
          - Results showed that the sub-group of students of previous poor performance, which had used VR-ENGAGE, benefited the most of all the sub-groups from the educational game
          - Sub-group of VR-ENGAGE students of previous average performance had also benefited more than the respective sub-group that had used the simple ITS since they made fewer mistakes (difference was statistically significant).
          - Student with good academic performance did not have any significant difference in their improvement through the use of the game or the use of the other software. However, one important finding is that the performance of previously good students has not deteriorated.
      - Video Games and Learning: Teaching and Participatory Culture in the Digital Age (==Botturi==)
        - Botturi (2008) [here](https://www.academia.edu/103004134/Rediscovering_the_Roots_of_Games_in_Education) 
        - Having consulted a number of standard instructional technology texts and reference books at both the graduate and undergraduate levels (Alessi & Trollip, 2001; Anglin, 1995; Heinich, Molenda, Russell, & Smaldino, 1999; Jonassen, 1996, 2004; Lever-Duffy, McDonald, & Mizell, 2003; Maddux, Johnson, & Willis, 1992; Smith & Ragan, 1999), we found that very few of them considered game to be an instructional resource, technique, or tool.
          - In the rare instances where game was mentioned, it was regarded as a motivational activity to supplementary learning in the classroom. The readers (mostly pre-service teachers) were cautioned against using game for play in the classrooms.
          - Firstly, it seemed unlikely for word such as “activities” and “competitive exercises”, if found printed on box-covers of video games, to generate any interest from gamers – or from young students. 
          - Secondly, with respect to the latest developments of the research on games such definitions sound nowadays rather simplistic. 
          - Thirdly, people enjoyed playing games because games are fun, and this is what makes them learn. If educators view games merely as activities and competitive exercises, they will use them (or design them) as such, hence, jeopardizing the real potential of games.
        - Implication #2: games = delimited
          - Introducing a game in the classroom means creating a sort of free zone in which **only the game exists, without any influence of extrinsic elements such as evaluation, grades, mandatory work**, or other. Playing requires conditions, and this is probably the most challenging issue in the use of games in formal education.
        - Implication #3: play = voluntary
          - Each player must deliberately choose to play, and take it seriously. Considering that being obliged to play is not playing, using games in the classroom can be difficult.
        - Implication #4: games play with identity
          - Once players enter the “magic circle” of a game, they must respect its rules. Playing along with other players means accepting the game world’s reality and negotiating one’s identities within the game world as if it is real. Players should be ready for this “reality/identity change.” This type of negotiation may come naturally for children (it’s just another make-belief)
          - RPG games involve taking on an alternate identity, shown to be very effective 
        - 4 types of games. Actually, these can be seen as four dimensions of playing (or generations of fun), which are represented in different degrees in actual games
          - 1. Agon is the game of competition, such as sports
          - 2. Alea is the game based on chance and risk-taking, such as in gambling
          - 3. Mimicry is the game of make-believe, such as adventure games, or childish make-believe games
          - 4. Ilinx is breath-taking games, in which the challenge to the senses and the overcoming of fear generates pleasure, such as in bungee jumping
      - Gamifying the Classroom: Tips from the Trenches (==Kennette==)
        - Kennette (2019) [Transformative Dialogues: Teaching and Learning Journal](https://www.academia.edu/40898383/Gamifying_the_classroom_Tips_from_the_trenches)
        - Laster (2010) reported after a semester of monster-fighting (test/quiz), crafting (various writing assignments), and quests (presentations), the **class average increased by an entire letter grade** compared to the previous semester’s non-gamified class average.
        - TURNING ENTIRE COURSE INTO A GAME
          - Students earn items they can use later in the course (resubmit a homework, retake a quiz)
        - INCORPORATING GAME ELEMENTS
          - Competing for best participation in discussion, best grade, against another class
          - Assignments may be presented as gaming stories
            - Feedback on the assignment provides the ending to the story based on the assignment grade (A = victim released without harm; B = victim released but injured, etc.).
          - Escape rooms and other similar games have grown in popularity and can be incorporated into the classroom setting (Nicholson, 2018). Breakout EDU sells puzzle-based boxes for educators [here](https://breakoutedu.com/)
        - GENERAL GAMIFICATION
          - Freedom to fail, immediate feedback, feeling of progress, choice/responsibility for outcomes, story-telling (ALL ARE PRESENT IN MMORPG/RPG GAMES)
        - LIMITATIONS OF GAMIFICATION
          - Issues with intrinsic/extrinsic motivation: Gaming is inherently intrinsically motivated, but the use of badges, leaderboards, points, etc may shift that to extrinsic. Removing the gamification elements may be negative b/c of loss of extrinsic rewards
          - Personality differences of students: some people are less likely than others to be driven by
      - Games-Based Experiences for Learning (==Bober==)
        - Bober [Futurelab](https://www.academia.edu/28867208/Games_Based_Experiences_for_Learning)
        - Taxonomy of digital game learning elements
          - Challenge, fantasy/narrative, feedback, goals, sensory stimuli, social aspects/community, active learning, adaptivity, assessment, realism, competition, control, creativity, mystery, puzzle solving, rapid decision making, relevance/interest to learner, reward, role, rules
      - Toward Understanding the Potential of Games for Learning: Learning Theory, Game Design Characteristics, and Situating Video Games in Classrooms (==Vicari==)
        - Vicari (2014) [Computers in Schools](https://www.academia.edu/6822194/Toward_Understanding_the_Potential_of_Games_for_Learning_Learning_Theory_Game_Design_Characteristics_and_Situating_Video_Games_in_Classrooms)
        - Might be good to design a game in which players can choose which game modes they play (like Minecraft, choosing survival or creative mode based on what you feel like/personality type, etc)
        - Games for Learning Institute (G4LI)
          - Alliance of game designers, computer scientists, and education researchers, has urged game designers to distinguish between three categories of mechanics: game, learning, and assessment (pg 5)?
        - In traditional classroom learning, negative feedback is often discouraging to students (Sadler, 1998). However, this is not generally the case in video games. As Gee (2007) noted, “In video games, losing is not losing, and the point is not winning easily or judging yourself a failure. In playing **video games, hard is not bad and easy is not good**” (p. 125). Players are allowed, repeatedly, to try again if they do not succeed, which is not common in classroom-based learning.
        - Motion-sensing tech in new games: players adjust the width of their outstretched arms in proportion to a fraction falling down the screen. This is an innovative example of leveraging emerging input paradigms to incorporate a learning theory, in this case embodied or grounded cognition
        - High-power (expansive) vs low-power (arms tucked into body) positions' effects on math anxiety (Isbister, 2011). Suggested that high-power positions can impact a user’s emotional state, raising conﬁdence levels
      - Learning as Fun: What Video Games Do that Classrooms Do Not (==Luna==)
        - Luna (2012) [Penn Libraries](https://www.academia.edu/74102452/Learning_as_Fun_What_Video_Games_Do_that_Classrooms_Do_Not)
        - Gameplay components: interactivity/choice, instantaneity/failure, intrinsic motivation, perspective/roleplaying, socialization
          - Students can choose difficulty level (new = easy mode, experienced = normal mode)
        - Classroom components: cram for test, do not try again, no instantaneous feedback/adaptation, extrinsic motivation
          - ALL students take the same "difficulty mode" of class
          - Students often asked to learn vast amounts of information usually far removed from the actual scenarios in which they are applicable
        - Not in article: intrinsic = doing something for the sake of doing it (enjoyment, curiosity, personal satisfaction) extrinsic = doing something for external rewards (points, recognition, achievements)
          - Intrinsic motivation --> hedonic gameplay, extrinsic motivation --> utilitarian gameplay
        - Case studies: capacity of games to teach both inside and outside of the classroom.
          - History class simulation: students asked to consider the motivations/choices, each assigned a country to represent in WWII. "most people would not care what a Non-Aggression Pact was unless someone offered to enter into one with them"
          - Cellcraft: outside classroom, player operates a single cell + controls functions to protect from viruses
          - Khan Academy uses a skill tree: players can choose what to learn next (unlocked topics)
      - Experiences of Middle School Students Following a Financial Literacy Simulation (==Caniglia==)
        - Caniglia (2019) [Journal of Education and Practice](https://www.academia.edu/125587615/Experiences_of_Middle_School_Students_Following_a_Financial_Literacy_Simulation)
        - Article describes the experiences of middle school students’ following the Reality Store, a financial simulation. The activity, divided into 15 different stations, provided hands-on experiences and opportunities for students to think about their futures and career choices in relation to their economic well-being, similar to JA finance park
        - Among the popular financial literacy apps available on the market are: Mindblown Labs, LearnVest, Mint, Pocket Budget, and Pocketguard (Tabalba, 2017)
        - With reference to financial literacy, researchers have concluded that teaching financial literacy in the abstract appears to be ineffective (Braunstein & Welch, 2002), whereas highly interactive, reality-based courses in fiscal responsibility are effective
        - DEMOGRAPHICS:
          - 156 8th graders, 90% eligible for free/reduced lunch, all received some FL education before program
        - SIMULATION:
          - Students assume the role of a 25-year-old adult who may or may not be the sole income provider for a family. They receive an occupation, monthly salary, and their # children to take care of
          - Students learn to subtract savings, taxes, and health insurance amounts from their income. The amount leftover is what they have to spend during the simulation activity. The simulation involves community volunteers who represent actual businesses in the community; they set up and staff booths representing real-life businesses. 
          - By visiting all appropriate booths, students spend their salaries on items typically found in a monthly budget, including housing, utilities, groceries, insurance, childcare, and transportation. 
          - Throughout the activity, students keep track of their finances and attempt to complete the simulation with a positive balance. During the post‐simulation lesson, students reflect on their experience and what they learned by completing a self-assessment
          - 3 Components: 
            - 1. Prep - students introduced, receive characteristics like marital status/kids, salaries, taxes. Students choose occupation
            - 2. Event - students visit 15 stations, before end of simulation, students faced with an unexpected life event (winning lottery, getting in an accident, etc)
            - 3. Evaluation + discussion - 
        - RESULTS:
          - Students overwhelmingly felt that the Reality Store was worthwhile (n=145, 93%) and enjoyed the experience (n=142, 91%).
          - Simulations are a useful tool for teaching financial literacy concepts. However, where such simulation activities are used, the experiences should include realistic scenarios, clear instructions for participants with a preview of what to expect, and a thorough debriefing where students discuss their experiences and what they learned.
      - Game based learning - A Different Perspective (==Royle==)
        - Royle [Innovate](https://www.academia.edu/166112/Game_based_learning_a_different_perspective)
        - Ways of engagement
          - The structure of the game provides motivation and the urge to solve problems for the problem's sake alone.
          - The backstory or narrative provides the believability or authenticity of engagement.
          - Characterization makes the player's role in the narrative believable so that the player can engage fully in the game.
        - Cheats should be intrinsic + extrinsic to the game
          - Within the game, cheats may take the form of clues embedded in artifacts, spoken or written texts, or other characters who give extra information or mediate progress through the game. External cheats require users to read complex instructional text in order to solve a problem, promoting literacy.
      - Games and Learning (==Steinkuehler==)
        - Steinkuehler (2014) [Handbook of the Learning Sciences](https://www.academia.edu/37217432/Games_and_Learning)
        - From the "games and history" section
          - "Replaying history" option - maybe students could "replay finances"
          - Gameplay as an aid to prep before class: example of 3 groups (one playing COD 2, one playing Civilization IV, one not playing a game) for 5 weeks before learning about WWII
            - 1st person shooter players attending more to local tactical elements and the historical simulation players attending more to global strategic elements, gameplay was a successful prep for additional activities
        - Exogenous vs endogenous assessments to gameplay
          - Exogenous designs create assessments that interrupt, or are peripheral to, the learning environment, and can be quickly constructed to build measures into a wide variety of environments. Endogenous designs integrating assessment practices into the game flow such that mastering the learning environment is itself the learning outcome, not merely a means to an alternative goal
      - Video Games as Education (==Zaidan==)
        - Zaidan [IEML](https://www.academia.edu/41662034/Video_Games_as_Education)
        - Most frequently-documented case of an entertainment game’s pedagogical applications is certainly Minecraft
          - Massively multiplayer sandbox game
          - Nebel, Schneider, and Rey (2016) reported that the game has been used to teach subjects as varied as literacy, sustainable planning, social skills and project management, and to conduct research in the fields of virtual reality and human-computer interaction.
          - Minecraft and SimCity are virtually endless constructivist games, no predetermined storyline/narrative that the player must follow. HOWEVER, educators have found that constructionist and situated learning can be achieved with equal efficacy in games that feature defined narrative structures, and where players are cast as specific characters with their own distinct personalities.
      - Think Games on the Fly, Not Gamify: Issues in Game-Based Learning Research (==Slota==)
        - Slota (2014) [Journal of Graduate Medical Education](https://www.academia.edu/24889337/Think_Games_on_the_Fly_Not_Gamify_Issues_in_Game_Based_Learning_Research)
        - Games and simulations not the same?
          - Manipulation of a simulation is designed to be more realistic than a game
        - Games regularly change to sustain player interest, with patches adding new dimensions and enriching the overarching game narrative, and they explore, expand, and build upon existing storylines (eg, Lord of the Rings, Star Wars) to take advantage of players’ prior experiences. [Helping SpikyPOV about how games do not do well enough introducing novelty]
          - If course designers were to incorporate these principles (eg, building from established narratives; encouraging the use of social, meta-game–like resources), it might be possible to make supportive learning environments extremely effective because they would extend beyond the behaviorist token economies instantiated as gamification.
      - Engaging Online Students Through the Gamification of Learning Materials: The Present and the Future (==McGrath==) *
        - McGrath (2013) [Electric Dreams](https://www.academia.edu/83880638/Engaging_online_students_through_the_gamification_of_learning_materials_The_present_and_the_future)
        - When people are in the optimal flow experience, they are in such a psychological state that, during the activity, they **do not care about their environment** (Intal & Cagiltay, 2007; Killi, 2005; Ozelik, et al.,2013). Players temporarily lose track of time, surroundings, and the actual environment that they are in. Studies show that participants perceive higher levels of flow, and apply** in-depth problem solving strategies with computer games** (Liu, Cheng & Huang, 2011; Ozelik et al., 2013).
        - Reports from the corporate world suggest that the use of game-based learning results in a knowledge transfer four times greater, and in knowledge retention ten times greater, than traditional methods ( “Total Learning”, 2008; Wankel et al., 2010).
        - The principle behind scenario-based learning is that a good program consists of a story in which students play a key role – the role that the student might perform in real life or might need to perform in the future (Iverson & Colky, 2004). Students are placed in a realistic scenario, where they take on the key role of the protagonist
        - "We developed this approach in an undergraduate **financial accounting** subject at the University of New England. Within this subject students assume the role of an ‘accountant’ and undergo tasks and problems that an accountant would stumble across in the **real world**. The work may be done individually or in a team environment, allowing a richer learning experience. Supporting materials and resources may be provided, and **online mentors may be available to answer questions and provide guidance as needed**" (SCAFFOLDED FINANCIAL LITERACY GAME?)
        - "the success of the game depends on **how willing the players are to lose themselves in the game** and how interactive they become in it. These games combine narrative elements of a story that are built up and presented to players across a whole range of online and offline media (Owings, 2009)"
        - 3 main areas of ARG (alternate reality games) that benefit the user's development
          - Tech confidence
          - Collaboration skills
          - Community
      - Using Video Games to Foster Financial Literacy (==Filament Games==)
        - [Froehling](https://www.filamentgames.com/filament-author/alyssa-froehling) (2023) [EdTech/GameBasedLearning/K12](https://www.filamentgames.com/blog/using-video-games-to-foster-financial-literacy/)
        - Filament Games = educational game [developer](https://www.filamentgames.com/about/), prioritizes making learning fun + scaffolding + go to market
        - Cabello writes that “Games that include some sort of in-game economy and introduce the building blocks of real-world finance are a great starting point,” mentioning Roblox, Civilization, and Minecraft. 
        - "[In this same article from Finance Magnates](https://www.financemagnates.com/thought-leadership/how-video-games-can-improve-financial-skills/), the author notes that many in-game financial landscapes cultivate negotiation, peer-to-peer trading, and [system-based](https://www.filamentgames.com/blog/challenging-systems/) exchanges. Within these digital environments, players learn the art of making their virtual assets work effectively, offering vital lessons that can often be scarce in the [real world](https://www.filamentgames.com/blog/using-digital-games-to-teach-real-world-issues/). However, the mere presence of financial systems in games does not inherently impart financial literacy."
        - Examples of FinLit games:
          - [JA Titan](https://www.filamentgames.com/project/ja-titan/) - role of a company exec in a manufacturing sector
            - efficiently allocate funds, balance investor requirements, cater to various stakeholders’ needs
            - CRITIQUE: Does target financial skills, but not at the personal level: more of an economic/business administration focus
          - [Fintropolis](https://www.allyfintropolis.com/) - game within Minecraft to teach FinLit
            - Using a medium that kids are likely already familiar with and enjoy
            - Players can navigate a city, choosing a career, open a bank account, practice with a budget, taxes, credit/vs debit, investing, etc
            - CRITIQUE: No internal grading system, students might get distracted, associating gameplay with previous Minecraft experiences
      - Games, Motivation, and Learning: A Research and Practice Model (==Kumar==)
        - Kumar (2002) [Simulation and Gaming](https://www.academia.edu/1922344/Games_motivation_and_learning_A_research_and_practice_model?nav_from=df7240a1-5c03-456c-8d63-b45120daf3f9)
          - Article focuses on psychology of games + elements of successful educational games (more theoretical and general (great article))
        - "The goal is to develop learners who are self-directed and self-motivated, both because the activity is interesting in itself and because achieving the outcome is important." (445)
        - Gaming is not a one-and-done activity, instead, players continually pick it up and sometimes must be told to put the game away. 
        - Essential game characteristics:
          - Thornton and Cleveland (1990) = interactivity. 
          - de Felix and Johnston (1993) = dynamic visuals, interaction, rules, and a goal
          - Gredler (1996) = complex task, the learner’s role, multiple paths to the goal, and learner control. 
          - Malone (1981) = challenge, fantasy, complexity, and control. 
          - Thomas and Macredie (1994) = actions have no real-world consequences. 
          - Baranauskas, Neto, and Borges (1999) = challenge and risk. 
          - Crookall, Oxford, and Saunders (1987) = rules, strategy, goals, competition/cooperation, chance
        - Table with game characteristics and researchers associated with them (447)
        - Author conclusion: game characteristics can be described in terms of six broad dimensions or categories: fantasy, rules/goals, sensory stimuli, challenge, mystery, and control.
        - Fantasy:
          - Rieber (1996): fantasy contexts can be exogenous or endogenous to the game
            - Exogenous: fantasy overlaid on learning content
            - Endogenous: fantasy better integrated into learning content (ex. flying a plane while learning about physics)
        - Rules/Goals:
          - Clear, specific, and difficult goals lead to enhanced performance (Locke & Latham, 1990)
        - Sensory Stimuli:
          - Rieber (1991) argued that animated graphics enhance the motivational appeal of instructional activities and found that students overwhelmingly choose to return to practice activities that include dynamic graphics.
        - Challenge:
          - Not too easy or too difficult = optimal, Malone and Lepper (1987), similar to FLOW state
          - How to get optimal challenge level: 
            - Clear goals, but uncertain outcome
            - Progressive difficulty levels, multiple goals, certain amount of informational ambiguity
            - Performance feedback/scores to track progress toward goals
            - Goals = meaningful to individual
        - Mystery:
          - 2 types of curiosity = sensory + cognitive
          - Curiosity stimulated by an information gap in our existing knowledge that is intermediate—not too simple or too complex (similar idea to scaffolding - adjusted difficulty so that the ZPD (zone of proximal development) does not reach anxiety/overload or boredom)
        - Control:
          - Games evoke a sense of personal control when users are allowed to select strategies, manage the direction of activity, and make decisions that directly affect outcomes, even if actions are not instructionally relevant
        - Addictive behavior towards games (451)
          - Addiction - returning to an activity repeatedly
          - Determinants of motivation?
          - How motivation links to instructional outcomes?
          - FLOW STATE
          - At what point do players stop playing obsessively?
            - Dekkers and Donatti (1981): simulation games became less effective the longer the game was used (suggesting that perhaps trainees became bored over time) [this contributes to the SpikyPOV about games not doing well enough with novelty]
          - Instructional game = oxymoron?
        - Vroom (1964) reported that the valence of an activity (or the attractiveness of outcomes) plays a major role in effort expenditure
        - Game cycle
          - Recurring judgment-behavior-feedback loops that characterize game play
          - Can lead to changes in locus of control (the perception that outcomes are a result of one’s own control), self-efficacy (perceptions of competence and mastery), and valence (the value placed on the activity)
      - Endogenous Fantasy – Based Serious Games: Intrinsic Motivation and Learning (==Gunter==)
        - Gunter (2007) [World Academy of Science](https://www.academia.edu/123816279/Endogenous_Fantasy_Based_Serious_Games_Intrinsic_Motivation_and_Learning)
        - Germane load: degree of effort for processing new info (modified by how motivated/interested the student is in the material/presentation method)
        - Research premise: if serious games can reduce intrinsic/extrinsic/germane load, they should increase learning efficacy
        - Need a fine balance with regard to cognitive load: games should decrease the barrier to entry (cognitive load), keeping students engaged, but not be so involved that the student is distracted/overwhelmed by the gameplay mechanics (where gameplay outshines learning components) [594]
        - 2 large-scale games: 
          - Eve-online 
            - MMORPG with economic systems, player-determined objectives, 
            - "Anecdotally, players have been known to claim that what they learned from playing this game has actually helped them succeed in their college economics classes"
            - "What the game misses by not being designed for a specific curriculum is goal-specific learning materials (content relevance), its ability to encourage players to transfer their newly gained knowledge to other contexts, and the number of levels as what is found in World of Warcraft so that naturalization of content is assured."
          - World of Warcraft
            - Good as it has a level system
            - Would need content focus
        - 3 key design principles:
          - relevant to the players, their needs, and previously introduced content
          - engaging to encourage continued play
          - gameplay context hierarchically and repeatedly utilized within the game in such a way that it parallels Bloom’s taxonomic views on knowledge acquisition
      - Taking Educational Games Seriously: Using the RETAIN Model to Design Endogenous Fantasy into Standalone Educational Games (==Gunter==)
        - Gunter (2008) [EdTech Research and Development](https://www.academia.edu/29030856/Taking_educational_games_seriously_using_the_RETAIN_model_to_design_endogenous_fantasy_into_standalone_educational_games)
        - Factors contributing to ineffectiveness of educational games
          - Teachers reluctantly give into pressures to incorporate games, hoping they will be effective because the content is housed within a game
          - Developers pressured to quickly retrofit educational content into existing games, or build generic games that can be used for different educational contexts (possible examples include MC education edition, civilization edu, simcity edu, roblox education, etc)
          - Prensky's attributes involved with educational gaming (513)
        - Immersion Hierarchy (515)
        - 2 requirements to be relevant and holistic
          - Academic content used in a hierarchical manner: Bloom’s concept of the nature of knowledge acquisition and question construction
            - Content again used by the player–learner to apply synthesize, and/or create new knowledge on that next higher level, which, in turn, makes that content more automatic and naturalized (scaffolding)
          - Content must be intrinsically coupled with fantasy context of the game
            - The concept of not interrupting the ﬂow of the game while providing progressive feedback is the major differentiation between endogenous and exogenous fantasy.
        - SDT (self-determination theory) [518]
        - RETAIN Model
          - Relevance Embedding Translation Adaptation Immersion & Naturalization
          - Table 4 shows differing degrees of adherence to factors linked to success (524)
        - ARCS motivational model (520)
          - Attention, Relevance, Conﬁdence/Challenge, and Satisfaction/Success.
        - Bloom's Taxonomy
          - 6 levels of knowledge acquisition, linked to mastery learning
      - Archetypes of Gamification: Analysis of mHealth Apps (==Schmidt-Kraepelin)== 
        - Schmidt-Kraepelin (2020) [JMIR mHealth and uHealth](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC7605978/)
        - (*) = Best applications for FinLit
        - **Archetype 1: Competition + Collaboration (*)**
          - The competition and collaboration elements as well as internal rewards aim to help users in reaching externally and self-set goals, thus evoking compliance and behavioral change
          - May draw on continuous narrative but often relies on episodic style
          - Most effective for 3-8th
        - Archetype 2: Pursuing Self-Set Goals Without Rewards
          - Not cooperative collaboration, but supportive collaboration (connection to social media) + competition
        - **Archetype 3: Episodical Compliance Tracking (*)**
          - No negative reinforcement or social components (competition/collaboration)
          - Narrative = always episodic
          - Most effective for 3-5th
        - Archetype 4: Inherent Gamification for External Goals
          - No competitive/collaborative elements, relies on positive reinforcement
          - "aims to improve the users’ health through an episodic narrative of externally set goals"
        - **Archetype 5: Internal Rewards for Self-Set Goals (*)**
          - No competition, but different forms of collaboration, solely drawing on internal rewards (points, badges, etc)
          - Users set their own goals and may experience positive or negative feedback as a result
          - Most effective for 6-8th
        - **Archetype 6: Continuous Assistance Through Positive Reinforcement (*)**
          - No rewards or progressive user advancement
          - Only positive reinforcement
          - Most effective for K-2
        - Archetype 7: Positive and Negative Reinforcement Without Rewards
          - No competition, collaboration, or rewards
          - Goals set externally
          - Positive and negative reinforcement strategies are implied, but user advancement is only presentational and not progressive
        - Archetype 8: Progressive Gamification for Health Professionals
          - Progressive user advancement (new content unlocks or difficulty increases)
          - Aims to change attitude of users (unlike 7)
      - Game on: Can Gamification Enhance Productivity? (==Rahiman==)
        - Rahiman (2023) [F1000 Research](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC10905147/) 
        - Major results: 
          - Variables such as perceived adoption and usefulness in the gamified system is significantly associated with job engagement. Similarly, employee’s recognition and perceived motivation have a positive impact on productivity. The study identified job engagement mediating factor to enhance organizational productivity in a gamified system
          - Effectiveness depends on design of gamified system
        - Objective: explain casual relation between role of gamification towards productivity/job engagement
        - Hypotheses (all accepted)
          - H1: Employees perceived adoption of the gamified HRM system is significantly associated with job engagement
          - H2: Employee recognition in the gamified HRM system has a significant impact on productivity
          - H3: Employees perceived usefulness in the gamified system is positively associated with job engagement
          - H4: Employees perceived motivation in the gamified system is significantly associated with productivity
          - H5: Employee Job engagement is a mediating factor to enhance productivity
        - Challenges with implementation
          - "As per past studies, at the institutional level, it is challenging to integrate gamification in large firms due to the regulations ([Sailer et al., 2017](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC10905147/#ref83)). On the other hand, in small firms, gamification is an unwanted overhead since the outcome of post-implementation is not attractive"
          - Very difficult to apply gamification in ministry/government organizations
      - Practices, Purposes and Challenges in Integrating Gamification Using Technology: A Mixed-Methods Study on University Academics (==Kabilan==)
        - Kabilan (2023) [Educational Inf Tech](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC10071252/)
        - Gamification can be further advanced - 5 main themes
          - Motivating students' learning
          - Facilitating thinking skills + solving problems
          - Engaging student learning
          - Facilitating interactions
          - Achieving specific teaching + learning goals
        - 2 models to facilitate + enhance students' learning though gamification
        - Recent SLR studies also show that findings on gamification results are inconsistent and thus, more understanding is needed to further advance our knowledge in the use of gamification for learning (Oliveira et al., [2022](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC10071252/#CR45)).
        - Drivers include the capacities and abilities of gamification to i) attract students’ attention, ii) motivate students, iii) facilitate interactive learning and, iv) facilitate students’ learning
        - Barriers are: 
          - i) lack of resources, time and facilities
          - ii) students’ lack of interest
          - iii) short-term impact
          - iv) uncontrolled learning in the classroom
        - Integration of gamification (with technology) “requires a thoughtful interweaving of all three key sources of knowledge: technology, pedagogy, and content” (Mishra & Koehler, [2006](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC10071252/#CR36), p. 1029).
        - Criterion 1 – ‘Approaches to teaching that influence, motivate and inspire students to learn’; Criterion 2 – ‘Development of curricula and resources that reflect a command of the field’; Criterion 3 – ‘Approaches to assessment and feedback that foster independent learning’; Criterion 4 – ‘Respect and support for the development of students as individuals’ and; Criterion 5 – ‘Scholarly activities that have influenced and enhanced learning and teaching”
        - Motivating Student Learning
          - Lessons that utilize gamification involve the use of “interesting teaching aids” that motivate the student to attend classes. On the contrary, this is not the case when “lecture slides are used” (L15oe)
        - Facilitating thinking skills and solving problems
          - L45i sees gamification as a “brainstorming” device that, according to L2i, is an important element “in cultivating critical thinking and problem-solving skills”. In such situations, students become “very participative” when they are solving the problems and completing the tasks collaboratively. This is because “the students will try to help their peers while completing the tasks” (L25oe)
        - Engaging students’ learning
          -  The academics emphasize on the fun and interactive learning that gamification propagates—“active learning”(L1oe) that allows “knowledge absorption” (L11oe) and “helps reinforce students’ learning and eliminates boredom” (L27oe) by giving them “the opportunity to see the real-world applications” (L11oe).
          - “students are very much engaged in learning” if “it is (integrated with) technology game-based” on the students’ smartphones.
        - Facilitating interactions
          - 3 types of interactions (peer interaction, student–teacher interaction, and student-content interaction)
          - Gamification used to enhance class interaction between lecturer and students
        - Achieving specific teaching and learning goals
          - gamification = tool to be used at the “beginning stage” with the aim to “trigger some concepts” because once the concepts are unraveled, “the students would be able to comprehend the lesson easily”
          - may also be valuable for learning at later stages of lessons, specifically for “retention” of knowledge, ideas, and concepts (L38oe), reinforcement of learning (L35oe, L61i) and assessment (L12oe). It could be used to test students’ “knowledge before starting a lesson” and after a lesson (L18i, L30i) and also to assess students’ understanding (L71oe, L85oe) and “students' comprehension on course content”
          - *It will be used after lecture. Reinforcement. Also for reinforcement and assessment. Gamification cannot be used for the whole course I am teaching English, it cannot be the main focus (L16i)*
          - *For recapping a lesson, I test students on what they have learned. I will ask students to create their own Kahoot. They have to create their question and answers. Students can learn from each other (L31i)*
          - *During the last five minutes of class, students reflect on the lesson and write down what they’ve learned (L20i)*
        - Challenges in integrating gamification
          - Time factor
          - Teachers need to be clear on what must be done
          - Suitability of content for gamification (HE profs have trouble integrating gamification for their content)
          - Usability/accessibility of technology
        - “To explore technologies in relationship to subject matter in authentic contexts” (Mishra & Koehler, [2006](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC10071252/#CR36), p. 1045), gamification could be an authentic context of learning, whereby students are the focus of teaching, are actively engaged and are cognitively stimulated (Tan et al., [2022](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC10071252/#CR58)).
      - Financial Literacy Games — Increasing Utility Value by Instructional Design in Upper Secondary Education (==Platz==)
        - Platz (2025) [Edu Sci](https://www.mdpi.com/2227-7102/15/2/227?utm_source=chatgpt.com)
        - Educational games as a learning medium have inherent motivational potential. However, this often cannot be empirically supported ([Wouters et al., 2013](https://www.mdpi.com/2227-7102/15/2/227?utm_source=chatgpt.com#B87-education-15-00227)). A proactive financial education, which lays the foundation for lifelong engagement with the topic of finance, is particularly relevant in school-based financial education ([Pfändler, 2021](https://www.mdpi.com/2227-7102/15/2/227?utm_source=chatgpt.com#B57-education-15-00227); [van Campenhout, 2015](https://www.mdpi.com/2227-7102/15/2/227?utm_source=chatgpt.com#B81-education-15-00227)).
        - Game-Based Learning and Serious Games to Promote Financial Literacy
          - Idea of games is complex because broad concept of games
          - 5 defining characteristics of games:
            - Rule-based, simulated systems that use causal rules
            - Responsive, allowing players to act promptly and decisively
            - Challenging
            - Cumulative, reflecting previous actions
            - Inviting, providing an interesting and engaging environment
          - The successful integration of GBL into the educational process requires a careful design to minimize learners’ cognitive load by avoiding superfluous information and focusing on essential learning objectives ([Mayer & Johnson, 2010](https://www.mdpi.com/2227-7102/15/2/227?utm_source=chatgpt.com#B49-education-15-00227))
        - Promotion of Utility Value Through Financial Literacy Games
          - Perception of the subjective value of an activity (valence) and the expectation of one’s success can have a positive effect on persistence and performance ([Eccles et al., 1983](https://www.mdpi.com/2227-7102/15/2/227?utm_source=chatgpt.com#B21-education-15-00227); [Wigfield & Eccles, 2000](https://www.mdpi.com/2227-7102/15/2/227?utm_source=chatgpt.com#B86-education-15-00227)). 
        - Research Question and Hypotheses
          - Players with strategic game mechanics and direct reflection prompts perceive a higher utility value than players without strategic game mechanics and/or direct reflection phases.
            - Confirmed: utility value increases significantly more in group III than in other groups, which applied strategic game mechanics and direct reflection prompts
          - For players with a strategic game mechanic and direct reflection prompt, the individual financial interest will increase more than those without a strategic game mechanic and/or direct reflection prompts. 
            - Not confirmed
        - Moonshot Game
          - Upper secondary school students (11th-12th grade)
          - Played in groups of three to five people and involves alternating phases of playing and reflecting. At the beginning, each player chooses one of six life dreams representing different values and allowing for individual identification ([Platz et al., 2021](https://www.mdpi.com/2227-7102/15/2/227?utm_source=chatgpt.com#B62-education-15-00227)). The life dreams are divided into three phases of increasing complexity, which the players must achieve one after the other by managing scarce resources under changing economic conditions.
          - Game field represents different economic conditions
          - Each player receives starting capital and must subsequently manage their own income and expenditure. The game is based on drawing event cards related to resources such as jobs, education, and investment. The focus is on achieving their life dreams by managing scarce resources. During the game, participants document their financial transactions to create a balance sheet at the end of each year. The rules of Moonshot are based on real-life conditions but have been simplified
          - Random + strategic game mechanic 
            - Random 
              - students rolled a die to get a random event card and then chose whether to buy or use what was on the card
            - Strategy-based
              - Students had time tokens and chose which event cards to play, letting them manage their time and money more actively. This meant students using the strategy version didn’t have to wait for random events—they could focus on what they wanted, and their success depended more on their own choices, similar to the board game Risk
          - Direct + open reflection prompts
      - Andrzej Marczewski - Gamification Expert Articles (==Marczewski==)
        - [Visionar](https://visionar.design/blog/andrzej-marczewski-gamification-user-types-designer/) Interview
          - 80/20 rule for employees (but possibly also students?)
            - One of the things that defines play (for Marczewski) is freedom/autonomy. He believes it is a key element of intrinsic motivation + creativity.
            - Even the illusion of autonomy = motivating
            - 20% of time dedicated to pursuing personal projects
          - Key thing to avoid when gamifying a system = relying on rewards (possibly only appeals to player type)
          - Decreasing returns are inevitable: idea is for gamification to lower barrier to entry + cause them to stay for intrinsic reasons (very similar to scaffolding)
          - Gamification will eventually just be a part of good design
          - "Invest in people and they will invest in you"
        - [Hexad](https://www.gamified.uk/user-types/) Types (most people = mixed types)
          - **Philanthropist (purpose)**
            - Motivated by helping others, contributing without expecting rewards
            - Serious fun, meaning, caretaking, access, collect & trade, gifting/sharing, sharing knowledge
          - **Socializer (relatedness)**
            - Driven by social interaction/relationships/community
            - People fun, guilds/teams, social network + status, social discovery + pressure, competition
          - **Free Spirit (autonomy/self expression)**
            - Enjoys exploring/creating/freedom/independence
            - Two subtypes = creator + explorer
            - Easy fun, exploration, branching choices, easter eggs, unlocking content, creativity tools, customization
          - **Achiever (mastery)**
            - Seeks personal growth/learning/overcoming challenges
            - Hard fun, challenges, certificates, learning/new skills, quests/levels, boss battles
          - **Player (rewards)**
            - Motivated by earning points/badges/tangible incentives
            - Points/XP, physical rewards & prizes, leaderboards, badges & achievements, virtual economy, lottery/game of chance
          - **Disruptor (change)**
            - Desires to disrupt/innovate/provoke change within system
            - Innovation platform, voting/voice, development tools, anonymity, light touch, anarchy
        - Benefits of knowing the Hexad
          - Personalization + balanced engagement + user diversity
          - System resilience (regarding disruptors)
        - [Test](https://gamified.uk/UserTypeTest2023/user-type-test.php?lid=) Determining Types = Gamification User Types Hexad Scale
        - Most common = Philanthropists + Socializers 
        - Significant associations with the big 5 personality traits [here](https://www.gamefulbits.com/wp-content/uploads/2016/09/2016-The-Gamification-User-Types-Hexad-Scale.pdf#page=1.29)
          - Big 5 = openness to experience, conscientiousness, extraversion, agreeableness, neuroticism
        - [HEXAD](https://www.gamified.uk/user-types/): A Player Type Framework for Gamification Design
          - 4 basic intrinsic types = achiever, socializer, philanthropist, free spirit
            - 
          - Motivated by RAMP - relatedness, autonomy, mastery, purpose
          - Other two types = more complex (player + disruptor)
            - 
            - 
        - The Gamification User Types Hexad Scale (Tondello)
          - Tondello (2016) [HCI Games Group](https://www.gamefulbits.com/wp-content/uploads/2016/09/2016-The-Gamification-User-Types-Hexad-Scale.pdf#page=1.29)
          - Background
            - Studies indicating need for personalization in games, but difficult to map user personality to design elements
            - This study created a 24-items survey response scale to score users’ preferences towards the six different motivations in the Hexad framework
            - Significant associations with Big 5 Traits (ocean)
            - Gaps addressed here: lack of standard assessment protocol for user's preferences based on Hexad + no empirical validation associating Hexad user types and game design elements 
            - SDT provides basis for Hexad model concerning intrinsic/extrinsic motivation
            - Yee's research deals with MMORPGs more than general gamification models
            - Other gamification models presented before the Hexad: categorizing players based on behavior
              - What differentiated Marczewski's Hexad model: it examined motivations (both intrinsic + extrinsic)
                -

          - Marczewski (2021) [eLearning Industry](https://elearningindustry.com/marczewski-gamification-user-types)
          - Bartle's types did not translate well to non MMO games/non game related systems (gamified systems)
          - Some other models of types = Nicolle Lazaro’s 4Keys2Fun, Nick Yee’s Model of Player Motivations, Amy Jo Kim’s Social Player Types
