      - The Intrinsic Motivation RAMP (==Marczewski==)
        - Detailed [explanation](https://www.gamified.uk/gamification-framework/the-intrinsic-motivation-ramp/) of the relatedness, autonomy, mastery, and purpose
        - RAMP Framework Breakdown
          - 1. Relatedness
            - Definition: The desire to be connected to others and feel a sense of belonging.
            - Application in Gamification: Fostered through communities, social status, and meaningful connections.
            - Key Insight: This is described as the "glue" that holds a gamified system together. When points and badges lose their appeal, the social connection keeps users engaged.
            - Examples: Internal social networks, forums for sharing and feedback, and status based on peer recognition rather than just leaderboards.
          - 2. Autonomy * Definition: The need for freedom, control, and meaningful choice.
            - Application in Gamification: Providing users with options on how they engage or learn, avoiding the feeling of being stifled or micromanaged.
            - Key Insight: Autonomy fosters creativity and innovation. The article cites Google's 80/20 rule (allowing employees time for personal projects) as a prime example of autonomy leading to success (e.g., Gmail).
            - Examples: Allowing meaningful choices in learning paths or giving employees freedom in how they execute tasks.
          - 3. Mastery * Definition: The process of becoming skilled and overcoming challenges that scale with ability.
            - Application in Gamification: Creating a journey where difficulty increases alongside skill, potentially leading to a state of "Flow."
            - Key Insight: Users need to feel their skills are improving. The article references video game onboarding (training levels) and Malcolm Gladwell's "10,000-hour rule" to illustrate the importance of continuous challenge and practice.
            - Examples: Tutorials that guide users, escalating challenges as proficiency grows, and opportunities for skill development over time.
          - 4. Purpose
            - Definition: The need for meaning and the feeling that actions contribute to something greater than oneself.
            - Application in Gamification: connecting tasks to a larger cause or altruistic outcome.
            - Key Insight: People are motivated by contributing to the greater good. Wikipedia is used as an example of people working freely for a shared purpose.
            - Examples: Charity donations as rewards, answering questions on forums to help others, and tasks that clearly contribute to a larger organizational or social goal.
      - The Future Of Learning: Balancing Good And Bad Screen Time (==Price==)
        - Price (2025) Future of Education
        - Children 8-12 = 5hrs, teens up to 9hrs screen time
        - Not all screen time is good/bad
        - 3 Elements of best gamified experiences
          - Mechanics: measurable components - points/rankings
          - Dynamics: player's personalized experience - feedback/tasks/competition (like MMOs or Sandboxes?)
          - Aesthetics: emotional responses to the game - elements of companionship/storytelling/entertainment
      - Octalysis Gamification Framework (==Chou==)
        - Chou [Website](https://yukaichou.com/gamification-examples/octalysis-complete-gamification-framework/)
        - Octalysis: insight into 8 key motivations (applicable to everything we do)
          - [1] Epic Meaning + Calling
            - Player believes they are doing something greater than themselves, "chosen" to do something
            - Devotes a lot of his time to maintaining a forum or helping to create things for the entire community
          - [2] Development + Accomplishment
            - Internal drive of making progress, developing skills, eventually overcoming challenges
            - Core drive that is easiest to design for (points, badges, leaderboards mostly focus on)
          - [3] Empowerment of Creativity + Feedback
            - Users engaged in creative process, have to repeatedly solve problems + try different combinations
            - Not only need ways to express creativity, but need to see results, receive feedback, and respond
            - Legos + painting: evergreen mechanics (game designer no longer needs to continuously add more content to keep activity fresh/engaging)
          - [4] Ownership + Possession
            - Motivated because they feel like they own something (making them want to improve the thing they own + own even more) -> deals with many virtual goods/currencies 
            - If person spends more time customizing their skin, they will feel a heightened sense of ownership
            - Makes collecting stamps/puzzles fun, reason that Apple has products on major display
          - [5] Social Influence/Relatedness
            - Mentorship, acceptance, social responses, companionship, competition, envy
            - This drive = well studied in psychology + consumer behavior
          - [6] Scarcity + Impatience
            - Drive of wanting something because you can't have it
            - Many games have appointment dynamics (come back in 2hr to get your reward) -> motivates them to think about it longer since they cannot have it right away
            - How Facebook took off (gradually opening access to more schools, then to everyone)
            - With social influence, is the drive for designer brands maintaining their prestige
          - [7] Unpredictability + Curiosity
            - Unpredictability causes the brain to think about it often
            - Reason for watching movies/reading books (especially cliffhangers)
            - Factor of gambling addiction/when companies do sweepstakes
            - Skinner Box experiments
          - [8] Loss + Avoidance
            - Avoid losing previous progress, etc
            - Opportunities that are fading away utilize this drive (people feel that if they don't act immediately, they lose the opportunity to act forever) -> companies stating that a sale ends soon, relates to scarcity/impatience
        - Types of Drives
          - Right Brain Drives -> Intrinsic Motivation
            - Empowerment, Social Influence, Unpredictability 
          - Left Brain Drives -> Extrinsic Motivation
            - Accomplishment, Ownership, Scarcity
          - Black Hat Drives -> Stick
            - Scarcity, Avoidance, Unpredictability
            - Creating sense of urgency in short term user engagement, making users feel obsessed/proactive/focused
            - Example = Duolingo notification: "You're about to lose your streak!"
          - White Hat Drives -> Carrot
            - Accomplishment, meaning, empowerment
            - Creating sense of wellbeing, higher purpose, meaning, happiness
            - Example = most of gamification tactics (achievements, progress tracking, points, etc)
        - More In-Depth Analysis
          - The Octalysis Framework, developed by Yu-kai Chou, identifies 8 Core Drives (motivators) that influence engagement and behavior in gamification design.
          - The Core Drives:
            - Epic Meaning & Calling — feeling part of something greater or chosen for a purpose.
            - Development & Accomplishment — internal drive for progress, skill mastery, and overcoming challenges.
            - Empowerment of Creativity & Feedback — motivation through creative problem-solving and seeing results.
            - Ownership & Possession — desire to own, improve, and protect something, virtual or real.
            - Social Influence & Relatedness — social factors like mentorship, acceptance, competition, and companionship.
            - Scarcity & Impatience — wanting something because it is rare or limited.
            - Unpredictability & Curiosity — desire to find out what happens next, engagement through uncertainty.
            - Loss & Avoidance — motivation to avoid negative consequences and losing progress.
          - The Octalysis Framework also distinguishes between Left Brain (extrinsic/logical) and Right Brain (intrinsic/emotional) Core Drives symbolically for design purposes.
          - White Hat Core Drives (positive motivators) create satisfying, empowering experiences while Black Hat Core Drives (urgent, anxiety-inducing motivators) can drive behavior but may leave a negative emotional impact.
          - Expert Integration: Yu-kai Chou’s framework elevates gamification from superficial mechanics (points, badges, leaderboards) to a powerful tool that taps into fundamental psychological needs for meaning, mastery, ownership, social connection, curiosity, and avoidance of loss. 
            - This holistic understanding aids designers and organizations to create richer, more sustainable motivation. This approach aligns with contemporary behavioral science and ethical design mandates, underscoring [Octalysis](https://xn--yu-kai%20chous%20framework%20elevates%20gamification%20from%20superficial%20mechanics%20(points,%20badges,%20leaderboards)%20to%20a%20powerful%20tool%20that%20taps%20into%20fundamental%20psychological%20needs%20for%20meaning,%20mastery,%20ownership,%20social%20connection,%20curiosity,%20and%20avoidance%20of%20loss-rw00s.%20this%20holistic%20understanding%20aids%20designers%20and%20organizations%20to%20create%20richer,%20more%20sustainable%20motivation%20systems.in%20practice,%20a%20deep%20grasp%20of%20these%20drives%20enables%20the%20crafting%20of%20experiences%20where%20users%20feel%20genuinely%20inspired,%20challenged,%20connected,%20and%20in%20control,%20turning%20routine%20tasks%20into%20compelling%20journeys.%20this%20approach%20aligns%20with%20contemporary%20behavioral%20science%20and%20ethical%20design%20mandates,%20underscoring%20octalysis%20as%20a%20leading%20model%20for%20transformative%20engagement%20design.octalysisgroup+4/) as a leading model for transformative engagement design.​
            - YouTube [Video](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=-hz96Sx7S5U)

          - In-Depth Explanations of Each Drive
            - Can you use Gamification for Evil? 
              - I have a bit more juice left in my time machine, so we're going to use it one more time to conduct an A/B test experiment.
              - Scenario A
                - Yesterday, your boss gave you a promotion and entrusted you with a new project with a budget of $100,000. Today, your boss invites you to her office to tell you more about the project expectations.
                - "Success with this project not only means growth for the company, but more importantly, 20% of the profit will be donated to the charity of your choice (CD1) (CD3). I want to be very clear: success here means you'll be right on track for your next promotion in a few years (CD2)."
              - Scenario B
                - Yesterday, your boss gave you a promotion and entrusted you with a new project with a budget of $100,000. Today, your boss invites you to her office to tell you more about the project expectations.
                - "You have 3 months to show me traction on this project or else I'm pulling you off the team and giving the project to someone else (CD6) (CD8)."
              - Recap
              - How did you feel through each scenario?
                - In Scenario A, your boss appeals to CD1, CD3, and CD2, which are White Hat Core Drives. You are motivated to accomplish your objectives because you want to help donate to the charity of your choice and because you want to move up in your career. These are positive motivators.
                - In Scenario B, your boss appeals to CD6 and CD8, which are Black Hat Core Drives. You are motivated to accomplish your objective because you don't want to lose your job and you're motivated to move fast because you have a tight deadline. These are negative motivators.
              - An intelligent and strategic Gamification designer must understand how to use White Hat and Black Hat techniques to drive urgent action while making the users feel good about their actions.
              - White Hat vs Black Hat
                - White Hat Core Drives are motivation elements that make us feel powerful, fulfilled, and satisfied. They make us feel in control of our own lives and actions. The White Hat Core Drives are represented at the top of Octalysis:
                  - CD1: Epic Meaning & Calling
                  - CD2: Development & Accomplishment
                  - CD3: Empowerment of Creativity & Feedback
                - In contrast, Black Hat Core Drives, make us feel obsessed, anxious, and addicted. While they are very strong in motivating our behaviors, in the long run they often leave a bad taste in our mouths because we feel weâ€™ve lost control of our own behaviors. The Black Hat Core Drives are represented at the bottom of Octalysis:
                  - CD6: Scarcity & Impatience
                  - CD7: Unpredictability & Curiosity
                  - CD8: Loss & Avoidance
              - While the benefits of White Hat Gamification are quite obvious, it does have a critical weakness: it does not create a sense of urgency.
                - Urgency is where Black Hat Gamification excels.
              - [Read my blog post about White Hat vs Black Hat Gamification](https://yukaichou.us5.list-manage.com/track/click?u=0e4d88e02b5b077b7bee2ca33&id=1df8e61730&e=ca8d409c61)
              - [Bad shifts from White Hat to Black Hat](https://yukaichou.us5.list-manage.com/track/click?u=0e4d88e02b5b077b7bee2ca33&id=a7f3836355&e=ca8d409c61)
              - [When to use White Hat Gamification](https://yukaichou.us5.list-manage.com/track/click?u=0e4d88e02b5b077b7bee2ca33&id=448038f855&e=ca8d409c61)
              - [When to use Black Hat Gamification](https://yukaichou.us5.list-manage.com/track/click?u=0e4d88e02b5b077b7bee2ca33&id=a74fa4ca7a&e=ca8d409c61)
              - [How to use Black Hat Gamification for fundraising](https://yukaichou.us5.list-manage.com/track/click?u=0e4d88e02b5b077b7bee2ca33&id=ad7baf01e4&e=ca8d409c61)
              - [Manipulation and ethics](https://yukaichou.us5.list-manage.com/track/click?u=0e4d88e02b5b077b7bee2ca33&id=c73a427577&e=ca8d409c61)
              - [How to transition between White Hat and Black Hat Gamification](https://yukaichou.us5.list-manage.com/track/click?u=0e4d88e02b5b077b7bee2ca33&id=08794591fe&e=ca8d409c61)
              - [The fall of Plants vs Zombies](https://yukaichou.us5.list-manage.com/track/click?u=0e4d88e02b5b077b7bee2ca33&id=f8d708165c&e=ca8d409c61)
            - CD 1: Epic Meaning and Calling
              - Story:
                - I'm going to give you a job to do. For this job, your task is to consistently monitor hundreds of pages on a forum. If you notice that there is a mistake on a page, then you must correct the mistake as quickly as possible. Furthermore, you must keep the pages updated with the most recent news and information about the topic. Oh, and most importantly, I'm not going to pay you to do this job. Do you accept my offer?
                  - If you're like most humans, your natural reaction is, "Are you kidding me?!?! You expect me to do this mundane work for free?" But believe it or not, thousands of people happily do this type of work every day, and they do it for free.
              - A cause greater than yourself - This is the power of CD1: Epic Meaning & Calling. And the example I gave you above, well, I'm sure you've used this forum many times already -- it's Wikipedia.
              - CD1 is the need or the urge to be a part of something much bigger than just yourself. When this drive is activated, participants choose to be members of your system and will take action not because it necessarily benefits them directly, but because it benefits a greater cause.
              - Gamification YouTube series: [Epic Meaning & Calling](https://yukaichou.us5.list-manage.com/track/click?u=0e4d88e02b5b077b7bee2ca33&id=1b0a09e92e&e=ca8d409c61)
              - [full blog post about CD1](https://yukaichou.us5.list-manage.com/track/click?u=0e4d88e02b5b077b7bee2ca33&id=8054128d6f&e=ca8d409c61)
              - [How Apple uses CD1](https://yukaichou.us5.list-manage.com/track/click?u=0e4d88e02b5b077b7bee2ca33&id=5da0e09de2&e=ca8d409c61)
              - [How parents use CD1](https://yukaichou.us5.list-manage.com/track/click?u=0e4d88e02b5b077b7bee2ca33&id=4eba131d99&e=ca8d409c61)
              - [Top 10 Gamification examples for the human race](https://yukaichou.us5.list-manage.com/track/click?u=0e4d88e02b5b077b7bee2ca33&id=019f9ccdcf&e=ca8d409c61)
            - CD 2 Development and Accomplishment
              - Story:
                - Do you remember in 3rd grade when you got straight A's on your report card? You ran home as fast as you could to show it off to Mom.
                - Or do you remember when your high school basketball team won the championship? You took the long way to class just to walk by and admire the trophy that you had helped earn.
                - Or how about when you won the employee of the month award at your job? You took a picture of your face on the wall and posted it on Facebook for all your friends to see.
              - These are all examples of CD2: Development & Accomplishment, the Core Drive that motivates through a sense of growth towards a goal and accomplishing it. Points, badges, and HALO
              - You've experience CD2 throughout many video games. In Pokemon, you were driven by CD2 (and many other Core Drives) to level up your Pokemon to get stronger, beat your competitors, and earn badges in each city.
              - In the early days of Gamification, companies tried to use PBLs (points, badges, and leaderboards) to motivate their users. However, these companies didn't understand the principles behind CD2, thinking that users would be motivated to complete an action simply by slapping a badge on their profile.
              - CD2 isn't PBLs; CD2 is the motivation to complete a challenge and accomplish an objective. PBLs are a way to recognize a player for their accomplishments.
              - What makes an achievement worth it?
                - Jane McGonigal, renowned game designer and PhD in Performance Studies, points out that challenges and limitations are what make games fun. For example, if I challenged you to pick up a ball and put it in a hole, no one would have any fun. But if I added limitations, rules, and obstacles -- such as a funny stick, putting the hole 10 feet off the ground, or requiring that you only kick the ball -- we suddenly have fun and engaging sports: golf, basketball, and soccer.
                - To motivate a user through CD2, you must give a user a challenge. Challenges must be optimized for every phase of the user journey: if you make the challenges too difficult during Onboarding, then the user will churn; if you make the challenges too easy during the Endgame, then the user will churn as well. As a human-focused designer, you must find the right balance to the challenge.
              - Gamification YouTube series: [Development & Accomplishment](https://yukaichou.us5.list-manage.com/track/click?u=0e4d88e02b5b077b7bee2ca33&id=858dfa7049&e=ca8d409c61)
              - [Read my blog post about CD2](https://yukaichou.us5.list-manage.com/track/click?u=0e4d88e02b5b077b7bee2ca33&id=ea42be143d&e=ca8d409c61)
              - [How eBay and Amazon use CD2](https://yukaichou.us5.list-manage.com/track/click?u=0e4d88e02b5b077b7bee2ca33&id=3fb109f34d&e=ca8d409c61)
            - CD 3: Empowerment of Creativity and Feedback
              - Story:
                - My friend Jun has been trying to get me to use Betterment for some time now. It's supposed to "automate my investing", but the irony is that I keep telling him that I don't have time to automate my investing. :P
                - Finally, Jun recently sat me down and showed me the cool things you can do with Betterment. A feature that stood out to me was the advice page.
                - Even though Betterment is a set-it-and-forget-it type of product, this Advice feature drives users to consistently play with their allocation to optimize their results. This is the power of CD3: Empowerment of Creativity & Feedback.
              - The top half of Octalysis is White Hat Gamification (it makes you feel good), while the right side of Octalysis is Right Brain Gamification (it's intrinsically enjoyable -- experience oriented). CD3 is located on the top right of Octalysis -- the Golden Corner.
                - This is important because CD3 makes you feel good and you take action because you find the action enjoyable on its own. For example, kids play with legos because the action of creating, building, and designing with the legos is fun on its own, even if there is no one to show off to.
              - CD3 is consequently one of the hardest Core Drives to implement.
              - Meaningful Choice vs Choice Perception
                - Meaningful Choice: whatever you choose will completely alter your journey.
                - Choice Perception: the choice is an illusion meant to drive the user to make a specific selection.
              - Pizza is an example of a product that utilizes CD3: you have the option to customize the size, the amount of cheese, the toppings, and the crust, leading to a unique experience every time.
              - Car companies are giving consumers meaningful choices as well. Check out how Tesla gives users the option to customize their car, giving consumers the true power of choice and selection.
              - Have you heard of the "[Ugly Jerry Effect](https://yukaichou.us5.list-manage.com/track/click?u=0e4d88e02b5b077b7bee2ca33&id=0e66d3926a&e=ca8d409c61)"? The goal of Ugly Jerry is to make something else look more appealing. This is often employed in pricing pages, where the company offers the user bad pricing (usually very expensive to anchor the user into thinking that the product is high quality) next to very appealing pricing (that is usually less expensive, which makes the user feel like they're getting a great deal).
              - Here are examples of Choice Perception:
                - Check out the example above from Survey Monkey. They sandwich the Gold Package next to two Ugly Jerrys: Select Package and Platinum Package. To make sure the user selects the Gold Package, they add the phrase "Best Value" (Glowing Choice -- Game Technique #28), and highlight the Gold Package so that it stands out (Desert Oasis -- Game Technique #38).
            - CD 4: Ownership and Possession
              - Story:
                - In college, my friends Jun, Rees, and I would compete vigorously against each other in Super Smash Bros Melee. Our battles were so intense and filled with trash-talk that at times we wouldn't speak to each other for days because we were sore losers.
                - There was an unspoken rule that we would never play as the other person's character. Jun played as Falco, Rees was Shiek, and I was Dr. Mario. It wasn't just because our character win-stats would get skewed, but more importantly, it was because we felt ownership over our characters. Dr. Mario was mine, and when Rees played with Dr. Mario, it was like he was defiling my character.
                - This is the power of CD4: Ownership & Possession.
              - Money and the accumulation of wealth is just one way to motivate your employees. Just look at how motivated sales people are to achieve their end-of-year bonus.
              - But money is just one way to motivate employees through CD4. Ownership is not just about the accumulation of wealth, but also the intangible ownership you feel over companies and projects.
              - As an entrepreneur, I raised over $1M for my startups. In exchange for funding, investors usually ask for equity in the company. Though the investors are also driven by CD4 to get as much ownership of the startup company as possible, they must be careful to not take too much of the company, or else the entrepreneur will no longer feel like they own it; rather, they'll feel like they're working solely for their investors.
              - Software developers are in extremely high demand in Silicon Valley. Technology startups often cannot compete against the Facebooks and Apples of the world in terms of salary to hire the best developers, so they use stock options (the right to own a piece of the company) as a way to motivate developers to join the startup.
              - CD4 makes you feel ownership of the experience, and when combined with CD8, it makes you feel like you can't leave the product for its competitor because you'll be losing all of your personalized and customized data and recommendations.
              - McDonald's Example:
                - McDonald's used CD4 effectively in its Monopoly Game. McDonald's wants people to buy more burgers, so it created the McDonald's Monopoly game where every time you hit the Win-State of buying a burger you get a piece of property on the Monopoly Board.
                - Once you accumulate all the properties, McDonald's will give you great cash prizes and rewards. Now, like most of these collection games, there will be a few pieces that are extremely rare, and as a result, people are willing to spend real money to acquire these properties.
                - That's somewhat odd, because users are paying money in exchange for a part of the reward, which by itself is technically not worth anything, but because people are so desperate about completing a set that is almost finished, they are highly motivated to complete it.
              - Gamification YouTube series: [Ownership & Possession](https://yukaichou.us5.list-manage.com/track/click?u=0e4d88e02b5b077b7bee2ca33&id=6ba9cddbf9&e=ca8d409c61)
              - [blog post about CD4](https://yukaichou.us5.list-manage.com/track/click?u=0e4d88e02b5b077b7bee2ca33&id=184db83864&e=ca8d409c61)
              - [Fun story about how we treat ownership](https://yukaichou.us5.list-manage.com/track/click?u=0e4d88e02b5b077b7bee2ca33&id=c1fe6c8c39&e=ca8d409c61)
              - [Learn about the science behind consistency](https://yukaichou.us5.list-manage.com/track/click?u=0e4d88e02b5b077b7bee2ca33&id=8398e7823f&e=ca8d409c61)
              - [Learn about the science behind identity](https://yukaichou.us5.list-manage.com/track/click?u=0e4d88e02b5b077b7bee2ca33&id=cd5a4a66d2&e=ca8d409c61)
              - [Learn about the science behind commitments](https://yukaichou.us5.list-manage.com/track/click?u=0e4d88e02b5b077b7bee2ca33&id=c947ae0fe2&e=ca8d409c61)
            - CD 5: Social Influence and Relatedness
              - Story:
                - In doing research for my book, I spent some time playing many games. I would often play just long enough to analyze the 4 Phases of a Player's Journey (Discovery, Onboarding, Scaffolding, Endgame) and then quickly move on to the next game. I don't want to get addicted to a game, as that would distract from the purpose of doing research for my book.
                - I didn't realize that I would encounter a game that utilized CD5 so well that it delayed the launch of my book by 2 months.
                - I discovered a game called Parallel Kingdom on the iPhone that started off like any other RPG. I chose a character, got some items, and set off on a quest. I then was suddenly messaged by somebody that was introduced as my "mentor". My mentor spent time with me on the Onboarding phase, gave me some of his items, and helped me gain some momentum.
                - I thought, "I can't possibly quit this game now and let my mentor down. He just gave me his valuable items! I can't let his effort go to waste." I therefore played, not just for myself, but to make my mentor proud. Two months later, I had to force myself to quit the game so that I could move on with my work. I never did get a chance to say goodbye to my mentor.
                - That is the power of CD5: Social Influence & Relatedness, friends that buy together stay together.
              - In 2008, Groupon emerged as an ecommerce site that utilized Group Quests (Game Technique #22) that incredibly drove shopping behavior. Consumers would only be able to achieve the deal if enough people purchased it as well, driving consumers to ask their friends to join in on the purchase. This growth technique lead to massive amounts of word-of-mouth for Groupon.
              - In 2010, Forbes announced that Groupon was "on pace to make $1 billion in sales, faster than any other business, ever.” That’s not bad for utilizing a time-tested Game Technique in business.
              - If all of your friend's jumped off a bridge, would you too?
              - A hotel wanted to persuade their guests to reuse their towels. To accomplish their objective, they conducted an A/B test experiment by leaving a note in the bathroom. The note had two variations:
                - Please help us save the environment by reusing your towel.
                - 80% of the guests that stayed in this room reused their towels. You should too.
                - Note #2 won by a landslide. The hotel utilized Conformity Anchor (Game Technique #58) to drive guest behavior through CD5 -- "if everyone else is doing it, then I should too."
              - Another of my favorite examples of Conformity Anchor is how infomercials figured out how to dramatically increase viewer behavior. Infomercial companies also ran an A/B test:
                - The infomercial showed a screen of representatives with the words, "Call now. Representatives are standing by."
                - The infomercial showed a screen of representatives desperately answering phone calls with the words, "Call now. If you get a busy signal, please try again."
                - Again, the variation that utilized CD5 to drive consumer behavior won by a landslide. Because the viewer saw that it was the norm to purchase the product, they were more likely to purchase it as well.
              - Easy to implement; difficult to master
                - Most companies recognize that spending times with friends is an intrinsically fun activity; however, with every company going after social and every website trying to make users invite their friends, your system has to be effective to create a truly rewarding experience.
            - CD 6: Scarcity and Impatience
              - Story:
                - Harvard, Stanford, and Yale make up some of the most prestigious universities in the US. Prestige comes from many factors: success of the alumni, quality of the education, and price to attend. All of these factors are important, but one factor stands as the base of prestige; one Core Drive is the root in which value is derived.
                - Acceptance rates for Universities (2016):
                  - Harvard University: 5.2%
                  - Stanford University: 4.7%
                  - Yale University: 6.3%
                  - University of San Diego: 52%
                  - Arizona State University--Tempe: 84.3%
                - As you can see from the figures above, the most prestigious universities in the US also have the lowest acceptance rates. If Harvard had an 84% acceptance rate, it would instantly lose its prestige and value. This is the power of CD6: Scarcity & Impatience.
              - In Oren Klaff’s book Pitch Anything, he explains the concept of Prizing, and how it ties into three fundamental behaviors from our croc brains:
                - We chase that which moves away from us
                - We want what we cannot have
                - We only place value on things that are difficult to obtain
              - Sales people have a mantra that Klaff explains is wrong: always be selling; instead, if sales people want to dramatically improve results, then they should always be leaving. If you are always leaving the discussions, it means that you are not desperate, are highly sought after, and do not depend on this deal. You are the Prize. Klaff claims that, when you do that, money will flow in as the ultimate commodity to win that Prize.
                - Through his methods, Klaff has raised over $450 Million and claims to continue so at a rate of $2 million a week.
              - Scarcity is a marketer's best friend
                - Men, you and I have been tricked. Until the mid 20th century, diamond engagement rings were a small and dying industry in America. That is, until Gerold M. Lauck turned the diamond into a rare status symbol in 1938.
                - "Not only is the demand for diamonds a marketing invention, but diamonds aren’t actually that rare. Only by carefully restricting the supply has De Beers kept the price of a diamond high.", [Rohin Dar of the Huffington Post](https://yukaichou.us5.list-manage.com/track/click?u=0e4d88e02b5b077b7bee2ca33&id=9884193dd5&e=ca8d409c61).
                - “Common sense tells us that the only way to increase the value of diamonds is to make them scarce, that is to reduce production."
                - "Here’s how De Beers has controlled the diamond supply chain for most of the last century. De Beers owns most of the diamond mines. For mines that they don’t own, they have historically bought out all the diamonds, intimidating or co-opting any that think of resisting their monopoly. They then transfer all the diamonds over to the Central Selling Organization (CSO), which they own."
                - That is how marketers and branders turned an otherwise useless, abundant item into a scarce symbol of love and success.
              - It's 5pm. Time for a beer!
                - Restaurants are typically slow from 4pm - 6pm on weeknights. To increase business, restaurants utilize Appointment Dynamics (Game Technique #21). If you take the Desired Action of showing up at the right time, then you hit the Win-state of discounted beer or appetizers. It's a win-win for everyone: the restaurant increases business during slow times and customers feel like they've earned a great discount.
              - Too much of a good thing can be bad. Mobile games have taken this truism to heart, and utilized Torture Breaks (Game Technique #66) to compel users to obsess and keep coming back to play.
                - Candy Crush, which is one of the most successful games in the world incorporates the Torture Break very well. After losing a life, the game pauses and forces you to wait 25 minutes before you can gain another life and proceed to the next level. This draws players to painfully think about those slow-passing 25 minutes and compels players to take action through obsessive thought. 
                  - How effective is CD6? Well, Candy Crush is reportedly making $2 million per day.
            - CD 7: Unpredictability and Curiosity
              - CD7: Unpredictability & Curiosity is a powerful Black Hat Core Drive that is intrinsically thrilling. 
                - By using techniques that generate curiosity, companies can drive their customers to engage with their product; by utilizing unpredictability, companies can retain these customers for much longer into the Endgame Phase.
              - But be careful, CD7 combined with other Black Hat Core Drives, such as CD6, creates obsessive and addictive behaviors.
                - Just take a look at the obsessive compulsive behavior that gambling creates: people are paying money for the thrill of chance, the chance to bet your money on the hope of winning big.
                - Or take a look at the lottery:
                  - "In a typical 6/49 game, six numbers are drawn from a range of 49 and if the six numbers on a ticket match the numbers drawn, the ticket holder is a jackpot winner—this is true no matter in which order the numbers appear. The probability of this happening is 1 in 13,983,816."
                  - In fact, just take a look at all of the things that are more likely to happen to you than winning the lottery:
                    - Being Crushed by a Meteorite: 1 in 700,000
                    - Dying in a Plane Crash: 1 in 11,000,000
                    - Becoming the Next Warren Buffett: 1 in 785,000
                - If you play the slow-and-steady game of building wealth by consistently investing your money in an index fund, then you're almost assured a comfortable retirement.
                - But there's no thrill in slow-and-steady; there's no excitement in comfortable. People want to leave their fate with lady luck. That's the power of CD7.
              - What's in the bag today?
                - You know those beauty samples that retailers hand out for free at the mall? Well, a clever company called [Birchbox](https://yukaichou.us5.list-manage.com/track/click?u=0e4d88e02b5b077b7bee2ca33&id=4d52f4a5b3&e=ca8d409c61) utilized CD7 to not only get customers to pay for these free samples, but to subscribe to a monthly shipment.
                - Why do people pay for free samples? The key here is that they're not paying for samples, they're paying for the thrill and excitement of getting a box with new items every month. If customers knew ahead of time what products they would receive, then CD7 would no longer be utilized and Birchbox would sell dramatically less subscriptions.
            - CD 8: Loss and Avoidance
              - Story:
                - "Well, I'm already 1 year in with 1 year left to go. I might as well finish law school - even though I hate it - or else my whole first year would have been a complete waste of time and money."
                - And so it was that my friend was consumed by The Sunk Cost Prison (Game Technique #50).
                - Today, my friend is working as a litigation lawyer in Downtown Los Angeles. He has a wife, a kid, and a very comfortable life. There's not a day that goes by that he doesn't complain about this job and regret the decision he made to stay in law school. This is the power of CD8: Loss & Avoidance.
              - CD8 motivates through the fear of losing something or having undesirable events transpire. CD8 is a powerful motivator that utilizes Black Hat techniques which result in a high sense of urgency and obsession; however, in the long-run this puts the user in a state of discomfort.
                - CD8 complements many of the other Core Drives for an interesting reason: often it manifests as the reversal of the other Core Drives: you don’t want to look like a loser in front of your friends (CD5), hence you make a purchase; you don't want to lose your home and possessions to a fire (CD4), so you buy home owners insurance.
              - Studies have shown over and over that we are much more likely to change our behavior to avoid a loss than to make a gain. It forces us to act differently and plays by different mental rules. 
                - In fact, Nobel Prize winner Daniel Kahneman indicates that on average, we are twice as loss-averse compared to seeking a gain. This means that we have a tendency to only take on a risk if we believe the potential gain would be double the potential loss if the risk were realized.
              - I use Yesware to make my emails more intelligent. I recently met the founder of a competing product, Boomerang, who promised me that Boomerang was way better than Yesware. I told him I'd give it a try once I got back to the office.
                - It's been over a year and I still haven't been to the Boomerang website.
                - It's not that I don't trust the Boomerang CEO; rather, I'm motivated to not switch products: Status Quo Sloth (Game Technique #85). I already know all the hot keys for Yesware and most importantly, I'm too lazy to download a new Chrome extension. It's just easier to not make the switch, even if Boomerang is way better than Yesware.
            - Left Brain Core Drives (the left side of Octalysis)
              - CD2: Development & Accomplishment
              - CD4: Ownership & Possession
              - CD6: Scarcity & Impatience
              - The Core Drives on the left side of Octalysis are associated with logic, calculations, and ownership. Left Brain Core Drives are extrinsic motivators – a user is motivated because they want to obtain something, whether it be an intangible good or a tangible item.
            - Right Brain Core Drives (the right side of Octalysis)
              - CD3: Empowerment of Creativity & Feedback
              - CD5: Social Influence & Relatedness
              - CD7: Unpredictability & Curiosity
              - The Core Drives on the right side of Octalysis are related to creativity, self-expression, and social aspects. Right Brain Core Drives are intrinsic motivators: a user doesn't need a goal or reward to use their creativity, to hangout with friends, or to feel the suspense of unpredictability – the activity itself is rewarding on its own.
            - White Hat Gamification (the top of Octalysis)
              - CD1: Epic Meaning & Calling
              - CD2: Development & Accomplishment
              - CD3: Empowerment of Creativity & Feedback
              - The top Core Drives of Octalysis are very positive motivators. 
                - If something is engaging because it lets you express your creativity, makes you feel successful through skill mastery, and gives you a higher sense of meaning, it makes users feel very good and powerful.
            - Black Hat Gamification (the bottom of Octalysis)
              - CD6: Scarcity & Impatience
              - CD7: Unpredictability & Curiosity
              - CD8: Loss & Avoidance
              - The bottom Core Drives of Octalysis are more negative motivators.
                - If you are always doing something because you don’t know what will happen next, you are constantly in fear of losing something, or because there are things you can’t have, even though you are extremely motivated to take action, it often leaves a bad taste in your mouth. This is because you feel like you are not in control of your own behavior.
            - [Octalysis Tool](https://www.yukaichou.com/octalysis-tool/?mc_cid=e64e1856f2&mc_eid=ca8d409c61)
      - 4 Experience Phases of Gamification (==Chou==)
        - Discovery: why would someone begin the experience?
          - [Article](https://yukaichou.com/gamification-study/4-experience-phases-gamification-1-discovery-phase/) + [video](https://yukaichou.com/gamification-video-course/finally-beginners-guide-gamification-21-90-discovery-phase/)
          - Reason why people want to start, investigate product/service
          - Attitude toward product during initial awareness stage
          - Starts when people hear about product, ends when they sign up to use it
          - Happens because of unpredictability/curiosity drive
          - Customer decides if worth time/attention
          - Critical to create differentiation
          - Need to give them context so they behave in the appropriate way (collaborative, fun, serious, efficient, etc)
          - Similar to marketing, but focus on motivations instead of conversions (just signing up  ≠ active users - need to know motivation)
          - Motivation carries over to other stages
          - Core drive(s) in this phase:
            - [7] Unpredictability/Curiosity (weak sense)
            - [1] Epic Meaning/Calling?
            - Many organizations optimize for [5] Social Influence/Relatedness (share with friends, social media, etc)
            - [8] Loss/Avoidance (my boss/teacher told me to use this) - not good for long term engagement 
            - [2] Development/Accomplishment: feeling of earning something -> sense of ownership (earning coupon -> taking advantage of coupon, unlike randomly getting coupon in mail)
          - To optimize discovery phase:
            - Maximize [7] Unpredictability/Curiosity
            - Focus on [5] Social Influence/Relatedness (using social proof), in turn encouraging [6] Scarcity + Impatience
            - Use extrinsic rewards at beginning
            - If possible, also implement [1] Epic Meaning/Calling, sense of losing something if they don't participate 
        - Onboarding: how does player learn rules/tools?
          - [Video](https://yukaichou.com/gamification-video-course/finally-beginners-guide-gamification-22-90-onboarding-phase-players-journey/)
          - Set context of game experience through environment
          - Train users to behave in a certain way so they achieve what they need
          - Have to make users feel smart (even if many great features, they need to not get confused and leave the game -> important to access [2] Development + Accomplishment for this
          - Important to determine where first major win state is [FMWS] (where user says, "wow this is cool"), count how many minutes it takes for user to get there
            - Not when user creates profile, upload photo, hear first song
            - Example: game asks player to defeat a monster before asking player's name/gender
            - Users should invite friends after FMWS
          - Consumers always aware of what they are giving/taking, when they are giving too much and not getting any value -> point at which they drop out (app asking you to sign up before giving you anything)
          - Start introducing anticipation
          - Not as much [8] Loss/Avoidance, but can have some in the beginning
          - Start bringing in [1] Epic Meaning + Calling, new beginning of something cool, great journey, something amazing is about to happen, comes out of nowhere, exciting and different
          - Most difficult drive to implement (in onboarding) = [3] Empowerment of Creativity + Feedback, since whole point of onboarding is to make game seem simple, make player feel smart, but still want to give player a little bit of choice, feel like they have options, multiple things they can do (customizing character, building ownership
        - Scaffolding: why does player return daily to engage in activity loop?
          - [Video](https://yukaichou.com/gamification-video-course/scaffolding-phase-beginners-guide-gamification-episode-23/)
          - Main activities of game - one of the most important stages
          - How to make user come back and complete repetitive behaviors on a daily basis
          - Must keep giving players feedbacks to remind them of [1] Epic Meaning + Calling
          - Sense of progress/journey, nothing goes in circles
          - [3] Empowerment of Creativity + Feedback = crucial to scaffolding
            - White hat + intrinsic drive
            - Make sure players have meaningful choices (can't have if everyone plays the game the same way)
            - Express style + strategy
            - Problem of many gamification platforms: no meaningful choices 
            - To enable this drive, implement triangularity (from Jesse Schell)
              - Instant gratification reward with small reward or delayed gratification, higher reward path
          - [4] Ownership + Possession - very important in scaffolding
            - Nir Eyal - "Hooked" -> after a trigger, action, and variable reward, want user to put something into the system (an investment) so there is a reason to come back
            - Start introducing collector's items (people still won't care that much about collection because they won't know what it is for, but this builds endgame interest
          - [5] Social Influence/Relatedness - important in this phase
            - Focus is not competition (though some is ok), but collaborative play
          - [6] Scarcity + Impatience
            - Hint at what player could have in the future (they don't really know about it yet), creates stronger desire to keep going in game
          - [7] Unpredictability + Curiosity
            - Random events with unpredictable rewards (like gambling, keep engagement)
          - [8] Loss + Avoidance
            - For some incentive but don't penalize players too heavily (demotivates them)
          - Game loop: what is the cycle of game activities?
            - Many gamified platforms simply award badges for completing activities (as an incentive), but what can they use these badges/points/currency to unlock? Need to utilize rewards/resources to level up faster (success breeds success)
          - Understand how to incorporate easter eggs, social status, etc
        - Endgame: how to engage/retain veteran players?
          - [Article](https://yukaichou.com/gamification-study/4-experience-phases-gamification-4-endgame/)
          - Users have done everything there is to do (at least once), figuring out why they should continue to play game
          - In Wow, real game starts when character has reached max level (case of well-designed games)
          - Endgame ≠ where game ends, but where user has reached highest level, transitioning from basic daily scaffolding to advanced mechanics for only the best players
          - Other gamification models call this phase "mastery"
            - Mastery suggests an ending, while endgame -> still a game that one can continue to play
          - [1] Epic Meaning + Calling
            - Difficult to install more of during endgame, less profound
            - In endgame, need to have meaning fulfillment (which should have started in scaffolding)
              - For things during scaffolding, now time to see more feedback on the good work they have done + impact made
            - Implementing elitism (when a veteran player feels that they belong to an elite group, they are motivated to stay in the group to maintain its status), veteran may also mentor newer players (feeling like a respected leader) -> related to [5] Social Influence/Relatedness 
          - [7] Unpredictability/Curiosity
            - Problem with endgame: things become monotonous, no longer new surprises
            - In endgame, incorporate as many random/unpredictable events as possible (rewards, challenges, social settings)
              - Aurum could do this by increasing the IRL component, as unpredictable things happen IRL, the student could turn to Aurum to simulate possible choices, see long-term effects of multiple factors at once
            - Easter eggs, mystery boxes, social treasures = great ways to entertain + engage the user
            - Some developers keep adding new content to experience
              - Works, but becomes difficult to scale - once new content stops, user leaves
                - Again, could be resolved by having more IRL factors, turns the game into more than a pastime, but something useful for daily adult life
          - [4] Ownership + Posession
            - Relaxing way to create activity (but not necessarily engagement) -> have users continue to accumulate things in endgame
            - Alfred Effect: make users feel so comfortable that they constantly come back to feel that they belong
              - Sense of control, organization, automation (naming/filing systems, website that remembers purchasing/payment/shipping preferences, etc)
            - Collection Sets
              - When a player has many pieces within a set, they become motivated to complete the set
          - [6] Scarcity + Impatience
            - Dangle rewards/content that is almost unattainable to keep users engaged
            - Extraordinarily difficult challenges: almost not meant to be beaten, but leave the player feeling that there is still something to obtain
            - Even difficult challenges become monotonous/boring -> once user looses interest in the carrot, appeal is gone
              - Issue with left-brain black hat drives -> much harder to keep people indefinitely without giving them a joyful experience
            - Mix moats with unpredictability: randomly generated challenges that require great skills/planning to overcome
          - [3] Empowerment of Creativity + Feedback
            - If players maximize this, they will likely stay in the endgame for the long run
            - Examples: Poker, Majong, Professional Sports, Chess, Starcraft, Shooting games, and League of Legends-like games -> balanced, yet dynamic environments, filled with meaningful choices
              - IRL components in endgame stage of Aurum means infinite meaningful choices
            - 3 is most difficult to design for: easy to slap on layers of game mechanics and techniques, but making the core activity itself meaningfully engaging is a great challenge
            - Break down tasks into interchangeable pieces/combinations where users can make meaningful decisions
            - Give users as much autonomy as possible, have their skillsets match those of others
          - [5] Social Influence/Relatedness 
            - Facilitate competition between players -> can create abundance of activity in endgame (Street Fighter, Starcraft, Super Smash Bros)
          - [2] Development + Accomplishment
            - Difficult in endgame (player has already done everything at least once)
            - Never make any challenge too easy, but continue to use feedback mechanics (to show progress to veteran)
          - [8] Loss/Avoidance
            - Veterans built up many things they can lose (motivated by embarrassment in front of new players)
            - FOMO toward new features being added
            - Status Quo Sloth
              - User has built a habit of committing desired actions, feels uncomfortable when deviating from actions
            - Many endgame stages put together thoughtlessly -> designers hope that the same black hat, left brain drives will continue to work
              - When users feel demoralized because of their experience, they no longer derive joy from the game
      - Actionable Gamification (==Chou==)
        - [Book](https://mcusercontent.com/0e4d88e02b5b077b7bee2ca33/files/57883a1d-6ccc-ff12-ef4c-5a7870df0d97/Actionable_Gamification_Full.03.pdf?utm_source=Octalysis+Courses&utm_campaign=c5f07a0c0a-EMAIL_CAMPAIGN_2025_02_09_08_34&utm_medium=email&utm_term=0_e9faa3b9d1-c5f07a0c0a-136342071&mc_cid=c5f07a0c0a&mc_eid=55b421eda6) not about why gamification (human-focused design) is valuable/why you should use it, but about HOW to be successful in applying gamification principles/techniques to real world situations
          - Design experiences that motivate behavior instead of slap some game elements on top of the current system
        - Charles Coonradt (1984) -> "Why would people pay for the privilege of WORKING HARDER at recreational sport/hobby than they would work at a job where they are paid?" (why are hobbies preferable to work?)
          - Clearly defined goals
          - Better scorekeeping
          - More frequent feeback
          - Higher degree of personal choice of methods
          - Consistent coaching
        - CH 2: The PBL (points, badges, leaderboards) Fallacy
          - Why do kids stay up late (with the associated risks) to engage gaming grunt work (especially when grunt work is so hated - and requires a strong work ethic - IRL)?
            - They are excited about leveling their character up, they see the big picture, like the sense of accomplishment + use of creativity
            - Desire such feelings so much that anything in the way (even grunt work) is worth doing and worth doing urgently
          - Gamification became a buzzword like social media
          - First step of gamification is not deciding what design elements to implement, but how one wants the player to feel
        - CH 3: Octalysis Framework
          - Everything someone does is based on 1+ of the core drives (when none of the drives are active, there is 0 motivation, no action results)
          - Hidden 9th drive: sensation = physical feelings that bring pleasure to the senses (other drives influence psyche)
            - Even pleasurable activities when lacking curiosity, relatedness, creativity, scarcity, etc -> become unappealing
          - Level 1: Octalysis to IRL System
            - X skewed toward right brain drives
            - LinkedIn skewed toward left brain drives
            - Level 1 = sufficient for many companies to start understanding why products don't engage users (higher levels = organizations truly committed to pushing metrics + longevity of gamified system)
          - Level 2: Designing for all 4 phases 
          - Level 3: Factoring in different player types 
            - Bartle's player types (achievers, socializers, explorers, killers) -> most recognized model in gamification design
            - But level 3 Octalysis doesn't need Bartle's player types (Bartle himself claims that his 4 types might not be suitable for gamification environments (43) -> different types of people motivated differently
            - Level 3 allows designer to understand + design for how everyone is feeling at different stages
            - Graphic displaying phases vs types (43)
        - CH 4: Putting Gamification in its Place
      - How Can Stealth Assessment in Games Measure and Support Learning? (==ALN==)
        - [ALN](https://www.michiganassessmentconsortium.org/wp-content/uploads/LP_STEALTH_ASSESSMENT_IN_GAMES.pdf) (2024) 
        - Stealth assessment blurs the distinction between learning and assessment 
          - While playing the game, students continually produce rich sequences of actions as data points that are captured in log files
          - Good alternative to summative assessment methods that have remained the same across decades
          - Engaging form of formative assessment (assessment for learning) in which data is collected throughout a course of learning with the goal to provide targeted support to the learner along the way
        - Problems with traditional/summative assessments
          - Traditional selected-response tests used because they are easy to deliver and score, but limitations include 
            - Limited number of items: can't fully access what has been taught or learned in class
            - Measure learning at a single point in time: summarized by an overall score
            - Rarely any constructive feedback
            - Leave lower-performing students even further demotivated and falling behind
            - Time spent administering tests, handling make-up exams, and going over test responses is not conducive to learning
          - Shute (2016) found that traditional tests:
            - Predict poorly
            - Don't measure all relevant determinants of important learning-related criteria
            - Are subject to coaching + motivational differences among students
        - Benefits of stealth assessment
          - Data logs captured through stealth assessment automatically **scored by in-game rubrics, then aggregated in real-time by Bayesian networks or other statistical models**, which show evolving estimates of mastery levels on targeted competencies (explained more by Shute, Lu, and Rahimi (2021), showing how to develop + validate a particular stealth assessment)
          - Fun and engaging for most people
          - Can reduce test anxiety
          - Allow for recording students’ interactions in detail (i.e., via the accumulation of log data generated by keystrokes, mouse clicks, choice patterns, and so on), which can be used to analyze students’ learning progress
          - Can be designed to provide ongoing feedback and other real-time learning supports, which traditional assessment does not (scaffolding overlap)
          - Discrete methods include log files and eye tracking (possibly invasive?), continually collects student data and examines their progression of cognitive and non-cognitive variables throughout gameplay
          - By having assessments that are continuous and ubiquitous, students are no lonxger able to “cram” for an exa
        - Evidence-centered design (ECD)
          - Moving from log file data to valid inferences about competency is critical, yet difficult
          - 4 models of ECD
            - Competency model: operationalizing the construct we want to assess (e.g., conceptual physics understanding, creativity, persistence) and defining the claims to be made about students’ competencies
            - Evidence model: automatically scoring and accumulating valid evidence (i.e., observables) of a claim about students’ competencies (i.e., unobservables)
            - Task model: detailing the nature and form of tasks (e.g., game levels) that will elicit the evidence needed for the evidence model
            - Assembly model: specifying the number, types, and sequencing of tasks
        - Features of well-designed stealth assessment
          - Use/creation of a technology-rich environment (like a digital game)
          - Application of ECD to design, develop, and implement the core models described above
          - Embedding of stealth assessment into the code of the technology-rich environment
          - Creation of capabilities for system to provide formative assessment/feedback and adaptivity of game levels, supports, etc
      - Stealth Assessment: Measuring and Supporting Learning in Video Games (==Shute==)
        - Shute (2013) [The MIT Press](https://direct.mit.edu/books/oa-monograph/3700/Stealth-AssessmentMeasuring-and-Supporting)
        - Reasoning for changing traditional assessments
          - Old ways don't work well: basic abilities to read, write, and do math are not enough for most jobs like they used to be. Now, people need to think critically, solve new problems, be creative, and work well with others.
          - Traditional tests are limited: often don't show what students can *do* with their knowledge, usually separate from real learning activities (don't help students learn as they go), rarely measure higher order thinking/complex skills
        - Reasoning for video games to help learning/assessment
          - Exist everywhere (nearly all young people play digital games)
          - Naturally teach important skills (reading, problem solving, persistence, collaboration)
          - Engage students
          - Access skills in real time
        - Stealth assessment explanation
          - Method of measuring student learning inside the game, without interrupting play or making them feel like they are taking a test
          - How it works:
            - Game designed so important skills (like problem solving/creativity) are needed to succeed
            - Game tracks what players do (every choice, action, strategy)
            - Student actions are analyzed to determine how well student demonstrates skills/knowledge
          - Benefits:
            - Players remain engaged and motivated
            - Teachers and students get real-time feedback
            - Complex skills measured in a natural, meaningful context
      - Basics of Neural Collaborative Filtering (NCF) [overview]
        - Approach to recommender systems, designed to predict user preferences (like product ratings or content choices) based on past interactions
        - Basic idea:
          - Replaces the dot product (linear interaction model - traditional collaborative filtering) with a neural network, learns more complex interactions between users and items
        - Flexible architecture - can be adapted to different types of data (implicit, explicit, side info)
        - Scalable - deep learning tools (e.g., TensorFlow, PyTorch) make it trainable on large datasets
        - Better generalization - especially for cold-start/sparse data problems
        - Common examples = Netflix, Amazon, educational platforms (like Duolingo)
      - Factors Contributing to Minecraft's Success [overview]
        - Minecraft's Gameplay Mechanics and Narrative Structure
          - Primarily a sandbox game with minimal prescribed narrative. 
          - Players start in a procedurally generated world with basic tools and must gather resources, build shelter, and survive. While there are some challenges, such as surviving the first night, there's no inherent "crisis" or "hole" that players must climb out of. 
          - The game's open-world sandbox gameplay allows players to explore, build, and survive in a world filled with creatures, resources, and dangers.
        - Key Factors in Minecraft's Success
          - Open-ended creativity: Players can build virtually anything they imagine, fostering creativity and imagination.
            - Possibility for Aurum
          - Multiple play styles: Creative mode, survival mode, multiplayer, and other modes cater to different player preferences.
            - Possibility for Aurum (different game concepts)
              - God game - similar to creative mode (can have multiplayer aspects but not as involved as there is only one mayor per town)
              - Multiplayer simulation - similar to survival
          - Emergent gameplay: Players create their own goals and stories, leading to a unique experience.
          - Social elements: Multiplayer worlds, sharing creations, and collaborative building encourage social interaction and community engagement.
          - Accessibility: Simple mechanics are easy to learn but offer depth, making the game appealing to players of all ages and skill levels.
          - Modding community: Extensive customization possibilities through mods and user-generated content extend the game's replayability.
        - Man in Hole vs. Minecraft
          - Man in Hole story involves:
            - Starting in a dire situation
            - Struggling to overcome obstacles
            - Gradually climbing out of the crisis
            - Achieving resolution/success
          - Minecraft involves:
            - Starts players in a neutral position (empty world with basic tools)
            - Offers immediate agency and choice
            - Focuses on creation rather than recovery
            - Has no predetermined "hole" to escape from
            - Emergent Narrative and Player Agency
        - Minecraft's success comes from what game designers call **emergent narrative** - stories that arise naturally from player actions and choices rather than being scripted. Players create their own "man in the hole" moments, such as getting lost in caves or facing difficult monsters, but these aren't built into the core design. The game's appeal lies in its sandbox nature and player agency rather than following any traditional storytelling structure.
      - Learning By Gaming, Coding, and Making with EDUMING: A New Approach to Utilizing Atypical Digital Games for Learning (==Pietrusky==)
        - Pietrusky (2025) [Down Church Studios](https://arxiv.org/html/2504.13878v1)
        - The paper argues that existing digital learning models—such as Gamification, Game-Based Learning (GBL), and Serious Games—often fail to maximize learning because they position the game as a closed system that students passively consume.
        - The EDUMING concept is presented as a method to overcome this limitation by applying the principles of Constructionism, a learning theory developed by Seymour Papert. Constructionism posits that deep, meaningful learning occurs when learners are engaged in the active construction of tangible, external, and shareable artifacts.
        - Core Problem Being Solved - Existing Educational Game Models Categorized As Follows:
          - Gamification: Applies game elements (points, badges) to a non-game task, primarily boosting motivation, but not necessarily teaching specific knowledge.
          - Game-Based Learning (GBL) / Serious Games: Complete games designed for learning/simulation, but the user only interacts with the final product.
        - The EDUMING Solution & Differentiation (from traditional educational games)
          - Turning the learning game into an adaptable template the learner can modify - shifting activity from playing to creating
          - Comparison: Traditional Educational Games vs EDUMING
            - Traditional:
              - Closed structure - predefined rules, levels, and endings
              - Learner = consumer, passive user absorbing content
              - Goal = knowledge transfer & enjoyment
              - Users experience the mechanics
            - EDUMING:
              - Open template - An adaptable game (source code) with an integrated development environment (IDE)
              - Learner = creator/developer, active participant modifying the source code and rules
              - Goal = in-depth learning through construction, digital fluency, and problem-solving
              - Users build and alter the mechanics, leading to a deeper understanding of the underlying principles
        - Key Contributions of the EDUMING Approach
          - Simplifies Game Development for Educators: By relying on existing, adaptable game templates (using platforms like Unity or GameMaker Studio 2), the barrier to entry for educators and students who want to engage in game creation is significantly lowered, reducing development time and costs compared to building a game from scratch.
          - Fosters Constructionist Learning: The core benefit is promoting deep learning. For example, to understand a physics principle, a student must modify the game's code that governs gravity or momentum, making the concept tangible.
          - Encourages Active Shaping of Media: Learners are encouraged not just to consume digital media, but to actively change and shape it by diving into the source code, fostering critical thinking about the medium itself.
          - Flexible Learning Focus: The focus of an EDUMING game is defined by the individual user and the pedagogical goal, not by a rigid, predefined educational outcome, making it versatile for various learning contexts.
        - Application(s)
          - The EDUMING concept is designed to be highly adaptable for various contexts and subject areas. For the purpose of the empirical study, designers developed and tested a specific prototype game PCLQ:
            - While PCLQ was built to promote spelling, the authors explicitly state the concept's broad potential, noting that the game template: "could also be adapted so that numbers must be submitted instead of letters, or it could be used for a completely different subject area."
        - Takeaways for Aurum
          - Fixed Simulation -> Modifiable Templates:
            - Allow users to directly change the variables of the simulation (e.g., change the student loan interest rate from 6% to 2%, or alter the compounding frequency)
          - Predefined Quests -> Combination Environment:
            - Provide financial building blocks (APIs, asset classes, debt models) that users combine to create their own unique financial artifacts or scenarios
          - Passive Feedback -> Artifact Sharing & Peer Review:
            - Users share their customized models or "financial theorems" (e.g., "The 'High-Interest' Savings Model I Built"). Learning occurs via peer discussion and critique.
          - Constructivist learning & UGC in Aurum 
            - Within a randomly generated world, users build their businesses from the resources/opportunities available
              - Different from many other gamified FinLit platforms where users follow a predetermined path (if more game-based, e.g., [Spent](https://playspent.org/)) or predetermined curriculum (if more module-based, e.g., [Khan Academy](https://www.khanacademy.org/college-careers-more/financial-literacy))
            - UGC: Users form their own multiplayer groups 
              - Joining each other's worlds 
              - Trading 
              - Working for/with one another
      - Gamification Case Studies: Measurable ROI and Proven Success
        - The gamification market is rapidly expanding, estimated between $3 billion to $12 billion depending on sources, with proven ROI driving adoption across industries.
        - The Octalysis Group, led by Yu-kai Chou, has demonstrated transformative business impact including:
          - A 712% increase in sales for a major hotel chain through human-focused gamification design.
          - A 175% performance boost for a leading airline’s loyalty program.
          - A sales staff engagement platform that increased revenue by 28.5% and key performance indicators (KPIs) by 59%.
        - Enterprise-wide successes: Caixa Bank in Brazil exceeded a 5% annual sales growth goal by achieving a 49% increase in six months, adding $1 billion in revenue. SAP’s community network saw a 400% usage increase and 96% more feedback via gamified reputation systems. AstraZeneca’s gamified medicine training achieved a 99% completion rate among agents.
        - Sales gamification highlights: Autodesk’s gamified free trial boosted trial usage by 54%, buy clicks by 15%, and channel revenue by 29%. Domino’s Pizza’s “Pizza Hero” app increased sales revenue by 30%. Microsoft improved sales by 10% through gamification in virtual sales meetings.
        - Educational and lifestyle impacts: Deloitte Leadership Academy increased return users by 46.6%. Foldit gamers solved a 15-year protein problem in 10 days. Popular apps like Duolingo have over 300 million users motivated by gamified learning. Fitbit and MyFitnessPal report over 50% improvements in user adherence and engagement with gamified wellness programs.
      - Points, Badges, and Leaderboards: The Gamification Fallacy
        - Points, badges, and leaderboards (PBLs) are common gamification tools designed to drive engagement through extrinsic rewards but often fail to sustain long-term motivation.
        - Studies show that over-reliance on extrinsic rewards can reduce intrinsic motivation, causing users to focus on rewards rather than meaningful engagement or mastery of skills (crowding out effect).​
        - Badges lose value if they are easily earned or do not represent true achievements, leading users to perceive the system as superficial or untrustworthy.​
        - Leaderboards create competition but can also provoke anxiety or discouragement in less competitive or novice users, reducing participation rates.​
        - Research indicates that badges and leaderboards did not significantly improve academic performance, though they were viewed positively as motivational tools by most students.​
        - The early days of gamification mistakenly equated adding PBLs with creating engaging experiences. However, effective gamification transcends these superficial elements by focusing on human psychology and intrinsic motivations such as autonomy, mastery, and purpose. 
        - Expert Integration: Yu-kai Chou’s Octalysis Framework stresses that game elements should support desired emotional experiences rather than being implemented as ends in themselves.Personally, the PBL fallacy reflects a broader challenge in design: prioritizing "what" over "why." Points and badges can spark quick engagement but risk becoming mere decoration without thoughtful alignment to underpinning motivations. 
        - To truly engage users deeply and sustainably, gamification must foster intrinsic drivers and create meaningful, progressive experiences that resonate emotionally and cognitively. Authentic gamification turns grunt work into purposeful action by clarifying the “why,” helping users see beyond instant rewards to the broader value of growth, community, and challenge. 
          - This distinction explains why many PBL-heavy systems fail to produce lasting engagement but well-designed gamification systems succeed in transforming behavior and satisfaction.This rewrite separates objective data (DOK1) about PBL efficacy and limitations from nuanced, expert-backed reflections and personal insights (DOK2) on the psychology of engagement and best practices for gamification design, drawing on academic findings and gamification thought leadership.​
      - Evaluation of Student Core Drives on e-Learning during the Covid-19 with Octalysis Gamification Framework (==Marisa==)
        - Marisa (2020) [International Journal of Advanced Computer Science and Applications](https://thesai.org/Downloads/Volume11No11/Paper_14-Evaluation_of_Student_Core_Drives_on_E_Learning.pdf)
        - Study Demographics & Infrastructure Context
          - 419 students across East Java and West Java, Indonesia in August 2020. 
          - Goal: capture the "emergency" state of e-learning during the pandemic
          - The Reality of Tools: Most institutions were not ready. 53.81% of students were using "Google Classroom combined with other applications" rather than a centralized system. Only 20.48% used a dedicated, integrated e-learning application.
          - The Baseline: Despite these fragmented tools, students' willingness to participate was surprisingly resilient, with participation scores consistently rating as "High" across the board.
        - **Analysis of the 8 Drives**
          - For each drive, the researchers asked a specific question, measured the gap between actual and ideal motivation, and proposed specific game mechanics to fix it
          - CD1: Epic Meaning & Calling
            - The Concept: Believing you are doing something greater than yourself.
            - The Survey Question: "The existence of the e-learning system used gives students the opportunity to participate in maintaining the stability of the learning process during the Covid-19 period".
            - The Score: 7/10 (Actual: High | Target: Very High)
            - Analysis: Students felt a moral duty to keep learning going during the crisis. It was an "emergency system" motivation.
            - Recommendation: To sustain this, institutions should clearly communicate the objectives and benefits of every material, reinforcing the "mission" of successful learning.
          - CD2: Development & Accomplishment
            - The Concept: The internal drive of making progress, developing skills, and overcoming challenges.
            - The Survey Question: "The mechanism of the e-learning system that is used encourages students to improve their achievement".
            - The Score: 6/10 (Actual: High | Target: Very High)
            - Analysis: This was one of the lowest scores. Current feedback systems (grades) are boring and do not make students feel like they are "leveling up".
            - Recommendation: Adopt classic game mechanics like Points, Levels, Badges, and Leaderboards to visualize progress.
              - Caution: avoid falling into the PBL gamification fallacy 
          - CD3: Empowerment of Creativity & Feedback
            - The Concept: Users are engaged because they can figure things out and try different combinations.
            - The Survey Question: "The existing e-learning system mechanism gives students the opportunity to freely choose the media to collect assignments or consult".
            - The Score: 7/10 (Actual: High | Target: Very High)
            - Analysis: Students liked the flexibility (e.g., choosing how to submit work), but it wasn't fully utilized.
            - Recommendation: Use "Blank Fills" (allowing students to write free notes) and "Instant Feedback" mechanics so students can see the immediate result of their creativity.
              - Analysis: immediate results are crucial for engagement & motivation - easily accomplished in a game setting
          - CD4: Ownership & Possession
            - The Concept: Users are motivated because they feel they "own" something.
            - The Survey Question: "The e-learning system mechanism used has user personalization facilities that motivate students to be responsible for the existence of their respective accounts".
            - The Score: 7/10 (Actual: High | Target: Very High)
            - Analysis: Having a personal login account created a basic sense of ownership, but it was static.
