            - Recommendation: Implement Avatars that represent the student and "Virtual Goods" that they can earn and collect, deepening their attachment to the profile.
              - Analysis: ownership accomplished easily in game setting (and implemented poorly in a traditional classroom setting or online modules)
          - CD5: Social Influence & Relatedness
            - The Concept: Everything related to social elements: mentorship, acceptance, social responses, companionship, and competition.
            - The Survey Question: "The mechanism of the e-learning system that is used provides encouragement to be more competitive or collaborate between friends".
            - The Score: 6/10 (Actual: High | Target: Very High)
            - Analysis: This was a weak point. The current systems are isolating and do not foster enough competition or cooperation.
            - Recommendation: Use "Group Quests" (collaborative tasks), "Friending", and "Social Treasures" (rewards that can be given to others).
              - Analysis: CD5 is incredibly easy and natural to accomplish in multiplayer games (like MMORPGs). Players form entire social circles in such games - this was the original reason Discord was created.
          - CD6: Scarcity & Impatience
            - The Concept: The drive of wanting something because you can't have it.
            - The Survey Question: "The mechanism of the e-learning system that is used sometimes provides challenges that are not mandatory, for example in the form of time-limited quizzes that cause curiosity to try".
            - The Score: 6/10 (Actual: High | Target: Very High)
            - Analysis: Random, optional challenges existed but weren't compelling enough to drive urgency.
            - Recommendation: Use "Countdown Timers" (visual urgency) and "Appointment Dynamics" (tasks available only at specific times) to force students to take action or miss out.
              - Caution: use sparingly to motivate behavior at specific times (as with appointment dynamics) but do not let this (or other Black Hat drives) dominate the gameplay experience - people do not like feeling coerced into action
          - CD7: Unpredictability & Curiosity
            - The Concept: Not knowing what is going to happen next.
            - The Survey Question: "The e-learning system mechanism used includes a formative quiz facility which is announced unscheduled...".
            - The Score: 7/10 (Actual: High | Target: Very High)
            - Analysis: Unscheduled quizzes kept students on their toes, checking their accounts frequently.
            - Recommendation: Implement "Random Rewards" and "Rolling Rewards" (lottery-style feedback) to keep students anticipating positive surprises
          - CD8: Loss & Avoidance
            - The Concept: The drive to avoid something negative happening.
            - The Survey Question: "...achievement board facilities... encouraged students to always maintain/increase levels so that they are not judged poorly...".
            - The Score: 6/10 (Actual: High | Target: Very High).
            - Analysis: Students didn't fear "losing" their status enough. The stakes felt low.
            - Recommendation: Use "Progress Loss" (if you stop, you lose ground) and "Status Quo Slots" to make students actively work to keep their current standing.
              - Caution: use sparingly to create urgency but do not let this (or other Black Hat drives) dominate the gameplay experience - people do not like feeling coerced into action
        - Structural Balance (The Octagon)
          - The study also evaluated the *balance* of the motivation using the Octalysis visualizer:
            - White Hat vs. Black Hat: The study found a "good balance" between White Hat (positive, feeling in control – Drives 1, 2, 3) and Black Hat (negative, urgency, fear – Drives 6, 8).
            - Right Brain vs. Left Brain: There was also a balance between Right Brain (Creativity, Social, Unpredictability) and Left Brain (Logic, Ownership, Scarcity) motivations.
        - Summary of the "Gap"The "Gap Analysis" is the core conclusion of the paper.
          - The Status Quo: Students accept online learning (Likert "High").
          - The Problem: They are not *excited* or *deeply engaged* by it (Octalysis score 6.5/10).
          - The Solution: The gap between "Acceptable" (6.5) and "Excellent" (10) can only be bridged by moving beyond functional tools (like Google Classroom) and integrating the specific psychological mechanics listed above.
    - Learning Science
      - ZPD (Zone of Proximal Development) [==multiple==]
        - Overview [here](https://www.sciencedirect.com/topics/psychology/zone-of-proximal-development)
        - Defined as the difference between a child's “**actual developmental level** as determined by independent problem solving” and the child's “**potential development** as determined through problem solving under adult guidance or in collaboration with more capable peers” (Vygotsky, 1978).
        - Inherently motivating and optimally challenging (involves transfer of responsibility, tailored to learner's level)
        - The ZPD has been extended by Valsiner (1997) into a system of zones which recognizes not only the importance of assisted learning, but also the factors which may assist or constrain learning, and, as Pressick-Kilborn and Walker (2002) have suggested, motivation. Valsiner's system of zones has been used by Pressick-Kilborn and Walker (2002) to understand the development of interest in a classroom learning community. **(****)**
      - The Problem with Experiential Learning (==Kirschner==) (*)
        - Kirschner et al (2006) [Educational Psychologist](https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/epdf/10.1207/s15326985ep4102_1?src=getftr&utm_source=sciencedirect_contenthosting&getft_integrator=sciencedirect_contenthosting) 
        - Goal of this article: to suggest that based on our current knowledge of human cognitive architecture, **minimally guided instruction is likely to be ineffective**
          - Long-term memory is now viewed as the central, dominant structure of human cognition. Every-thing we see, hear, and think about is critically dependent on and influenced by our long-term memory (it plays into everything we do)
        - "Any instructional theory that ignores the limits of working memory when dealing with novel information or ignores the disappearance of those limits when dealing with familiar in-formation is unlikely to be effective. Recommendations advocating minimal guidance during instruction proceed as though working memory does not exist or, if it does exist, that it has no relevant limitations when dealing with novel information, the very information of interest to constructivist teaching procedures."
        - All problem-based searching makes heavy demands on working memory. Furthermore, that working memory load does not contribute to the accumulation of knowledge in long-term memory because while working memory is being used to search for problem solutions, it is not available and cannot be used to learn.
        - Moreno (2004) concluded that there is a growing body of research showing that students learn more deeply from strongly guided learning than from discovery. Similar conclusions were reported by Chall (2000), McKeough, Lupart, and Marini (1995),Schauble (1990), and Singley and Anderson (1989).
          - Direct instruction involving considerable guidance, including examples, resulted in vastly more learning than discovery (against PBL, in favor of something like LBL)
        - Tuovinen and Sweller (1999) showed that exploration practice (a discovery technique) caused a much larger cognitive load and led to poorer learning than worked-examples practice.
        - **Problem-solving is an inefficient way of altering long-term memory because its function is to find a problem solution, not alter long-term memory.**
          - Problem-solving search overburdens limited working memory and requires working memory resources to be used for activities that are unrelated to learning. As a consequence, learners can engage in problem-solving activities for extended periods and learn almost nothing (Sweller et al., 1982).
          - Learners receiving guidance through process worksheets outperformed learners left to discover the appropriate procedures themselves.
        - Roblyer (1996) and Perkins (1991) examined evidence for minimally guided pedagogy in instructional design and instructional technology studies. Both researchers concluded that the available evidence does not support the use of minimal guidance and both suggested that some form of stronger guidance is necessary for both effective learning and transfer.
        - One of the most common ATI findings according to Kyllonen and Lajoie (2003) was “that strong treatments benefited less able learners and weaker treatments benefited more able learners” (p. 82). This conclusion anticipated the now recognized scaffolding effect.
        - Roblyer, Edwards, and Havriluk (1997) reported that teachers have found that discovery learning is successful only when students have prerequisite knowledge and undergo some prior structured experiences
        - The best known survey of the comparisons of PBL with conventional medical school instruction was conducted by Albanese and Mitchell (1993).
        - According to Elstein (1994) knowledge organization and schema acquisition are more important for the development of expertise than the use of particular methods of problem solving
        - Two major components of PBL are the explicit teaching of problem-solving strategies in the form of the hypothetico-deductive method of reasoning (Barrows & Tamblyn, 1980), and teaching of basic content in the context of a specific case or instance.
        - CONCLUSION
          - No body of research supporting instruction with minimal guidance. In so far as there is any evidence from controlled studies, it almost uniformly supports direct, strong instructional guidance rather than constructivist-based minimal guidance during the instruction of novice to intermediate learners. Even for students with considerable prior knowledge, strong guidance while learning is most often found to be equally effective as unguided approaches. Not only is unguided instruction normally less effective; there is also evidence that it may have negative results when students acquire misconceptions or incomplete or disorganized knowledge.
        - THIS STUDY WAS LATER REFUTED [here](https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/00461520701263368?casa_token=ycB2gm74kxkAAAAA%3AF1QW2r1Tw2WOgLO-SSYr8PrGSFL49KoRlMEz98gTbWgge4qrO5DoG9uXP4zVtitcHfmEx1JoE-66hQ#abstract)
      - Refuting Kirschner: PBL [problem-based learning = effective] (==Hmelo-Silver==)
        - Hmelo-Silver et al (2007) [Educational Psychologist](https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/00461520701263368?casa_token=ycB2gm74kxkAAAAA%3AF1QW2r1Tw2WOgLO-SSYr8PrGSFL49KoRlMEz98gTbWgge4qrO5DoG9uXP4zVtitcHfmEx1JoE-66hQ#abstract)
        - In their argument, Kirschner et al. contrast minimally guided instructional approaches with approaches that provide direct instructional guidance and assert that minimally guided instructional approaches are ineffective and inefficient.
        - We agree with [Kirschner et al. (2006)](https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/00461520701263368?casa_token=ycB2gm74kxkAAAAA%3AF1QW2r1Tw2WOgLO-SSYr8PrGSFL49KoRlMEz98gTbWgge4qrO5DoG9uXP4zVtitcHfmEx1JoE-66hQ#) that there is little evidence to suggest that unguided and experientially-based approaches foster learning. However,** IL (inquiry learning) and PBL are not discovery approaches and are not instances of minimally guided instruction**, contrary to the claims of Kirschner et al. Rather, PBL and IL provide considerable guidance to students.
        - There is an extensive body of research on scaffolding learning in inquiry- and problem based environments (Jackson, Stratford, Krajcik, & Soloway, 1994), and researchers have developed theory-driven and empirically based design guidelines for incorporating effective scaffolding strategies to support learning
        - The students who received the lecture after trying to explain the data learned much more from the lecture. In the context of students trying to explain data, the lecture provided scaffolding that helped students make sense of the data, and hence was more meaningful than the same lecture presented not as scaffolding for inquiry but as direct instruction.
        - Scaffolding can reduce cognitive load by automating the generation of data representations, labor intensive calculations, or storing information. By structuring the tasks and the available functionality (e.g., in computer-based environments), **scaffolding can restrict the options that are available to the learner at any point in time to make the task accessible and manageable **([Quintana et al., 2004](https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/00461520701263368?casa_token=ycB2gm74kxkAAAAA%3AF1QW2r1Tw2WOgLO-SSYr8PrGSFL49KoRlMEz98gTbWgge4qrO5DoG9uXP4zVtitcHfmEx1JoE-66hQ#))
        - Example - Model-It: a software environment that allows students to build object-based models of natural phenomena (such as the effects of pollutants on a stream ecosystem), has three functional modes: plan, build, and test ([Jackson et al., 1996](https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/00461520701263368?casa_token=ycB2gm74kxkAAAAA%3AF1QW2r1Tw2WOgLO-SSYr8PrGSFL49KoRlMEz98gTbWgge4qrO5DoG9uXP4zVtitcHfmEx1JoE-66hQ#)). The software restricts the options available to students such that students may only proceed to the build stage after they have planned their models, and they may not test it until they have identified some of the important objects and relationships in the system. Model-It further scaffolds students by allowing them to qualitatively express complex mathematical relationships as the software converts their verbally stated relationships into mathematical formulas used for running the model, thereby reducing cognitive load and situating the task within the learner's zone of proximal development.
        - Inquiry Learning (IL):
          - Significantly higher pass rates on high-stakes standardized exams for middle school students (Michigan Educational Assessment Program) in science classes that use inquiry-based materials compared to their peers in a large urban district in the Midwestern United States
          - The effect sizes reported were 0.44 (14% improvement in total score) for students in the first cohort and 0.37 (13% overall improvement) for students in the larger second cohort
          - "Inquiry students of all groups outperformed their comparison peers. The curriculum was also more effective (than traditional instruction) in increasing certain aspects of motivation and engagement, particularly among historically disadvantaged student groups."
          - Current **reforms** and the **inquiry approach** are **not substituting content for practices**; rather, they advocate that content and practices are central learning goals. IL models do in fact foster rich and robust content learning
      - Digital Simulation-Based training: A Meta-Analysis (==Gegenfurtner==)
        - Gegenfurtner (2014) [British Journal of Educational Technology](https://bera-journals.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/bjet.12188?casa_token=D6q7ccuIvQcAAAAA%3A5VbYCSzWhRvRdQ3pY3cZ8gzcGAXq4-AFM6hK38n_e3druy9uTxXph9z4ltWQkCrPCS43uOCZw4BqiPk)
        - This study focuses on the relationship between self-efficacy and transfer in digital simulation-based training environments
        - **User control **and **assessment after training** seem to be two powerful design elements that can help **make digital simulations more effective**
          - Other factors such as social, narrative or multimedia aspects of the simulation seem to be of lesser relevance for self-efficacy and transfer
        - **Digital simulations** seem promising to foster self-efficacy. For example, Backlund, Engström, Johannesson, Lebram and Sjödén ([2008](https://bera-journals.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/bjet.12188?casa_token=D6q7ccuIvQcAAAAA%3A5VbYCSzWhRvRdQ3pY3cZ8gzcGAXq4-AFM6hK38n_e3druy9uTxXph9z4ltWQkCrPCS43uOCZw4BqiPk#bjet12188-bib-0004)) reported that a driving simulator program evoked **positive efficacy beliefs**, which, in turn, resulted in higher levels of driving performance after training
        - The study of Chen *et al* ([2005](https://bera-journals.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/bjet.12188?casa_token=D6q7ccuIvQcAAAAA%3A5VbYCSzWhRvRdQ3pY3cZ8gzcGAXq4-AFM6hK38n_e3druy9uTxXph9z4ltWQkCrPCS43uOCZw4BqiPk#bjet12188-bib-0014)) provides an example of a multiplayer simulation that includes a team context: in their study, they trained teams in a military attack helicopter simulation. 
          - Social cognitive theory (Bandura, [1997](https://bera-journals.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/bjet.12188?casa_token=D6q7ccuIvQcAAAAA%3A5VbYCSzWhRvRdQ3pY3cZ8gzcGAXq4-AFM6hK38n_e3druy9uTxXph9z4ltWQkCrPCS43uOCZw4BqiPk#bjet12188-bib-0005), [2012](https://bera-journals.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/bjet.12188?casa_token=D6q7ccuIvQcAAAAA%3A5VbYCSzWhRvRdQ3pY3cZ8gzcGAXq4-AFM6hK38n_e3druy9uTxXph9z4ltWQkCrPCS43uOCZw4BqiPk#bjet12188-bib-0006); Schunk & Usher, [2012](https://bera-journals.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/bjet.12188?casa_token=D6q7ccuIvQcAAAAA%3A5VbYCSzWhRvRdQ3pY3cZ8gzcGAXq4-AFM6hK38n_e3druy9uTxXph9z4ltWQkCrPCS43uOCZw4BqiPk#bjet12188-bib-0057)) suggests that learners in multiplayer simulations or team-based activities have higher levels of self-efficacy and transfer of learning because of higher levels of social exchange and coregulation processes
      - Inquiring Minds Learn to Read, Write, and Think: Reaching All Learners through Inquiry (==Wilhelm==) (*)
        - Wilhelm (2015) [Middle School Journal](https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/pdf/10.1080/00940771.2010.11461738)
        - Reframing curricular topics as inquiry promotes engagement, literacy, and deep learning for all students, especially reluctant ones
        - How inquiry works - ask an essential question that is:
          - Engaging + Enduring
          - At the heart of the discipline: leading to knowledge used by professionals to practice in their field, to solve problems, and to create knowledge in that subject area.
          - In need of uncoverage: involving a background of foundational principles, rich concepts, procedures, and theoretical understandings that require unpacking, and that lead to knowing the “story behind the story” about how and why this knowledge was created in the way that it was, why it works as it does, and so forth.
          - NOT answerable through mere information retrieval, understood in one lesson, easily agreed upon
        - Based off Csikszentmihalyi's idea of FLOW
          - Clear **purpose**, intermediate **goals**, immediate and **continuous feedback** about one’s progress.
          - **Challenge** that requires an appropriate level of skill and assistance to meet the challenge (as needed to be successful).
          - A sense of **control and developing competence** (e.g., learners exercise their own voice, stake their opinions and identity, make meaningful choices, and name their growing competence). [similar to self-efficacy from other articles]
          - A focus on **immediate experience** (e.g., what is learned has current **relevance** and is connected personally to students as well as to the world; students make and do things with an immediate function; involves fun and sometimes humor).
            - This can be difficult when teaching FinLit to younger audiences, as they feel that retirement, mortgages, and even college funding is far off
          - **Social interaction** (e.g., learners are involved in group work, use peer assistance, negotiate and share what is learned, participate peripherally but legitimately in larger communities of practice [see Lave & Wenger, 1991].
          - It is compelling to see how traditional information transmission approaches like **lectures, worksheets, formulaic assessments, and textbook reading** (all vociferously and explicitly rejected across the study’s informants) **meet none of the conditions of flow.**
            - However, inquiry approaches necessarily and by design do meet all of them (really true?)
          - CONCLUSION: not all aspects of inquiry learning (like the essential question) may be relevant/beneficial for FL, but the elements of flow and student autonomy for learning might be. Inquiry focuses heaving on **student engagement**
        - DIFFERENT ARTICLE ON INQUIRY LEARNING [here](https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/epdf/10.1002/tea.20347) 
          - Student learning outcomes could be categorized as follows: 
            - Students in the treatment with higher amounts of inquiry saturation did statistically significantly better than those in treatments with lower amounts of inquiry
            - The two (or more) treatments in the study had the same inquiry saturation, and all groups did statistically significantly better on the post-test than the pre-test
            - Findings were mixed regarding the effect of inquiry saturation on student learning
            - There was no statistically significant difference found in student conceptual learning even when there was one treatment with more inquiry saturation than the other treatments in the study
            - The treatment with higher inquiry saturation did statistically worse than the treatment with lower inquiry saturation
          - In a 2-week follow-up assessment, the retention of concepts related to controlling variables in experiments was better for students who received the discovery teaching (higher inquiry saturation).
      - Productive Failure [PF] (==Song + Kapur==)
        - Song (2018) [Educational Technology Research and Development](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s11423-018-9600-3)
          - PF instructional design involves two phases: (1) students first engage in unguided problem solving activities to elicit their prior knowledge, **particularly the failure to solve the problem**; and (2) students then use this information to consolidate and aggregate new knowledge **after the teacher helps solve misconceptions**
          - Students in Class 1 with PF instructional design significantly outperformed those in Class 2 in conceptual understanding
            - To sum up, PF instruction design with less or no guidance prior to problem solving in science learning does not guarantee successful problem solutions. **What matters is the pedagogical design of the learning activities.**
          - The students in Class 2 without PF design were aware of what problem they were expected to explore and how to solve the problem prior to the collaborative work. This might have prevented them from maintaining shared understanding and team organization, which in turn, might influence the quality of their solutions to the problem such that the solution might be “shallow” without identifying deep features.
          - The findings indicate that (1) the students in Class 1 gained deeper understanding of conceptual knowledge and produced better group artifacts in collaborative problem-solving quality than those in Class 2; and (2) the students in Class 1 were more positive in facing the challenges in their project-based learning process, and developed a sense of ownership of their learning. The findings imply that PF instructional design is conducive to developing primary students’ collaborative solving competency in science learning in a seamless learning environment. 
          - Other sources ([here](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s10648-015-9352-0)): studies on "productive failure" and "invention learning" show that minimally guided tasks before explicit instruction can benefit novices, seemingly contradicting CLT (cognitive load theory, which is a baseline for scaffolding)
        - Kapur (2011) [Journal of the Learning Sciences](https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/10508406.2011.591717)
          - **PF** (**productive failure**, in which students collaboratively solved complex problems on average speed *without* any instructional support or scaffolds up until a teacher-led consolidation) or **DI** (**direct instruction**, in which the teacher provided strong instructional support, scaffolding, and feedback)
            - Although PF students generated a diversity of linked representations and methods for solving the complex problems, they were ultimately unsuccessful in their problem-solving efforts. Yet despite seemingly failing in their problem-solving efforts, PF students significantly outperformed DI students on the well-structured and complex problems on the posttest
          - Compared to DI, PF seems to engender deeper conceptual understanding without compromising performance on well-structured problems
          - To explain PF, we need to explain why students from the PF condition performed better, on average, than students from the DI condition. What were the kinds of processes that PF students were involved in that made for better learning and performance?
            - Evidence suggests that (a) PF groups were, on average, able to generate multiple RSMs for solving the complex problems; (b) with a few exceptions, PF groups were not successful in solving the problems; yet (c) PF students, on average, outperformed DI students on the posttest. It seemed that PF students exhibited strong ownership of the RSMs they developed
            - PF design gave students opportunities to engage and develop their meta-cognitive and self-regulatory functions, which in turn are a critical component of learning and problem-solving expertise. In contrast, in the DI design, the well-structured problems may not have afforded such opportunities
        - Holmes (2013) [Instructional Science](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s11251-013-9300-7)
      - Structure Vs Discovery (Cognitive Load Theory and Problem-Based Learning)
        - The Core Debate: Structure vs. Discovery
          - 1. Cognitive Load Theory (The "Guide" Approach)
            - Philosophy: Beginners are easily overwhelmed. You must optimize the demands placed on their brain.
            - Method: Reduce *Extraneous Load* (distractions) and *Intrinsic Load* (difficulty) initially.
            - Goal: Build foundational "schemas" (mental shortcuts) first. Only introduce complexity progressively once the basics are mastered.
            - Role: More guided; the teacher/system manages the flow to prevent burnout.
          - 2. Constructivism (The "Experience" Approach)
            - Philosophy: Knowledge isn't "told," it's "built" by the learner through experience.
            - Includes: Problem-Based Learning (PBL), Inquiry-Based Learning, Experiential Learning, Zone of Proximal Development (ZPD), and Productive Failure (PF).
            - Goal: Student ownership and "deep" understanding.
            - Role: The student takes authority; the teacher facilitates rather than lectures.
        - The Evidence: Which Works Better? 
          - Finding A: Facts vs. Problem Solving (Medical Education)
            - The Data: Meta-analyses show PBL students score *slightly lower* on basic fact retention (multiple choice tests) compared to traditional students.
            - The Flipside: PBL students score *better* on clinical problem-solving tasks and are better at transferring reasoning strategies to new problems.
            - Why it matters for FinLit: Financial Literacy is rarely about memorizing definitions; it is about making choices in new situations. Therefore, PBL/Constructivism may be better suited for FinLit than for memorizing medical anatomy.
          - Finding B: Richness of Structure (Wu)
            - The Data: Students in constructivist-oriented instruction developed "richer" cognitive structures regarding biological reproduction than those in traditional classes.
            - Implication: Constructivism builds a more flexible, connected web of knowledge.
            - Hsin-Kai Wu
              - Who: Researcher in Science Education (National Taiwan Normal University).
              - Focus: Constructivism and Scientific Reasoning.
              - Why Follow: To validate that "messy" learning (constructivism) leads to richer mental maps than linear instruction.
              - Where: *International Journal of Science Education* (various publications).
        - The Bridge: Productive Failure & InventionThe Concept:
          - "Scaffolded learning is inherently dynamic." You can use Productive Failure (PF) to let students struggle first (Constructivism), then step in with Cognitive Load strategies to explain the solution.
        - The "Invention" Study (Holmes)
          - The Setup: Students were asked to "invent" a method to solve a problem *before* being taught how to do it.
          - The Result: Students who invented their own methods learned better than those who just evaluated pre-designed ones.
          - Guided vs. Unguided: Interestingly, the Guided Invention (GI) groups (those who received *some* hints during the invention phase) were significantly more likely to create multiple methods than those left completely alone (Unguided Invention).
          - N.G. Holmes
            - Who: Physics Education Researcher (Cornell University).
            - Focus: Critical Thinking and "Invention" in labs.
            - Why Follow: She provides the nuance to "Productive Failure." It isn't just "let them fail"; it is about *how* you guide that invention process so they generate multiple ideas without getting totally lost.
            - Where: *Instructional Science* (2013).
        - The Mechanism: Cognitive *Learning* Theory Explicit vs. Tacit Knowledge
          - 
          - Explicit Knowledge: Can be physically demonstrated or verbally told (e.g., "The formula for compound interest is...").
          - Tacit Knowledge: Deeply rooted in process, intuition, and function. It cannot be fully explained; it must be *felt* through experience (e.g., the gut feeling that a stock is too risky).
        - The MMORPG Connection (Monem)
          - The Study: A case study of an adolescent gamer.
          - The Finding: Tacit knowledge is acquired almost exclusively through Experiential Learning. You cannot "lecture" a student into having financial intuition; like a gamer, they must play the game to develop the instinct.
        - Reza Monem
          - Who: Educational Researcher (published in *The Qualitative Report*).
          - Focus: Metacognition and Digital Play (MMORPGs).
          - Why Follow: To link video games to deep learning. He provides the academic vocabulary ("Tacit Knowledge") to explain why a simulation/game teaches things a textbook cannot.
          - Where: *The Qualitative Report* (2015).
        - Summary for Aurum
          - Use Constructivism/PBL for the application side (simulation/game) to build Tacit Knowledge and Transferable Skills.
          - Use Cognitive Load Theory for the instructional side (tutorials/feedback) to prevent overwhelm during the learning process.
          - Use Productive Failure as the bridge: Let them try and fail in the game first (Invention), then give them the structured lesson.
      - Main elements of FLOW (==Csikszentmihalyi==)
        - Focuses of the book
          - Clear goals
            - Through game objectives, quests, leveling up, XP
          - Immediate feedback
            - Visual/audible changes in game experience/metrics of player
          - Challenge/skill balance
            - Engaging the right age group for each type of game (starting simple with younger students + then older students playing more advanced/intricate/complex/involved game)
          - Action/awareness merge
            - Naturally responsive without having to overthink the process, able to just execute actions without getting hung up on thinking through the process 
          - Intense concentration
            - So engaged that distractions are blocked out (characteristic of video games)
          - Loss of self-consciousness and perception of time
            - Doubt/fear of failure disappear 
          - Sense of control
            - Feeling in command of their actions even in challenging moments
            - Sense of autonomy (that they are acting of their own accord and not doing busywork or being handheld through the process)
          - Intrinsic motivation
            - Activity done for its own sake, not external rewards (would have to balance this with ideas of larger prizes students could get for their success in the game
      - Bloom's Taxonomy
      - Piaget's Learning Theory Overview
        - Active exploration instead of passive memorization (constructivist)
        - Children think in terms of schemas (mental frameworks)
          - Can be added to/modified
        - 4 stages of cognitive development 
          - Sensorimotor (0-2)
            - Learning from environment and simple actions through immediate experience 
            - No mental representations
            - End of stage = object permanence and goal-directed behavior
          - Preoperational (2-7)
            - Thinking = symbolic + imaginative (pretend play)
            - Egocentric (struggling to see other POVs) 
          - Concrete Operational (7-11)
            - Logical understanding/cause and effect
          - Formal Operational (12+)
            - Abstract thinking + problem solving
            - Understanding of hypothetical + moral reasoning
        - Article [here](https://www.academia.edu/20194855/Financial_literacy_programs_targeted_on_pre_school_children_Development_and_evaluation) (Kalish)
          - Individuals learn by reconciling inconsistencies in understanding - equilibration (10)
          - Children in preoperational stage have difficulty understanding different dimensions of money (shapes, sizes, colors, textures) (18)
          - Example: young children (3-4yrs) understood what money was, could explain that it is used to buy things, but would often take things from "stores" without paying in role playing situations
            - Slightly older children (4-5yrs) paid for their purchases in the mock store, but did not seem to understand that money has different denominations (if they did understand that, they tended to assign one denomination to a single item of purchase)
            - 5-6 yr olds understand that some denominations of money do not have enough value to buy some things, but didn't understand change, saying "that's what storekeepers do"
            - Logic involved in understanding the processes in the storekeeper situation, according to Piaget, is not present until around 7 yrs (concrete operational stage)
          - Fiske (1991): 4 significant ways that people interact
            - Communal Sharing
            - Authority Ranking
            - Equality Matching
            - Market Pricing
            - Relevant for children's understanding of exchange =  communal sharing + market pricing (20)
      - The Sweet Spot: When Children’s Developing Abilities, Brains, and Knowledge Make Them Better Learners Than Adults (==Gualtieri==)
        - Gualtieri (2022) [Perspect Psychol Sci](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC9442275/#abstract1)
        - Cases where younger children perform/learn better than adults/older children?
          - Learning language, using probabilistic info, detecting causal relations, remembering certain info, solving problems
          - Age-specific
            - Around 4 years of age, children show numerous advantages in reasoning, decision-making, and memory over older learners
            - Perceptual and memory benefits between the ages of 5 and 8
            - Critical period for language acquisition tends to end around the age of 12
          - When children more accurately detect causal patterns
            - One experiment showing how 4-5 year olds more accurately learned a causal rule than adults (Lucas 2014)
          - When children have better memory
            - Preschoolers have better verbatim memory for rhymes than adults (Kiraly 2017)
        - Why younger children?
          - Children's long term memory improves over time, but especially before age 7
          - "Less is more" hypothesis: children's limited working memory causes them to only focus on simpler, more fundamental concepts. Adults have a larger working memory, which can cause them to take in too much info and become overwhelmed (not able to detect patterns as easily with all the noise)
            - Providing support for this proposal, empirical work has shown that adults with more limited working memory capacity detected stronger correlations within the data they were given (Kareev et al., 1997).
            - Older children and adults can process more information, but they tend to rely too much on their prior beliefs, disposing them to make biased conclusions.

      - The intrinsic motivation of Richard Ryan and Edward Deci (==Ryan + Deci==)
        - O'Hara (2017) [American Psychological Association](https://www.apa.org/members/content/intrinsic-motivation)
        - Development + implications of SDT 
        - Ryan states: "We're interested in what we would call **high-quality motivation**, when people can be wholeheartedly engaged in something and really can have both their best experience and their best performance"
        - Puzzle study: one group paid for correct puzzle solutions, one was not. Following the sessions, paid group stopped playing to read magazines, unpaid group kept working (paid group lost intrinsic motivation)
        - Extrinsic motivation can subvert intrinsic, by undermining basic worth of activity (but both are needed, how to balance?)
      - Video Gaming May be Associated with Better Cognitive Performance in Children (==NIH==) (*)
        - NIH (2022) [News Releases](https://www.nih.gov/news-events/news-releases/video-gaming-may-be-associated-better-cognitive-performance-children)
        - Background: ADHD significantly more common in children who played 3+ hr/day of video games compared to children who played none
        - New research: children who did 3+ hr/day performed better on cognitive skills tests involving impulse control and working memory compared to children who had never played video games
          - Children 3+ hr/day = faster and more accurate on both cognitive tasks than those who never played. 
          - MRI scans: children 3+ hr/day = higher brain activity in regions of the brain associated with attention and memory than did those who never played
            - More brain activity in frontal brain regions (more cognitively demanding tasks), less brain activity in brain regions related to vision
          - Did not find cause for reckless/violent behavior
      - Good Video Games and Good Learning (==Gee==)
        - Gee (2005) [Phi Kappa Phi Forum](https://ocw.metu.edu.tr/pluginfile.php/2380/mod_resource/content/0/ceit706/week7/PaulGee.pdf#page=1.28) + [article](https://www.legendsoflearning.com/blog/james-paul-gee-game-based-learning/)
        - Learning Principles of Good Games
          - 1. Identity
            - Learning requires student to take on a new identity
            - Games cause learners to adopt/build an identity
          - 2. Interaction
            - Games react to user inputs (HCI)
          - 3. Production
            - Players are producers not consumers, they help “write” the worlds in which they live — in school, they should help “write” the domain and the curriculum that they study
          - 4. Risk Taking
            - Good games lower the consequences associated with failure, so players encouraged to take risks (something traditional school affords less freedom for)
          - 5. Customization
            - Different difficulty levels, good games allow players to solve problems in different ways, players choose distinctive attributes for character
            - Custom curricula should not only be about self-pacing, but about real intersections between curriculum and learner's interests/desires/styles
          - 6. Agency
            - Rare in school, feeling of ownership + control over character, choices, and environment
          - 7. Well-Ordered Problems
            - "Research shows that when when learners are left free to roam in a complex problem space — as they sometimes are in permissive “hands-on” environments — they tend to hit on creative solutions to complex problems, but these solutions do not lead to good hypotheses about how to solve later, even easier problems (Elman 1991)."
            - In good video games, the problems players face are ordered so that the earlier ones are well built to lead players to form hypotheses that work well for later, harder problems. (scaffolding)
          - 8. Challenge + Consolidation
            - Good games = offering of challenging problems, allowing players to solve these problems until solutions are automatic, then new set of problems = rethink mastery, learn anew, integrate new learning with old mastery (Cycle of Expertise)
            - "In school, sometimes the poorer students do not get enough opportunity to consolidate, and the good students do not get enough real challenges to their school-based mastery"
          - 9. "Just in Time" and "On Demand"
            - Why textbooks so inefficient: people struggle to deal with lots of words out of context
            - Games allow for flexibility: when player feels a need for it, wants it, is ready for it, can make good use of it
          - 10. Situated Meanings
            - In learning words, it is more helpful to have situated meanings (instead of just verbal meanings)
            - "Games always situate the meanings of words in terms of the actions, images, and dialogues that they relate to, and show how they vary across different actions, images, and dialogues. They do not just offer words for words. School should not either." 
          - 11. Pleasantly Frustrating
            - Games feel doable but challenging (flow)
            - School is often too easy or too hard for students in the same classroom
          - 12. System Thinking
            - Players encouraged to think about things in terms of relationships, not isolated events/facts/skills
          - 13. Explore, Think Laterally, Rethink Goals
            - Think thoroughly before moving on (think laterally not just linearly)
          - 14. Smart Tools + Distributed Knowledge
            - In video games, the characters you control are like "smart tools." They have built-in skills and knowledge that the player can use
            - Military game: soldiers know how to move and form battle lines, player needs to know when and where to have them do it
          - 15. Cross-Functional Teams
            - Multiplayer games: players often have teams, must each master their own specialty
          - 16. Performance Before Competence
            - Players can perform before they are competent (unlike most schools, where competence comes before performance)
            - Language learning: start speaking before perfectly understanding grammar, but schools typically require students to read/study a lot before 
      - Stealth Assessment (==Shute==)
        - Shute (2021) [Routledge Encyclopedia of Education](https://files.eric.ed.gov/fulltext/ED612156.pdf)
        - Typical classrooms 
          - Continue to differentiate learning and assessment 
          - Don't keeping up with the rapid advances of technologies and the needs of a modern education (requiring high-level skill sets such as critical thinking, creativity, collaboration, and communication)
            - Because modern skills are hard to measure through traditional assessment, well-designed digital games are deemed a viable alternative for assessment of such skills
        - Stealth assessment 
          - Aims to blur the boundaries between game play, learning, and assessment
          - Can quantify both easy-to-measure things (declarative knowledge) and hard-to-measure skills (processes and products related to creativity/problem solving skills)
          - Does not trigger test anxiety (assessments = invisible + part of engaging environment)
          - Provides ongoing feedback, adaptive support, and personalized assessments to each student 
          - Can facilitate a flow experience during gameplay/assessment 
        - How stealth assessment works
          - Student interactions produce datapoints, stored as log files
          - 3 models of the Evidence-Centered Design (ECD) framework 
            - Competency model (CM): defines what is intended to be assessed (knowledge, skill, other attributes). Students classified into various levels (low, medium, and high) based on their actions within the game/environment relative to CM variable
            - Evidence model (EM): delineates what students do (like the observables) comprising the indicators of the competencies of interest. 2 parts include
              - Scoring rules: used to score observables via weighted evidence
              - Statistical models: set values of the evidence, accumulate all relevant evidence, and statistically link the observables to the unobservables
            - Task model (TM): describes task types and characteristics (like the task difficulty and format) that can elicit evidence from student performance about the competency of interest 
      - Math Academy Framework Analysis
        - 1. Core Objective: Scalable Expert Tutoring
          - The Problem: Solves Bloom’s "2 Sigma Problem" (1984), which showed 1:1 tutoring outperforms classroom instruction by two standard deviations.
          - The Solution: Automates expert tutoring decisions using a computational engine rather than human labor.
          - Mechanism: Combines a manually curated Knowledge Graph with a dynamic memory management algorithm (FIRe) to optimize content logistics.
        - 2. The Knowledge Graph (Static Architecture)
          - Structure: A directed acyclic graph (DAG) mapping thousands of mathematical concepts from 4th grade to advanced university topics (e.g., Multivariable Calculus).
          - Granularity:
            - Topics: ~3,000 discrete units (approx. 10x more granular than standard textbooks).
            - Knowledge Points: 3–4 atomic skills per topic.
            - Questions: 20+ template-based variations per knowledge point.
          - Edge Types:
            - Prerequisite (A→B): gates access. You must master A to attempt B.
            - Encompassing (B→A): governs credit flow. Practicing B implicitly practices A.
          - Curation: Manually constructed by domain experts to ensure valid pedagogical dependencies, described as "at the edge of human scale."
        - 3. The Algorithm: Fractional Implicit Repetition (FIRe)
          - Purpose: Prevents the "Tsunami of Review" inherent in standard spaced repetition systems (like Anki) when applied to hierarchical subjects.
          - Implicit Credit (Lightning Bolts):
            - Successfully solving an advanced problem sends weighted credit "downwards" to all encompassed foundational topics.
            - Prevents redundant reviews of elementary skills (e.g., doing Calculus automatically refreshes Algebra).
          - Repetition Compression:
            - System identifies "dominator nodes" — single advanced topics that encompass multiple due reviews.
            - Serves one complex problem to clear a stack of overdue simple reviews.
          - Interference (Growing Trees):
            - Failure on a foundational node propagates penalties "upwards."
            - Advanced topics dependent on the failed skill are flagged as unstable and scheduled for sooner review.
        - 4. Adaptive Calibration
          - Student-Topic Learning Speed (S):
            - Calculated for every student-topic pair: S∝Topic DifficultyStudent Ability​.
            - S>1.0: Student is fast/topic is easy. Reviews are spaced out more aggressively.
            - S<1.0: Student struggles. System tightens review schedules and disables implicit credit, forcing explicit review of the basics.
          - Diagnostic Inference:
            - Uses transitive inference to determine the "Knowledge Frontier."
            - Correct answers on advanced nodes assume mastery of all prerequisites, allowing rapid placement.
        - 5. Pedagogical Interface & Cognitive Load
          - Active Learning:
            - Eliminates passive video lectures.
            - Uses concise text and immediate problem-solving to leverage the Testing Effect (retrieval practice).
          - Subgoal Labeling:
            - Complex problems are chunked into hierarchical steps with descriptive labels (e.g., "Find the common denominator").
            - Based on research by Richard Catrambone; improves transfer to novel problems by teaching structure rather than rote definition.
          - Gamification:
            - XP Economy: 1 XP ≈ 1 minute of focused effort.
            - Incentives: Rewards "good habits" (checking work) to drive automaticity.
        - 6. Validation
          - Eurisko Program (2020-2023):
            - High school students utilized the framework to complete university-level curriculum (Linear Algebra, Differential Equations).
            - Students successfully reproduced graduate-level AI research (e.g., *Blondie24*).
            - Demonstrated a 4x acceleration factor compared to traditional schooling.
        - The Math Academy Way is an evidence-based approach to math learning, centered on active learning, deliberate practice, and expert tutor emulation through a tech platform, focusing on immediate problem-solving with minimal initial instruction, then providing focused feedback and review to build automaticity and mastery, explained in detail in their book, The Math Academy Way.
          - Core Principles:
            - Active Learning & Deliberate Practice: Students spend most of their time solving problems, not just listening, with carefully chosen exercises for specific skill improvement.
            - Minimal Instruction: Short explanations are given right before practice, with students diving into problems within minutes.
            - Expert Tutor Emulation: The platform uses AI to mimic how an expert tutor guides learning, providing tailored feedback and explanations when mistakes are made.
            - Automaticity: A key goal is to make core math skills automatic, allowing deeper understanding later, using techniques like spaced repetition.
            - Knowledge Graphs: Structuring knowledge to show connections between concepts, helping learners build a robust understanding.
          - How it Works (Platform):
            - Diagnostic Test: Assesses existing knowledge to place students correctly.
            - Lesson Structure: Short intro -> Worked Example -> Practice Problems (often multiple-choice).
            - Feedback Loop: Wrong answers trigger detailed explanations; persistent errors might pause the lesson for other topics.
            - Gamification: Students earn points (XP) for completion and accuracy, with leaderboards.
            - Spaced Repetition: AI schedules reviews for topics needing reinforcement.
      - Math Academy 
      - BOOK REVIEW: Mindset by (==Dweck==)
        - DOK1: Key Facts
          - Carol Dweck’s book "Mindset" introduces two fundamental mindsets: the Fixed Mindset, where intelligence and abilities are seen as static, and the Growth Mindset, which views these qualities as developable through effort and learning.​
          - People with a Fixed Mindset tend to avoid challenges, fear failure, and see effort as fruitless if innate ability is perceived lacking, while those with a Growth Mindset embrace challenges, see failure as an opportunity to improve, and persist in learning.​
          - Research shows that adopting a Growth Mindset can significantly enhance resilience, motivation, learning outcomes, and even leadership effectiveness by shifting focus from proving oneself to continuous development.​
          - It is possible for individuals to hold different mindsets across domains and to consciously change from a fixed to a growth mindset through awareness, reflective practice, and adopting new perspectives.​
          - Notable examples include Christopher Reeve’s recovery effort, demonstrating determination transcending physical limitations, reflecting core Growth Mindset principles.​
        - DOK2: Expert and Personal Insights
          - Carol Dweck’s groundbreaking work reframes failure, effort, and motivation through a psychological lens that empowers individuals to transcend limitations imposed by self-doubt and societal labels. The distinction between fixed and growth mindsets not only elucidates why some succeed and others falter but provides actionable pathways to foster productive, learning-oriented ways of thinking.
        - Expert Integration
          - This mindset theory aligns closely with gamification expert Yu-kai Chou’s focus on intrinsic motivation drivers and behavioral science principles, underscoring the importance of effort recognition and challenge as motivation inside and outside learning environments. Carol Dweck’s ethical caution (acknowledging that mindsets fluctuate and require consistent nurturing) adds necessary nuance, advocating for compassionate, sustained self-improvement rather than one-time fixes.
        - Analysis
          - The transformative power of the Growth Mindset lies in its universality — applicable to academics, personal development, professional growth, and relationships. It encourages a shift from outcome obsession to process orientation, cultivating long-term fulfillment and adaptability.
          - Integrating "Mindset’s" insights enriches any knowledge framework on growth, learning, and achievement by anchoring emotional and cognitive development within a scientifically supported motivational model.
      - BOOK REVIEW: Differentiation and the Brain (==Sousa & Tomlinson==)
        - DOK1: Key Facts
          - Neural Plasticity: The brain is highly adaptable, forming approximately 700 new neural connections per second during childhood, amounting to more than 2.5 million connections per hour (National Scientific Council on the Developing Child, 2007).
          - Reticular Activating System (RAS): Filters about 11 million bits of sensory information per second down to about 50 bits processed consciously, prioritizing relevance and emotional salience (Zimmermann, 1986).
          - Desirable Difficulty: Optimal learning occurs within a “Goldilocks zone” of challenge that is neither too easy nor too hard; mild stress can improve memory formation by 30-40%, while excessive stress impairs it up to 60% (Vogel & Schwabe, 2016).
            - Zone of Proximal Development -> where learning happens best
          - Dopamine and Interest: When learners engage with subjects that interest them, dopamine release improves memory retention by up to 40%, correlating with an average 23% boost in test scores for interest-aligned instruction (Adcock et al., 2006; Hidi & Renninger, 2006).
          - Collaborative Learning & Mirror Neurons: Group work stimulates mirror neurons, enhancing learning retention by up to 50% compared to solitary study (Johnson & Johnson, 2009).
          - Tiered Assignments: Tailoring work to readiness levels improves outcomes by around 30%, supporting equitable learning progress while respecting individual capacities (Tomlinson, 2014).
          - Feedback: Specific, regular feedback drives up to 57% more improvement than grades alone by guiding brain rewiring (Hattie & Timperley, 2007).
        - DOK2: Expert and Personal Insights
          - Sousa and Tomlinson’s work synthesizes recent neuroeducational research with classroom strategies, illuminating why differentiated instruction is not merely a pedagogical preference but a neurological imperative. The book breaks down complex brain science (neural plasticity and the role of neurotransmitters like dopamine) into accessible concepts linking brain function directly to teaching practice.
        - Expert Integration
          - From an expert standpoint, their emphasis on the brain’s filtering mechanism (RAS) underlines the necessity of making learning personally meaningful and emotionally safe to capture learner attention in today’s distraction-rich environments. The “desirable difficulty” concept resonates strongly with cognitive psychology’s insights on optimal stress and learning—too little engagement breeds boredom, too much triggers shutdown.
      - Habit Formation and Learning in Young Children (==Whitebread==)
        - Whitebread (2013) [Money Advice Service/University of Cambridge](https://altorwealth.com/wp-content/uploads/2024/04/the-money-advice-service-habit-formation-and-learning-in-young-children-may2013.pdf)
        - The Nature of Habits: The report redefines "habit" not just as a repeated action (like brushing teeth), but as "habits of mind" (unconscious mental processes that determine how a child approaches problems)
          - Inductive Learning: Children are "statistical learners" who detect patterns in their environment from birth. If they see parents planning and saving, they internalize that pattern; if they see impulse spending, they internalize that.
          - Imitation: Children use "mirror neurons" to copy behaviors. They imitate not just actions, but the *intentions* behind them
        - The Engine of Financial Literacy: Executive Functions
          - The report identifies Executive Functions (EF) as the biological foundation for money management. These develop rapidly between ages 3 and 7.
            - Inhibition (Delay of Gratification): The ability to suppress an instinctive reaction. This is the cognitive basis for saving money (choosing a larger reward later over a small reward now).
            - Working Memory: The ability to hold information in mind while processing it. This allows children to plan for the future rather than just reacting to the present.
            - Attentional Flexibility: The ability to switch focus or rules. This helps in problem-solving and adapting to new financial constraints
        - Key Influencers
          - Parents: The most effective parenting style for developing these habits is "Authoritative" (high warmth, high standards). This style encourages the child to self-regulate rather than just following orders ("Authoritarian") or doing whatever they want ("Indulgent").
          - Teachers: Effective classrooms are those that encourage Self-Regulated Learning (SRL). In "High-SRL" classrooms, children are given autonomy and open-ended tasks, which builds the "mastery mindset" needed for financial resilience.
          - Advertising:
            - Children under 8 generally do not understand the persuasive intent of ads (i.e., that ads are trying to sell them something, not just tell the truth).
            - High exposure to ads is linked to higher materialism and "pester power" (demanding goods)
        - Developmental Milestones for Money
          - Counting - 2–4 Years: 
            - Children learn rote counting but may not understand "cardinality" (that the last number counted equals the total amount) until later
          - Conservation - < 7 Years: 
            - Young children judge value by physical size. They often prefer five pennies over one dime because "five is more than one"
          - Exchange - 5/7 Years: 
            - Children begin to understand you must "give up" money to get goods. Before this, they may expect to get the money back or get "change" even if they paid the exact amount
          - The Future - 7/8 Years: 
            - "Saving" requires visualizing a future self. Children under 7 struggle to judge future distances (e.g., "next week" vs. "next month" feels the same)
        -  The "Age 7" Threshold
          - The report states that while complex understanding comes later, the core behaviors are present by age 7:
          - The "Habits of Mind": The study redefines habits not just as actions, but as "unconscious mental propensities" like persistence and impulse control. 
            - These develop rapidly between ages 3 and 7.
          - The Basic Concepts: By age 7, children have typically developed the concepts of Counting (cardinality), Exchange (money is traded for goods), and Value (coins have different worths).
          - The Limitation: Children under 7 struggle to grasp the "future" beyond a few weeks, which limits their ability to understand long-term saving without visual aids.
          - The Implication for Learning
            - Because these habits form so early and subconsciously:
              - Knowledge is Weak: Teaching "explicit forms of financial curriculum knowledge" (like interest rates or banking) does not work to change behavior in young children.
              - Modeling is Strong: The only effective "levers" for this age group are observing parents and practicing basic skills like sharing and waiting.
              - Executive Function is Key: Financial literacy at this age is actually just self-regulation (the ability to inhibit an impulse)
        - Conclusion
          - By age 7, the "habits of mind" (planning, delaying gratification, self-regulation) are largely formed
            - Analogy: operating system and apps - the OS has already been largely built and is unlikely to drastically change without serious intervention. Subsequent learning = apps built on top of OS
          - Financial education for young children should therefore focus on executive function training (playing games that require patience, planning, and inhibiting impulses) rather than explaining interest rates or banking
          - Justification for Sandbox Economy Games being one of the 4 main ways kids learn FinLit (despite not being intentionally designed for education)
            - These cognitive functions have already been formed by age 7, and many 7 year olds have played/do play Sandbox Economy Games -> reason to suggest games play a role in certain childhood cognitive developments, even if only through habit/practice (and even subconsciously).
  - ==DEMOGRAPHIC SURVEYS/MEASURING FINLIT/CURRENT STATUS==
    - OCED [==multiple==]
      - PISA (2024) [OECD](https://www.oecd.org/en/publications/pisa-2022-results-volume-iv_5a849c2a-en/full-report/component-12.html#boxsection-d1e9244-758fee69dd)
      - Graphics showing student familiarity with financial concepts
        - Least understood concepts = diversification, depreciation, dividend, compound interest, ROI
          - Learnt about a wage (73%), a budget (70%) or a bank loan (68%) in school over the preceding 12 months and still know what these terms mean. However, fewer than one in three 15-year-old students, on average across OECD countries and economies, reported that they had learnt about **diversification (18%), depreciation (22%), dividend (24%), compound interest (25%) or return on investment (27%) and still know what they mean**
      - Brazilian schools' FL program
        - The program included financial literacy lessons integrated into the curriculum with both in-school and take-home components
        - Mixed results short term, but analysis of 16,000 students over 9 years showed reduced borrowing from expensive sources and fewer late payments
        - More grads chose entrepreneurship over traditional jobs
    - Measuring Financial Literacy (==Huston==)
      - Huston (2010) [The Journal of Consumer Affairs](https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/j.1745-6606.2010.01170.x)
        - The struggles of defining FL, at the time this was written there weren't many tests for assessing FL
        - There should be a standardized test for measuring FL, but curriculum for teaching it should be tailored to the individual
    - Measuring Scaffolding with TSI and SLA (==Hermkes==) (*)
      - Hermkes (2016) [Learning and Instruction](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0959475217305364)
      - DESIGN
        - The general learning objective was to set up a school cafeteria as a virtual [enterprise](https://www.sciencedirect.com/topics/social-sciences/company), with a special focus on the accounting skills that are necessary to run a company. Students were encouraged to figure out their own way to solve the issues raised by the tasks in a creative way, alternate solutions being appreciated. The validity of a solution had to be judged by the students themselves according to the issues raised.
          - A tablet computer was provided for each small group to take notes. Further learning material was placed at the students' disposal on their tablet computer; toy money was distributed to keep track of financial transactions.
        - Variables: Student level of attainment (SLA), teacher strength of intervention (TSI)
          - Since [Wood (1980)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0959475217305364#bib31), CSP has been regarded as a valid principle for the measurement of scaffolding. 
          - At the beginning TSI should be 0-2 and go higher (to 4 or 5) if SLA is unchanged
          - Generally speaking, instructional support should start with a diagnostic question (TSI = 0). However, it may be that students reveal their state by asking a question, so that the teacher reacts immediately at a higher TSI. In those cases, a TSI of 1 or 2 would be appropriate to commence.
    - 47+ Fascinating Financial Literacy Statistics in 2023 (==Turner==)
      - Turner (2025) [Annuity](https://www.annuity.org/financial-literacy/financial-literacy-statistics/)
      - Behaviors:
        - In 2019, 60% of U.S. adults had credit card debt.
        - In 2021, 86% of teens were interested in investing, but 45% said they didn’t invest because they didn’t feel confident.
      - Education:
        - As of 2021, 75% of teens report learning about personal finance from their parents. 52% of teens learn from school and 42% from social media.
        - 73% of teens reported wanting more personal finance education in 2021, and 75% of teens lack confidence in their knowledge of personal finance.
    - New Survey: Americans Overwhelmingly Support Financial Education in Schools (==Castelli==)
      - Castelli (2025) [American Bankers Association](https://www.aba.com/about-us/press-room/press-releases/new-survey-americans-support-financial-education-in-schools)
      - Survey of 4,475 American adults, interviews conducted online
        - Data were weighted to approximate a target sample of adults based on age, race/ethnicity, gender, educational attainment, and region
      - Consumers (87%) agree that financial concepts should be taught in high school and 72% believe they would be better off financially if they had learned the basics of personal finance at an earlier age
      - Plurality of consumers (38%) learn about money and financial concepts mainly from family, while only 15% selected school as the place they learned the most
      - A 2024 report from the Global Financial Literacy Excellence Center found that the average American scored 48% on a financial literacy test, with only 16% of Americans scoring between 75-100% on the test
    - Financial Literacy Statistics: Financial Knowledge by Key Demographics (==Woodall==)
      - Woodall (2024) [Moneyzine](https://moneyzine.com/personal-finance/financial-literacy-statistics/)
      - The percentage of US adults with poor financial literacy increased from 20% in 2017 to 25% in 2023.
      - Financial literacy is correlated with income, as only 28% of Americans earning less than $25,000 per year are financially literate.
      - Individuals with very low levels of financial literacy are 7x more likely to spend 20+ hours/week dealing with personal finance-related issues.
      - What people do/don't know:
        - At a success rate of 58%, Americans are most knowledgeable about debt and borrowing, followed by 
          - Saving at 55%
          - Consuming at 50%
        - At the other end of the spectrum, US adults scored considerably low on questions regarding risk comprehension – answering only 35% of the questions correctly. This is followed by 
          - Insuring at 42%
          - Investing at 44%
      - Status of Gen Z:
        - Gen Z and Gen Y have the lowest financial literacy rates among US generations, at 38% and 45% correct answers, respectively. (P-Fin Index)
        - 74% of teens in the US don’t feel confident in their personal finance knowledge.
        - Although 62% of them say they learned about saving, 50% learned about earning, 44% learned about banking, and 42% learned about budgets, only 23% of US teens say they know how to make a budget.
        - Largest portion of teens, or 75%, learn about personal finance from their family, while only 52% learn about it at school. Interestingly, almost as many, 48%, say they learn about personal finance on social media, which may be why so many US teens are open to more personal finance education
        - Pew Research reports that 34% of young adults received financial support from their parents in the past year, showcasing a disconnect between financial literacy aspirations and real-world financial autonomy.
        - 88% of Americans say that they weren’t fully prepared to handle money in high school.
    - Children Develop Their Spending and Saving Habits by the Age of 5 (==Bailey==)
      - Bailey (2018) [World Economic Forum](https://www.weforum.org/stories/2018/01/children-develop-their-spending-and-saving-habits-by-the-age-of-5/?utm_source=chatgpt.com)
      - The Spendthrift-Tightwad Scale has long been used to measure adults’ emotional reactions to spending money, says study lead author Craig Smith, research investigator at the University of Michigan Center for Human Growth and Development
        - Tightwad = emotional pain in spending, Spendthrift = tends to overspend
      - In the study, Smith and colleagues created a Spendthrift-Tightwad Scale for Children, and asked 225 kids various questions about their emotional response to saving and spending
        - “This is similar to adults, in that beyond how much they like the item, their emotional orientation towards spending and saving uniquely predicted if they spent or saved,” Smith says.
        - If spendthrift kids were identified early, special interventions could help them learn to think about the negative consequences of overspending.
        - Connection between reactions to idea of spending matches with actual spending habits with children, showing a link that suggests children have already formed conceptions on their spending/saving habits. 
    - Skills or Degree? The Rise of Skill-Based Hiring for AI and Green Jobs (==Bone==)
      - Bone (2024) Buegel Future of Work and Inclusive Growth project
      - Study presents an analysis of a large dataset of online job vacancies in the UK between 2019 and 2022 to investigate the rise of skill-based hiring for AI and green jobs
        - Discerning between importance of formal qualifications (like a college degree) and skills-based qualifications including:
          - Hard skills
            - Machine learning, data analysis, renewable energy system design, generative Adversarial Networks (GANs), environmental law compliance, sustainable operations
          - Soft/strategic skills
            - Problem-solving, creative thinking, risk assessment, project management, adaptability, human-AI collaboration
              - All of these are entrepreneurial skills
      - How Job Market Has Changed:
        - Increased importance of skills in hiring processes due to the evolving demands of the labor market and challenges in finding suitable candidates solely based on formal qualifications
        - Highlights the relevance of skills-based hiring for emerging occupations, particularly those related to the digitalization and sustainability transitions
        - Changing skills demands in the labor market are driven by technological advancements, automation, and the **need for non-routine tasks**
        - Discusses the potential shift from degree-focused to skills-based hiring, considering the limitations of formal education in reflecting the skills acquired through non-formal and informal learning
        - Degrees are losing ground
          - "Mentions of university education requirements for AI roles declined by 15%” and “AI skills command a 23% wage premium — more than degrees up to the master’s level."
        - Adaptability across disciplines matters
          - Jobs now require blending technical ability with adaptive creativity — hallmarks of entrepreneurial mindset, not narrow task-doing (as that is being outsourced to AI)
      - Findings:
        - Employers have started to prioritize **skills** **over formal qualifications** in these emerging fields due to labor shortages
        - Decline in the mention of university education for AI roles and a significant increase in the number of skills advertised, alongside a wage premium for AI skills
        - Recommends exploring alternative skill-building formats to address talent shortages in AI and green jobs
    - Future-Proof Your Career: The Top 10 Skills Employers are Looking for in 2025 (==St. John's==)
      - [Here](https://www.stjohns.edu/news-media/johnnies-blog/top-skills-employers-are-looking-for-2025?utm_source=chatgpt.com)
      - Skills recruiters are actively looking for
        - Technical Skills:
          - Data Literacy and Analytics
