          - Digital Fluency and Technology Aptitude
        - Entrepreneurial Skills:
          - Creativity and Innovation
          - Adaptability and Resilience
          - Critical Thinking and Complex Problem-Solving
          - Leadership Skills and Influence
        - People Skills
          - Emotional Intelligence and Empathy
          - Cultural Competence and Diversity
          - Environmental Sustainability Awareness
      - It used to be enough to have a college degree, but now (because of the large number of college grads and the use of AI in the workplace) employers are looking for people who exhibit higher-level skills
    - Why Don’t Schools Teach Taxes? (==TCI==)
      - [Here](https://www.taxcrisisinstitute.com/why-dont-schools-teach-taxes/) 
      - Video
        - Rockefeller wanted a nation of workers, not thinkers
          - School system follows this even now - students expected to sit down, be quiet, perform repetitive tasks for 8 hours
          - People in education are highly "educat-able"
          - Aim of education is not to produce well-rounded populus, but to reduce as many individuals as possible to the same level of awareness, to suppress originality, discourage critical thinking + imagination
          - Hip-hop was initially used to push positive messages to the youth, including financial independence and ownership, now not at all
          - Whole generation addicted to loops of social media, current education system trains people not to be philosophers, doers or builders, but to be perfectly content living out their life where they currently stand (settling for mediocrity)
      - #1 reason schools don’t teach students about filing taxes:
        -  Too many people require a different process to file income taxes correctly
      - Since teachers aren't tax professionals, this can be complex, and some might argue, not worth the time it takes to implement. 
      - Why Teach Students About Taxes?
        - Personal finance and taxes are much more important topics than other subjects taught in school
        - Although schools may not be able to provide concrete instructions as the IRS or a tax professional can, they are still able to teach basic concepts that help students be more prepared for work and tax filing
        - The United States allows individuals to start working at age 16, which makes high school and even elementary school an ideal setting for learning about personal finances
      - In the United States, it is estimated that only 12% of schools teach personal finances. This includes both elementary and high schools
      - A 2016 PwC study indicates that approximately 78% of teachers feel they need a strong financial curriculum
      - At home, parents are also starting to realize the importance of teaching their kids about finances and requesting that schools take more interest in the topic
      - Most personal finance courses — when offered — include only light touch content on taxes:
        - Basic tax terminology (income tax, payroll tax)
        - Reading a W-2
        - Possibly filling out a mock 1040
      - But strategic instruction (e.g., tax optimization, deduction strategies, marginal vs. effective tax rates) is almost never included, especially for high schoolers
    - What Should My Credit Utilization Ratio Be? (==Luthi==)
      - Luthi (2022) [FICO Scores and Credit Reports](https://www.myfico.com/credit-education/blog/credit-utilization-be#:~:text=What%20Should%20My%20Target%20Credit,the%20amounts%20owed%20score%20ingredient.)
      - Some financial experts recommend keeping your credit utilization ratio below 30%.
        - However, the data doesn't support the implication that your credit score will dip once your utilization ratio crosses the 30% threshold.
      - The lower your ratio is, the better. Generally, keeping it below 10% (and consistently paying bills on time) can help you build and maintain a good FICO Score.
      - Be careful about having a utilization ratio of 0%. This is because it signifies that you're not using your credit cards at all, giving FICO less information about how you manage your money. 
        - While a 0% utilization ratio won't cause your FICO Scores to drop significantly, it can prevent you from achieving maximum points for the amounts owed score ingredient.
      - How to lower credit utilization ratio (CUR)
        - Lower spending
        - Don't close old credit cards (unless you're at risk of overspending and getting into credit card debt, or there's an annual fee or security deposit and you no longer use the card)
        - Make payments strategically: pay the balance before the monthly statement to keep CUR low (can also make multiple payments throughout the month to keep the balance low at all times)
  - ==METHODS RESEARCHED==
    - IBL (inquiry-based learning)/ SCIL (student-centered inquiry learning)
    - PF (productive failure)
    - PBL (problem-based learning)
    - PjBL (project-based learning)
    - Gamification
    - ZPD (zone of proximal development): the difference between a student's current abilities and their potential. Determined through problem solving under adult guidance or through collaboration with more able peers (intellectual asymmetry)
    - Scaffolding: using the ZPD and a vicarious consciousness
    - ==MULABE== (school specialized in econ/finance education) BET (basic economics test) = **43.1% difference** before/after program, PFT (personal finance test) = **29.9% difference** before/after program **[5th/6th grade application]**
    - ==FFFL (financial fitness for life)==: A comprehensive curriculum that covers various financial topics: budgeting, saving, investing, and credit management. Incorporates interactive lessons, assessments, and real-world applications to reinforce learning. **[elementary+ applications] (11% difference). **Comes with a parent guide for each grade level. 
    - ICS (I can save): Emphasizing practical saving strategies through engaging activities, workshops, and tools for tracking savings. Often includes incentives for saving behaviors and goal-setting exercises.** (10.5% difference) [2nd-4th grade]**
    - MyPath Savings: Combines financial education with access to savings accounts, often in partnership with local banks. The program includes workshops on saving, budgeting, and using banking services effectively. **[9th-10th grade] (difference not quantified by tests)**
    - MCE (My classroom economy): A classroom economy model where students earn "classroom currency" through participation and complete tasks. Students manage budgets, save for rewards, and simulate real-world financial transactions. [elementary applications]. **(6% difference) [3rd-8th grade]**
    - ==JA Finance Park====:== An immersive simulation where students take on roles, manage a virtual household budget, and navigate financial decisions related to income, expenses, and savings. The program often includes field trips to real financial institutions. [elementary applications] **(35% difference) [NEED STUDENT AGES, 7th-12th grade?]**
    - Researching/presenting: students research and present whether a given company was a good investment **(15.8% difference) [9th-11th grade]**
    - SaveWise: A program offering resources, tools, and workshops focused on saving, budgeting, and financial planning. It provides practical tools for tracking expenses and setting savings goals. **[8th-9th grade] (difference not quantified by tests, qualitative)**
    - MoneyWise: A mix of online resources, interactive tools, and offline materials that cover a wide range of financial topics. The program is designed to be accessible and engaging for diverse audiences. **(FL 14.85%, Financial self-efficacy 23.67%, planning behaviors 27.84%, acting behaviors 14.79%) [26-35 yr olds]**
    - Open Hands: A community-based program that provides financial education workshops and resources targeting underserved populations. It often includes partnerships with local organizations to improve access to financial resources. **(16.85% difference) [college juniors and seniors]**
    - Kwartalino: An interactive online platform that gamifies financial education. It includes quizzes, challenges, and rewards for learning about money management and financial concepts.** [Kindergarten] (difference not quantified by tests, qualitative)**
    - LMM (learning money matters): Focuses on practical money management skills through workshops and real-world applications. The program includes budgeting exercises, savings challenges, and discussions about financial decision-making. **(difference not quantified by tests) [4th-10th grade]**

  - ==ITC MONETIZATION==
    - Taxation in Six Concepts: A Students Guide
      - **Realization** is a problem for the tax code, happens when an asset changes ownership, but it might not be taxed until later. 
        - Profits are not taxable until a realization event occurs
        - Permits tax deferral - delay paying taxes without interest or penalties
        - Instead of a client *buying* a property from you and you having to pay tax on the profit, you and the client SWAP properties (code doesn't treat the swap of property as an occasion for realization of gain) (5). 
        - BONUS DEPRECIATION (5)
      - 1031 Exchange
        - IRS [form](https://www.irs.gov/pub/irs-news/fs-08-18.pdf#page=4.45)
      - **Substance over form: what taxpayer really did is different from what taxpayer said they did**
        - **Materially participating, safe harbor set of constraint (good faith, this is what is required, abiding within the tax code)**
        - **Case law: special case where you can do something**
        - **IRS still getting money, but from the solar company**
      - Income shifting: sending invoices to individual that works less (earns less, lower tax rate)
        - If couple married, then same tax rate, defeating purpose of income shifting
        - Can be legal or illegal (7)
        - Section 102 gift exclusion: prevents income shifting (avoiding taxes because of progressive income tax, paying taxes at a lower rate than one should)
          - Gifts are not reported as income (for the recipient) and not deducted from income (for the donor)
          - True gifts taxed at donor's rate to prevent income shifting
      - Uses view: income doesn't include money spent on business inputs, only money spent on personal uses
        - Income = consumption + ∆ savings
        - 40k income + stock (if stock worth 50k) = total income of 90k
          - Restricted choice: if employee has no choice about spending/saving the 50k, then no income (recognized in code section 83)
          - If employee has to work for 5 years before the stock ownership vests, then he won't be taxed until after the waiting period
      - Fringe benefits
        - When fringe benefits amount to personal consumption, they are included in income at fair market value
        - Tax law (sections 105 + 106) allows employers to provide tax-free health insurance to workers: cheaper to pay workers in health insurance than cash
      - Business gift: donor deducts, recipient excludes, gift is entirely untaxed (goes beyond income shifting, where no tax is paid)
        - Legal at time of Duberstein (section 102 = gifts excludible, section 162 = deduction for business expenses), but SCOTUS ruled that the car was income
        - Code section 102(c) attacks exclusion, gifts ARE taxable income.
        - Code section 274(b) attacks deduction (33)
      - Tax rates
        - Marginal: rate payable on next dollar of income
        - Average: rate payable on income as a whole
        - Marginal more useful for calculating investments
      - Tax breaks
        - Deductions: reduce taxable income (and related to marginal tax rates)
          - DEPRECIATION: In real estate, can deduct cost of building over time
          - How much is the depreciation worth? - client's marginal rate x deduction (41)
          - Deductions have different values for different taxpayers, depending on marginal rate (42)
          - Depreciation can generally be carried over to the next year (MACRS and section 179
        - Credits: reduce the tax owed (dollar-for-dollar)
          - Low income housing credit (41)
          - Tax credits usually must be used in the same year, people need to owe enough in taxes, allowing the credit to be fully used
      - Income vs wealth tax (50)
        - Income: taxes those who get richer in a year 
        - Wealth: taxes total resources, regardless of year's difference in finances
      - Basis (50)
        - Section 1001: Gain = amount realized - basis
        - Basis measures what taxpayer invested in an asset
        - Good for taxes bc it reduces net income for tax purposes. 
          - Taxpayers want to claim basis ASAP
        - Depreciation permits gradual basis recovery
      - Basis rules for gifts and bequests (61)
        - Code sections 1014 and 1015 (some of the best giveaways in the code), authorizing taxpayers to legally shift income and defer tax
          - 1015: allows son (gift receiver) to take a carryover basis
          - An implicit in section 1001: a gift is not a realization event 
          - Allows for considerable income shifting (what section 102 aims to prevent)
          - Discontinuity in law: If father gives son 150k, entire amount taxed at father's rate since gifts not deductible. But if father gives son property with a low basis (relative to fair mkt value), pair can shift income and have it taxed at son's rate (61)
        - Inherited property = even better
          - Heirs take property with basis equal to fair market value, no one pays tax on the 50k gain, 100% tax forgiveness (on property not cash
          - Tax disadvantages on people who make gifts of depreciated property
      - Gains and losses
        - Taxpayers should wait to sell appreciated assets and sell depreciated assets at the first chance (holding onto gains allows for tax deferral, and if until death, then tax forgiveness - section 1014)
        - Taxpayer shouldn't give the property away or to heirs (sections 1015 and 1014 prohibit them from getting the depreciation)
      - Corporate dividends = not deductible
        - Double tax on corporate income: (1) corporation itself (2) shareholder with corporate dividend
          - 3 rules (1) corporations are taxable entities (2) dividends are income [reportable on shareholders' tax returns (3) corporations may not deduct dividends
          - Exacto Spring problem (98)
    - FAQ
      - Does the investor keep the ITCs?
      - How quickly can depreciation be claimed?
      - How could business synergy work?
      - Anti-abuse regulations (Sec. 701, 7701(o), and 704), substance over form?
        - Can structure so that safe harbor specifications are met
      - Are credits + depreciation insured?
    - Section 269 (question from Andy Price)
      - designed to prevent taxpayers from acquiring corporations primarily for the purpose of tax avoidance
      - Since the proposal involves a partnership rather than a C corp, Section 269 is not the primary concern—instead, the earlier-mentioned partnership-specific rules (Sections 701, 7701(o), and 704) are more relevant.
    - General Solar Info
      - Average Residential System = 5-10 kW = 0.005-0.01 MW
        - Avg price = $2.50-3.50/watt
        - We charge $4
      - Average Commercial System = 100-200 kW = 0.1-0.2 MW
      - Depreciation Rules
        - If system used for business, can depreciate up to 85%
        - If eligible, 60% bonus depreciation applies in 2025 (phased down from 100% in 2022).
        - Example
          - System cost: $20,000
          - ITC claimed: 30% → $6,000
          - Basis reduction: 50% of ITC = $3,000
          - → Depreciable basis = **$17,000**
          - Bonus depreciation (60%) in year 1:
            - $17,000 × 60% = $10,200 (Year 1)
          - Remaining basis = $6,800
          - Depreciated over 5 years using MACRS

    - Category 4 - OWGs (not designed for learning)
      - [Eve Online](https://www.eveonline.com/): MMO
        - Set in a sci-fi universe
        - Game components: exploration, trade/economy, warfare, mining/industry, politics/alliances
        - FinLit overlap: 
          - Budgeting/expense management 
            - Manage in-game currency to buy ships/equipment/resources
          - Income streams 
            - Mining, trading, freelancing. Active/passive income
          - Market economics
            - Fully player-run market with supply/demand
          - Risk management/insurance
            - Can insure ships, choosing right level of insurance
          - Scams/fraud awareness
            - Social engineering scams, Ponzi schemes, corporate fraud
      - [World of Warcraft](https://worldofwarcraft.blizzard.com/en-us/): MMORPG
        - Set in a persistent, evolving fantasy world
        - Game components: quests/storytelling, combat/strategy, crafting/economy, collaborative combat, guilds/social groups, exploration/problem-solving
        - FinLit overlap:
          - Budgeting/expense management 
            - Players must manage resources, balancing income/expenses when buying gear, trying to not overspend on cosmetic things when they need performance things first
          - Income streams
            - Completing quests, selling collected items
          - Market economics
            - Auction house: realistic marketplace, prices fluctuate on demand, inflation (too much gold)
          - Risk management/insurance
            - Spending all their gold on a high-value item that might depreciate, expensive repairs
          - Saving/investing
            - Save for expensive items, invest in rare items, auction house like stock market (buy low, sell high)
          - Entrepreneurship/negotiation
            - Craft + sell items for profit, negotiating trade deals
      - [Minecraft](https://www.minecraft.net/en-us): Sandbox
        - Set in a pixelated, open-concept world
        - Game components: build, mine/gather resources, craft/innovate, survive/manage resources, explore, collaborate, fight players/mobs
        - FinLit overlap:
          - Budgeting/expense management 
            - Choosing how to spend resources efficiently, running out of resources causes problems in game
          - Income streams
            - Mining/selling materials, farming/selling crops, trading with villagers, running in-game businesses
          - Market economics
            - Multiplayer economy driven by supply/demand, rare resources come at higher prices than common ones
          - Risk management/decision-making
            - Exploring dangerous areas for valuable materials, borrowing resources from others
          - Saving/investing
            - Storing valuable materials, spending resources to upgrade increases efficiency over time
          - Entrepreneurship
            - Players can run shops together
    - Types of Game UIs (visual structures and player POV)
      - 1. First-Person View (1P)
        - Camera: From the eyes of the character
        - Used In: Minecraft (Survival), Call of Duty, VR games
        - Experience: Highly immersive; good for exploration
        - Limitations: Can be overwhelming for young players; complex to implement
      - 2. Third-Person View (3P Over-the-Shoulder)
        - Camera: Follows character from behind
        - Used In: The Sims 4, Fortnite, YouTuber’s Life
        - Experience: Balanced immersion + control
        - Limitations: Requires 3D environments and character animations
      - 3. Top-Down (Bird’s-Eye View)
        - Camera: Directly overhead; full visibility
        - Used In: Stardew Valley, SimCity, old-school Zelda
        - Experience: Strategic, world-focused, commonly used in god games
        - Variants: 2D grid (pixel-style) or isometric
      - 4. Isometric View
        - Camera: Angled 3D without perspective distortion (2.5D)
        - Used In: RollerCoaster Tycoon, Clash of Clans, Fallout 1 & 2
        - Experience: Gives spatial layout without full 3D, commonly used in god games
        - Ideal For: Building, simulation, base expansion games
      - 5. 2D Side View (Platformer Style)
        - Camera: Side scrolling
        - Used In: Terraria, Mario, Limbo
        - Experience: Good for physical challenges and simplified navigation
        - Limitation: Not great for management or complex choice systems
      - **6. Flat 2D Manager View (Aurum MVP)**
        - Camera: Static UI, player controls systems or people through menus/buttons
        - Used In: Tiny Tower, BitLife, Egg Inc., AdVenture Capitalist
        - Experience: Simulation-style; players act more like managers or “narrative gods”
        - Strengths:
          - Clean UX
          - Easy to teach real-world systems (economics, time management, finance)
          - Works well with limited animations or a focus on dashboard-style interfaces
        - Ideal For: MVPs, education games, tycoon systems
      - 7. Point-and-Click / Visual Novel UI
        - Used In: Choices, Doki Doki Literature Club, Reigns, Episode
        - Camera: Static backgrounds + text/dialogue choices
        - Experience: Narrative-driven; decision-making emphasized
        - Visuals: Often highly polished 2D artwork
      - 8. Text-Based UI (Minimal Visuals)
        - Used In: AI Dungeon, old MUDs, BitLife (early version)
        - Experience: Imagination-driven; often stat-heavy
        - Strengths: Max flexibility, minimal development cost
        - Limitations: Not very engaging visually for younger audiences
      - 9. Map-Based Interface (World-as-Menu)
        - Used In: SimCity (macro), Risk (strategy), early Pokémon (map nav), BitLife (countries)
        - Experience: The world is navigated via a clickable map
        - Strengths: Gives spatial sense without building a world to explore
        - Great for: Travel, territory unlocking, branching world expansion
      - 10. Hybrid UI (e.g., Sims-Lite + Manager UI)
        - Used In: Game Dev Tycoon, Two Point Hospital, Animal Crossing: Happy Home Designer
        - Experience: Player acts both as a manager and mild first-person designer
        - Strengths: Encourages attachment to characters/places
        - Challenge: Higher dev time and art needs
      - 11. Mobile HUD-Centric UI (Tap Economy Games)
        - Used In: Design Home, Cooking Dash, Idle Miner Tycoon
        - Experience: Tap-based interaction, often driven by task lists or status bars
        - Visuals: Cluttered with monetization layers unless done carefully
      - 12. AR or VR Interfaces
        - Used In: Pokémon GO (AR), Beat Saber (VR)
        - Not ideal for MVP, but worth noting for future long-term innovation
      - Isometric Life Simulation Interface (Hybrid 2.5D) 
        - What Aurum V1 will use
        - Isometric, explained [here](https://games.themindstudios.com/post/how-to-make-game-like-brawl-stars/)
          - Summary of 2, 2.5, 3D [here](https://www.quora.com/What-type-of-games-are-more-profitable-2D-2-5D-or-3D-games-I-want-to-go-into-mobile-game-development-not-just-for-the-money)
    - Design Styles
      - Flat design
      - Skeuomorphism
  - ==LEGAL/REGULATORY==
    - **Investment Advice and Securities Regulation**
      - Goal: avoid being classified as an unregistered investment advisor (IA), triggered by providing advice about securities for compensation
      - **Investment Advisers Act of 1940 (IAA)**
        - Goal: To monitor and regulate the activities of investment advisers and ensure they act as fiduciaries**.**
        - The IA Test: A firm meets the definition if it, for compensation, is in the business of advising others about securities. 
          - Aurum's model risks meeting this test if its AI provides personalized recommendations on stocks or investment strategies.
        - The Publisher's Exclusion: This is Aurum's main defense. It exempts publishers of "bona fide newspaper, news magazine or business or financial publication of general and regular circulation". 
          - Aurum must keep its content general and impersonal to qualify; personalized AI advice could jeopardize this exemption.
      - **SEC Release: Definition of Investment Adviser**
        - This interpretive release explains how the IAA applies to financial planners and others who offer related services. Key for understanding where Aurum could cross the line.
    - **Data Privacy and Youth Protection**
      - **COPPA Rule (Children's Online Privacy Protection Act)**
        - Goal: To place parents in control over what information is collected from their young children online.
        - Requirements: Operators covered by the rule must provide direct notice to parents and obtain verifiable parental consent (VPC) before collecting personal information (including names, geolocation, photos, or persistent identifiers) from children under 13.
        - Personal Info: This definition is broad and includes biometric identifiers and persistent identifiers (like IP addresses or cookies) used to recognize a user over time.
        - Data Retention: Operators are prohibited from indefinitely retaining children's personal information; it can only be kept as long as necessary for the purpose it was collected.
      - **Verifiable Parental Consent (VPC)**
        - COPPA requires obtaining verifiable parental consent before collecting personal information from children under 13.
        - The operator must first give the parent a direct notice that clearly explains:
          - The specific personal information the operator intends to collect from the child.
          - How the operator intends to use that information.
          - The identities or categories of any third parties to whom the information might be disclosed.
          - The parent's right to review or request deletion of the child's data.
    - **Legal Disclaimers & Messaging**
      - Goal: To fulfill legal compliance requirements (set by agencies like the SEC and FTC), inform users of risks, and protect the company from lawsuits stemming from user financial decisions.
      - Key Messaging: The disclaimers must clearly and prominently state that the content is: "For informational purposes only" and "does not constitute financial, legal, or investment advice".
      - Risk Mitigation: They must explicitly state that Aurum is not a financial advisor and that past performance is not indicative of future results (especially for any simulations).
- Surprising Findings Post-Implementation
  - Mandatory FinLit = higher credit scores + lower delinquency rates
    - A study by the Federal Reserve found that students in 3 states with mandatory FinLit courses continued to have higher credit scores and lower delinquency rates compared to those in control states [here](https://www.federalreserve.gov/pubs/feds/2014/201468/201468pap.pdf) 
  - "Protecting" was the category with largest improvement after the FinLit course (similar to low risk-assessment abilities before FinLit education?). 
    - Actions the pilot group took significantly more than the control group did: checking phone bill for accuracy, not exceeding phone plan, knowing how much cash is in purse/wallet. Interestingly, the pilot group performed worse than average on their understanding of bond prices. [here](https://financialliteracy.champlain.edu/wp-content/uploads/2023/04/Prepped-for-Success-Report_120915-2.pdf)
  - Demographic info + results (gender/first language)
    - ESL (english second language) students had a greater score difference than EFL (english first language) students. Focus on soft skill development and problem solving gave students the confidence to make personal financial decisions. [here](https://www.jstor.org/stable/26573525)
    - Women tend to be more risk-averse than men, investing less in risky assets. After a strong FinLit education, they invest in such assets ~ equally. [here](https://www.jstor.org/stable/48738996)
    - Women, minorities, and first-generation college students consistently score lower on FinLit assessments
    - On average, individuals with more patience appear to be more financially literate (Bianchi 2018; Oberrauch and Kaiser 2022) [here](https://gflec.org/wp-content/uploads/2024/04/WP2024-2.pdf)
  - Online finance classes were more successful than F2F finance classes [here](https://www.jstor.org/stable/48796900)
  - Having FinLit education led to higher likelihood of having a will [here](https://www.finrafoundation.org/sites/finrafoundation/files/2024-10/What-We-Leave-Behind-Financial-Education-and-Estate-Planning.pdf) and lower likelihood of taking out payday loans [here](https://www.finrafoundation.org/sites/finrafoundation/files/2024-10/Does-State-Mandated-Financial-Education-Affect-High-Cost-Borrowing_0_0_0.pdf)
  - Parental involvement - many people learn FinLit from parents, whose financial habits are often adopted by their children. Direct parental involvement through the program is limited (pg 191). The ENEF financial education project actually prioritizes this, proving extremely successful [here](https://www.jstor.org/stable/43861592)
- Terms List - Research Context
  - *An expansion of the Purpose node - what is in-scope of the project listed & defined specifically below
  - Behavioral/Cognitive Psychology
    - Flow State (optimal learning circumstance)
      - Person is completely immersed in activity, fully focused/engaged, often loosing track of time, sense of control, intrinsically motivated, challenge + skill balanced
      - Facilitated by clear goals, tasks = skill level, distractions minimized, guided by choice + intrinsic motivation
      - 9 Signs of Flow:
        - Clear goals
        - Challenge-skill balance (not too easy or too hard)
        - Intense concentration 
        - Merging of action and awareness (feeling one with the task)
        - Loss of self-consciousness (not worrying about others' perceptions)
        - Altered sense of time
        - Immediate feedback
        - Sense of control
        - Intrinsic motivation (task enjoyable for its own sake)
    - Piagetian Theory (stages of cognitive development)
      - Children's ability to reason is defined by schemas (mental frameworks - how information fits together)
        - Active exploration instead of passive memorization (constructivist)
        - Learning happens by assimilation (fitting new information into schema) and accommodation (adjusting schema for information that previously contradicted it)
        - Cognition develops in 4 distinct stages
          - Sensorimotor (0-2)
            - Learning from environment and simple actions through immediate experience 
            - No mental representations
            - End of stage = object permanence and goal-directed behavior
          - Preoperational (2-7): symbolic (but not logical) thinking, egocentric
            - Thinking = symbolic + imaginative (pretend play)
            - Egocentric (struggling to see other POVs) 
            - Centration: focusing on one aspect of a situation, ignoring others (e.g., only the height of a liquid in a glass, not the width)
            - No conservation
            - Focus on appearances, not logic
          - Concrete Operational (7-11): logical thinking with concrete events - understands conservation
            - Logical understanding/cause and effect (but only to concrete situations)
            - Understanding of conservation
            - Decentration: understands multiple aspects of a situation
            - Reversibility: can mentally reverse actions (pouring water back will make it look the same again)
            - Classification: can sort objects and understand order
          - Formal Operational (12+)
            - Abstract thinking + problem solving (and use of deductive logic)
              - Scientific reasoning and planning (thinking through all possibilities in a puzzle)
            - Understanding of hypothetical + moral reasoning
            - Metacognition: thinking about thinking
      - Helpful concepts 
        - Preschool (2-5 years)
          - Magical/wishful thinking, logical solutions sometimes not sufficient
          - Poor concept of causation
          - Egocentric perception (like giving a toy to an upset person)
          - Play stories more detailed (4-5 years)
          - Rules = absolute
        - Older childhood (6-12 years)
          - Understand multiple POVs
          - Rules can change with mutual agreement
          - 4th-5th grade: learning to read --> reading to learn
        - Adolescence (12+)
          - Abstract thinking, logic --> scientific solution
    - Extrinsic Motivation (external rewards/pressures)
      - Motivates action because of the expected outcome, not from personal/inherent enjoyment
      - Examples: studying for a grade, working for a paycheck, following rules to avoid punishment
      - External rewards: grades, trophies, money, recognition, avoiding penalties
    - Intrinsic Motivation (internal passion/enjoyment)
      - Doing something because it is enjoyable, interesting, or driven by an innate passion
      - Examples: reading/solving a puzzle for pleasure, learning a language out of curiosity, practicing a hobby because it is rewarding to improve
      - Likely outcomes: better retention, persistence, mastery, personal satisfaction, lifelong learning (instead of good performance/test taking)
    - Self-Efficacy (confidence in one's own ability to succeed)
      - Leads to better motivation + increased effort, persistence, higher achievement, increased likelihood of facing challenges
      - Sources (Bandura's 4):
        - Mastery experiences
        - Vicarious experiences
        - Social persuasion
        - Physiological states
    - RAS: Reticular Activating System (neurological filter)
      - Determines which stimuli are important enough to reach higher brain areas (allows for concentration and ignoring distractions 
        - Processes 11 million bits/second, only allows 50 bits/second to reach conscious mind
      - Selective attention: focus on important info while ignoring irrelevant noise
      - Especially responsive to new/unexpected stimuli (helping to quickly shift attention to respond to environment)
      - Connects to CLT and scaffolding: both prioritize the optimization of working memory load and working within the ZPD (zone of proximal development) to ensure that learning activities are neither to difficult (causing excessive frustration) or too easy (causing boredom)
      - RAS is the metric for determining what is within the ZPD
    - SDT: Self-Determination Theory (Control, Capability, Connection)
      - People have natural tendancies/capacities for growth, based off of 3 primary psychological needs (autonomy, competence, relatedness)
      - Needs being met -> intrinsic motivation, psychological wellbeing, optimal functioning (ie. FLOW)
    - Goal Gradient Effect (working harder when goal is closer)
      - Motivation + behavior change when achieving a goal is near
      - The closer someone perceives a goal to be, the harder and faster they are more motivated to work 
    - Octalysis (8 core drives for human behavior)
      - Described [here](https://workflowy.com/#/88609928af1a)
      - 8 drives, divided into left/right brain and white/black hat drives
  - Learning Science
    - Scaffolding (temporary support -> independence)
      - Connected to Vygotsky’s ZPD, helping students work within it until they gain independence
      - Characteristics: temporary, responsive, supportive, strategic
      - I do, we do, you do (as referenced by learning expert)
      - Lots of teacher support -> no/minimal teacher support
      - Increased confidence (self efficacy), problem solving, independent thinking, deeper understanding, while reducing cognitive overload (aligned with CLT)
      - Once student level of attainment (SLA) is understood (preferably through endogenous/stealth assessment), platform can adjust 
        - (1) CONTINGENCY, (2) FADING, (3) TRANSFER OF RESPONSIBILITY
      - 6 scaffolding functions: 
        - (a) sparking situational interest
        - (b) reducing the complexity and difficulty of tasks
        - (c) keeping learners focused on their goal
        - (d) highlighting crucial features of a task
        - (e) motivating disappointed learners
        - (f) providing solutions and models of a task
    - CLT: Cognitive Learning Theory (working memory load)
      - Focus on internal mental processes: how people process/store/retrieve info
        - Emphasis on working memory limits, scaffolds reduce cognitive load (teaching at the appropriate level, so student is neither overwhelmed or bored)
        - Goal: preserve intrinsic load [inherent difficulty of task/topic] and minimize extrinsic load [outside burdens on learning experience, typically caused by environment]
      - Schema idea (Piaget's learning theory): new learning most effective when connects to existing schemas
      - Worked Example Effect:
        - Novice learners benefit from seeing worked-out solutions rather than immediately attempting to solve the problems independently (against productive failure)
        - By reducing extraneous cognitive load, worked examples allow learners to dedicate more cognitive resources to understanding the task and building a mental model
        - Why/how they reduce cognitive load:
          - Reduces search effort
          - Provides clear guidance
          - Fosters schema construction
    - PBL: problem-based learning (learning through problem solving)
      - Presented with a problem before being taught the answer, learn content through the process of problem solving
      - Learning through doing
      - Main idea diverges from scaffolding/CLT, likely not ideal teaching model and seems prone to harming self efficacy if done wrong
    - ZPD: Zone of Proximal Development (learning zone)
      - Sweet spot of learning, not too easy or too difficult
      - ZPD = range between what learner can do on their own, and what they can do with guidance/support
      - Scaffolding is teaching within the ZPD
      - Aligned with CLT: processing new ideas by linking to current abilities
    - Constructivism (learner-developed knowledge)
      - Students build knowledge through experience and reflection (draws from experiential learning)
      - Branch of CLT, learners construct their own understanding through active learning
      - Building mental models (like Piaget's theory), focus on active + personalized learning
      - Social interaction + making mistakes matter
    - PF: Productive Failure (struggle first, teach later)
      - Learners attempt to solve complex problems before being formally taught how
      - Goal is not to succeed initially, but to engage deeply/make mistakes/generate ideas so that later instruction is more effective
      - PF likely to harm self efficacy, especially when implemented with younger learners (lower cognitive maturity, lesser prior knowledge, lower self-regulation/emotional resilience)
    - Protege Effect (learning through teaching)
      - Phenomenon where the act of teaching/preparing to teach significantly enhances one's own understanding/retention of the material
      - Underpins concept of role-playing games 
      - Why it works
        - Students have to put more effort into understanding content/methods, since they know they will be teaching it to another person (active explanation vs passive test-taking, need to prepare for dynamic conversation instead of responding to assessment questions)
        - Contributes to sense of ownership + agency -> increased confidence
        - Greater motivation (increased sense of responsibility and purpose, feels more meaningful to help someone)
        - Improved communication skills
    - SCT: Social Cognitive Theory (learning through imitation + confidence development)
      - Importance of observational learning, imitation, and modeling in behavior development
      - Observational Learning
        - People learn by watching others (not that people *don't* learn by direct experience)
      - Reciprocal Determinism
        - Behavior, cognition/emotions, and environmental influences interact + shape each other
      - Self-Efficacy
        - Belief/confidence in one's ability to succeed shapes their motivation/actions
      - Modeling
        - People more likely to adopt behaviors demonstrated by others, especially if behaviors = rewarded (very true for young children -> family financial socialization)
    - FFS: Family Financial Socialization (how family influences financial behavior)
      - Includes financial attitudes, behaviors, and knowledge that is imparted on (especially young) children by their families
      - Has significant impact on children's development (mentally/socially)
      - Parents are the first to model behavior/teach young children
    - SLT: Situated Learning Theory (importance of context for learning)
      - Learning happens in context, not in the abstract
      - Key Ideas
        - Learning as social participation - learning is not passive absorption of abstract knowledge, but the active participation in a community of practice (group of people who share a craft/profession)
          - Newcomers are "legitimate peripheral participants," gradually moving toward full participation through observation, guided practice, and increasing engagement with community
        - Context matters - knowledge/skills best acquired in environments that mirror those of when they will be used
          - Abstract, decontextualized instruction (lectures, note memorization) are less effective than learning embedded in real-world tasks/settings (like the importance of learning a language by immersing oneself in the speaking environment, also the basis for endogenous fantasy, or fantasy elements within a game that share the context of the game's content - financial football = example of poor endogenous fantasy because its context is so removed from its content [personal finance])
        - Co-construction of knowledge - learning = collaborative process where meaning is negotiated and constructed through social interaction
          - Community provides opportunities for learners to share experiences, reflect, and interpret information together
        - Progression from novice to expert - learners start at periphery of community, observing and participating in simple tasks (like Montessori school)
  - Gamification Design
    - GBL: Game-Based Learning (learning is the game)
      - Aims of GBL/how different from traditional learning
        - (1) using action instead of explanation
        - (2) creating personal motivation and satisfaction
        - (3) accommodating multiple learning styles and skills
        - (4) reinforcing mastery of skills
        - (5) providing an interactive and decision-making context
      - How it accomplishes these
        - Clear learning goals
        - Immediate feedback
        - Progressive challenge (scaffolding is intrinsic to game's structure)
        - Motivation + engagement 
        - Safe failure
        - Active learning
      - Gamification vs GBL
        - Gamification: adding game elements to non-game activities, learning happens in traditional contexts, but with levels, points, badges, etc (can lead to gamification fallacy)
          - Example: getting points for doing homework -> extrinsic motivation
        - GBL: learning happens through gameplay, the game is the learning
          - Example: using Sims to learn urban planning -> intrinsic motivation
      - Theoretical Foundations of GBL
        - Constructivism
        - SLT
        - Experiential learning
        - Flow theory
      - Benefits
        - Increased motivation/engagement
        - Encouragement of exploration, experimentation, and agency
        - Development of critical thinking, collaboration, and digital literacy
        - Safe environment for failure
        - Personalized learning paths
      - Challenges
        - Must be not only entertaining, but also aligned with learning goals
        - Risk of shallow engagement if mechanics aren't meaningful
        - Requires time, resources, thoughtful design
        - Needs effective assessment strategies
    - Gamification (use of game mechanics in non-game contexts)
      - Thorough understanding of human motivation to design systems that are engaging and rewarding
      - Game components used: points/XP, badges, levels, quests, leaderboards, streaks, feedback loops, avatars, narratives, multiplayer aspects (but should not commit gamification fallacy by limiting to these)
      - Reasons for effectiveness: autonomy, competence, relatedness, mastery
        - Related to self-determination theory and flow state
        - More interactive, personal, meaningful than non-gamified processes (especially those with extrinsic motivators)
    - Serious game (game designed for purpose other than entertainment)
      - Use of gameplay to achieve learning goals
      - Examples: budget hero, cashflow, duolingo
      - Characteristics: full game mechanics, narrative/context reflective of real world, can include endogenous assessments
      - Benefits: experiential learning, safe/productive failure, problem solving, suitable to diverse learning styles
    - Endogenous Assessment (tests integrated within gameplay)
      - Assessments = continuous + ubiquitous, built into game itself, happening naturally as a part of the gameplay (within the game loop, so as not to divert/remove them from gameplay to be assessed)
        - Tasks/challenges double as assessments (e.g., resource management in The Sims assesses decision-making)
        - No separation between gaming and testing - unlike traditional assessments, there is no  explicit "assessment phase," rather, progress is measured through natural interactions
          - Allows for testing to be continuous, spanning throughout the game, instead of being limited to specific measures 
      - Game = evidence of learning
      - Authentic, motivating, immediate feedback, data-rich
      - Misconception: endogenous assessment is just being assessed by the game (game has an integrated "quizlet" used for tests, rather than paper quizzes issued by the teacher)
        - Why wrong: such assessments are just a superficial layer added to the game experience, not integrated within gameplay. Included assessment ≠ endogenous assessment. 
    - MMORPG (structured multiplayer gameplay)
      - Customizable avatars, persistent world, quests/XP, alliances, PvE + PvP, economic systems
      - Examples: WoW, Final Fantasy XIV, Eve Online
    - MOOC (massive open online course)
      - Massive: 100,000+ students can take it at once
      - Open: Anyone can sign up (usually free or cheap)
      - Online Course: It replicates a college lecture hall digitally (Video Lectures + Multiple Choice Quizzes)
      - The Format: You watch a video, then answer a quiz. (Passive)
      - The Problem: Because they are passive and lonely, people quit. The average completion rate is <10% because there is no "hook" or gamification to keep them there
      - The Big Players: Coursera, edX, Udacity, Khan Academy
      - Comparison to Aurum:
        - MOOCs democratized access to information (anyone can watch a Harvard lecture), but they failed to solve engagement. They are just digital textbooks. Aurum is the next evolution: we don't just let you watch the lecture; we let you play the simulation.
    - Sandbox (open world gameplay)
      - Grants players large freedoms in exploring/creating/playing - not restricted by rigid objectives/linear storyline
      - Player-driven objectives, exploration/creativity, minimal restrictions
      - Examples: Minecraft, Sims, Roblox
    - Endogenous Fantasy (original to game mechanics/narrative)
      - Deeply integrated within game's context, feels authentic to content
      - Unlike exogenous fantasy
        - Introduced into game from outside sources (pop culture, literature, other media)
        - Implemented to increase engagement (but can become a major source of distraction)
        - Players may feel a disconnect between the material they are learning and the external factors
    - HCI: Human computer interaction (how people interact with tech)
      - Human-centered design/usability
      - Multi-modal interaction
      - VR/AR
    - Evergreen Mechanics (facilitating continual gameplay)
      - Gameplay mechanics that allow the game to be enjoyable for its own sake without constant intervention from the game designer
      - Not tied to trends/temporary content, instead focusing on human motivations/behaviors
    - Appointment Dynamics (motivation at certain times)
      - Scheduled engagement - mechanics that encourage players to return to a game at specific, predefined times to take specific actions and receive rewards
        - Create a sense of urgency and anticipation, potentially fostering habits and encouraging consistent player engagement
      - Drives targeted - 2, 6, 8, but can also be tweaked to include 4 and 5
      - 2 types ADs:
        - One-time appointments - like product launch events, play on scarcity to amplify user engagement
        - Recurring appointments - effective tools for habit-building and user immersion
      - Effects of ADs:
        - Rewards/consequences
        - Habit building
        - Anticipation/scarcity (octalysis)
      - Examples:
        - Daily login bonuses
        - Limited-time events
        - Farming games
        - Fitness apps
        - Happy hour at restaurants
      - Why they work:
        - Psychological triggers
        - Habit formation
        - Social reinforcement
        - Simplicity
    - Game Loop (structure that keeps game running/processing)
      - Sequence of steps that repeats continuously, processing input, updating game state, rendering changes on screen
      - What player does -> how game reacts -> what new decision player makes
        - Act - player does something (build house, invest money, etc)
        - React - game gives feedback (resources used, income rises, etc)
        - Evaluate - player interprets feedback (decides if that was a good use of money)
        - Decide - player chooses next move (save more?)
        - Repeat cycle
    - Persistent World (world exists/evolves when player offline)
      - World continues to exist, operate, and evolve independently of players' interaction with it
      - Lasting player impact: actions taken by players can have permanent effects on world that other players will experience
      - Social dynamics: persistent worlds are built to support many players simultaneously -> enables social structures like guilds, player driven economies, alliances, competitive/cooperative gameplay
      - Shared game state: all players experience the same state of the world, as maintained on servers through distributed systems -> consistency + persistence of data 
    - Gamification Fallacy (misconception that game components increase engagement)
      - Misconception that incorporating the PBL (points, badges, leaderboards) trifecta in a non-game context is sufficient to create an engaging/motivating experience. 
      - Misunderstanding of basic idea of gamification (implementing game components in non-game contexts to increase engagement + motivation)
      - Ignores complexities of human motivation, behavior, and emotional engagement 
        - Assumes that people will be motivated by the promise of rewards, rather than by intrinsic factors (autonomy, mastery, purpose)
      - Results in superficial gamification
        - Lacking depth - superficial rewards, not richly engaging experience
        - Overemphasis on extrinsic motivation - not fostering genuine interest in activity
        - Ignoring player's psychology - neglecting autonomy, self-efficacy, and emotional engagement 
        - Failure to create meaningful interactions - shallow interactions lacking substance/depth, leading to decreased engagement/motivation
    - Hedonic vs Utilitarian Gamification (2 different approaches/goals of gamification)
      - Hedonic - focus on fun experience, motivated through intrinsic rewards (satisfaction/entertainment/enjoyment)
        - Fantasy + storytelling
        - Emotional connections + social interactions
        - Novelty + surprise
        - Rewards/feedback that stimulate positive emotions
      - Utilitarian - focus on practical/functional experience to help users achieve specific goals/complete tasks efficiently, motivated through extrinsic rewards (benefits/savings/productivity gains)
        - Clear goals/objectives
        - Feedback + progress tracking
        - Rewards/incentives promoting efficiency + productivity
        - Tools + features that support task completion 
      - Key Differences
        - Motivation (hedonic = intrinsic, utilitarian = extrinsic)
        - Focus (hedonic = enjoyable, utilitarian = functional)
    - Stealth Assessment (covert endogenous assessment)
      - Type of endogenous assessment, specifically aimed at assessing player performance while player is unaware
      - Emphasis placed on not interrupting gaming experience, so game will continuously and discretely test the player while they are focused on play
      - Features
        - Invisible integration - assessments woven into game mechanics (reduces cognitive load, as player focuses on playing the game, not interrupted by assessments outside of gameplay)
        - Dynamic feedback - adjusts game's challenges based on player performance to support optimal learning within ZPD (zone of proximal development)
        - Focus on abstract skills - targets competencies that are typically hard to measure (creativity, resilience, conceptual understanding)
        - Evidence-based - leverages psychometric models + educational data mining to ensure validity and reliability
    - Digital Nudges (in-game guides to encourage/discourage specific choices)
      - Dynamic messages to player, informed by behavioral psychology to leverage how humans naturally think and behave
      - Help player navigate journey without coercion (goal is to make player feel smart, make them think they discovered the solution on their own)
      - Common nudges:
        - Social proof - "90% of players chose this path" (nudge players toward popular/recommended actions)
        - Progress indicators - XP bars (motivate players to finish tasks)
        - Salience - glowing items/icons (draw attention to important/time sensitive elements)
        - Goal priming - "complete this to unlock the boss fight" (motivate action by highlighting rewards/consequences
        - Time pressure - countdown timers/limited-time offers (create sense of urgency, encourage quick decision-making)
      - Why/how they work:
        - Cognitive load theory (CLT)
        - Loss aversion/FOMO
        - Progress bias/goal gradient effect
    - Decision Logs (records of player choices)
      - Help track how players think, what they prioritize, and how they solve problems 
      - Enables endogenous assessment 
      - Examples: 
        - Math Strategy Game: order of problem solving steps, method chosen (shows reasoning process, not just result)
        - History Simulation: policy choices in a role-playing scenario (captures historical reasoning, trade-offs)
        - Science Sandbox: hypotheses tried, variables tested (encourages scientific thinking and reflection)
        - Coding Game: paths through logic puzzles, debugging steps taken	(reveals computational thinking strategies)
    - ITS: Intelligent Tutoring Systems (AI personalized instruction/feedback)
      - Gamified interface ≠ necessary for classification as an ITS 
      - Key features:
        - Personalized scaffolding
        - Adaptive feedback
        - Instant data
        - Error diagnosis
        - Student modeling
      - Examples: Khan Academy, Duolingo, Synthesis Tutor
      - Benefits:
        - Greater learning outcomes
        - Increased engagement
        - Tailored experiences
        - Cost-effective + scalable
      - Challenges:
        - Data privacy
        - System biases
        - Need for continuous updates
    - Emergent Narrative (stories from player interactions, not prescribed by designers)
      - Story emerges from gameplay, created from player interactions with game's systems and mechanics, rather than being predetermined by game designers
      - Similar to branching narrative
      - Story not told TO the player, but emerges FROM the player's actions/decisions
      - What it's different from:
        - Embedded narrative - predetermined and delivered through cutscenes/dialogue/scripted events (this is what current FinLit simple games and most CompSims follow, unlike OWGs where players create their own adventure)
        - Explicit narrative - story clearly presented through game's mechanics
      - EN games rarely give players anything more than a basic premise and a few little moments along the way
